Distributed KV Cache for a Claude-Code-Style LLM Assistant

A staff+ NALSD walkthrough: 50 M DAU, λpeak = 100 K req/s, TTFT p99 ≤ 400 ms, max context 1 M tokens — built up in twelve bottleneck-driven iterations from a naive Llama-3-70B + TP=2 + B=1 anchor to a P/D-disaggregated fleet with paged blocks, prefix caching, continuous batching, speculative decode, context parallelism, fault tolerance, and a pooled cross-task KV store (a write-back victim cache for cross-turn conv K,V).

Cites K1 serving fundamentals / K2 KV cache / K17 attention kernels · impl detail in router.html / scheduler.html / kv_store.html · multimodal sibling: distributed_kv_cache_image.html · ~45 min

What this page is

TL;DR. This is the scenario page. It walks one Claude-Code-style fleet from naive to staff-plus by adding one technique per iteration, each justified by a measured bottleneck. Knowledge pages own the formulas and constants; this page cites them and turns them into a fleet sized in λpeak, $/mo, and tail-latency budget.

  • Is: the workload single-source-of-truth (§1.3), the bottleneck spine (§1.6), and a per-iteration sequence each with its why-now, design choices, number chain, what breaks, and SLO impact.
  • Is not: the per-formula derivations (those live in K1 / K2); the Router-internal data structures (live in router.html); the Scheduler-internal data structures and engine-loop (live in scheduler.html); the pooled kv-store-job interior — how PoolConvIndex and the Cuckoo membership filter are built, who gossips the filter to whom, and the exact lookup path (live in kv_store.html).
Number-source discipline. Every numeric on this page carries one of: [G] given (SLO / hardware constant / flagged assumption) · [G-FA] flagged assumption (would confirm in interview) · [G — K{N}] sourced from a knowledge page (cite, don’t restate) · [D] derived — formula and substituted values printed inline. Bare unsourced numbers are a defect.

1. Scenario & Requirements (S0)

Notation introduced in this section — the workload + hardware constants every later section cites. Full values/tags/sources in the tables of §1.3 / §1.5; this is the at-a-glance card.

λavg / λpeak
fleet arrival rate — avg 30 K / peak (the sizing target) 100 K req/s.
Thidden_sys / Tuser_sys_mean
prompt prefix lengths — platform prompt 4 K (identical fleet-wide) / per-user prompt 1 K tok.
Tconv_hist_median / …p99 / …p99.9
conversation history at admit — 8 K / 256 K / 1 M tok (log-normal tail).
Tuser_msg_mean
this-turn user message = 200 tok (the genuinely-new tokens on a warm turn).
Tin_mean
mean total prompt at admit = sum of the above ≈ 13.3 K tok.
Tout_mean / …p95 / …p99 / Tout_max
reply length — 400 / 1.5 K / 3 K / hard-cap 65 K tok.
intra_session_hit_rate
fraction of turns that hit the prior turn’s task = 0.80 (the reuse lever Iter 5 pulls).
DAU / avg_session_lifetime
daily active users ≈ 50 M (B2C; tenant = end user) / turn-1-to-last gap = 150 s.
active_concurrent_sessions_peak
live sessions at peak = λpeak×(1−hit_rate)×lifetime ≈ 3 M (Little’s law).
Fpeak / MFU
per-GPU peak BF16 = 989 TFLOP/s / sustained Model-FLOPs-Utilization = 0.40.
HBM / PCIe / NVMe / RDMA / NVLink BW
per-GPU HBM 3.35 TB/s; PCIe Gen4 25 GB/s; NVMe-GDS 7 GB/s; RDMA per flow 25 GB/s; NVLink-4 900 GB/s.
B1 … B12
the bottleneck spine — each is one constraint a named iteration retires (§1.6).
fleet_tasks
sizing convention = ⌈ λpeak / per_task_throughput × headroom ⌉ (no hard-coded task constant).

1.1  Functional shape & non-goals

What the fleet serves

  • HTTP/gRPC LLM-completion endpoint for a Claude-Code-style assistant: a chat / code-edit / repo-Q&A surface where each user turn carries a long conversation history.
  • Each prompt is composed of four segments: hidden system prompt (platform-owned, identical fleet-wide, ~4 K tokens), an optional user-tier system prompt (per-tenant, 0–2 K), the linear conversation history, and the current user message.
  • Linear chat only — no branching / forking. (Load-bearing for Iter 5’s flat-hash pick over a radix tree.)
  • Streamed output via Server-Sent Events (SSE) or gRPC server-stream.
  • Max end-to-end context: 1 M tokens; rare but supported — drives Iter 9 context parallelism.

Non-goals (named, not implicit)

  • Image / PDF / video attachments — LLM-only.
  • Branching / multi-turn forks — would force a radix tree (K2 #apc); linear chat picks flat-hash.
  • Per-tenant fine-tuned variants — shared base model only.
  • Tool execution — handled by the client; not in the serving page.
  • Cross-region / multi-metro — one-metro deployment.
  • Quantization — BF16 weights + BF16 KV throughout. INT8 / FP8 KV is a real production technique (K14) but explicitly out of scope here, so cost numbers are not lifted by a quantization win.

1.2  Non-functional requirements

DimensionTargetTagWhere it bites
TTFT (time-to-first-token) p99≤ 400 ms[G]Prefill compute · Iter 4 (HoL) · Iter 5 (skip prefill)
TPOT (time-per-output-token) p99 — a.k.a. inter-token latency (ITL)≤ 30 ms[G]Decode HBM bandwidth · Iter 6 (continuous batching) · Iter 7 (spec)
Streaming startfirst token within TTFT[G]SSE contract from Iter 0
Max context (input + output)1 M tokens[G]Iter 9 context parallelism
Max output (single request)65 K tokens[G]Extreme-tail decode budget · Iter 7
Avg QPS (λavg)30 000 req/s[G]Autoscale floor (Iter 1b)
Peak QPS (λpeak)100 000 req/s[G]Fleet sized at peak with headroom; peak-to-avg ≈ 3.3×
Multi-tenantshared model + per-tenant fairness[G]Iter 5 (content-only chained hash) · Iter 8 (token bucket)
Geographicsingle metro[G]One-metro RDMA assumptions in Iter 2 / Iter 3b
Durability / RPO0[G]KV is reproducible from prompt_tokens; recompute is the FT primitive (Iter 10)

1.3  Workload composition — single source of truth

Every λ, token count, and session-population number on this page is declared once here and cited downstream. If something downstream disagrees with this section, this section wins.

Reuse profile — the lever Iter 5 pulls

SymbolWhat it isValueTagSource
intra_session_hit_rateturn-N hits turn-1’s task’s KV (via Iter 1b CH-on-conv_id)0.80[G-FA]Flagged
DAUdaily active users — B2C consumer scale; each “tenant” is an end user~50 M[G-FA]Flagged; ChatGPT/Gemini-class
User-skewtop 10% of DAU → ~60% of active conv_idsconsumer skew[G-FA]Flatter than B2B Pareto (top 1% → 90%)
avg_session_lifetimetime between turn-1 and last turn of a conversation150 s[G-FA]Flagged
active_concurrent_sessions_avgconcurrent “live” sessions at λavg~900 K[D]30 000 × (1 − 0.80) × 150 = 900 000
active_concurrent_sessions_peakconcurrent live sessions at λpeak~3 M[D]100 000 × (1 − 0.80) × 150 = 3 000 000
users_with_active_conv_peakusers currently chatting at peak (~1 conv / user)~3 M[D]= active_concurrent_sessions_peak at ~1 conv/user
Derivation note for active_concurrent_sessions. Little’s law on the “new sessions” stream: arrivals_per_s × lifetime. Arrivals = λ × (1 − intra_session_hit_rate) because turns within a live session don’t open new sessions. At λpeak: new sessions arrive at 20 000 / s; live for 150 s → 3 M concurrent. This is the population Iter 5 pin sets are sized against and the Iter 3b pool QPS is sized for.

Fleet-sizing convention (no “770 tasks” constant)

The number of serving-prefill-job / serving-decode-job / router-job tasks is derived per-iteration from fleet_tasks = ⌈ λpeak / per_task_throughput × headroom ⌉; final fleet count materialises at S6 once every iteration has fixed its per-task throughput. Every per-task count on the page shows its derivation; the page never carries an unsourced fleet constant.

1.4  Flagged assumptions (would confirm in interview)

  • Conversation history is log-normal: median 8 K, p99 256 K, p99.9 1 M (drives Iter 9 fork threshold).
  • intra_session_hit_rate = 0.80.
  • Per-tenant user_sys mean length 1 K.
  • Output distribution: mean 400 / p95 1 500 / p99 3 000 / max 65 K.
  • Average session lifetime 150 s.
  • DAU ~50 M, B2C; top 10% of DAU → ~60% of active conv_ids.
  • Block size = 16 tokens (vLLM default; cited at K2).
  • MFU (Model FLOPs Utilization, per K1 §3.7) = 40% sustained on prefill TP=8 H100.

1.5  Per-task hardware envelope

One serving-*-job task = one bare-metal host. Specs are fleet-homogeneous; Iter 1a’s parallelism DC defends TP=8 against the alternatives below.

ComponentSpecTagWhy this number
GPU8× NVIDIA H100 SXM5 80 GB HBM3[G]DGX-H100 / HGX-H100 reference platform
Intra-task GPU interconnectNVLink 4 (900 GB/s per GPU)[G — K20]TP=8 AllReduce fits inside one NVLink domain
Sustained GPU FLOPs (BF16, MFU=40%)8 × 989 × 0.40 ≈ 3.17 PFLOP/s[D][G — K1 §3.1] + [G — K1 §3.7]
HBM BW aggregate (TP=8)8 × 3.35 TB/s = 26.8 TB/s[D]Decode HBM BW floor; [G — K1 §3.1]
CPU2× AMD EPYC 9554 (64 c / 128 t) or Xeon Platinum 8480+[G-FA]DGX-H100-class host
CPU RAM1 TB DDR5 ECC[G-FA]Iter 1a DRAM tier; ~800 GB usable after OS / scheduler overhead
CPU↔GPU busPCIe Gen 5 ×16 / GPU; design uses Gen 4 numbers conservatively (25 GB/s)[G — NEW_BOE]DRAM-tier transfer BW
Local SSD1× NVMe Gen 4 ~7 TB; 7 GB/s read via GPUDirect Storage[G-FA]Iter 1a SSD tier; single-NVMe sufficient (Iter 1a DC2)
Network8× NDR400 InfiniBand (400 Gb/s/GPU); ~25 GB/s effective per flow[G — K20]Cross-task RDMA — Iter 2 / Iter 3b

Why each envelope row is sized this way

  • 8× H100 NVLink-connected — the canonical DGX-H100 / HGX-H100 reference; TP=8 inside the NVLink domain is ~18× faster than TP=16 across NIC (cite K20).
  • 1 TB DRAM — ~600–800 GB usable after OS, leaves room for the DRAM tier (~conv-count derivation lives in Iter 3 multi-tier residency table).
  • 7 TB single NVMe — holds ~1.5 K active sessions worth of cached KV at workload-median size; matches the long-tail dormant population.
  • NDR400 IB — supplies the ~25 GB/s cross-task BW Iter 2 (gRPC stream of one request’s KV) and Iter 3b (pool fetch) both depend on.
  • Remote DRAM pool tier (L3, Iter 3b) — not per-task hardware: a separate kv-store-job reached over the NDR400 fabric above provides the shared cross-task cold tier — an RDMA-fronted DRAM front (L3-remote, ~25 GB/s, ~1–3 µs RTT) over an NVMe back (L5-remote). It ranks above where local NVMe used to sit — which DC7 retires, leaving the local stack HBM → DRAM only — because local tiers die with the task while the pool survives a reroute. Capacity = kv_store_job_task_num × per-task DRAM, sized at S6 (working-set re-derivation pending, §9b.4). Optional scale-up transport: CXL. Full spec: §2.3 jobs + Iter 3b.

1.6  Bottleneck spine — B1…B12

Each B-row is one constraint a later iteration eliminates. The arc in §0 is exactly this table read column-three left-to-right.

IDBottleneckEliminated by
B1Decode recomputes all prior K, V → O(L3) per requestIter 1a — KV cache
B2Decode at B=1 is HBM-bandwidth-bound, MBU ~1% (K1 §3.5)Iter 6 — continuous batching on decode pool (depends on Iter 3 paging + Iter 4 FA v2 foundation)
B3-capacityKV is the dominant HBM consumer on one taskIter 1a — HBM / DRAM / SSD tiering
B3-fragmentationContiguous slab over-allocates ~5.9× (workload mean) up to ~24× (p99-tail; vLLM reports ~22×) (K2 #paging)Iter 3 — PagedAttention
B4One task = no scale — can’t serve λpeakIter 1b — scale-out + Router
B5Shared prefix (hidden_sys + user_sys + history) recomputed every requestIter 5 — prefix caching
B6Long-prefill head-of-line (HoL)-blocks the prefill pool (at p99 = 256 K, βcold ≈ 65 s)Iter 4 — chunked + packed prefill on FA v2 varlen kernel
B7KV reuse is task-local; cache-oblivious LB would destroy any Iter 5 hit rateIter 1b — CH-on-conv_id (not least-loaded)
B8Whale conv_id / vnode-collision skew on the consistent-hash ringIter 1b — BLCH spill + autoscale on wait-time
B8bPer-user abuse / runaway-client flooding (B2C; not B2B hot-tenant skew)Iter 8 — token bucket + max-concurrent-conv cap
B9Prefill + decode rooflines colocated: fused step pays max(prefill_time, decode_time) with both resources under-saturatedIter 2 — P/D disaggregation
B10(i) Cross-pool KV transfer after P/D split; (ii) 1 M-context KV > one task’s HBMIter 2 (gRPC); Iter 9 (Context Parallelism)
B11Serving-task failure mid-flight loses local KVIter 10 — recompute-as-fallback on CH ring rebalance
B12Cross-turn conv K,V has no shared home after the P/D split — (i) the prior assistant output lives only on DⒶ (decode), so a warm prefill on a cold/rebalanced PⒶ must recompute the ~400-tok delta (§8.5, §11.6); (ii) long-tail-user user_sys cold prefill when reuse-recency LRU + ref_count can't keep it HBM-resident (the between-turns evicted tail + BLCH spill + autoscale-fresh + brand-new users). Load-bearing at consumer scale.Iter 3b — pooled KV store (write-back victim cache, conv_id-keyed, eviction + length-gated admission — NOT write-through). Introduced right after paging; at consumer scale (later) the user_sys working set overflows onto this already-existing store when HBM residency can't hold it.
—Cost-only: decode-pool $/req at vanilla autoregressiveIter 7 — speculative decoding (no SLO movement, pure $/mo)

2. Core entities & Borg-style topology (S1)

Notation introduced in this section — the entities (data structures + IDs), the Borg vocabulary, and the jobs. Full field shapes in the tables below.

Job / Task
Borg vocabulary — job = a logical service; task = one replica (one host / process group). Used consistently; never “node/server/pod”.
conv_id / tenant_id
conversation identity (the CH routing key) / end-user identity (B2C: tenant = user).
request_id / seq_id
one inbound request / one in-flight sequence on a task.
Request / Sequence
entities — the inbound shape (conv_id, tenant_id, prompt_tokens[], …) / the live decode unit (seq_id, phase, block_table[], …).
KV Block
entity = block_id, block_hash, ref_count, last_access, tier_locs — the cached-KV accounting unit (fields land across Iter 1a/3/5).
ConvIndex
This IS a map (per-task, local): conv_id → {block_ids[], total_tokens, tier_tag, last_turn_ts}; resolves tier-residency in O(1).
Conversation / RoutingDecision
Router-side sticky-owner map (conv_id → current_task_id) / one emitted route (request_id, target_task_id, ring_version, epoch).
Tenant / PoolConvIndex
tenant config (tier_class, user_sys_hash) / the pooled store’s primary map conv_id → {owning_pool_task_id, block_ids[], tier} (Iter 3b).
Jobs
router-job, serving-job (→ serving-prefill-job + serving-decode-job at Iter 2), broker-job, kv-store-job, L4 LB — each tied to the iteration that justifies it.
*_task_num
per-job replica counts (router_task_num, prefill_task_num, decode_task_num, kv_store_job_task_num) — derived per iteration, finalized at S6.

These are the field-by-field shapes referenced by downstream iterations and by the impl-plan’s Pythonic dataclasses (router.html / scheduler.html).

2.2  Borg-style topology vocabulary

Job = a logical service. Task = a single replica of that service (one host, one process group). The page uses these two words consistently — never “node / server / machine / instance / pod” as standalone shorthand for “where it runs.” A serving task is the TP=8 H100 host; eight GPUs jointly hold one model copy.

2.3  Jobs in the final design

Per-task counts are derived per iteration; final fleet count lands in S6. The “Introduced at” column ties each job to the iteration that justifies its existence.

JobPer-task hardwareWhat it runsIntroduced at
router-jobCPU-only; statelesscache-aware Router (CH-on-conv_id, BLCH spill, single-flight); router_task_num derived from per-request CPU budget × λpeakIter 1b
serving-job (unified)TP=2 H100 at Iter 0; TP=8 H100 from Iter 1aUnified scheduler + kernels; splits into serving-prefill-job + serving-decode-job at Iter 2Iter 0
serving-prefill-jobTP=8 H100 per taskPrefill scheduler + Flash Attention v2 varlen kernel (Iter 4); chunked + packed prefill; prefill_task_num sized at S6Iter 2
serving-decode-jobTP=8 H100 per taskDecode scheduler + Flash Decoding (Iter 6) + CUDA-graphs captured-set + SpecDecode draft+verify (Iter 7); decode_task_num sized at S6Iter 2
broker-job3 tasks (HA quorum)Redis Streams / NATS — ring-version gossip, sketch fan-out, heartbeat (cite at Iter 1b)Iter 1b
kv-store-jobRDMA-fronted, NVMe-backed (Mooncake-style)Cross-task pooled KV store for all cross-turn conv K,V (assistant deltas stranded on DⒶ by the P/D split + long-tail user_sys), conv_id-keyed. Runs as a write-back victim cache (admit on eviction + length gate — predictor-free; NOT write-through — write-through at λavg would cost ~3.84 TB/s of mostly-never-read ingest). Warm prefills pull the asst delta from here (Option 2) instead of recomputing it; the intra-request prefill→decode Hop (Option 0) coexists underneath. Lookup is a direct CH(conv_id, pool_ring) probe on local miss (an existence summary is an optional §S1 sidecar); kv_store_job_task_num = max(storage-sized, QPS-sized).Iter 3b
L4 LBmanaged (NLB / GCP L4)stateless forwarding to router-job; TLS termination; SSE-stream-aware connection tableIter 0 (before the Router; behind it at Iter 1b)

2.4  Parallelism strategy — foreshadowing Iter 1a DC3

The hardware spec in §1.5 names TP=8 as the serving task. That number is defended at Iter 1a DC3 against TP=2 / TP=4 / TP=16 / PP=2 / DP-only / EP — not assumed up-front. K6–K10 cover the parallelism mechanics; this page’s job is to pick a winner against this workload’s constraints.

3. API / interface contracts (S2)

Notation introduced in this section — the public contract fields + partition keys. Reuses conv_id / tenant_id / block_id from §2.1; the SLO names (TTFT, TPOT) are owned by §1.2.

POST /v1/conversations
mints a conv_id (the route key) — {tenant_id, user_system_prompt?} → {conv_id, created_ts}.
…/{conv_id}/messages
the hot path — one turn; streams SSE {token, idx} under the TTFT/TPOT SLOs.
user_sys_hash / version
content hash + version of a tenant’s user-system prompt (set via POST …/user_system_prompt; replicated to Routers).
Idempotency-Key / Retry-After
write-dedup header (5-min window per conv_id) / back-off hint carried on 429 (from the Iter 8 token bucket).
Partition keys
conv_id (Conversation → sticky decode task; Pool conv), block_id (KV Block → task-local), tenant_id (tenant config → replicated).

3.1  Public REST surface

Method + PathRequestResponseSLO
POST /v1/conversations{tenant_id, user_system_prompt?}{conv_id, created_ts}p99 ≤ 50 ms
POST /v1/conversations/{conv_id}/messages{role, content[], stream:true}SSE {token, idx} eventsTTFT p99 ≤ 400 ms; TPOT p99 ≤ 30 ms
GET /v1/conversations/{conv_id}—{turns[], created_ts}p99 ≤ 100 ms
DELETE /v1/conversations/{conv_id}—204p99 ≤ 50 ms
POST /v1/tenants/{tenant_id}/user_system_prompt{prompt_tokens}{user_sys_hash, version}p99 ≤ 200 ms
GET /v1/health—{status, version}—
  • Pagination: cursor-based on turns[]; cursor opaque (server-side state).
  • Idempotency: Idempotency-Key header on writes; 5-min dedupe window per conv_id (Router-side; details in router.html).
  • Error model: 4xx not retryable; 5xx retryable with exponential backoff; 429 Too Many Requests carries Retry-After from Iter 8 token-bucket.
  • Compatibility: URL-versioned /v1/; 12-month overlap when /v2/ lands.

4. Back-of-envelope — the ratios that pick design (S3)

Notation introduced in this section — the model constants + the two cost formulas every iteration substitutes into. The β family (per-request setup time by tier) has its own table at §4.0; this card covers the rest.

P / n_layers / d_model
Llama-3-70B constants — params 70×109 / layers 80 / hidden width 8 192.
n_kv_heads / d_head / bytes_per_element
KV heads 8 (GQA) / head dim 128 / BF16 = 2 B.
prefill_FLOPs(N)
= 2·P·N + 4·n_layers·N²·d_model — linear (weights) + quadratic (attention) prefill cost for N tokens.
prefill_time(N, TP)
= prefill_FLOPs(N) / F_sustained; at TP=8 the denominator is 3.17×1015 FLOP/s. This is βcold(N).
F_sustained
per-task sustained compute = TP × Fpeak × MFU = 3.17 PFLOP/s at TP=8.
KVper_tok
KV bytes per token = 2·n_kv_heads·d_head·n_layers·bytes ≈ 320 KB/tok (the canonical literal carried downstream).
s*
quadratic-crosses-linear context length = 6·d_model ≈ 49 K tok; above it prefill is attention-dominated.
BW paths
the dataflow bandwidths (§4.3): HBM 3.35 TB/s, HBM aggregate 26.8 TB/s, PCIe 25 GB/s, NVMe-GDS 7 GB/s, RDMA 25 GB/s, NVLink-4 900 GB/s.
K1-first rule. Every formula already present in K1 (serving fundamentals) or K2 (KV cache) is cited, not re-derived. Numbers that need a workload-specific substitution get their substituted equation shown inline.

4.0  Notation — the β family (per-request setup-time symbols)

TL;DR. Every β on this page is a per-request time the GPU spends preparing one request to produce its next token — the cost a queued request pays before it sees its first token (or, for βper_decode_tok, the gap between tokens). Different β subscripts name which tier or stage that prep work lives in. The same β-family powers the Iter 1a wait-time formula (approx_wait_time_ms = Σ over queue of β(tier_residency_for(r))) and is referenced symbolically throughout.

SymbolWhat it isFormulaLives whereFirst cited at
βHBM HBM-resident hit — the per-request setup constant when K/V are already in HBM. Just kernel launch + sampling setup; ~ms scale, near-zero compared to a re-prefill. ~ms constant (concrete value at compose) Iter 1a HBM tier §4.3 (BW table)
βDRAM(N) DRAM-tier hit — KV for the request lives in host DRAM; the prep work is the PCIe swap-in from DRAM to HBM. Linear in N. N × KV_per_tok / PCIe_BW Iter 1a DRAM tier Iter 1a wait-time formula
βSSD(N) SSD-tier hit — KV lives on local NVMe; prep is the GPUDirect-Storage read into HBM. Linear in N. N × KV_per_tok / NVMe_GDS_BW Iter 1a SSD tier Iter 1a wait-time formula
βcold(N) Cold — nothing cached anywhere: the request’s K/V do not exist on this task and must be re-computed from prompt_tokens via prefill. This is the prefill time. At N = Tin_mean = 13 K it is 730 ms on TP=8 (§4.1 table); at p99 N=256 K it blows up to ~67 s — the bottleneck Iter 4 chunked prefill addresses. prefill_time(N) (per §4.1) fallback when triple-tier (HBM/DRAM/SSD) misses §4.1 prefill table
βper_decode_tok Per-output-token gap during decode — not a per-request setup like the others, but the per-token tick time during decode. Listed here because it shares the notation; equals the time to re-read all weight bytes from HBM once (decode is BW-bound, K1 §3.5). weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU decode-pool every tick §5.4 (Iter 0 number chain)
βchunk Per-chunk prefill kernel time on the FA v2 varlen kernel (Iter 4) — the time to process one chunk of C tokens through all 80 layers; the unit of work the chunk-granular prefill scheduler interleaves across requests (Chunked → Packed → Varlen). Lands at Iter 4. βchunk = prefill_time(C) · at C = 4 K → 190 ms (linear-dominated; §4.1 table N=4K) [D] Iter 4 prefill scheduler Iter 4 wait-time formula (§10.5)
  • Why β instead of “latency”. “Latency” conflates queue wait, prefill, and decode. β isolates just the prep work for one request so it sums cleanly: wait_time(r) ≈ Σr′ ahead of r β(tier_residency_for(r′)). This is the routing signal the Iter 1b Router consumes.
  • Ordering. βHBM ≪ βDRAM(N) ≪ βSSD(N) ≪ βcold(N) across the realistic N range. The Iter 1a wait-time formula derivation shows the crossover: DRAM beats re-prefill at every N and SSD at N ≥ ~1.9 K (the crossover, §6.5); DRAM beats SSD by ~3.6× (25 GB/s vs 7 GB/s).

4.1  Per-request prefill compute — the quadratic term matters

K1 §3.3 owns the formula. The page repeats only the substituted numbers because the iteration sequence keeps citing them.

prefill_FLOPs(N) = 2·P·N + 4·n_layers·N²·d_model = 1.4·10¹¹·N + 2.62·10⁶·N² for Llama-3-70B: P = 70·10⁹, n_layers = 80, d_model = 8 192 [G — K1 §3.3] prefill_time(N, TP=8) = prefill_FLOPs(N) / (8 × 989 TFLOP/s × 0.40) = prefill_FLOPs(N) / 3.17·10¹⁵ FLOP/s [D]
N (tokens)Linear PFLOP — 2·P·NAttn PFLOP — 4·n_layers·N²·d_modelTotal PFLOPβcold(N) at TP=8Tag
1 K1.4e11 × 1e3 = 1.4e14 = 0.142.62e6 × 1e6 = 2.62e12 = 0.0030.1430.143 / 3.17 = 45 ms[D]
4 K (hidden_sys)1.4e11 × 4e3 = 5.6e14 = 0.562.62e6 × 1.6e7 = 4.2e13 = 0.0420.600.60 / 3.17 = 190 ms[D]
8 K (median conv)1.4e11 × 8e3 = 1.122.62e6 × 6.4e7 = 0.171.291.29 / 3.17 = 410 ms[D]
13 K (Tin_mean)1.4e11 × 1.33e4 = 1.862.62e6 × 1.77e8 = 0.462.322.32 / 3.17 = 730 ms[D]
32 K1.4e11 × 3.2e4 = 4.482.62e6 × 1.024e9 = 2.687.167.16 / 3.17 = 2.26 s[D]
256 K (p99)1.4e11 × 2.56e5 = 35.82.62e6 × 6.55e10 = 171.7207.5207.5 / 3.17 = 65.5 s[D]
1 M (p99.9)1.4e11 × 1e6 = 1402.62e6 × 1e12 = 2 6202 7602 760 / 3.17 = 871 s[D] → forces Iter 9 CP
  • Where the quadratic crosses linear. K1 §3.3: s* = 6 × d_model = 49 K. Below ~49 K, prefill is ≈ linear; above it, attention dominates — the table shows the crossover already in motion at 32 K (attention ≈ 60% of linear) and locked-in at 256 K (attention is ≈ 5× linear).
  • Why this drives Iter 9. The 871 s at 1 M context is > 2 000× the 400 ms TTFT SLO; no single-task TP=8 can amortise that prefill. Context parallelism (Iter 9) partitions the sequence dimension across CP ranks; cite K11 + K17 (Ring Attention).

4.2  Per-token KV size — K2 owns the formula

KV_per_tok = 2 · n_kv_heads · d_head · n_layers · bytes_per_element = 2 · 8 · 128 · 80 · 2 = 327 680 B [G — K2 D31] Canonical literal carried downstream: 320 KB/tok (the ~2.4% rounding sits well inside the workload uncertainty; labels like “CP fork ≈ 800 K tok at 256 GB” assume this rounded value)
ContextKV bytesTag
Tin_mean ≈ 13 K13 000 × 320 KB = 4.16 GB / req[D]
p99 conv = 256 K256 000 × 320 KB = 82 GB / req[D] (one full H100’s HBM)
p99.9 conv = 1 M1 000 000 × 320 KB = 320 GB / req[D] → CP fork at Iter 9

4.4  The naive design hits a wall at

Set the anchor — the cost shock Iter 1a is measured against. Full derivation in §5 (Iter 0); the formulas live here so the chain is auditable in one place.

iter0_req_per_s_per_task = 1 / req_time_iter0 [D] serving_task_num_iter0 = ⌈ λpeak / iter0_req_per_s_per_task ⌉ [D] $/mo_iter0 = serving_task_num_iter0 × $_per_TP2_task_per_mo [D] where $_per_TP2_task_per_mo = 2 GPUs × $_per_GPU_hr × 720 hr/mo = 2 × $12.29 × 720 = $17 698 / task / mo [G — K1 §3.10]

Every later iteration’s $ delta is measured against $/mo_iter0; the §5 substitution lands the absurd-on-purpose anchor number.

5. Iter 0 — Naive Design (the anchor)

Bottlenecks unaddressed (pre-design): every B-row in §1.6. This iteration’s job is to set the floor; later iterations are measured against this one’s req-time, fleet size, and $/mo.

Notation introduced in this section — the parallelism/batch knobs + the per-iteration number-chain metric family (every later iteration re-derives these with an _iterN suffix).

TP
tensor-parallel degree — GPUs jointly holding one model copy. TP=2 here (the smallest that fits 140 GB of weights); becomes 8 at Iter 1a.
B
concurrency — sequences in one fused step. B=1 here; the decode-side knob that becomes Bdecode at Iter 6.
c
start-of-decode cache length (= Tin_mean at admit) — the context a decode tick attends over.
weights_per_GPU(TP)
= P × bytes / TP — the per-tick weight read. 70 GB/GPU at TP=2, 17.5 GB/GPU at TP=8.
Load / Compute
the two sides of the decode tick; tick = max(Load, Compute) (K1 §3.5). Decode at B=1 is Load-bound.
βper_decode_tok
per-output-token tick = weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU — 20.9 ms at TP=2, 5.22 ms at TP=8.
MBU
Model-Bandwidth-Utilization — ~1% at B=1 (one weight read makes one token); the waste Iter 6 amortizes.
req_time
per-request wall time = prefill_time + Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok (the _iterN-suffixed metric family starts here).
req_per_s_per_task
per-task throughput = 1 / req_time — 0.089 here; the number every iteration lifts.
serving_task_num
fleet size = ⌈ λpeak / req_per_s_per_task ⌉ — the absurd ~1.13 M-task anchor.
$_per_GPU_hr / $/mo
price line = $12.29/GPU/hr; monthly fleet $ = tasks × GPUs/task × $_per_GPU_hr × 720.
L4 LB / L7 LB
ingress layers — L4 = transport (5-tuple, SSE-native, line-rate; CHOSEN); L7 = application (parses HTTP, buffers SSE).

5.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① HTTP/SSE — sync request; server streams tokens back over the same connection.
  • ② Round-robin fan-out — L4 LB picks the next serving-task on a counter; cache-oblivious by design (this is the B7 bug Iter 1b fixes).

5.2  Why we start here (the anchor, in numbers)

The anchor’s job

  • Set the floor every later iteration is measured against. No enabling priors; no design choices defended beyond “this is the smallest fittable shape.”
  • Why TP=2 and not bigger? Llama-3-70B in BF16 = 70·10⁹ × 2 B = 140 GB of weights. One H100 80 GB cannot hold it. TP=2 is the smallest shape that fits: weights sharded to 140 / 2 = 70 GB per GPU, leaving ~10 GB per GPU for activations + B=1 KV. Anything below this is impossible; anything above this is unmotivated until a workload number proves the extra hardware.
  • Why not start at TP=8? That would bake in Iter 1a’s conclusion before measuring it. The TP=8 pick is defended at Iter 1a DC3 against TP=2 / TP=4 / TP=16 / PP=2 with cache-aware reasoning; starting there would skip the comparison.
  • Why B=1? At B>1 with no cache, contiguous-slab layout (the only thing pre-PagedAttention) over-allocates HBM for zero throughput gain — decode is HBM-BW-bound and the per-tick weight read happens once whether B=1 or B=4. B>1 only earns its keep after PagedAttention (Iter 3) frees the HBM headroom and there’s any reused work to amortise (Iter 1a cache).

The cost shock as motivation (number derived in §5.4)

The substituted numbers below tell the “cost shock” story: at TP=2 with no cache and no batching, λpeak needs > 10⁶ tasks — not a typo. The absurdity is the point; it justifies every later iteration.

5.3  Design choices at Iter 0

DC1 — Parallelism shape

Problem: a 140 GB model must fit somewhere; one H100 has only 80 GB HBM.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • TP=1 — doesn’t fit (140 GB > 80 GB).
  • TP=2 — CHOSEN: smallest TP that fits (70 GB / GPU; ~10 GB headroom for activations + B=1 KV).
  • TP=4 / TP=8 — fittable but unmotivated at Iter 0; richer TP comparison deferred to Iter 1a DC3 (where cache + batching create the constraint that picks the winner).
  • PP=2 (pipeline parallelism, K8) — works mechanically but pays a pipeline bubble at small fleets; deferred to Iter 1a DC3 alternatives.
  • FP8 / INT8 weight quantization to fit on TP=1 — rejected: quantization explicitly out of scope per §1.1 non-goals; named here only for completeness so the reader can see it was considered.

Why TP=2 wins here:

  • Fittable minimum. The unique shape that fits under BF16.
  • No constraint yet. Every richer TP pick needs a constraint Iter 0 hasn’t measured.

DC2 — Concurrency (B)

Problem: how many sequences run in a fused step at once?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • B = 1 — CHOSEN: simplest; one request at a time per task.
  • B > 1 with contiguous-slab KV layout — worse: HBM pressure for ~0 throughput gain (decode HBM-BW-bound; weights re-read once per tick regardless of B per K1 §3.5). Compounds the pre-PagedAttention static-max reservation problem (B3-fragmentation).
  • B > 1 with paging — needs Iter 3 PagedAttention; not available yet.

Why B=1 wins here:

  • Worse on HBM. With no cache (B1) and no paging (B3-fragmentation), B>1 is strictly worse on HBM.
  • No upside. Rooflines don’t move — zero throughput gain.

DC3 — Ingress (L4 LB, even at Iter 0)

Problem: clients need a stable public endpoint; serving-tasks churn underneath.

First: what L4 vs L7 actually means.

LayerWhat it inspectsWhat it can route onSSE behaviourCost / latencyExamples
L4 LB (transport layer, OSI L4) IP + TCP/UDP port only; payload is opaque bytes 5-tuple hash; source-IP affinity Native — a TCP stream stays stitched to one backend for its lifetime; long-lived HTTP/SSE just works Line-rate (forwards at near-NIC speed); single-digit µs added latency AWS NLB · GCP TCP Network LB · Linux IPVS · MetalLB
L7 LB (application layer, OSI L7) Parses HTTP / gRPC headers + (optionally) body Path, host, header, cookie, method, gRPC service/method, body-content patterns Easy to break: many L7 LBs buffer until response-complete unless explicitly told to stream chunks; needs X-Accel-Buffering: no-style config or per-route streaming flags Parses every request — ~100s of µs per request; per-backend connection pools; full TLS state AWS ALB · GCP HTTPS LB · Envoy · NGINX · HAProxy (in HTTP mode)

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Direct client → serving-task — rejected: DNS / NAT brittleness; each task churn breaks every active client.
  • Managed L4 LB (AWS NLB / GCP L4) — CHOSEN: stable VIP, TLS termination, line-rate fan-out, SSE-aware by construction (TCP-stream-bound).
  • L7 LB (Envoy / NGINX) — rejected at ingress: (i) no header-based routing is needed yet (the Router lands at Iter 1b and does the L7-style decisions itself); (ii) L7 buffering defaults can stall SSE first-token until the response is complete — the exact opposite of what TTFT wants; (iii) extra parsing tax on every request at λpeak.
  • Custom load-balancer task — rejected: would re-implement what a managed L4 LB ships for free.

Why L4 LB wins here:

  • Line-rate + SSE-friendly. Forwards at λpeak; TCP-stream stitched to one backend.
  • Zero ops surface. Managed product, nothing to run.
  • Survives every iteration. Iter 1b inserts the Router behind it.
  • L7 logic moves to Router. CH-on-conv_id, BLCH spill, single-flight live at our application layer — cache-aware routing needs KV state per task, which a generic L7 LB doesn’t have.

5.4  Number chain (the substituted derivation)

Step 1 — per-token sustained compute at TP=2

TP=2 sustained FLOP/s = 2 × 989 TFLOP/s × 0.40 (MFU) = 791.2 TFLOP/s = 0.79·10¹⁵ FLOP/s [D] (TP=8 sustained = 3.17·10¹⁵ from §4.1 ⇒ TP=2 = 1/4 of TP=8, consistent with the §6 Iter 1a note)

Step 2 — prefill time for the mean request

prefill_FLOPs(Tin_mean) = 2.32 PFLOP (from §4.1 table row N=13 K) [D] prefill_time(Tin_mean, TP=2) = 2.32·10¹⁵ / 0.79·10¹⁵ = 2.93 s [D]

Step 3 — per-token decode time at TP=2 (K1 §3.5: tick = max(Load, Compute))

# Decode tick = max(Load, Compute) per K1 §3.5; we show both, then max picks Load. # Anchor: B = 1, c = Tin_mean = 13 K (start-of-decode cache length, §1.2). # --- Load side (weights-only shortcut; KV re-read ≈ 0 at B=1, see note) --- weights_per_GPU(TP=2) = (P × bytes_per_element) / TP = (70·10⁹ × 2 B) / 2 = 70 GB / GPU [D] Load(TP=2) ≈ weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU = 70 GB / 3.35 TB/s = 70·10⁹ / 3.35·10¹² = 0.0209 s = 20.9 ms [D] # Per-GPU form == aggregate form (the TP factor cancels — this is WHY TP buys linear decode speedup): # weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU = (total_weights / TP) / (HBM_BW_total / TP) = total_weights / HBM_BW_total # TP=2: 140 GB / 6.7 TB/s = 20.9 ms TP=8: 140 GB / 26.8 TB/s = 5.22 ms # More GPUs in the TP group raise aggregate HBM BW proportionally while total_weights stays fixed. # --- Compute side (weights pass + attention pass, K1 §3.5) --- # 2·P·B = new token through every weight, ×B requests (the 2× rule). # 4·B·n_layers·c·d_model = new Q attends over c cached K/V entries (QKᵀ + AV = two matmuls, MAC=2), per layer, per request. Compute(TP=2, B=1, c) = (2·P·B  +  4·B·n_layers·c·d_model) / (TP × F_peak) = (2 × 70·10⁹ × 1  +  4 × 1 × 80 × 13 000 × 8 192) / (2 × 989·10¹²) = (1.4·10¹¹  +  3.41·10¹⁰) / 1.98·10¹⁵ = 1.74·10¹¹ / 1.98·10¹⁵ = 0.0000879 s = 0.09 ms ← attention pass adds ~24% over weights pass at c=13 K [D] βper_decode_tok(TP=2) = max(Load, Compute) = max(20.9 ms, 0.09 ms) = 20.9 ms / tok (load-bound, MBU≈1%, compute idle 99.6% of the tick) [D] [G — K1 §3.5] # Notes: # (i) Load uses the weights-only shortcut. The full K1 §3.5 form adds B·c·KV_per_tok / (TP·HBM_BW), # which at B=1, c=13 K is 4.16 GB / 6.7 TB/s = 0.6 ms (+3% on Load) — elided here because # Iter 0 is B=1; the KV term only becomes load-bearing at Iter 6's Bdecode. # (ii) Total decode FLOPs for one request over Tout tokens (K1 §3.5 closed form, MAC=2 throughout): # 2·P·B·Tout + 4·B·n_layers·d_model · (Tout·Tin + Tout(Tout−1)/2) # — attention term linear in Tin, quadratic in Tout. Attention-vs-weights ratio # = (2·n_layers·d_model / P) · (Tin + (Tout−1)/2) — crossover where they equal # lands at Tin + Tout/2 ≈ P / (2·n_layers·d_model) = 70·10⁹ / (2 × 80 × 8 192) ≈ 53 K (see crossover.html#B2). # Workload mean (Tin=13 K, Tout=400) sits at ~25% of crossover; # Tout_p99=3 K at ~27%; Tout_max=65 K (hard cap, rare) at ~85%. # Compute side stays well below Load at every realistic Tin, Tout — Iter 0 is # load-bound across the whole workload distribution. (Long-output regime is L3 territory.) # # How we USE the 53 K crossover: # 1. Validates the weights-only TPOT shortcut above: workload sits at ~25% of it -> the attention # term is a minority of the tiny compute side -> Compute << Load -> TPOT = Load holds at EVERY # realistic context, not just the mean. # 2. Regime boundary: below 53 K, decode per-token cost is ~flat (a constant weight read, context # barely matters); above it the attention/KV term dominates and cost scales WITH context -> long- # context decode needs a KV-partition / split-attention fix, not a weight-BW one. This is where # decode strategy switches (the 1 M-context tail is exactly the above-crossover regime). # 3. Picks the throughput knob: below 53 K decode is weight-read-bound -> raise B to amortize the one # weight read; above it it is per-sequence-KV-bound -> batching stops amortizing, must shrink/partition KV. # Load-side twin: note (i)'s elided KV-read term crosses the weight read by the SAME physics -- # weights are a fixed cost amortizable across B; KV reads are per-sequence and grow with context.

Step 4 — request time and per-task throughput

req_time_iter0 = prefill_time(Tin_mean, TP=2) + Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok(TP=2) = 2.93 s + 400 × 20.9 ms = 2.93 + 8.36 = 11.29 s / req [D] # Per-task throughput = B / req_time (B = in-flight requests sharing one task's wall-clock). # B=1 at Iter 0 -> the task runs requests strictly serially: one holds the task for its full # 11.29 s lifetime (prefill -> all decode ticks -> finish) before the next admits. Hence B/req_time = 1/req_time. # (Once a later iteration batches decode at B>1, throughput decouples from 1/req_time.) iter0_req_per_s_per_task = B / req_time_iter0 (B=1) = 1 / 11.29 ≈ 0.0886 req/s/task [D]

Step 5 — fleet size at λpeak

serving_task_num_iter0 = ⌈ λpeak / iter0_req_per_s_per_task ⌉ = ⌈ 100 000 / 0.0886 ⌉ ≈ 1 128 700 tasks [D] equivalent GPUs = 1 128 700 × 2 ≈ 2.26 M H100 [D]

Step 6 — monthly cost (cite K1 $/GPU/hr line)

$_per_TP2_task_per_mo = 2 GPUs × $12.29 / GPU / hr × 720 hr / mo = 2 × $12.29 × 720 = $17 698 / task / mo [D] · [G — K1 §3.10] $/mo_iter0 = serving_task_num_iter0 × $_per_TP2_task_per_mo = 1 128 700 × $17 698 ≈ $19.97 billion / month [D]
Sanity check — this is meant to be absurd. $20 B/mo on one Claude-Code-style fleet is bigger than the GDP of a few small countries.

And the §5.4 chain is already cheating — it assumes the within-request KV cache (the cache the attention kernel ships with, per §5.5 B1 + K1 §3.5's with/without-cache matrix). Without that cache, every decode step would re-compute K, V for all prior positions, i.e. prefill(c+1) every output token:

req_timeno_cache = prefill_TTFT + Tout_mean × prefill_time(Tin_mean, TP=2)
                = 2.93 s + 400 × 2.93 s = ~1 170 s/req                              [D]
no_cache_per_task = 1 / 1 170 = 8.5·10−4 req/s/task                          [D]
no_cache_fleet    = ⌈ 100 000 / 8.5·10−4 ⌉ ≈ 117 M tasks → 234 M H100 → ~$2.07 T/mo  [D]

So the within-request cache already buys ~104× per-task throughput (and ~$2 T → $20 B / mo): §5.4's "absurd" $20 B is the residual after that win, not before it. The cache earns its keep before Iter 1a ever fires — Iter 1a's job is to make the cache an owned design surface (tiering, eviction) so the population that fits per task multiplies (~15× from SSD-to-HBM size ratio, §6.6).

The chain's other levers and their $ delta: Iter 1a TP=2→TP=8 = 4× single-task throughput (§6.6); Iter 5 prefix caching cuts mean prefill load ~86% across turns (§1.6 B5); Iter 6 batches decode by Bdecode×. Both the absolute figure and the precise vendor-pricing line are settled at S6 against the live pricing constant; the formulas above are the auditable contract.

5.5  What breaks (every B-row at once)

  1. B1 (cache not yet a design surface) — the §5.4 number chain implicitly uses the standard HBM-resident KV cache that ships with any production attention kernel (K1 §3.5’s decode max(load, compute) assumes it; that’s how we got TPOT ≈ 21 ms instead of recomputing K, V every step). What Iter 0 does not have is the cache as an owned design surface — no eviction policy, no tier policy, no tagged tier residency per block, no spill on watermark. Recovery: Iter 1a makes the cache explicit and tiers it to DRAM + SSD.
  2. B3-capacity (no tier) — HBM is the only KV residence; one request’s 4.16 GB at Tin_mean consumes ~half the ~10 GB / GPU headroom alongside 70 GB of weights; concurrency = 1 by construction. Recovery: Iter 1a tiers KV to DRAM + SSD.
  3. B4 (single-task = no scale) — even ignoring cost, one task can’t serve λpeak — 0.09 req/s &Lll; 100 K req/s. Recovery: Iter 1b scales out.
  4. B7 (cache-oblivious LB) — L4 round-robin sends turn-N of a conversation to a random task; the (eventual) cache hit rate falls to 1 / serving_task_num. Recovery: Iter 1b CH-on-conv_id via the Router.
  5. B9 (P/D colocated) — prefill is compute-bound and decode is BW-bound, but the same task pays max of the two on every tick. Recovery: Iter 2 splits the pools.
  6. B11 (no FT) — a task death loses every in-flight request’s state; client SSE drops mid-stream. Recovery: Iter 10 recompute-as-fallback on CH ring rebalance.
  7. (Every other B-row also applies; the seven above are the ones whose failure surface is visible at Iter 0’s steady state.)

5.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (no system)Post-iter (Iter 0)ΔNote
TTFT p99∞~2.9 s↓Fail: 7× over the 400 ms SLO — cold prefill at Tin_mean on TP=2.
TPOT p99∞~21 ms↓Meets 30 ms — decode is load-bound but TPOT is fine; the problem is throughput, not per-token latency.
Max-context supported0≈ 32 K (HBM-headroom-bound; per-req KV ∼ 10 GB)↓1 M not supported — Iter 9 lands much later.
Max-output supported0≈ 65 K (decode at 20.9 ms/tok → 22 min for max output)↓Mechanically supported; commercially impossible at the throughput floor.
Tenant fairnessn/a→→No mechanism — addressed at Iter 8.
$/mo at λpeak—~$20 B / mo↓Absurd-on-purpose anchor; every later iteration measures its $ delta against this row.

Iter 0 lands the floor — nothing meets SLO except TPOT, and even that is academic because per-task throughput is 11.3 s/req. Iter 1a (next) introduces the KV cache and tiering, picks TP=8, and turns the per-task numbers into something defensible at single-task scope.

6. Iter 1a — KV cache + HBM/DRAM/SSD tiering + TP=8 (single-task)

Bottlenecks addressed: B1 (cache correctness) · B3-capacity (HBM is the dominant consumer; tiering relieves it) · the TP=8 part of DC3 anticipates B2’s Bdecode headroom. Deferred: B4 + B7 + B8 (fleet topology → Iter 1b); B3-fragmentation (paging → Iter 3); B9 + B10 (P/D roofline → Iter 2).

Notation introduced in this section — the canonical routing signal + its β-by-tier inputs, the tier-residency lookup, and the eviction knobs.

approx_wait_time_ms(task_id, t)
the page’s one routing signal = Σ over queue ∪ running of β(tier_residency_for(r)) — expected delay before the next admit sees its first token. Local self-monitor here; Router-read from Iter 1b.
βHBM / βDRAM(N) / βSSD(N) / βcold(N)
per-request setup time by which tier holds the KV — HBM ~ms; DRAM/SSD = N·KVper_tok/BW; cold = re-prefill. Ordered βHBM ≪ βDRAM ≪ βSSD ≪ βcold (full table at §4.0).
queue(task_id,t) / running(task_id,t)
requests admitted-but-not-started / in-flight on a task at time t — the two sets the wait sum walks.
tier_residency_for(r)
O(1) ConvIndex lookup → which tier (HBM/DRAM/SSD) holds r’s prior KV, or cold.
Tgossip
signal recompute / broker-gossip cadence = 100 ms.
α + β·n
the full transfer-cost model — fixed per-transfer overhead α (RTT + setup) + size term β·n (β=1/BW); the βt(N) rows keep only β·n (α negligible locally, hypersensitive at crossovers).
Ncrossover (a.k.a. N*)
token count where a tier swap-in ties re-prefill — SSD vs cold ≈ 1.9 K tok; DRAM beats cold at every N (no positive crossover).
BWt
bandwidth of tier t (PCIe 25 / NVMe 7 / RDMA 25 GB/s) — the divisor in βt(N).
ref_count / last_access
per-block in-use counter (pin while > 0) / recency stamp — the coldness gate is last_access ∧ ref_count==0 ∧ session_idle.
low_watermark / high_watermark
the HBM/DRAM eviction band — spill starts at low, reclaims the non-pinned LRU tail up to high (a pair, anti-thrash).
T_swap
D2H eviction-coalescing window (~5 ms) — batch dirty blocks per transfer rather than per tick.
DWPD
NVMe Drive-Writes-Per-Day endurance budget — bounds the sustained SSD eviction rate.

6.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① HTTP/SSE — client to L4 LB; stable VIP, TLS terminate.
  • ② Round-robin — L4 LB to the one Iter 1a task (trivial; Iter 1b adds the Router and replaces this hop).
  • ┄┄ Dashed grey — asynchronous tier spill on HBM watermark / DRAM pressure. Discipline is non-cascading: a cold block (last_access ∧ ref_count==0 ∧ session_idle) skips straight to the cheapest adequate tier rather than walking every hop. Per-tier victim: HBM = LRU + ref_count pin (ref_count>0 never evicted); DRAM / SSD = plain LRU.
  • ┄┄ Dashed red — triple-tier miss → cold re-prefill fallback (pays βcold(N)).
  • Solid black on the right — promote on hit (the tier holding the KV ships it back into HBM before the next decode tick).

6.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • B1 is the textbook reason a KV cache exists at all. Without one, decode recomputes K, V over the full prompt at every step — req_time scales as O(L3) (per K1 §3.5: a decode tick that recomputes prefill at length c+1 grows linearly with c, summed over Tout tokens). The standard production attention kernel ships with HBM-resident KV — Iter 0’s number chain implicitly relied on this. Iter 1a’s job is to make the cache explicit in the design and start owning its eviction, tier policy, and routing.
  • B3-capacity flips next. At Tin_mean = 13 K, per-request KV = 13 000 × 320 KB = 4.16 GB. With 70 GB / GPU of weights (TP=2 baseline), only ~10 GB / GPU headroom remains — barely room for one or two requests’ KV alongside activations. Tiering creates the spill capacity that lets the task hold ANY concurrency, which Iter 6’s B_decode will then actually use.
  • TP=8 lands here, not at Iter 0. Iter 0 picked TP=2 because that was the fittable minimum. TP=8 is justified because (a) Iter 6’s B_decode demands per-GPU HBM headroom that TP=2’s ~10 GB can’t deliver; TP=8 gives ~56 GB usable / GPU after weights (17.5 GB) + activations (~6 GB at B=1); (b) TP=8 stays inside one NVLink-4 domain — TP=16 crosses to NIC AllReduce, ~50× collective-step latency (K20); (c) TP=4’s 35 GB / GPU weight share leaves less HBM headroom and halves aggregate BW vs TP=8. Full comparison at DC3 below.
  • Why not skip tiering and oversize HBM? Can’t — H100 is 80 GB / GPU, fixed in this hardware era. Either shard weights wider (TP=16+ pays NVLink-domain penalty) or tier. DRAM (1 TB / host) and SSD (7 TB / host) are already on the box; using them is free, and not using them is a choice to pay βcold(13 K) = 730 ms on every cache miss vs βDRAM(13 K) = 166 ms (4.4× gap; see §6.5 crossover table).
  • Why NOT also do scale-out here? B4 / B7 / B8 (multi-task routing, cache-stickiness, skew) are a different bottleneck family. Splitting 1a (single-task capacity + correctness + TP pick) vs 1b (fleet topology) keeps each iteration’s design choices defensible against one concrete constraint at a time.

6.3  Why split Iter 1 (1a vs 1b)

  1. 1a = per-task structure: what one serving-task looks like internally — the KV cache, the tier hierarchy, the parallelism degree, and the local wait-time self-monitor.
  2. 1b = fleet topology: how the tasks compose under a Router — consistent hashing, BLCH-spill skew handling, autoscale signal. 1b consumes the same wait-time number 1a introduces — one signal, two consumers, two horizons.
  3. The split lets each iteration carry a tight bottleneck citation (1a → B1 + B3-cap; 1b → B4 + B7 + B8) and lets the wait-time formula land at 1a (as a local self-monitor) before becoming a routing signal at 1b. Monolithic “Iter 1 does six things” would hide every individual constraint.

6.4  Design choices

DC1 — Cache layout

Problem: how is KV laid out in memory? One contiguous slab per request, or paged blocks?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Contiguous per-request slab — CHOSEN at Iter 1a: each admitted request reserves a contiguous KV slab sized to its max_reservation_tokens; standard pre-PagedAttention layout. Simple allocator (bump-pointer); zero block-table indirection.
  • Paged block pool (vLLM-style 16-token blocks) — deferred to Iter 3: bundling it here would hide the layout decision under the cache headline. Iter 3 (PagedAttention) is the iteration whose bottleneck citation (B3-fragmentation) demands paging; surfacing it now would not be defensible against a single constraint.

Why contiguous slab wins at Iter 1a:

  • Capacity solved by tiering. B3-capacity (“HBM is the dominant consumer”) is addressed by tiering, not paging.
  • Fragmentation not yet binding. Static-max reservation wastes ~5.9× workload-mean / ~24× p99-tail (vLLM reports ~22×), but only becomes the dominant gap after Iter 6 wants B_decode on the decode pool.
  • One bottleneck per iteration.

[param] KV storage tiers: HBM-only → HBM + DRAM + NVMe-SSD — Iter 1a. Why: HBM-only sends every eviction to βcold (730 ms at 13 K, ~2× over the TTFT SLO); adding DRAM (βDRAM 166 ms) + single-NVMe SSD (βSSD 594 ms) keeps evicted KV ~4× faster than re-prefill on hardware already in the box. (Local NVMe is later retired at Iter 3b — see that [param].)

DC2 — Storage hierarchy

Problem: where does KV live when HBM fills up? The whole tier ladder, or just HBM with re-prefill on eviction?

Possible solutions enumerated (each option is a strict superset of the one above it on capacity):

  • HBM only: every eviction falls to βcold(N) re-prefill. At Tin_mean = 13 K, 730 ms per miss — one cache miss already overshoots the TTFT p99 SLO by ~2×. Rejected.
  • HBM + DRAM: covers the warm L2; the PCIe Gen 4 DRAM swap-in is ~4.4× faster than re-prefill at workload mean (βDRAM(13 K) = 166 ms vs βcold = 730 ms). Useful, but with ~800 GB usable host DRAM and ~2.69 GB per warm conv (8.4 K tok = median 8 K history + 400-tok reply; §9.4 basis), ~300 conv KVs fit — well short of the long-tail dormant population.
  • HBM + DRAM + single-NVMe SSD — CHOSEN: covers the cold L3 at NVMe-Gen-4 7 GB/s via GPUDirect Storage. βSSD(13 K) = 594 ms (still beats βcold = 730 ms by ~19% at workload mean; the gap grows to 5.6× at p99 = 256 K because βcold has a quadratic term and βSSD is linear). Per-task SSD holds ~2.4 K conv KVs at median size.
  • HBM + DRAM + 4-NVMe-striped SSD: 4× the SSD BW (~25 GB/s aggregate via RAID/RAID-like striping). Over-engineered at this workload — single-NVMe IOPS budget covers the expected spill rate per §6.7 NVMe-DWPD calculation; rebuild + parity cost of 4-stripe doesn’t justify itself until cache thrash dominates. Flagged for re-evaluation at scale-up.

Why the three-tier ladder wins:

  • Beats re-prefill. Every tier wins at N ≥ ~1.9 K (§6.5 crossover table).
  • Free hardware. Zero incremental HW — DRAM and NVMe are already on the box (§1.5).
  • Natural eviction order. The BW gradient (HBM 3.35 TB/s → DRAM 25 GB/s → SSD 7 GB/s) sets it.
  • 4-NVMe deferred. Worth it only once SSD residence becomes the next bottleneck — not yet.

[param] TP (tensor-parallel degree): 2 → 8 — Iter 1a. Why: TP=2’s ~10 GB/GPU headroom can’t hold Iter 6’s Bdecode active slots; TP=8 gives ~56 GB/GPU usable + 26.8 TB/s aggregate HBM BW and stays inside one NVLink-4 domain (TP=16 crosses to NIC AllReduce, ~50× slower). Cliffs on both sides → 8. Full enumeration below.

DC3 — Parallelism degree (deferred from Iter 0)

Problem: how many GPUs per task? Picked under three simultaneous constraints — (i) weights must fit; (ii) per-GPU HBM headroom must support Iter 6’s B_decode; (iii) collective AllReduce must stay inside one NVLink domain.

Possible solutions enumerated (seven axes; the chosen winner is defended against each):

  • TP=2 (Iter 0 baseline): fits (70 GB / GPU) but leaves ≤ 10 GB / GPU headroom for activations + KV — can’t hold Iter 6’s B_decode active slots in HBM. Half the per-task FLOPs and HBM BW of TP=8. Rejected on headroom.
  • TP=4: 35 GB / GPU weights → ~35 GB / GPU usable; better than TP=2 but still half the per-task aggregate BW of TP=8. The βper_decode_tok gap follows straight from the load-bound decode tick βper_decode_tok = weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU (§5.2), with weights_per_GPU = (P × 2 B) / TP = 140 GB / TP and per-GPU HBM BW fixed at 3.35 TB/s: TP=4 → 35 GB / 3.35 TB/s ≈ 10.5 ms / tok vs TP=8 → 17.5 GB / 3.35 TB/s ≈ 5.2 ms / tok — a clean factor of 2×, because doubling TP halves each GPU’s weight shard while per-GPU HBM BW is unchanged and all TP GPUs stream their shards in parallel (wall-clock tick = per-GPU shard / per-GPU BW). Both clear the 30 ms TPOT SLO at B=1, but the higher 10.5 ms floor hits that ceiling at a much lower Bdecode once Iter 6 batches decode (the batch’s KV re-reads stack on top of the weight load), throwing away decode-throughput headroom. Rejected on BW.
  • TP=8 — CHOSEN: 17.5 GB / GPU weights → ~56 GB / GPU usable for KV; one NVLink-4 domain (900 GB/s/GPU all-to-all); aggregate 26.8 TB/s HBM BW + 3.17 PFLOP/s sustained. Sweet spot on all three constraints. K-cites: K6/K7.
  • TP=16: 8.75 GB / GPU weights (lots of headroom!) but crosses one NVLink-4 domain into NIC-mediated AllReduce; per-layer collective latency penalty ~50× (K20). For a 70 B model with 80 layers, this destroys TPOT. Rejected on collective.
  • PP=2 (pipeline parallelism, K8): two pipeline stages on 4 GPUs each; pipeline bubble at small fleets means a constant tax on req_time = (pp_stages − 1) × stage_time. PP is more useful with large micro-batches — not the regime here (B=1). Rejected on bubble.
  • EP (expert parallelism, K9): N/A — Llama-3-70B is a dense model; no MoE experts to route.
  • DP (data parallelism at the fleet level, K10): orthogonal — each task here IS a DP replica. The number of replicas is sized by the Router at Iter 1b, not at Iter 1a. Deferred.
What “one NVLink-4 domain (900 GB/s/GPU all-to-all)” means. NVLink is NVIDIA’s direct GPU-to-GPU interconnect; NVLink-4 is the generation on the H100. An NVLink domain is the set of GPUs wired together through on-board NVSwitch chips into a single all-to-all fabric — every GPU can talk directly to every other GPU in the domain at full link speed, with no NIC or network hop in between. On the DGX/HGX-H100 board that is exactly 8 GPUs (§1.5 hardware row), and each GPU gets 900 GB/s of aggregate NVLink bandwidth to the rest of the domain (18 NVLink-4 links × 50 GB/s bidirectional each).
  • Why TP cares. Tensor parallelism does an AllReduce across all TP GPUs after every layer (80 layers, on every decode tick) to sum each GPU’s partial output. That collective runs at NVLink speed (900 GB/s) only while all TP GPUs sit in one domain — it never touches the slow HBM-load path; it is a separate, additive cost on top of βper_decode_tok.
  • The cliff at TP=16. 16 GPUs span two boards, so the AllReduce must cross the NIC over RDMA/InfiniBand (~25–50 GB/s per flow) instead of NVLink — a ~50× per-step collective penalty ([G — K20]). At 80 layers × one AllReduce/layer × every output token, that tax lands squarely on TPOT and is fatal. This is why the NVLink-domain size (8 on H100) is a hard ceiling on TP — independent of whether the weights would “fit” at TP=16.

Why TP=8 wins:

  • Three constraints converge. Weight fit, HBM headroom for Bdecode, and NVLink-domain locality all push toward the same number.
  • Cliffs on both sides. Above TP=8 the NVLink boundary dominates; below it, per-GPU HBM headroom shrinks below what Iter 6 needs.
  • A curve intersection, not a guess. The unique point where three curves meet.
  • Survives hardware churn. An H200 (141 GB / GPU) might shift this to TP=4, but the NVLink-domain ceiling at 8 GPUs keeps TP≤8 across generations.

DC4 — Batch size (concurrency, B)

Problem: with the KV cache now correct (B1) and HBM relieved by tiering (B3-capacity), can we raise B above 1 at Iter 1a? The Iter 0 pick was B=1 against a different constraint set (no cache, no tiering); the constraints just changed, so the decision deserves a fresh look.

What “slot” means in this page. A slot is a single parallel lane in the GPU’s active batch — if B = 4, the task has 4 slots and runs 4 sequences in lockstep through one kernel launch. Each slot is a pre-allocated KV memory carve-out in HBM: in the contiguous-slab world (Iter 1a) it’s a fixed-extent contiguous region; in the paged world (Iter 3) it becomes a logical handle to a list of paged blocks. One slot holds at most one in-flight request at a time, for that request’s full lifetime (admit → prefill → all decode ticks → finish). Slots are reusable across requests over time — when a request finishes, its slot is freed and the next admitted request takes it. So B is the count of concurrent in-flight requests, and the per-slot HBM cost is what bounds it. Symbolically: HBM_for_KV ≥ B × per_slot_HBM. The DC below is about how big per_slot_HBM has to be under the current layout, which sets the ceiling on B.
Why B is bounded by HBM only — even though the cache is tiered. per_slot_HBM counts HBM, not DRAM/SSD, because “tiered” and “in the active batch” are two different states of a KV:
  • Compute can only touch HBM. The attention kernel reads the entire K,V history of an in-flight sequence from HBM on every decode tick (3.35 TB/s); there is no “decode directly from DRAM/SSD” path — PCIe DRAM (25 GB/s) and NVMe SSD (7 GB/s) are 134× / 479× slower and not in the kernel’s address space. So a request occupying a slot must hold its full KV resident in HBM for its whole lifetime (admit → prefill → all decode ticks → finish). That is why per_slot_HBM is an HBM cost.
  • The tiers hold KV for conversations that are NOT in-flight. Between turns (a user reads turn 5’s reply for 30 s before sending turn 6) the conversation isn’t computing and isn’t in a slot — its KV is parked in DRAM/SSD to dodge βcold re-prefill. The instant turn 6 is admitted, that KV is swapped back into HBM (paying βDRAM/βSSD once) and again counts against B.
  • The two axes are orthogonal. Tiering scales the retained conversation population (cache hit rate, fewer cold re-prefills — the dormant long tail); B is the count of sequences computing simultaneously, a pure HBM residency/bandwidth question. OS analogy: HBM slots are CPU cores running threads; DRAM/SSD tiers are swap — swap lets you retain many processes, but the number you can run at once is bounded by cores, not swap.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • B=1 — CHOSEN (still): one in-flight request per task. Carries the Iter 0 default forward against the new constraint set.
  • B>1 colocated (one unified serving task running both prefill and decode) — rejected, but not on HBM. The binding constraint is prefill TTFT:
    • HBM check — three regimes. Per-slot reservation = (prompt_len(r) + max_new_tokens(r)) × KV_per_tok, set at admit (contiguous slab can’t extend mid-decode — reserve worst-case-for-this-admit upfront, hold until the request finishes). HBM-for-KV budget per TP=8 task ≈ 450 GB (§9.4 derivation). Why actual-Tin but max-Tout — the two terms differ in what’s knowable at admit: the prompt length is known (you observe it on arrival), so each regime below reserves the request’s real prompt_len; the output length is unknown until the model emits EOS, and since the slab can’t grow mid-decode, the only safe reservation is the hard generation cap Tout_max = 65 K (the one value the request can’t exceed). Reserving Tout_mean = 400 instead would OOM the ~half of requests that run past the mean — a mean is not a ceiling. At the workload mean that forces 65 K of output room for ~400 actual tokens (~160× output-term over-allocation) — exactly the waste the later paging fix removes by allocating per actual token. The B cap depends on admit length:
      • Mean admit — Tin_mean + Tout_max = 13 K + 65 K → 78 K × 320 KB ≈ 25 GB/slot → B=18 fits.
      • p99 admit — Tin_p99 + Tout_max = 256 K + 65 K → ~103 GB/slot → only B≈4 fits.
      • Max admit — Tin_p99.9 + Tout_max = 1 M + 65 K → ~341 GB/slot → fits at B=1 only (⌊450 / 341⌋ = 1 slot — 76% of the 450 GB KV budget, leaving no room for a second slot; OOMs at B=2).
      Pre-PagedAttention engines worked around this with admission control (reject long prompts) or hard context caps — neither survives the 1 M tail. Iter 3 paging eliminates reservation entirely (pay per actual tokens, not worst-case). For this DC, the workload-mean regime is what matters: HBM does not bind at B=18 — prefill TTFT does (next).
    • Prefill TTFT scales linearly with B — this is the binding constraint. The prefill compute formula is Compute = 2 · params · tokens / (TP × Feff) [G — K1 §3.3]; batching B sequences in one prefill kernel multiplies tokens by B, so prefill_time(B, N) ≈ B × prefill_time(1, N) on the linear term (attention’s quadratic term adds on top, but doesn’t change the conclusion):
      prefill_time(B=1, Tin_mean=13 K, TP=8) = 730 ms [D — §4.1] prefill_time(B=2, Tin_mean each) ≈ 2 × 730 ms = 1.46 s prefill_time(B=18, Tin_mean each) ≈ 18 × 730 ms = 13.1 s TTFT SLO = 400 ms [G — §1.2] ⇒ already missed at B=1 cold; B=2 misses by 3.7×; B=18 by 33×.
      Even ignoring the SLO miss, doubling B doubles TTFT for every request in the batch — the linear-in-B regression is structural. So prefill is locked at B=1 independent of HBM.
    • Decode at B>1 would be a real throughput win. Decode tick is HBM-BW-bound: the per-tick weight read happens once regardless of B (K1 §3.5), so raising B amortizes that read across B sequences → MBU climbs from ~1% (B=1) toward ~B% (until BW or compute saturates); per-tick TPOT roughly unchanged at small B; per-task token-throughput scales ~linearly with B. Decode wants B↑.
    • Colocated, prefill and decode pull in opposite directions — and prefill wins. Every fused step pays max(prefill_time, decode_time); at any B>1 the step is dominated by the linear-in-B prefill cost, throwing away the decode amortization win. The unified task can only honor TTFT by holding B=1 — but holding B=1 means decode keeps wasting HBM BW. This is exactly the roofline asymmetry that motivates Iter 2 (P/D disaggregation): split the pools so prefill can keep B=1 (TTFT-safe) and decode can independently raise B to consume the HBM-BW headroom.
  • B>1 on a paged-block KV layout — deferred, not rejected: paged blocks let HBM track actual length per slot, not worst-case reservation (recovers the 6× over-allocation). But the layout fix doesn’t change the prefill-TTFT-linear-in-B story above — paging unlocks the decode-side B>1 only, which is the Iter 2 + Iter 3 + Iter 6 sequence: split the pools (Iter 2) so the decode pool can be reasoned about in isolation, fix HBM accounting on it (Iter 3), then raise B (Iter 6).

Why B=1 wins at Iter 1a:

  • Prefill TTFT binds. Colocated, the fused step is prefill-TTFT-bound at any B>1, throwing away the decode throughput win.
  • Not HBM. HBM (at B=18, workload mean) is not the constraint — TTFT is.
  • Fix is the P/D split. Iter 2 disaggregation keeps prefill at B=1 (TTFT-safe) and lets decode raise B (BW-amortization).
  • Sequenced after. Post-split the decode pool hits HBM-accounting (static-max reservation OOMs p99-context slots, §1.6 B3-fragmentation) → Iter 3 (PagedAttention) fixes it, then Iter 6 (continuous batching) consumes the freed headroom as B_decode.
  • One bottleneck per iteration.

DC5 — Per-tier eviction policy (which resident block leaves under pressure)

Problem: DC2 said where KV spills; it did not say who gets chosen as the victim when a tier hits its watermark — and whether the rule is the same on every tier.

Possible solutions enumerated (the two chosen per-tier rules, then the rejected alternatives):

  • HBM = LRU (Least Recently Used) + ref_count pin — CHOSEN: two questions, two mechanisms. ref_count answers “may I evict this at all?” — a block still pointed at by a live or shared sequence (ref_count > 0) is pinned, never a victim, regardless of recency. This is a correctness pin: a hot shared-prefix block is read once at prefill, not on every decode tick, so its last_access looks old → pure LRU would pick it and corrupt every sequence whose block-table points at it (dangling entries / mass recompute). LRU then only orders the evictable (ref_count == 0) tail. ref_count is maintained CPU-side (scheduler / block-manager, never the GPU): +1 on fresh allocation or prefix-match reuse, −1 on EOS / preemption / abort, atomic.
  • Local DRAM / SSD = plain LRU — CHOSEN: recency alone picks the victim; these tiers hold cold, not-actively-referenced KV, so no extra machinery is warranted (ref_count still pins any demoted shared block).
  • LFU (Least Frequently Used) for SSD wear: write churn is set by the demotion (admission) rate, not the victim choice — the policy picks which block drops, not how many get written in. LFU’s wear win needs a re-access-frequency signal that per-conv session-tail KV lacks; shared-prefix popularity is served at a later iteration by reuse-recency LRU + ref_count (a hot shared prefix is re-hit constantly, so it rides the LRU front), not an SSD policy. Plain LRU is simpler and no worse.
  • TTL (Time To Live) as a cache policy: KV is immutable (no staleness), capacity-LRU already bounds each tier, and writes are admission-driven so a TTL only adds churn. Its one real use — privacy / retention — is an optional cross-tier retention_TTL sweep (the §S3 sidecar), not a cache knob.
  • DRAM KV quantization (FP8 / INT4): out of scope — BF16 KV throughout (§1.1).

Why LRU + ref_count on HBM, plain LRU below:

  • Two questions, two mechanisms. ref_count = “evictable at all?” (correctness); LRU = “which evictable one first?” Conflating them corrupts shared prefixes.
  • The pin is load-bearing only on HBM. Active decode + hot prefix-sharing live there; lower tiers hold demoted/cold blocks → plain LRU suffices.
  • Churn is admission-driven. The victim policy doesn’t set write volume, so LFU buys no wear win — and the session-tail workload has no frequency signal to feed it.
  • No TTL, no quant. Immutable KV + capacity-LRU leave a cache-TTL nothing to do; compression is a separate, out-of-scope lever.

How ref_count + last_access + session_idle actually gate a victim, and how the watermarks that drive these policies are sized, is built out in cache_eviction_policy.html.

DC6 — Eviction discipline: strict cascade vs non-cascading tier-skip

Deep dive: the full demotion timing (when HBM→DRAM fires, who the victim is, how many leave), the DRAM→SSD pre-offload watermark derivation (sized from SSD write BW × batch-KV statistic × per-step latency), and the drop-to-recompute loss path live in cache_eviction_policy.html. This DC names the discipline; that page builds the mechanics and the numbers.

Problem: when HBM hits its watermark and a cold block must leave, does it walk down every tier one step at a time (HBM → DRAM → SSD), or skip straight to the cheapest tier it could ever be read back from?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Strict cascade: spill HBM → DRAM → SSD one hop at a time, dragging every cold block through every tier. Wastes DRAM and SSD residency on blocks that will never be read back from those intermediate tiers — a block headed for the cold floor still occupies a warm-DRAM slot on the way.
  • Non-cascading tier-skip — CHOSEN: a cold block skips straight to the cheapest adequate tier (or is discarded for recompute on the rare later miss). The eviction-candidacy test — the coldness gate, reused everywhere downstream — is a three-signal AND, all of which must hold: last_access old (recency) AND ref_count == 0 (no live or shared-prefix sequence still points at it) AND session_idle (the owning conversation has been quiet long enough its KV is unlikely to be touched this turn). Recency alone is insufficient: a ref_count > 0 block is in active use no matter when it was last read.
Who writes each coldness signal, and when — all three are maintained CPU-side; the GPU only runs attention over whatever the block table points at, so it never maintains this metadata:
  • last_access (per block) — recency timestamp. Updated when: the CPU scheduler stamps it = now on every engine step, for each block its batch touched that step (fresh allocation, a prefix-match reuse, or an active-decode read). By whom: the CPU scheduler / block-manager. It records “this block belonged to a scheduled sequence this step” — a coarse, CPU-visible proxy for recency, since the GPU’s per-tick HBM reads are never individually observed.
  • ref_count (per block) — live-reference count (DC5). Updated when: +1 on fresh allocation or prefix-match reuse, −1 on EOS / preemption / abort. By whom: the CPU scheduler / block-manager; atomic.
  • session_idle (per conversation) — a derived predicate, not a stored counter: evaluated as now − last_turn_ts > Tidle at eviction-candidacy time. It is backed by last_turn_ts (the conversation’s last-activity stamp), updated when a new turn for that conv is admitted (reset to now) and again on that turn’s decode completion. By whom: the CPU scheduler / Router; the GPU never touches it. Tidle is a tunable idle threshold, set well above the inter-turn reading gap so an actively-conversing user’s KV is never judged cold.

Why tier-skip wins:

  • Don’t pay residency you’ll never read. Forcing a cold block through DRAM and SSD burns capacity in tiers it will never be served from; skipping to the cheapest adequate tier frees those slots for genuinely warm KV.
  • The coldness gate is an AND, not an OR. The three-signal conjunction is what makes the skip safe — it never evicts a pinned or actively-referenced block just because its timestamp looks old.
  • One gate, reused. The same last_access ∧ ref_count==0 ∧ session_idle test is the eviction-candidacy primitive every later tier and policy builds on.
Not yet available here — clean-drop. A cheaper move — free an HBM slot with no copy-down at all because a clean copy already exists below — is not possible at Iter 1a: it needs a dirty bit plus a global, content-addressed block index that only a later iteration’s key scheme provides. Here a tier holds a single canonical copy, so there is no provable clean copy below to drop. The matching “is this block worth writing down vs. discarding?” admission test also belongs to a later iteration; at Iter 1a a cold block simply tier-skips down or is recomputed on the rare miss.
Sidecar (not inherited). A learned P(continue) / LPC (Learned Prefix Caching) predictor can order session-tail eviction better than raw LRU — an optional upgrade, the §S2 sidecar. The main line, and every later iteration’s state and numbers, assume pure LRU; nothing downstream depends on it.

6.5  Wait-time formula — the canonical routing signal

TL;DR. Iter 1a introduces the page’s single canonical routing signal, approx_wait_time_ms. At Iter 1a it’s a local self-monitor (one task watches its own queue); at Iter 1b the Router reads it from every task. Same number, two consumers, two horizons.

The β family — per-request setup time by tier residency

β(r) = the per-request setup time the GPU pays getting r ready to produce its first token. Which β applies is set by which tier holds r’s KV (or fallback to re-prefill).

SymbolFormulaSubstitution source
βHBM~ms-scale constant (HBM-resident; kernel launch + sampling setup)—
βDRAM(N)N × KV_per_tok / PCIe_BW[D] §4.2 + §4.3 PCIe row
βSSD(N)N × KV_per_tok / NVMe_GDS_BW[D] §4.2 + §4.3 NVMe row
βcold(N)prefill_time(N)[D] §4.1 formula

The routing signal

approx_wait_time_ms(task_id, t) = Σ over queue(task_id, t) ∪ running(task_id, t) of β(tier_residency_for(r))
  • What it sums. Per-request setup time across every request ahead of the next one to admit.
  • What it predicts. Expected delay before the next-admitted request gets its first token.
  • Which β per term. The tier r is currently held on (HBM → DRAM → SSD), or βcold if all three miss.

Reading the variables

  • task_id — which serving task the snapshot is for. Each task computes its own number; later prose often elides this parameter because each consumer knows the task implicitly. (At Iter 1a there’s only one task, so the parameter is trivial; at Iter 1b it’s one of ~291 500.)
  • t — the time at which we’re sampling. The signal is recomputed and (from Iter 1b on) gossiped to the broker every Tgossip = 100 ms.
  • r — one request being summed over (one element of the union below).
  • queue(task_id, t) — the set of requests admitted to this task but not yet started on the GPU at time t. Lives as a FIFO deque on the task scheduler (see scheduler.html).
  • running(task_id, t) — the set of requests currently in-flight on this task at time t. Pre-P/D split (Iter 1a/1b), at B=1, this is at most one prefill plus the decode that was already underway.
  • queue ∪ running — the union: everyone in front of a hypothetical new arrival. Each consumes one β of setup before the next can start; summing gives the expected wait.
  • tier_residency_for(r) — O(1) lookup into the per-task ConvIndex hashmap (§2.1 entity: conv_id → {block_ids[], total_tokens, tier_tag, last_turn_ts}): is conv_id(r)’s prior KV in HBM, DRAM, SSD, or absent (cold — first turn of a new conv, or BLCH-spill target)? Per-task local hashmap because CH-on-conv_id deterministically pins each conv to one task — no cross-task lookup needed on the hot path.

Worked example — one task’s snapshot

Suppose serving task t_47 at some moment t_now holds:

RequestStateconv_id / turntier_residency_for(r)β(r)
r_aqueuedconv_X, turn-3HBM (last turn 10 s ago)βHBM ≈ 2 ms
r_bqueuedconv_Y, turn-5; 13 K prior KVDRAM (evicted from HBM 30 s ago)βDRAM(13 K) = 166 ms
r_crunning prefillconv_Z, turn-1; 13.3 K promptnone (brand-new conv, triple-tier miss)βcold(13.3 K) = 730 ms
approx_wait_time_ms(t_47, t_now) = β(r_a) + β(r_b) + β(r_c) = 2 ms + 166 ms + 730 ms = 898 ms [D]

A new arrival routed to t_47 would expect ~898 ms before its first token — this is the number the Router compares against Θ × global_median_wait_time (Θ ≈ 1.5) to decide BLCH spill at Iter 1b, and that the autoscaler watches at fleet p95 (Iter 1b DC4). The same task also uses it as a local back-pressure self-monitor at Iter 1a.

Approximations baked in (worth naming)

  • Each β contributes its full slot, not remaining work. A request half-way through prefill still counts as one full β. Over-estimates wait by ~half-a-β on average, but the bias is symmetric across tasks — the Router uses ratios (BLCH compares one task to the global median), so the bias cancels.
  • Decode tail of running requests is ignored. A request mid-decode contributes its prefill-tier β, not its remaining T_out_remaining × βper_decode_tok (= ~2 s at workload mean). At B=1 single-stream decode (Iter 1a/1b) the decode tail dominates at low load; this is surfaced at Iter 6’s continuous-batching formula evolution where the decode-pool slot occupancy lands explicitly via avg_seq_lifetime / Bdecode.
  • tier_residency_for(r) latches at admit-time. Once admitted, a request’s tier tag stays put for the wait-time sum even if the underlying tier transitions mid-flight (e.g. a background DRAM evict). The error is small because gossip cadence (100 ms) is faster than typical tier transitions.

The crossover — why tier choice on miss is by residency, not latency

A tier t with bandwidth BWt beats re-prefill when βt(N) < βcold(N). Setting the two equal and solving for N gives the crossover token count below which re-prefill would be cheaper than swapping in.

# Set tier swap-in = re-prefill, then solve for N: N × KV_per_tok / BWt = ( 2·P·N + 4·n_layers·N²·d_model ) / F_sustained # Divide both sides by N (N > 0), rearrange: Ncrossover = ( F_sustained × KV_per_tok / BWt − 2·P ) / ( 4 × n_layers × d_model ) # Constants (Llama-3-70B + TP=8): KV_per_tok = 3.2 × 10⁵ B (§4.2) 2·P = 1.4 × 10¹¹ FLOP/tok (§4.1 linear coefficient) 4 × 80 × 8 192 = 2.62 × 10⁶ (§4.1 attn coefficient) F_sustained = TP × GPU_peak_BF16 × MFU = 8 × 989·10¹² × 0.40 = 3.17 × 10¹⁵ FLOP/s (§1.5; per-task sustained compute, MFU=40% accounts for kernel-launch + memory-stall + collective-wait losses)

Substituting the two non-HBM tier bandwidths:

  • SSD vs cold (BWt = 7 GB/s): Ncrossover = (3.17e15 × 3.2e5 / 7e9 − 1.4e11) / 2.62e6 = (1.45e11 − 1.4e11) / 2.62e6 ≈ 1 870 tok. Below ~1.9 K, re-prefill is actually cheaper than SSD swap-in; the table’s N=1K row sits just under the crossover (SSD 45.7 ms vs cold 45 ms — tied) and N=4K is comfortably past it.
  • DRAM vs cold (BWt = 25 GB/s): the numerator goes negative — 3.17e15 × 3.2e5 / 2.5e10 − 1.4e11 = 4.06e10 − 1.4e11 = −9.94e10. No positive crossover: DRAM beats re-prefill at every N. The reason is that PCIe BW is fast enough that the linear prefill term (2·P·N / F_sustained) alone already exceeds the DRAM swap-in time, before the quadratic attention term even fires.

Net rule for the local self-monitor: walk the residency ladder (HBM → DRAM → SSD → recompute) and stop at the first hit. DRAM beats cold always; SSD beats cold for any N ≥ ~1.9 K (every realistic request); HBM is always free.

The full transfer-cost model is α + β·n — what the βt(N) above drop, and why it’s OK at median. A transfer costs a fixed per-transfer overhead α (RTT + dispatch + the metadata/index lookup) plus a size term β·n (β = 1/BW_eff); the βt(N) = N·KV_per_tok/BW rows are only the β·n part. Why dropping α is fine for these tiers: local swap-in α = PCIe/GDS setup ≈ µs — negligible against the ms-scale β·n at the crossover (87 ms at 1.9 K). But beware the crossover’s α-sensitivity: at the crossover the two linear terms nearly cancel (recompute is only ~3% cheaper/tok than transfer), so a small α moves N* a lot: solving a·N² + b·N − α = 0 (a = 4·n_layers·d_model/F ≈ 8.3e−10, b = 2P/F − KV_per_tok/BW ≈ −1.55e−6) gives α=0 → 1.9 K, α=1 ms → ~2.4 K, α=5 ms → ~3.6 K. The remote-pool pull has α ≈ µs at median (RDMA RTT + CH-probe + index touch) but a ~ms p99 (RoCE PFC-pause / retransmit). Design choice: the pool admit gate (Iter 3b DC2) uses the median-α crossover (~1.9 K, α negligible); the p99-α tail (which would argue for a ~3.6 K gate) is absorbed not by a wider gate but by the abandon-to-recompute fallback (a pool fetch that blows its TTFT budget is dropped for local recompute, §9b failure modes).
N (tokens)βHBMβDRAM(N) [D]βSSD(N) [D]βcold(N) at TP=8 (from §4.1)Best tier above re-prefill
1 K~ms1 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 12.8 ms1 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 45.7 ms45 msSSD ≈ cold — crossover
4 K~ms4 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 51.2 ms4 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 183 ms190 msSSD wins by ~4%; DRAM by 3.7×
8 K (median)~ms8 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 102 ms8 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 365 ms410 msSSD wins by ~11%; DRAM by 4×
13 K (Tin_mean)~ms13 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 166 ms13 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 594 ms730 msSSD wins by ~19%; DRAM by 4.4×
32 K~ms32 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 410 ms32 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 1.46 s2.26 sSSD wins by 35%; DRAM by 5.5×
256 K (p99)~ms256 000 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 3.28 s256 000 × 320 KB / 7 GB/s = 11.7 s65.5 sSSD wins by 5.6×; DRAM by 20×
