  • Why DRAM widens its lead at large N: βDRAM / βSSD are both linear in N (transfer-dominated), while βcold has a quadratic attention term that dominates above N ≈ 49 K (K1 §3.3 crossover s* = 6·d_model). At N=256 K, βcold is 5.6× βSSD and 20× βDRAM; at 1 M context the gap explodes to ~68× (which is why Iter 9 CP is the real answer at p99.9, not just deeper SSD).
  • How the local self-monitor uses this at Iter 1a: on miss, the task picks the lowest-residency tier holding the request’s KV (HBM → DRAM → SSD → recompute); pays the matching β; updates approx_wait_time_ms. At Iter 1b the same number drives BLCH spill + autoscale.

6.6  Number chain — per-task throughput jumps 4× from Iter 0 (TP=8 effect)

Iter 1a’s structural wins (cache correctness, tier capacity) don’t change req_time at single-task B=1. But its DC3 design choice (TP=2 → TP=8) does — both prefill compute and decode HBM-BW gain 4×. The throughput jump is the TP=8 part of the iteration; the cache+tier part is the foundation that lets Iter 6 batch decode.

Step 1 — per-token sustained compute at TP=8

TP=8 sustained FLOP/s = 8 × 989 TFLOP/s × 0.40 (MFU) = 3 164.8 TFLOP/s = 3.17·10¹⁵ FLOP/s [D] (also cited in §1.5)

Step 2 — prefill time for the mean request at TP=8

prefill_time(Tin_mean, TP=8) = 2.32·10¹⁵ / 3.17·10¹⁵ = 0.73 s [D] (§4.1 table row N=13 K)     … 4× faster than Iter 0’s TP=2 prefill of 2.93 s.

Step 3 — per-token decode time at TP=8

weights_per_GPU(TP=8) = (P × bytes_per_element) / TP = (70·10⁹ × 2 B) / 8 = 17.5 GB / GPU [D] βper_decode_tok(TP=8) = weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU = 17.5 GB / 3.35 TB/s = 17.5·10⁹ / 3.35·10¹² = 0.00522 s = 5.22 ms / tok [D]     … 4× faster than Iter 0’s TP=2 decode tick of 20.9 ms / tok.

Step 4 — request time and per-task throughput

req_time_iter1a = prefill_time(Tin_mean, TP=8) + Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok(TP=8) = 0.73 s + 400 × 5.22 ms = 0.73 + 2.09 = 2.82 s / req [D] iter1a_req_per_s_per_task = 1 / 2.82 ≈ 0.355 req/s/task [D] … 4.0× Iter 0’s 0.089 req/s/task.

Step 5 — fleet sizing deferred

Iter 1a is single-task by construction (the “Why split Iter 1” rule); no serving_task_num or $/mo is owed at this iteration. At Iter 1b the Router replaces L4 round-robin and the fleet multiplies; the symbolic serving_task_num_iter1b = ⌈ λpeak / 0.355 × headroom ⌉ would land — but autoscaling-vs-static-peak (Iter 1b DC2) makes the steady-state number autoscale-tracked, not a single scalar.

Where the “cache makes B1 go away” framing actually lands. The Iter 0 number chain already used βper_decode_tok = weight-load only, which implicitly assumes the standard HBM-resident KV cache that any production attention kernel ships with. Iter 1a’s explicit contribution is (i) making the cache a design surface (eviction, tier policy, tagged tier residency per block); (ii) tiering the cache to DRAM + SSD so the population that fits per task multiplies by ≈ the SSD-to-HBM size ratio (~7 TB / ~450 GB ≈ 15×); (iii) flipping TP=2 to TP=8 (the 4× throughput jump above). B1’s textbook framing in §1.6 names why the cache exists; Iter 1a names what we do with the cache once we own it.

6.7  What breaks

  1. HBM-watermark evicts a hot conv’s tier-1 KV → next turn pays βDRAM instead of βHBM. Blast radius: bounded — one extra ~166 ms at Tin_mean. Detection: DRAM-swap-in counter increment; HBM hit-rate per §6.5 dashboard. Recovery: none needed (the spill is the recovery); tune low_watermark if rate exceeds budget.
  2. DRAM swap thrash under bursty admit → per-tick D2H stalls; TPOT spikes. Detection: PCIe utilisation + per-tick swap-in count. Mitigation: high DRAM watermark; mlock the active HBM-resident set; coalesce D2H batches every T_swap window rather than per-tick (declared in the SVG annotation).
  3. SSD wear under hot eviction churn → NVMe DWPD budget exhausted before the 5-yr lifetime. Mitigation: NVMe DWPD budget sized at expected eviction rate × 5-yr lifetime; rotate cold blocks at filesystem level; admission policy delays SSD writes for very-cold blocks. Detection: SMART wear-out indicator.
  4. Single task = SPOF — task death loses every in-flight KV. Recovery: addressed at Iter 1b (CH ring rebalance) + Iter 10 (recompute-as-fallback). No mitigation at Iter 1a; this is the consequence of single-task scope.

6.8  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 0)Post-iter (Iter 1a)ΔNote
TTFT p99~2.9 s (TP=2 cold)~730 ms (TP=8 cold)↓4× better — still fails 400 ms SLO on cold-prefill at Tin_mean; addressed at Iter 5 (prefix caching cuts unmatched to ~1.85 K → βcold(1.85 K) ≈ 80 ms).
TPOT p99~21 ms~5.2 ms↓Comfortably inside the 30 ms SLO; weight-load BW per GPU is 4× faster at TP=8.
Max-context supported~32 K~256 K at B=1 (per-GPU KV at TP=8: 256 K × 320 KB / 8 = 10.25 GB < 56 GB headroom)↓256 K is the workload-p99 sizing target, not the fit limit: the B=1 hardware ceiling is actually ~1.4 M (56 GB/GPU ÷ 40 KB/tok-per-GPU). 1 M even fits mechanically at B=1 (320 GB < 450 GB) — but only by dropping the task to a dedicated B=1, which destroys batch capacity; Iter 9 CP avoids that (fork at ~800 K).
Max-output supported≈ 65 K mechanically≈ 65 K at 5.2 ms/tok → ~340 s for max output↓Mechanically supported; SpecDecode at Iter 7 cuts $/tok further.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — addressed at Iter 8 (token bucket).
$/mo at λpeak~$20 B / mon/a (single-task scope)→Iter 1a is single-task by construction; fleet $/mo lands at Iter 1b once serving_task_num materialises.

Iter 1a turns the per-task numbers into something defensible at single-task scope: 4× throughput from TP=8, ~15× warm-conv residency from tiering, and the wait-time formula in hand as the canonical routing signal. TTFT still fails — Iter 5 prefix caching closes that gap; the 1.85 K unmatched-tokens-mean it lands on is the dominant TTFT lever on the page. Next: Iter 1b multiplies the fleet, picks the routing policy that preserves cache-stickiness across turns, and handles the skew B-rows (B7, B8).

7. Iter 1b — Scale-out + Router (CH-on-conv_id · BLCH spill · autoscale-on-wait-time)

Bottlenecks addressed: B4 (single task → fleet) · B7 (cache-oblivious LB at scale → CH-on-conv_id) · B8 (whale conv_id / vnode skew → BLCH spill). Deferred: B2 (continuous batching → Iter 6) · B3-fragmentation (paging → Iter 3) · B5 (prefix caching → Iter 5) · B6 (HoL blocking → Iter 4) · B9/B10 (P/D split → Iter 2).

Notation introduced in this section — the Router’s consistent-hash + skew machinery, the autoscale thresholds, and the per-job sizing chain. Reuses approx_wait_time_ms from §6.5.

CH / vnode / V
Consistent Hashing on conv_id — each task owns ~100 vnodes (virtual ring positions); V = total vnodes; lookup is O(log V).
BLCH / Θ
Bounded-Load Consistent Hashing — a sticky route spills to its 2nd-choice vnode when wait > Θ × global-median; Θ ≈ 1.5–2.0, spill capped ≤ 5%/epoch.
global_median_wait_time
fleet-wide median of approx_wait_time_ms (streaming t-digest/KLL sketch) — the BLCH comparison baseline.
ring_version / epoch
monotonic ring-topology version (bumped on autoscale) / routing epoch carried on each RoutingDecision for consistency.
R4 / placement_type
hot-spot autoscale (vnode-range split) — placement_type ∈ {hash-derived, explicit} distinguishes R4-inserted vnodes; co-adopted with BLCH at a slower horizon.
retract_threshold / T_retract
hysteresis for R4 — a split is undone once the hot task stays below retract_threshold × median for > T_retract (~5 min).
scale-up / scale-down thresholds
autoscale fires when per-task wait p95 > 0.5 × TTFT_p99_SLO (up) / < 0.2 × SLO (down); ramp capped ±10%/min. (These up/down coefficients are local to DC4 — not the §6.5 transfer-cost α.)
state spectrum A/B/C/D
the Borg state-management options — A read-replicas (CHOSEN) / B sharded / C quorum / D CRDT.
N_new_warm / Nprior
warm-turn genuinely-new tokens (= Tuser_msg_mean = 200) / prior-turns’ cached token count.
req_time_warm / req_time_cold
per-request time on a cache hit (~2.10 s) vs miss (~2.82 s); blended by intra_session_hit_rate into req_time_iter1b.
headroom
over-provision factor on fleet sizing = 1.3 (30%).
$_per_TP8_task_per_mo
cost of one TP=8 serving task = 8 × $12.29 × 720 = $70 790/mo.
per_decision_cpu_budget / router_task_num
Router cost per route (~10 µs → 100 K dec/s/task) / Router replica count = max(3 HA, demand).
gossip_msg_rate / broker_task_num
broker fan-in = serving_task_num / Tgossip (~2.9 M msg/s) / broker replicas = 3 (HA quorum).

7.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Client → L4 LB — HTTP/SSE; managed NLB terminates TLS; stable VIP across serving-task churn.
  • ② L4 LB → Router-job — round-robin across the 3 Router tasks; SSE stays stitched to its original TCP stream for the request lifetime.
  • ③ Router → serving (primary CH-on-conv_id) — the sticky route; turn-N of conv_id_X always lands on serving task A while A’s wait_time is below the BLCH threshold.
  • ┄┄ ④ Router → serving (BLCH spill, dashed red) — second-choice vnode when primary’s approx_wait_time_ms > Θ × global-median (Θ ≈ 1.5); trades one request’s cache hit for queue health.
  • ┄┄ ⑤ Serving → broker (async gossip) — per-task approx_wait_time_ms + ring-epoch ACK published every T_gossip = 100 ms as a Redis Stream entry.
  • ┄┄ ⑥ Broker → Router (async fan-out) — ring-version + per-task wait-time sketch streamed to every Router (read-replica pattern); ring updates rare (autoscale events), wait-time deltas at T_gossip cadence.

7.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • B4 is unavoidable. One Iter 1a task delivers 0.355 req/s; λpeak = 100 K req/s. The fleet ratio is 100 000 / 0.355 ≈ 281 690× — scale-out is the binding constraint, not an option.
  • B7 is the trap if scale-out is naive. At fleet scale with cache-oblivious LB (Iter 0’s round-robin), turn-N of a conversation lands on a random task → per-task cache hit rate falls to 1 / N_tasks ≈ 0 → every turn pays cold prefill → the entire Iter 1a tier hierarchy is wasted. CH-on-conv_id is the policy that preserves Iter 1a’s wins under multiplication.
  • B8 is the asterisk on CH-stickiness. CH-on-conv_id is uniform in expectation, but the heavy-tailed KV size distribution (~4 GB at Tin_mean → 82 GB at p99) makes per-task aggregate KV load variance high — a few unlucky tasks happen to land 2–3 whales by random chance and build unbounded queues. Need both (i) a per-request bounded-load variant — Google’s CHWBL (Mirrokni–Thorup–Zadimoghaddam, 2017) — for sub-second burst relief, and (ii) per-task hot-spot autoscale (vnode-range split) for persistent skew. Both ride the same approx_wait_time_ms signal at different time horizons.
  • Why NOT bundle P/D split here. B9 (rooflines colocated: prefill compute-bound vs decode BW-bound) is orthogonal to routing. Splitting the pools changes per-task throughput math; it does not change who picks the routing key. One bottleneck per iteration — P/D lands at Iter 2.

7.3  Design choices

[param] Θ (BLCH spill threshold): set to 1.5–2.0; spill budget ≤ 5%/epoch — Iter 1b. Why: a sticky CH-on-conv_id route spills to the second-choice vnode when its wait > Θ × global-median. Cliffs: Θ=1.0 spills on every blip (locality collapses); Θ→∞ lets whale tasks run unbounded; 1.5–2.0 is the elbow, with spill capped ≤ 5%/epoch so the cascade is bounded.

DC1 — Where does the routing decision live?

Problem: routing needs per-task KV-state awareness (which task holds which conv’s KV; which task is overloaded). A generic LB sees only transport state; an L7 LB sees HTTP headers but not the KV ring.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • L4 LB with sticky-conv hash — rejected: AWS NLB / GCP L4 only hash 5-tuple; can’t extract conv_id from HTTP body without breaking the L4 abstraction.
  • L7 LB (Envoy / NGINX) with custom filter — rejected: (i) SSE-buffering risk (default L7 LBs buffer until response-complete); (ii) body-parse tax at λpeak = 100 K req/s; (iii) the routing state (CH ring + wait-time) lives outside the LB → LB would need to call out per decision anyway.
  • Client SDK does routing — rejected on three counts, all rooted in the fact that the SDK only sees its own requests, never fleet state:
    • Ring epoch can’t converge across clients. The CH ring carries a monotonic epoch bumped on every autoscale / vnode-split; a route is only correct against the current epoch (a stale route lands on the wrong task and misses cache). A bump reaches a handful of Router tasks in ~one gossip period (100 ms); pushing it to millions of long-lived SDKs has unbounded skew — the ring would be perpetually inconsistent fleet-wide.
    • BLCH bounds are fleet-wide aggregates a client can’t compute. A spill fires on wait > Θ × global-median wait and is capped at ≤ 5%/epoch — both reference fleet-aggregated state (the streaming global-median sketch; the aggregate spill rate). One client can neither compare against the global median nor honor a global spill budget → either every client spills at once (thundering herd) or none do (whales run unbounded).
    • Untrusted + version-skewed. Clients are untrusted (can’t enforce admission / locality from the edge), and SDK-version skew makes two clients hash the same conv_id to different tasks even with identical ring data — the locality invariant (same conv → same task) breaks.
  • DNS-based stickiness — rejected: DNS TTL is minutes-scale; rebalance latency on autoscale events is unbounded.
  • Dedicated Router job — CHOSEN: stateless CPU service behind the L4 LB; reads the CH ring + per-task wait-time (from broker gossip); emits one (request_id, target_task_id, ring_version, epoch) decision per request.

Why a dedicated Router:

  • Different layers. Routing is application-level (cache locality, BLCH bounds); the LB is transport-level.
  • Single-purpose components. L4 LB stays line-rate; Router does the L7-style logic without buffering SSE.
  • Both stay. L4 LB in front (one VIP, TLS terminate, SSE-aware connection table); the Router sits behind.

DC2 — Routing key

Problem: pick the CH-hash key whose stickiness exactly matches the cache-locality unit (one conversation = one cached prefix-state).

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • L4 5-tuple — rejected: a TCP reconnect (mobile network swap, NAT churn) picks a different 5-tuple → same conv lands on different task → cache locality = 0.
  • Session cookie / TCP session ID — rejected: bound to TCP stream; mobile network swap or device restart kills the session; long-lived conversations (hours, days) can’t survive.
  • conv_id — CHOSEN: explicit application key, generated on POST /v1/conversations, survives reconnect, restart, and IP-NAT churn.
  • (conv_id, tenant_id) tuple — rejected: same conv_id always has the same tenant_id; bundling adds no entropy, just doubles the hash work.
  • (conv_id, turn_num) — rejected: each turn picks a different vnode → all turns are cold → defeats the entire premise.

Why conv_id:

  • Matches the locality unit. Smallest key whose collision rate matches the cache-locality unit.
  • Goldilocks granularity. Coarser (tenant_id) over-fragments; finer (turn_num) destroys locality.
  • Cheap lookup. Hash-input width fixed at admit-time → O(log V) Router latency, V = vnodes/task × N_tasks (~tens of MB ring fits in CPU cache).

DC3 — Skew handling (statistical co-location on the CH ring)

Problem: CH-on-conv_id is uniform in expectation, but the per-task aggregate KV load has high variance because KV sizes are heavy-tailed (4.16 GB at Tin_mean → 82 GB at p99 256 K history). A few unlucky tasks happen to land 2–3 whales by random chance, pushing their aggregate KV + queue depth out by orders of magnitude vs the fleet mean. Hard-pinning leaves these unlucky tasks unbounded.

Possible solutions enumerated (one bullet per variant; multiple co-adopted):

  • Pin hard (do nothing) — rejected: unbounded queue on the unlucky tasks; HoL-blocks every other conv_id that CH-hashed to the same task.
  • BLCH spill — CHOSEN (per-request, sub-second): when sticky task’s approx_wait_time_ms > Θ × global-median wait_time (Θ ≈ 1.5–2.0), Router routes this one request to the second-choice vnode. Trades that request’s cache hit for queue health. Real systems: Google CHWBL (Mirrokni–Thorup–Zadimoghaddam, 2017); Vimeo Ringpop variant.
  • R4 — hot-spot autoscale (vnode-range split) — CO-ADOPTED (per-task, ~minutes): when one task is persistently over wait_time threshold (longer than BLCH’s sub-second spill horizon), insert a fresh task into the ring and split the hot task’s vnode range 50/50. Half of affected conv_ids reroute to the new task; they lose HBM cache locality (DRAM/SSD residency from Iter 1a tier hierarchy may survive the move), and re-warm on next turn. Cost: standing $$ for the new task (~$70 790/mo) + one-time cache-miss wave on moved conv_ids. Why co-adopted with BLCH, not competing: BLCH bounds the burst at sub-second cadence; R4 drains the persistent hot spot at autoscale cadence. Both ride the same approx_wait_time_ms signal (DC4), just at different time horizons.

    Ring-map mechanics (how the new task lands at a specific ring position). Pure CH derives vnode positions via hash(task_id || vnode_idx), so “insert at a chosen position” isn’t possible without extending the data structure. Two implementations achieve the same R4 outcome, picked by hot-spot duration:

    Impl A — extended ringImpl B — pure-hash ring + side-map
    StorageSorted [(position, task_id, placement_type)]; R4 vnodes have placement_type = explicit at chosen positions; pre-existing stay hash-derived.Pure-hash ring unchanged; separate {hot_vnode → [task1, task_new_a, ...]} side-map.
    Routing lookupOne O(log V) ring lookup → final task.O(log V) ring lookup → primary; check side-map; if hit, pick via hash(conv_id) mod len(list) within rotation.
    Scale event costRing rebalance.Side-map update.
    Best forPersistent skew (hours+) — ring representation stays self-contained.Transient hot spots (~sec–min) — lighter add/remove cycle.
    Size at fleet scale~350–470 MB ring (see below).~350 MB ring + KB-scale side-map (~thousands of hot vnodes × ~100 B/entry).

    Both implementations preserve cache locality within the rotation by routing sticky-by-hash(conv_id); both build the new task’s cache organically (no KV copy). Production systems (Cassandra vnodes, Riak, Couchbase vBuckets) typically use Impl A for the steady ring; some (e.g., DynamoDB on-call rotation) layer Impl B on top for transient hot relief.

    Ring map size at fleet scale (291 500 tasks × ~100 vnodes/task = ~29 M ring entries): pure-hash CH with minimal (position, task_id) entry is 29 M × 12 B ≈ 350 MB; the R4-extended typed-and-padded form is 29 M × 16 B ≈ 470 MB (~33% more). The R4 overhead can be reduced to ~0 by bit-packing the placement flag into the MSB of task_id (31 bits = 2.1 B task IDs, plenty); the “fat” 470 MB form is the conservative reading. Either way both fit comfortably in Router RAM (Routers are CPU-only stateless tasks; broker-gossiped on rare ring-rebalance events, not the per-100 ms wait-time tick). Salt-search (try hash(task_id || salt_i) until landing near the desired arc) is the hash-preserving alternative but adds rejection-sampling overhead and isn’t exactly-placed.

    Hysteresis (retract condition). A task added to a rotation is removed once the original hot task’s wait_time stays below retract_threshold × global-median for > T_retract (~5 min, a few BLCH spill windows). Retract cost: side-map update (cheap, Impl B) or ring rebalance (heavier, Impl A). Without hysteresis, transient hot spots permanently bloat the rotation set; with too-aggressive hysteresis (small T_retract), the rotation thrashes. The T_retract tune target is “hot-spot duration p95” — long enough to outlast typical bursts, short enough to release ~95% of capacity within an autoscale window. Full data-structure detail in router.html.

  • Active load steal (migrate in-flight KV) — rejected: per-conv KV is 4.16–82 GB; cross-task transfer at NDR400 RDMA 25 GB/s = 4.16e9 / 25e9 ≈ 0.17 s at mean, 82e9 / 25e9 ≈ 3.3 s at p99. The BLCH spill decision is sub-millisecond and R4’s vnode-split rerouting happens at request boundaries (no in-flight migration) — explicit KV migration adds more latency than either alternative.
  • Replicate hot keys to N tasks — three sub-variants, each rejected for a different reason:
    • R1 — replicate to N busy neighbour tasks: each replica’s decode-tick HBM writes contend with the neighbour’s own decode HBM-BW (and the replicated KV competes for HBM capacity against the neighbour’s own convs). Reject: contention — replication just shifts the queue-health problem to the neighbour.
    • R2 — replicate to N dedicated empty tasks with continuous KV stream copied to each replica: per-tick RDMA cost is small — 320 KB / 25 GB/s + ~1 µs setup ≈ 20 µs per replica per tick at NDR400 InfiniBand, well inside the 5.22 ms tick budget — so contention does vanish. The killer is standing HBM commitment at fleet scale:
      whale_convs_concurrent = 3 M sessions × 1% whale share = 30 K [D — from §1.3] HBM_per_whale_at_p99 = 256 K tok × 320 KB/tok = 82 GB [D — from §4.2] extra_replica_HBM_total = 30 K × 82 GB × 1 extra replica (N=2) ≈ 2.5 PB dedicated replica HBM [D] extra_tasks = 2.5 PB / 450 GB usable per TP=8 task ≈ 5 600 extra serving tasks [D] extra_$_per_mo = 5 600 × $70 790 ≈ $400 M / month [D] (~2% of Iter 1b fleet $)
      Reject: the PB-scale standing HBM commitment is a worse trade than BLCH’s bounded-spill catastrophe budget (≤ 5% spill rate × one-time βcold payment) plus R4’s bounded standing-task cost. R2’s cost is continuous; BLCH+R4’s is bounded.

      Note on the “no KV copy” variant: if the new tasks do NOT receive a continuous KV stream and instead build cache organically from the requests routed to them, this reduces to R4 above (just under a side-map implementation; see R4 mechanics). The mechanism is then more accurately called “load distribution”, not replication — no state is replicated, only routing is diverted. The R2-with-state-copy framing is what carries the 2.5 PB HBM cost; the no-copy framing is R4.

    • R3 — replicate at block-hash level via a pooled store, on-demand fetch: replicate only addressable, immutable KV blocks (not the live mutating conv KV stream); spilled tasks fetch from the pool over RDMA only when needed. This is the structurally correct hot-key relief mechanism. Reject at Iter 1b: the supporting infra (pooled kv-store-job + global index + Cuckoo filter) doesn’t exist yet — this is what Iter 3b builds (a later iteration), which replaces BLCH’s catastrophe payment with a much smaller pool-fetch cost.
    Structural note: LLM serving is write-heavy on conv KV (each decode token appends; each turn appends), so the classic “replicate for read-heavy hot-key relief” trade only works when the replicated content doesn’t mutate per tick — which is true for read-only prefixes (a later prefix-caching iteration pins hidden_sys on every prefill task; Iter 3b's pooled store holds user_sys) but not for the live conversation KV.

Why BLCH + R4 together over the alternatives:

  • Two time horizons, no KV move. BLCH handles sub-second bursts (per-request spill, no standing cost); R4 handles persistent skew (per-task vnode split, bounded standing cost).
  • BLCH cliff. At Θ = 1.0 every minor wait-time blip spills → locality collapses; at Θ → ∞ whale tasks go unbounded; Θ ≈ 1.5–2.0 is the elbow, spill rate capped ≤ 5% per epoch (DC4) so cascade is bounded.
  • R4 cliff. Too-aggressive vnode-split lets the cache-miss wave dominate — trigger on persistent wait_time excess over > 5 min, not a transient spike.
  • Replication rejected. R1/R2 cost too much; R3’s smarter replication deferred to Iter 3b (the pooled store).

DC4 — Autoscale signal

Problem: what scalar does the autoscaler watch to decide “add/remove serving tasks”?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • CPU% / GPU% utilization — rejected: lagging. Once GPU is at 100% the queue is already long. Decode is HBM-BW-bound (not FLOP-bound) so GPU% can stay low while throughput saturates — the signal mis-reports.
  • QPS — rejected: ignores load mix. One 256 K-prompt request piles up the same prefill wait as ~90 13 K-prompt requests (65.5 s / 0.73 s ≈ 90; βcold scales as N² above s* = 49 K).
  • Queue depth — rejected: ignores tier-residency. βHBM-resident and βcold queued requests look identical in depth but their effective wait differs by ~700×.
  • approx_wait_time_ms (from §6.5) — CHOSEN: directly sums per-request β by tier residency; reflects the exact predicate the user cares about (time until I get my first token). Leading indicator — autoscale fires before TTFT SLO violation.

Why approx_wait_time_ms:

  • One coherent signal. Same scalar the Router consumes for BLCH (DC3), so routing + autoscale share one control plane.
  • Explicit triggers. Per-task wait_time p95 > α × TTFT_p99_SLO (α ≈ 0.5) → scale up; below β_thresh × SLO (β_thresh ≈ 0.2) → scale down.
  • Thrash guard. Bounded ramp rate (± 10% of fleet / minute).

DC5 — Ring + wait-time state management (the 4-option state spectrum)

Problem: every Router task must know the CH ring (which vnode → which serving task) + per-task wait-time. Ring changes on autoscale; wait-time deltas every T_gossip = 100 ms.

Possible solutions enumerated (the canonical Borg-style A/B/C/D spectrum):

  • A — read-replicas (CHOSEN): each Router caches the full ring locally; broker (Redis Streams) gossips ring-version + wait-time sketch. Read-path: zero-RTT in-process lookup. Write-path: ring-version bumps on autoscale events (rare); wait-time deltas streamed every T_gossip.
  • B — sharded ring (each Router owns a partition) — rejected: a routing decision for an arbitrary conv_id requires hopping to the partition owner → adds 1–2 ms RTT per decision → at λpeak places cross-Router RTT on every request.
  • C — quorum (etcd / Raft) — rejected: every routing decision becomes a quorum read = ~1–5 ms RTT × 100 K req/s = 100–500 K read ops/s on etcd — one etcd cluster can’t sustain that.
  • D — CRDT — rejected: overkill. Ring topology changes are infrequent (autoscale ~ per minute), deterministic (vnode add/remove are trivially commutative), and don’t need merge semantics; CRDT machinery is for concurrent-write conflict resolution that doesn’t exist here.

Why A (read-replicas):

  • Read-heavy, rare writes. Ring updates are rare; per-request reads at λpeak → push reads to in-process cache, reserve the broker for state-change writes.
  • Redis Streams over NATS JetStream. Its consumer-group model fits the “every Router consumes the same delta” pattern more naturally.
  • Redis Streams over Kafka. Kafka’s per-topic overhead is too heavy for ~100 K low-rate gossip msg/s; Redis Streams is in-memory and ships with the existing observability stack.

The Router actually holds two in-memory maps, with very different sizes and update cadences (stored separately, not unified, because bundling forces the wait-time tick to re-touch the ring-map pages and wrecks cache locality):

MapLayoutSize at fleet scaleUpdate cadence
Ring map (vnode → task)sorted [(position, task_id, [placement_type])]; binary search O(log V)350–470 MB (per R4 mechanics above)Ring rebalance events (~/min — rare)
Wait-time map (task_id → approx_wait_time_ms)hashmap; entry = (wait_time, last_update_ts, epoch_ack) ≈ 20 B; lookup O(1)291 500 × 20 B ≈ ~6 MBPer-task gossip every T_gossip = 100 ms
Derived: global-median wait_timescalar; backed by streaming quantile sketch (t-digest / KLL); O(log N) per gossip arrival~KBRefreshed per gossip cycle; cached between

Per-routing-decision lookup cost: 1–2 O(log V) ring-map lookups (primary + optional BLCH second-choice) + 1–2 O(1) wait-time lookups + 1 cached global-median read. All in-process; no broker RTT on the hot path. The 10 µs/decision budget from §7.4 Step 6 accommodates both. Full data-structure detail in router.html.

7.4  Number chain — what cache-stickiness buys

Step 1 — warm-hit prefill time

N_new_warm = Tuser_msg_mean = 200 tok [G — §1.3] prefill_FLOPs(N) = 2·P·N + 4·n_layers·N²·d_model [G — K1 §3.3] substituting Llama-3-70B constants (from §4.1): 2·P = 2 × 70·10⁹ = 1.4·10¹¹ (linear coeff) 4 × n_layers × d_model = 4 × 80 × 8 192 = 2.62·10⁶ (attn coeff) ⇒ prefill_FLOPs(N) = 1.4·10¹¹·N + 2.62·10⁶·N² prefill_FLOPs(200) = 1.4·10¹¹ × 200 + 2.62·10⁶ × 200² = 2.8·10¹³ + 1.05·10¹¹ ≈ 2.81·10¹³ FLOP prefill_time(200, TP=8) = 2.81·10¹³ / 3.17·10¹⁵ = 8.9 ms [D]
Warm-hit residency assumption. The 8.9 ms number above assumes the prior turns’ KV is HBM-resident (βHBM on top of prefill_time(200)). This holds at the workload mean: per task we resident ~3 M sessions / 291 500 tasks ≈ 10 active sessions, comfortably below the per-task HBM-KV budget of ~450 GB / 4.16 GB ≈ 108 sessions. On heavily-loaded tasks (above BLCH spill threshold, just before autoscale fires) the HBM-resident set can be exceeded and a warm hit pays βDRAM(Nprior) ≈ 166 ms at Nprior = 13 K instead — still ~4× faster than cold (βcold(13 K) = 730 ms). The req_time_warm = 2.10 s number used downstream is the HBM-resident case; the DRAM-spill case bumps it to ~2.26 s. Both well below cold.

Step 2 — average req_time at intra_session_hit_rate = 0.80

req_time_warm = prefill_time(200, TP=8) + Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok(TP=8) = 8.9 ms + 400 × 5.22 ms = 8.9 + 2 088 = 2.10 s [D] req_time_cold = (unchanged from Iter 1a) = 2.82 s req_time_iter1b = 0.20 × req_time_cold + 0.80 × req_time_warm = 0.20 × 2.82 + 0.80 × 2.10 = 0.564 + 1.678 = 2.24 s / req [D]

Step 3 — per-task throughput

iter1b_req_per_s_per_task = 1 / req_time_iter1b = 1 / 2.24 ≈ 0.446 req/s/task [D] … 1.26× Iter 1a’s 0.355 (the cache-stickiness lift) … 5.0× Iter 0’s 0.0886 (combined TP=8 + CH-on-conv_id)

Step 4 — serving-job fleet sizing at λpeak (with 30% headroom)

serving_task_num_iter1b = ⌈ λpeak × headroom / iter1b_req_per_s_per_task ⌉ = ⌈ 100 000 × 1.3 / 0.446 ⌉ = ⌈ 291 480 ⌉ ≈ 291 500 serving tasks [D] Apples-to-apples vs Iter 0 (no headroom): ⌈ 100 000 / 0.446 ⌉ = 224 215 tasks → $/mo comparison below

Step 5 — serving-job $/mo

$_per_TP8_task_per_mo = 8 GPUs × $12.29 / GPU / hr × 720 hr / mo = 8 × $12.29 × 720 = $70 790 / task / mo [D] · [G — K1 §3.10] $/mo_iter1b (with 30% headroom) = 291 500 × $70 790 ≈ $20.6 B / month [D] $/mo_iter1b (apples vs Iter 0) = 224 215 × $70 790 ≈ $15.9 B / month [D] → saves ~$4 B/mo (≈ 20%) vs Iter 0’s $19.97 B/mo

Step 6 — router-job sizing

per_decision_cpu_budget ≈ 10 µs [G-FA] (ring O(log V) lookup + BLCH check + epoch read; pure CPU) per_router_decisions_per_s = 1 s / 10 µs = 100 000 dec/s/task [D] router_task_num = max( 3 (HA quorum), ⌈ λpeak × 1.3 / 100 000 ⌉ ) = max( 3, 2 ) = 3 router-job tasks [D]

The Router is intentionally fat-headroom (3 tasks for 1.3 of required) — CPU cost is trivial vs serving fleet, and the blast radius of a Router task death is large (all in-flight routing decisions for its share of λpeak).

Step 7 — broker-job sizing

gossip_msg_rate = serving_task_num × (1 / T_gossip) = 291 500 × 10 msg/s = ~2.9 M msg/s aggregate [D] Per-msg size: 32 B sketch (task_id + p95 wait + epoch) Aggregate BW: 2.9 M × 32 B ≈ 93 MB/s — trivial for Redis Streams in-memory. broker_task_num = 3 (HA quorum, fixed) [G] (3 is the minimum quorum; the workload is BW-trivial, so sizing is HA-bound, not throughput-bound)

7.5  What breaks

  1. BLCH spill cascade — too many keys spill simultaneously → second-choice tasks congest → cascade. Detection: spill-rate counter per ring epoch. Mitigation: bounded spill budget per epoch (≤ 5% of routing decisions); excess → 429 (admission control), which is also where Iter 8’s token bucket plugs in.
  2. Ring rebalance churn — autoscale event triggers vnode moves → in-flight conversations on moved tasks lose their HBM-resident KV (DRAM/SSD residency survives the move, but HBM hit rate drops until re-warm). Detection: ring-version delta rate + per-conv HBM-tier-drop counter. Mitigation: bounded trigger (per-task wait_time variance > 2× steady-state for > 5 min); soft-drain old vnodes over T_drain before promoting new task.
  3. Stale ring epoch — Router’s view lags broker → routes to a task that is no longer the vnode owner for this conv_id. Detection: serving task tags its response header with ring_version_mismatch (it knows its own epoch and the request’s); Router increments mismatch_rate. Mitigation: serve best-effort, no automatic retry — during the T_drain soft-drain window the old owner still has the conv’s KV in DRAM/SSD, so a stale-routed request still hits a cache-warm task; locality survives. The next request for the same conv_id uses the Router’s now-refreshed ring view and lands on the new owner. Retries would amplify load without fixing the root cause (gossip lag, not task health), and a "fail back to client" path has nowhere useful to fall back to — the Router has no fresher ring than the broker just gave it. Alert: mismatch_rate > 1% sustained over 30 s pages oncall (gossip pipeline broken: broker partition, network split, or serving-task epoch ACK stuck). Floor is ≈ 0% in steady state because ring-version bumps are rare (autoscale events) and gossip cadence (T_gossip = 100 ms) is much faster than the request-arrival rate per Router.
  4. B6 (long-prefill HoL still unhandled) — one 256 K prefill (~65 s on TP=8) blocks the prefill+decode loop on its task. CH-on-conv_id sticks the whale to one task → that task’s queue tail spikes; BLCH spill helps queue depth but doesn’t shrink the 65 s individual cost. Recovery: Iter 4 chunked + packed prefill on FA v2 varlen.
  5. B2 (decode at B=1 still HBM-BW-wasted) — per-task throughput stuck at single-stream decode; MBU ≈ 1% (K1 §3.5). Recovery: Iter 6 continuous batching on the decode pool.
  6. B8b (per-user abuse) — a runaway client floods the same conv_id with rapid-fire turns; CH-stickiness amplifies the impact on the sticky task. Recovery: Iter 8 token bucket + max_concurrent_conv cap.

7.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 1a, single task)Post-iter (Iter 1b, fleet)ΔNote
TTFT p99~730 ms (cold at Tin_mean)~730 ms (preserved across fleet)→The Iter 1b win is the counterfactual: without CH-on-conv_id, fleet cache hit rate → 1/N_tasks ≈ 0 → every turn pays cold prefill → TTFT p99 collapses. Iter 1b preserves Iter 1a’s distribution at fleet scale.

Why the SLO miss is structural, not a routing failure. The 20% cold-prefill rate at the request level decomposes roughly as: (a) ~95% of cold cases = turn-1 of a new session (no prior state exists; ~5 turns/session at 150 s lifetime × ~1 turn/30 s → the first of every 5 requests is cold by construction); (b) ~5% of cold cases = BLCH spill + autoscale-fresh + stale-routing-cache. CH-on-conv_id can’t fix (a) — turn-1 has nothing to hit. The structural fix is Iter 5 prefix caching: turn-1’s prompt is mostly hidden_sys (4 K, platform-public → fleet-wide cached) + user_sys (1 K, per-user pin or Iter 3b pool fetch) — only ~200 tok actually unmatched → βcold(200) ≈ 8.9 ms. Iter 5 takes mean unmatched tokens from 13 300 → ~1 850 (~86% reduction; B5 row in §1.6).
TPOT p99~5.2 ms~5.2 ms→Decode is per-task; routing doesn’t touch this.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→Unchanged; Iter 9 CP fork lands at ~800 K.
λpeak supported0.355 req/s (1 task)100 000 req/s (~291 500 tasks)↓B4 cleared: the fleet now actually fits λpeak.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — Iter 8 token bucket.
$/mo at λpeakn/a (single-task scope)~$20.6 B/mo (w/ 30% headroom); ~$15.9 B (apples vs Iter 0)↓ ~20%Real-money saving from TP=8 + cache-stickiness. The deep cuts arrive at Iter 5 (mean prefill load −86%) and Iter 6 (decode by B_decode×).

Iter 1b clears B4 + B7 + B8 — the fleet physically fits λpeak, the per-conv cache locality from Iter 1a survives at fleet scale, and skew is bounded by BLCH spill. TTFT, TPOT, and max-context are preserved, not improved; cost is down ~20% vs Iter 0. Next: Iter 2 — P/D disaggregation splits the colocated prefill+decode rooflines (B9) and explicitly pays the cross-pool KV transfer (B10) over NDR400 RDMA. Both prefill-task and decode-task throughputs are then sized independently, and the page’s first cross-pool data-flow appears.

8. Iter 2 — P/D disaggregation + cross-task KV transfer (gRPC)

Bottlenecks addressed: B9 (rooflines colocated → split pools) · B10(i) (cross-pool KV transport via gRPC server-stream). Architectural iteration — no per-task throughput delta on its own; opens B9 here, with the throughput payout landing at later iterations. Deferred to later iterations: B2, B3-fragmentation, B5, B6, B10(ii).

Notation introduced in this section — the P/D-split task symbols, the per-pool wait signals, the decode-occupancy set, and the cross-pool transfer terms.

PⒶ / DⒶ
a prefill task / a decode task — the two pools the unified serving-job splits into. PⒶ never sees the assistant output (it lives on DⒶ) — the gap Iter 3b fills.
tp / td
identifiers for a specific prefill / decode task (the task_id per pool).
prefill_task_num / decode_task_num
independently-sized pool replica counts (prefill compute-bound, decode BW-bound) — ~19.9 K / ~271 K at B=1.
approx_wait_prefill(tp) / approx_wait_decode(td)
the Iter 1b signal split one-per-pool — prefill sums βprefill; decode = max(0, queue−free_slots) × (avg_seq_lifetime/B).
βprefill(r) / N_new(r)
prefill-pool setup = prefill_time(N_new(r)); N_new = genuinely-new tokens this turn (warm 200 / cold 13.3 K).
avg_seq_lifetime
decode-slot occupancy time = Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok ≈ 2.09 s.
live_conv_set(td) / free_slots / active_decode_capacity
convs actively decoding on a task (= live_convs set) / = B − |live_convs| / = B (the decode-slot ceiling, ≈1 here).
KVHandoff / KVHandoffAck
the cross-pool admit event — prefill streams the prompt’s K,V to decode (gRPC server-stream over RDMA); decode +1s live_convs.
N_xfer_* / slab_*
tokens / bytes shipped per turn — cold 13 K→4.16 GB, warm 200→64 MB, p99 256 K→82 GB.
RDMA_per_flow / xfer_*
per-flow RDMA BW = 25 GB/s; transfer time = slab / RDMA_per_flow (cold 166 ms, warm 2.6 ms, p99 3.28 s).
per_layer_xfer
layer-wise streaming alternative (DC2) — only the last layer’s ~2.1 ms is exposed; theoretical ceiling, rarely shipped.
xfer_budget_warm
placement slack — transfer time the warm-path TTFT SLO can absorb (~386 ms) → bounds prefill/decode host distance.

8.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Client → L4 LB — HTTP request opens an SSE stream; managed L4 LB stitches the TCP stream and terminates TLS (unchanged from Iter 1b).
  • ② L4 LB → Router — round-robin across the 3 Router tasks; SSE stays stitched to its TCP stream for the request lifetime.
  • ③ Router → prefill task (CH on prefill_ring) — the only Router-issued Route. Router carries two CH rings now (prefill_ring + decode_ring), both keyed on conv_id; it consults the prefill ring on the hot path. BLCH spill + autoscale-on-wait-time (Iter 1b) apply per-pool, independently.
  • ── ④ Prefill → Decode (KV gRPC server-stream) — the cross-pool data flow this iteration introduces. Prefill task picks the decode task via CH(conv_id, decode_ring) (its in-memory copy, gossiped by the broker), then ships only the new turn's K,V slab when the conv is warm on Dα, or the full prompt's K,V when Dα is cold. Stream is request-scoped (one stream per turn, closes on EOS).
  • ┄┄ ⑤ Prefill → Router (async PrefillHandoff) — tells Router which decode task picked up this conv_id so the next-turn Router lookup for the same conv can fast-path the binding. Not on the hot path; fire-and-forget.
  • ┄┄ ⑥ Both pools → broker (async gossip) — each prefill task and each decode task publishes its own approx_wait_* + ring epoch every T_gossip = 100 ms as a Redis Stream entry (one stream per pool).
  • ┄┄ ⑦ Broker → Router (async fan-out) — ring-versions + per-task wait-time sketches for both pools fanned out to every Router (read-replica pattern; per Iter 1b DC5 spectrum).
  • ⑧ Decode → Client (SSE stream) — output tokens stream back through the L4 LB to the client. The L4 LB’s SSE-aware connection table makes the prefill→decode internal handoff invisible to the client; the client’s TCP stream is bound to the LB-VIP, not to any one serving task.

8.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Enabling prior. Iter 1a’s KV cache made decode work O(L), not O(L3). At B=1 decode is BW-bound at MBU ≈ 1% [G — K1 §3.5] — the GPU compute units are idle 99% of every decode tick. Decode could safely run Bdecode >> 1 without saturating compute. But it can’t yet, because the same task that decodes also handles prefills (next bullet).
  • The roofline asymmetry that makes split unavoidable. Per §4.1 + §4.3 in one colocated fused step:
    • Prefill is compute-bound at B=1 already: prefill_FLOPs = 2·P·N + 4·n_layers·N²·d; both terms scale linearly with batch → TTFT grows linearly with B → must stay B=1.
    • Decode is HBM-BW-bound at B=1: per-tick weight reads = ~17.5 GB/GPU regardless of B (K1 §3.5) → wants B_decode ≈ MBU_target / MBU_at_B=1 (the actual B value is settled at a later iteration once the headroom equation is in place).
    • Colocated: every fused step pays max(prefill_time, decode_time) and the two are bottlenecked on different resources. Neither saturates: prefill underuses HBM BW (compute-stuck), decode underuses FLOPs (BW-stuck). Both pools waste cycles.
  • Disagg is the only way to scale each independently. Split serving-prefill-job (sized for FLOPs throughput, B=1) from serving-decode-job (sized for HBM BW, B raises at a later iteration). Each pool can pick its own batch policy and (in principle) its own GPU class — prefill on FLOPs-rich, decode on cheaper HBM-rich at scale. This page keeps the fleet homogeneous (TP=8 H100 on both pools); the homogeneous → heterogeneous step is a follow-on the architecture supports without restructuring.
  • Why not a chunked-prefill scheduler on the unified task instead of disagg? Chunked prefill keeps prefill + decode on the same task — they still share HBM BW and SM scheduling. It fixes B6 (long-prefill HoL), not B9 (roofline mismatch). Disagg and chunked prefill are complementary, not substitutes; the chunked-prefill scheduler is a separate, later move on top of the disagg topology.
  • Why gRPC server-stream cross-pool transport NOW (not RDMA)? At one-request transfer size, gRPC at ~25 GB/s effective is sufficient. Cold path (Tin_mean = 13 K): 4.16 GB / 25 GB/s = 166 ms [D — §8.5]. RDMA only earns its keep at pool-wide fetch patterns (where N² connection cost dominates vs a central store) — not the case at one-request streams here.
  • Why this iteration must land BEFORE any decode-pool layout or batching change. Decode-pool layout fixes (paging) and decode batching (continuous batching, CUDA-graphs captured-set) are decode-pool-only optimisations. If pools aren’t split, raising decode B would drag prefill into the same kernel launch and the captured-set would have to cover the cross-product of prefill+decode shapes. Disagg is the topology precondition that lets those optimisations act on the decode pool alone.

8.3  Design choices

[param] Serving fleet: unified → prefill_task_num + decode_task_num (independently sized) — Iter 2. Why: prefill is compute-bound, decode is BW-bound; one pool forces a shared roofline and a single B. Splitting lets each pool size to its own throughput — prefill stays B=1 (TTFT-safe) while decode can later raise B. (Decode B stays 1 here → becomes Bdecode at Iter 6.)

DC1 — Cross-pool KV transport (prefill task → decode task per request)

Problem: every request now requires moving one turn’s K,V (or the full prompt’s K,V on cold path) from a prefill task to a decode task. Pick a transport that fits the size, latency, and per-flow rate.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Shared filesystem (NFS / cluster FS) — rejected: write-then-read round-trip via the FS pays disk + network twice; cold-path 4.16 GB at NFS-realistic ~1 GB/s = ~4 s — an order of magnitude over the TTFT SLO. The FS server is also a single shared dependency the data plane shouldn’t touch.
  • gRPC server-stream over RDMA (NDR400 IB or 400 GbE RoCE) — CHOSEN: one stream per turn, prefill task is the server, decode task is the client. Effective ~25 GB/s per flow [G — K20]. Cold path 4.16 GB → 166 ms all-at-once or ~2 ms last-layer-only under DC2 layer-wise streaming (§8.5). The IB-vs-RoCE choice is operationally significant (HPC/AI clusters favor IB; cloud DCs favor RoCE on Ethernet) but bandwidth-equivalent at NDR400 / 400 GbE class; either delivers the per-flow rate this DC needs.
  • RDMA verbs direct (GPUDirect RDMA, no gRPC framing) — rejected at this iteration: a few percent faster on the wire (gRPC framing ≈ 3–5% overhead) but requires N²-style connection pools (every prefill task ↔ every decode task) and adds an ops surface (per-task QP state, fail-over). gRPC gives the same effective bandwidth with a simple per-turn stream. The connection-pool cost only justifies itself once a pool-wide fetch pattern lands (every decode → central KV store) — deferred to Iter 3b, which introduces that pattern (the pooled write-back/pull store).
  • UDP / QUIC custom protocol — rejected: would re-invent reliability + ordering on top of an already-reliable IB substrate; gRPC ships flow control + framing already.

Why gRPC + IB wins here:

  • Smallest sufficient. Hits the bandwidth bar at one-request transfer sizes without the connection-pool tax only a pool-fetch pattern would justify.
  • RDMA-direct not yet. It earns its keep only when every task talks to every task at λpeak — not here (request-scoped streams; prefill → one decode task).

DC2 — Transfer scheduling (when does the KV slab go on the wire)

Problem: should the prefill task wait for the full prefill to complete and then bulk-send the whole slab, or pipeline the transfer at some finer granularity (per-layer, per-chunk) overlapping with prefill compute?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Per-request bulk RDMA at end-of-prefill — CHOSEN: wait for the full prefill to finish → one bulk RDMA stream of the entire slab → decode starts. Cold path pays prefill + xfer + decode_setup = 730 + 166 + 5 = 901 ms. This is the production default (DistServe, Mooncake, vLLM disagg) because at 400 Gbps IB/RoCE, bulk transfer of a few GB is tens of ms — often comparable to or faster than the per-layer synchronisation overhead the alternatives need to amortise. Simpler implementation; one RDMA op per finished prefill.
  • Per-layer pipelined streaming — alternative, calculated for reference. Mechanism: ship each layer’s K,V as soon as that layer’s prefill completes, overlapped with the next layer’s compute.
    per_layer_compute = prefill_time(13 K) / n_layers = 730 ms / 80 = 9.1 ms per_layer_KV (cold) = slab_cold / n_layers = 4.16 GB / 80 = 52 MB per_layer_xfer @25 GB/s = 52 MB / 25 GB/s = ~2.1 ms per_layer_compute (9.1 ms) >> per_layer_xfer (2.1 ms) ⇒ every layer’s transfer fully hides behind the next layer’s compute ⇒ only last layer’s ~2.1 ms exposed in TTFT xfer_cold_exposed_layer_wise ≈ 2.1 ms (vs 166 ms bulk — ~80× reduction)
    Rarely shipped in production because it requires tight per-layer coupling between the prefill task’s kernel scheduler and the decode task’s per-layer acceptance — hard to implement correctly, fragile under retries / fail-overs, and the wall-clock savings (~150 ms in an iteration whose cold-path is already 730 ms over SLO) don’t justify the complexity once RDMA is doing the bulk transfer in tens of ms.
  • Per-chunk streaming — deferred. The practical pipelining answer, but requires the prefill task to already be running on a chunked scheduler. At Iter 2 there’s no chunking; the prefill kernel processes the full prompt in one go. Per-chunk streaming naturally lands at a later iteration that introduces chunked prefill (for HoL reasons); the KV-handoff protocol stays the same, only the “when to send” trigger moves from end-of-prefill to end-of-chunk.

Why per-request bulk wins at Iter 2:

  • Simplest + production-matching. Hits the bandwidth bar and matches how real P/D systems (DistServe, Mooncake, vLLM) ship today.
  • Sync complexity not worth it. Per-layer coupling buys a saving too small to justify at this iteration.
  • Fixed split cost. Iter 2 pays the 166 ms cold-path xfer; pipelining-by-chunks lands when the prefill scheduler becomes chunked.
  • Ceiling preserved for reference. The layer-wise calc above is the theoretical ceiling — production doesn’t pay the complexity to capture that last ~160 ms.

Steady-state per-task egress rate: the prefill task is busy at prefill_per_task ≈ 6.53 req/s (§8.5 Step 4) with mean payload 0.20 × slab_cold + 0.80 × slab_warm ≈ 0.884 GB/req → ~5.77 GB/s time-averaged per-task egress (bursty under bulk RDMA: a finished cold-path slab dumps 4.16 GB into the NIC over ~166 ms, then the task is back computing the next prefill). A single 50 GB/s NDR400/RoCE-400 GbE NIC absorbs this with ~9× margin. Fleet aggregate cross-pool BW = λpeak × mean(bytes/req) = 100 K × 0.884 GB ≈ 88 TB/s, spread across prefill_task_num × 8 NICs ≈ 160 K NIC flows.

DC3 — Routing model (how prefill picks decode)

Problem: after the Router routes to a prefill task, who picks the decode task — and on what key, to preserve the per-conv cache locality on the decode pool that Iter 1b just bought on the unified pool?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Router routes to both prefill and decode (two sequential decisions) — rejected: doubles Router hot-path work; adds one extra RTT (Router ↔ prefill task acknowledging the binding) before the prefill task can stream K,V to the chosen decode task. The decode pick is naturally co-located with the K,V producer.
  • Random / least-loaded decode pick by prefill task — rejected: cache-oblivious on the decode pool. Iter 1a’s tier hierarchy on decode-task Dα only earns its keep if turn-N of conv_id_X lands back on Dα. Random pick = decode-side cache hit rate → 1 / decode_task_num, replaying the B7 bug on the decode pool.
  • Fixed prefill↔decode pairs (bind one prefill task to one decode task at boot) — rejected: defeats independent autoscale. Pairing collapses the two rings into one and gives back the colocated-throughput ceiling per pair.
  • Prefill task picks decode task via CH(conv_id, decode_ring) — CHOSEN: same key as the Router used on the prefill side → cache stickiness preserved on both pools independently. Prefill task carries an in-memory copy of decode_ring (broker-gossiped, same Iter 1b DC5 read-replica pattern); decode pick is a local O(log V) lookup, no RTT.

Why CH(conv_id, decode_ring) on the prefill task wins:

  • Locality on both pools. Only choice that preserves it without putting the decode-pick on the Router hot path.
  • Self-recording binding. Async PrefillHandoff stores the binding so the next Router-side lookup for the same conv_id fast-paths.
  • Stale is harmless. Post-handoff Router state is best-effort — a stale binding just costs an extra prefill-side CH lookup for the same answer.

DC4 — Decode-pool batch size (B for the decode task)

Problem: the decode pool just got isolated from prefill. Decode at B=1 is HBM-BW-bound at MBU≈1% [G — K1 §3.5] — the GPU compute units are idle 99% of every tick. The whole point of splitting was to let decode pick its own B. So can we raise B on the decode pool now?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • B=1 on decode — CHOSEN (still, at this iteration): one in-flight decode slot per task. Carries the unified-task default forward onto the now-isolated decode pool.
  • B>1 on the contiguous-slab decode KV layout — rejected for the same reason as Iter 1a DC4: contiguous slab forces static-max reservation per slot. The decode pool inherits Iter 1a’s layout (paging is a later iteration); per-slot reservation max_reservation_tokens × KV_per_tok ≈ 78.8 K × 320 KB ≈ 25 GB against ~450 GB usable HBM caps B at ~18 even at the workload mean, and any p99-context slot inside an active batch blows the budget. Isolating the decode pool freed the FLOPs contention (prefill no longer competes for SMs), but it did not free the HBM accounting problem.
  • B>1 with padded contiguous slab (pad every slot to Tout_max) — rejected: makes the static-max problem strictly worse — reservation grows with the worst-case output budget on every slot regardless of actual length. The opposite of what the workload needs.
  • B>1 with paged blocks + continuous batching — deferred, not rejected: this is the structural answer (paging lets HBM track actual length per slot; continuous batching admits/evicts slots tick-by-tick so the active batch hits MBU target). It needs two prerequisites — a paged-block layout and a continuous-batching scheduler — both isolated to the decode pool the disagg split just created. Surfacing both at Iter 2 would bundle multiple iterations of work under one headline.

Why B=1 on decode wins at Iter 2:

  • Only half the problem freed. The split unlocks the resource-saturation path (no more SM sharing) but leaves the HBM-accounting path (static-max reservation) unfixed.
  • B>1 still OOMs. Without paging, raising B on decode blows up under the worst-case slot — same trap as Iter 1a DC4, now on the decode pool.
  • Sequenced fix. A later iteration rewrites the layout, then another raises B (the throughput multiplier this iteration invested in). Iter 2’s job is the topology precondition.
  • One bottleneck per iteration.

DC5 — Cross-pool transfer path: HBM→HBM fast path vs DRAM-bounce fallback

Problem: DC1 picked gRPC-over-RDMA as the wire protocol. Two physical paths can move bytes from prefill HBM into decode HBM using that same wire format:

(i) fast path: prefill HBM → NIC → wire → NIC → decode HBM (zero host-DRAM staging; needs GPUDirect-RDMA — NIC DMAs straight from/to GPU HBM via PCIe peer-to-peer, gated by NIC↔GPU peer- permission config) (ii) fallback: prefill HBM → PCIe → prefill DRAM → NIC → wire → NIC → decode DRAM → PCIe → decode HBM (two extra ~25 GB/s PCIe Gen4 bounces — the conservative path)

Pick a default; define when the other fires.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Always (ii) DRAM-bounce on every transfer — rejected: pays two unnecessary PCIe Gen4 round-trips. Cold-path 4.16 GB adds ~166 ms PCIe-in + ~166 ms PCIe-out on top of the 166 ms wire cost (partially overlapped by gRPC streaming, but still 100+ ms extra TTFT) [D — §8.5] — regression for no benefit whenever the fast path is available.
  • Always (i) fast path; fail the request if it’s unavailable — rejected: drops on any of receiver decode task at HBM watermark; GPUDirect peer-permission not configured for that NIC↔GPU pair; receiver task isolated for repair. Brittle — the exact failure modes the architecture should be hiding from clients.
  • Fast path (i) by default; transparently fall back to (ii) when receiver returns “no free HBM blocks” or the peer-path is unavailable — CHOSEN. Sender attempts (i) with a registered destination block; if the receiver's reservation fails, the sender retries via DRAM staging (which also doubles as the back-pressure release valve — sub-block below).

Why fast-with-fallback wins:

  • Fast is a free subset. Same wire, same gRPC framing, just skip the two PCIe bounces when both ends can stage HBM directly — zero cost to defaulting fast.
  • Fallback reason 1 — availability. GPUDirect-RDMA isn’t universal (PCIe topology + per-NIC peer-permission config; tasks under repair, mismatched NIC↔GPU domains).
  • Fallback reason 2 — back-pressure. It doubles as the release valve when decode HBM is momentarily full — the next sub-block shows why this doesn’t become the steady-state path despite the prefill/decode wall-clock asymmetry.

Decode-pool capacity imbalance & backpressure — when does the fallback actually fire?

TL;DR: imbalance is the core tension of P/D disagg, but it does not translate to perpetual fallback. Throughput is matched at the job level via asymmetric task counts (not per-task); bursts are absorbed by widening decode B at a later iteration (not by fallback firing); when the decode pool truly saturates, new-request intake is throttled — in-flight transfers are not.

The concern, step by step
  1. Prefill is fast per request — one parallel kernel pass over N_new input tokens; ~153 ms mean-mix [D — §8.5 Step 4].
  2. Decode is slow per request — Tout_mean = 400 sequential token steps at βper_decode_tok ≈ 5.22 ms each = ~2.09 s wall-clock per request [D — §6.6].
  3. So a 1:1 task ratio is catastrophic. If prefill_task_num = decode_task_num, decode-task HBM stays full → every incoming KVHandoff hits “no free blocks” → fallback path (ii) fires constantly → cold-path TTFT regresses from 166 ms to 300+ ms in steady state, defeating the topology.
Why the steady state is not perpetual fallback — the four levers
  1. Asymmetric replica counts (the dominant lever; sized at S6). The two jobs run with different task counts. From §8.5: prefill_per_task ≈ 6.53 req/s, decode_per_task ≈ 0.479 req/s → per-task ratio 13.6× in decode's favor. Sizing matches throughput at the job level: decode_task_num / prefill_task_num ≈ 13.6 (§8.5 Step 5: 19 909 prefill : 271 399 decode = 13.6:1 by construction). A 1:1 task ratio is a sizing bug, not an architectural property of P/D. The whole point of the split is the freedom to size each pool independently.
  2. Continuous batching on decode (deferred per DC4 to a later iteration). A later iteration introduces paged-layout + continuous batching together, raising decode B from 1 to ~100 (bounded by an HBM-headroom calculation). At that point each decode task holds many in-flight slots; a slow request doesn't stall others; arrivals join the running batch at the next tick. Burst absorption is by widening B, not lengthening queue.
  3. Decode is HBM-BW-bound, not compute-bound [G — K1 §3.5]. Per-tick weight reads = ~17.5 GB/GPU regardless of B (until HBM capacity caps B). Adding one more in-flight slot to an active decode batch is nearly free in compute — opposite of prefill, where every added request adds linearly to kernel FLOPs. A decode task running at B = 80 of Bmax = 100 can still cheaply accept one more, right up to the HBM cap.
  4. Backpressure throttles intake, not transfers. When the decode pool actually does approach saturation despite the above (peak burst, autoscale lag), the system slows the new-request faucet upstream rather than letting the cross-pool channel queue up. Multi-point — next.
Where backpressure acts (multi-point; scheduler picks per policy)
  • Router-level admission (most common). New client requests are queued at the Router or rejected with HTTP 429 / 503 (Too Many Requests / Service Unavailable). Protects in-flight requests' SLOs. Trigger signal: approx_wait_decode(t_d) aggregated over the decode pool exceeds a configured threshold — the same signal the autoscaler reads (one knob, two consumers).
  • Prefill-dispatch hold. Stop sending new prompts to prefill tasks; prefill tasks finish in-flight work and idle. New requests pile up in the Router queue. Used when the operator wants to drain the prefill pool cleanly (e.g. before a rolling restart of the prefill job).
  • KV-transfer flow control (the fallback path doubles as the valve). Prefill task finishes the K,V slab but holds it in prefill HBM (or spills to prefill DRAM — the (ii) path's first hop) until the chosen decode task signals free blocks. Analogous to TCP not sending until the receiver advertises window space. Once decode reports a free block, the transfer drains (fast or fallback depending on what unblocked first) and the prefill HBM is reclaimed for the next prefill.
  • Decode admission (the receiver's “no”). Decode block manager returns “no free blocks” when the per-task scheduler asks to reserve. Scheduler then chooses one of: (a) queue the request; (b) preempt a running decode request (evict its K,V — pays βDRAM(N) on resume); (c) reroute to the next vnode on CH(conv_id, decode_ring) (DC3 mechanism); (d) propagate backpressure upstream to Router.

What backpressure does NOT do. It does not kill in-flight requests. Decode keeps generating tokens for every slot already in live_conv_set(t_d); only new admits are throttled. The streaming HTTP response (SSE — Server-Sent Events) for in-flight requests stays open through the entire back-pressure event; clients see no interruption.

8.4  Wait-time formula delta (split per pool)

The Iter 1b unified approx_wait_time_ms splits into one signal per pool. Both ride the same broker-gossip pipeline; Router and the autoscaler consume them independently.

approx_wait_prefill(t_p) = Σ over queue(t_p) of βprefill(r) βprefill(r) = prefill_time(N_new(r), TP=8) [D — uses §4.1 formula] N_new(r) = tokens this turn the prefill task must compute K,V for: warm path (80%, intra_session_hit_rate from §1.3): N_new = Tuser_msg_mean = 200 tok (genuinely-new tokens only) (NOTE: PⒶ holds prior conv K,V only up through the prior *user* message via Iter 1a tier + Iter 1b CH-on-conv_id; it NEVER saw the prior *assistant* output — that K,V lives on DⒶ (decode). So a warm prefill must also re-materialize the ~400-tok asst delta. 200 is the genuinely-new floor; the recompute floor incl. the delta is ~600 tok — see §8.5 note.) cold path (20%): N_new = Tin_mean = 13.3 K tok (turn-1 of new conv, BLCH spill, or autoscale-fresh prefill task) approx_wait_decode(t_d) = max(0, queue(t_d) − free_slots(t_d)) × (avg_seq_lifetime / B) [D] avg_seq_lifetime = Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok = 400 × 5.22 ms = 2.09 s [D — §6.6] B = 1 at this iteration free_slots(t_d) = active_decode_capacity(t_d) − |live_conv_set(t_d)| active_decode_capacity(t_d) = B (= 1 at Iter 2; raised to Bdecode by a later iteration, bounded by Bmax,paged ≈ 106 post-Iter 3) live_conv_set(t_d) = {conv_id : admitted on t_d ∧ KV resident ∧ not yet EOS / Tout_max} — per-task scheduler set; conv enters on KVHandoff admit, leaves on EOS, Tout_max cap, or eviction ⇒ free_slots(t_d) ∈ {0, 1} at Iter 2 (idle or one live conv) [D]

How live_conv_set(t_d) is tracked in practice — the per-task scheduler maintains a single in-memory set live_convs: Set[conv_id] updated by four events; free_slots = B − len(live_convs) is then an O(1) read every tick:

  • +1 on KVHandoff admit. Prefill task hands over the prompt's K,V (per Iter 2 RDMA path); decode scheduler installs the conv into HBM, calls live_convs.add(conv_id), records admit_ts and output_count = 0. Atomic with the HBM-slot reservation so free_slots can never go negative.
  • −1 on EOS detection. Each tick, the kernel emits one new token per slot; if next_tok == eos_token_id, scheduler calls live_convs.remove(conv_id) and releases the HBM slab back to the free-list (Iter 2: contiguous slab; Iter 3+: paged blocks).
  • −1 on Tout_max cap hit. If output_count == Tout_max = 65 K before EOS, scheduler force-terminates the seq (same release path as EOS); response stream closes with a length finish reason.
  • −1 on watermark-driven eviction (rare during active decode at Iter 2). HBM low-watermark spill from Iter 1a evicts the LRU non-pinned conv to DRAM; at Iter 2 the active-decode set is pinned (cannot be evicted mid-decode — would cost βDRAM(N) per token), so this event fires only on already-paused / completed convs that haven't been formally released yet. Iter 3+ paged layout makes mid-decode partial eviction safer (single-block granularity), but the convention — remove from live_convs only when the seq is no longer actively decoding — carries over.
  • What changed. Iter 1b had a single per-task signal summed over a single queue. Iter 2 has one signal per pool, summed over each pool’s own queue. Router and BLCH spill (Iter 1b DC3) are unchanged in mechanism; they fire per pool, on the pool whose wait signal exceeds threshold.
  • Why the decode formula looks different. Decode-pool occupancy is governed by slot lifetime, not per-request prefill cost; an admitted decode slot ties up its share of HBM for the full avg_seq_lifetime. Queueing only kicks in when admit demand exceeds free_slots.
  • Tier-residency still routes the cost. βHBM ≪ βDRAM(N) ≪ βSSD(N) ≪ βcold(N) still holds on each pool independently; the decode pool resolves tier_residency_for(r) at KVHandoff time, the prefill pool at request admit time.

8.5  Number chain — the cross-pool transfer cost (the new term)

Per-task req/s does not move at Iter 2 (architectural iteration; B=1 on both pools). What appears is the new cross-pool transfer term that lives between prefill and decode. Per-pool throughput math is shown so the iteration’s zero-net-throughput claim is auditable; the throughput win itself lands at later iterations on each pool (prefill kernel-side wins on the prefill pool; decode batching wins on the decode pool).

Step 1 — KV slab size per turn (cold and warm)

cold transfer (turn-1, decode-task has nothing): N_xfer_cold = Tin_mean = 13 K tok [G — §1.3] slab_cold = N_xfer_cold × KV_per_tok = 13 000 × 320 KB = 4.16 GB [D — §4.2] warm transfer (turn-N, decode-task already has prior turns’ K,V on its sticky tier): N_xfer_warm = Tuser_msg_mean = 200 tok [G — §1.3] slab_warm = 200 × 320 KB = 64 000 KB = 64 MB [D] (decimal MB to match slab_cold and slab_p99 below; the precise 327 680 B/tok value from §4.2 gives 65.5 MB, the §4.2 canonical literal 320 KB × 200 gives 64 MB) p99 transfer (cold at p99 context): N_xfer_p99 = Tconv_hist_p99 = 256 K tok [G — §1.3] slab_p99 = 256 000 × 320 KB = 82 GB [D]

Step 2 — cross-pool transfer time at 25 GB/s effective per flow

Two reference calculations per DC2: per-request bulk (CHOSEN) is the primary cost; layer-wise streaming (alternative) is the theoretical ceiling.

RDMA_per_flow = 25 GB/s (NDR400 IB or RoCE-400 GbE) [G — K20] # Per-request bulk RDMA (CHOSEN; production default): xfer_cold = slab_cold / RDMA_per_flow = 4.16 GB / 25 GB/s = 166 ms [D] xfer_warm = slab_warm / RDMA_per_flow = 64 MB / 25 GB/s = 2.6 ms [D] xfer_p99 = slab_p99 / RDMA_per_flow = 82 GB / 25 GB/s = 3.28 s [D] # Layer-wise streaming (alternative; only the last layer exposed in TTFT): per_layer_KV (cold) = slab_cold / n_layers = 4.16 GB / 80 = 52 MB per_layer_xfer (cold) = 52 MB / 25 GB/s = ~2.1 ms [D] per_layer_KV (warm) = slab_warm / n_layers = 64 MB / 80 = 0.80 MB per_layer_xfer (warm) = 0.80 MB / 25 GB/s = ~32 µs [D] ⇒ xfer_cold_exposed_layer_wise ≈ 2.1 ms (last layer only) ⇒ xfer_warm_exposed_layer_wise ≈ 32 µs (last layer only)

Step 3 — per-request total time at Iter 2 (the cold-path regression)

req_time_iter2_cold = prefill_time(13 K, TP=8) + xfer_cold + Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok = 730 ms + 166 ms + 400 × 5.22 ms = 730 + 166 + 2 088 = 2 984 ms / req [D — §4.1, §6.6] req_time_iter2_warm = prefill_time(200, TP=8) + xfer_warm + decode_time = 8.9 ms + 2.6 ms + 2 088 ms = 2 100 ms / req [D] req_time_iter2_mix (at intra_session_hit_rate = 0.80): = 0.20 × 2 984 + 0.80 × 2 100 = 596.8 + 1 680 = 2 277 ms / req [D] vs Iter 1b: 2 240 ms (same workload mix, colocated) → net regression ≈ +37 ms (~1.6%) at the mean mix → cold TTFT: 730 ms → 896 ms (the 166 ms transfer cost is paid pre-first-token) → this is the price tag of the architectural split, paid at this iteration; pipelining the transfer (per-chunk overlap) lands at a later iteration alongside chunked prefill. Layer-wise streaming alternative (DC2 alternative — rarely shipped): req_time_iter2_cold_layer_wise = 730 + 2.1 + 2088 = 2 820 ms ([D]) req_time_iter2_warm_layer_wise = 8.9 + 0.03 + 2088 = 2 097 ms ([D]) req_time_iter2_mix_layer_wise = 0.20 × 2 820 + 0.80 × 2 097 = 2 242 ms → net regression vs Iter 1b ≈ +2 ms (~0.1%) — theoretical ceiling.
Warm-prefill caveat — the 200-tok figure is the genuinely-new floor, not the full warm-prefill cost.
  • Why PⒶ can’t actually stop at 200. Pre-split (Iter 1b) one unified task did prefill and decode, so it held the whole conversation — the “200 tok warm” claim was true. After the P/D split it is not: the prefill task PⒶ streams each turn’s input K,V to the decode task DⒶ, which then decodes the assistant reply locally. So DⒶ ends each turn holding the full conversation (inputs + its own ~400-tok decoded output); PⒶ never saw the assistant tokens. On turn N the chained prefix-match on PⒶ hits up through the prior user message but misses on the prior assistant output.
  • Consequence. To prefill the 200 genuinely-new tokens, PⒶ needs the full prior K,V in attention context — including the ~400-tok assistant delta it never computed. It must re-materialize that delta. So the real warm prefill is user_msg_N (200) + asst_{N−1} (Tout_mean = 400) ≈ 600 tok, with ~400 of those a pure redundant recompute of K,V DⒶ already holds.
  • Recompute floor (this iteration, no store). prefill_time(600) ≈ 27 ms vs prefill_time(200) = 8.9 ms. The +400 tok adds 0.80 × 400 = 320 prefill tok/req ≈ +12% prefill FLOPs on the mean prefill mix (and lifts prefill_task_num ~9%). It does not move req_time_warm materially: decode (2.09 s) dwarfs the ~18 ms prefill delta.
  • Store-pull floor (Iter 3b, a later iteration). Iter 3b's pooled cross-task KV store lets PⒶ pull the asst delta as cheap I/O (~5 ms RDMA) instead of recomputing it — the ~400 tok leave the prefill-FLOP count and warm GPU-prefill returns to the 200-tok floor. The Step 4–6 numbers below assume that store-pull floor (the expected steady state, since the inter-turn gap ≈ seconds ≫ the async writeback ≈ ms); the recompute floor is the +12%-FLOPs degraded case (against this iteration’s 2.76 K-tok mean prefill mix — the same +320 tok/req is a larger +17% once Iter 5 prefix caching shrinks that baseline to ~1.81 K, §11.6). The cross-pool Hop slab (Step 1, 200 tok → 64 MB) is correct either way — DⒶ already holds the prior context, so PⒶ ships only the new turn.

Step 4 — per-pool per-task throughput at B=1 (the floor each pool will exceed later)

prefill_per_task (B=1, mean-mix): prefill_time_mix = 0.20 × prefill_time(13K) + 0.80 × prefill_time(200) = 0.20 × 730 ms + 0.80 × 8.9 ms = 146 + 7.12 = 153 ms [D] prefill_per_task_iter2 = 1 / 153 ms ≈ 6.53 req/s/task [D] decode_per_task (B=1): decode_per_task_iter2 = 1 / (Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok) = 1 / 2.088 ≈ 0.479 req/s/task [D]

Step 5 — pool sizing at λpeak (at B=1; lifted by later iterations)

prefill_task_num_iter2 = ⌈ λpeak × 1.3 / prefill_per_task_iter2 ⌉ = ⌈ 130 000 / 6.53 ⌉ = ⌈ 19 908.12 ⌉ = 19 909 prefill tasks [D] decode_task_num_iter2 = ⌈ λpeak × 1.3 / decode_per_task_iter2 ⌉ = ⌈ 130 000 / 0.479 ⌉ = ⌈ 271 398.75 ⌉ = 271 399 decode tasks [D] total_serving_iter2 = 19 909 + 271 399 = 291 308 tasks [D] vs Iter 1b unified: 291 500 tasks → roughly flat at Iter 2 (~0.07% less) — the iteration does not save tasks by itself; it is the topology precondition for later per-pool throughput wins.
Why decode_task_num ≫ prefill_task_num here — and why that ratio inverts at Iter 6.
  • Root cause: holding time, not work per token. A prefill task holds a request for ~153 ms (mean mix) then releases the slot. A decode task holds it for Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok = 400 × 5.22 ms = 2 088 ms — the full output sequence. At B=1 per-task decode throughput is 2 088 / 153 ≈ 13.6× lower than prefill throughput, so the fleet count ratio is the same 13.6×. The 271 K decode tasks are not doing more work; they are each sitting idle between token steps waiting for the sequence to finish.
  • Batching inverts the ratio. At Bdecode = 106 (HBM-capacity ceiling, a later iteration):
    decode_per_task (B=106) = 106 / 2.088 s ≈ 50.8 req/s/task decode_task_num (B=106) = ⌈130 000 / 50.8⌉ ≈ 2 559 tasks (106× collapse vs 271 K)
    Prefill stays ~20 K (FLOPs-bound; batching helps less because prefill cost scales with total tokens in the batch). The ratio inverts: prefill pool ≈ 8× larger than decode pool — consistent with production P/D systems (Mooncake, SGLang) where the prefill fleet is ≥ the decode fleet for chat workloads.
  • What this iteration is actually doing. The 271 K figure is the B=1 floor, stated explicitly so the later iteration’s 106× reduction is auditable. Anchoring on 271 K as the expected production decode fleet would be wrong.

Step 6 — $/mo at Iter 2

$_per_TP8_task_per_mo = $70 790 [D — §7.4 Step 5] $/mo_iter2 = 291 308 × $70 790 ≈ $20.62 B / month [D] vs Iter 1b 291 500 × $70 790 ≈ $20.64 B/mo → ~0.07% lower (mirrors the Step 5 task-count drop — sum-of-pool sizing rounds slightly below the colocated total). Cross-pool transfer is sub-ms on the warm path, ~166 ms on the cold path — net request-time impact is ~1.6%. → the $ delta booked at this iteration is zero on purpose; the cost payout lands at a later iteration that turns this iteration’s architectural investment into a throughput win.

Step 7 — how far apart can the prefill-task host and the decode-task host sit?

At Iter 2 cold-path TTFT is already over the 400 ms SLO (730 ms prefill alone) regardless of placement. Two complementary binds emerge, neither one a tight SLO bind:

  • Bind A — warm-path TTFT SLO conformance (the path that is in budget at Iter 2):
prefill_time(Tuser_msg_mean=200, TP=8) + xfer_warm + first_decode_setup ≤ 400 ms 8.9 ms + xfer_warm + 5 ms ≤ 400 ms ⇒ xfer_budget_warm = 386 ms [D] slab_warm = 200 × 320 KB = 64 MB min_BW_warm = 64 MB / 386 ms = 0.17 GB/s [D] → very loose; clears everything but cross-continent.
  • Bind B — cold-path xfer should not dominate the (already-broken) cold cost: keep xfer_cold ≤ prefill_time(13 K) so the new cross-pool term doesn’t exceed the term it’s sitting next to. This is a sanity bound, not an SLO bound:
xfer_cold ≤ prefill_time(13 K, TP=8) = 730 ms [D — §4.1] slab_cold = 4.16 GB min_BW_cold = 4.16 GB / 730 ms = 5.7 GB/s [D] # RTT contribution at every distance class below is µs-to-ms; BW binds, not RTT.

Map both binds against the BOE distance table (per NEW_BOE.md, conservative bottom-of-range BW per the “always pick the lowest” rule):

Distance classBW (low end)RTT one-wayxfer_coldxfer_warmClears Bind A (warm SLO)?Clears Bind B (cold ≤ prefill)?
Same rack (~2 m)25 GB/s~1 µs166 ms2.6 ms✓✓
Same row (~10 m)25 GB/s~2 µs166 ms2.6 ms✓✓
Same data hall (~100 m)25 GB/s~5 µs166 ms2.6 ms✓✓
Same DC, diff building (~1 km)10 GB/s~10 µs416 ms6.4 ms✓✓ (xfer 416 ms < prefill 730 ms)
Same metro (~50 km)5 GB/s~250 µs832 ms12.8 ms✓✗ (xfer 832 ms > prefill 730 ms — transfer dominates)
Cross-region (~1 000 km)1 GB/s~5 ms4.16 s64 ms✓ (warm only)✗ (xfer 5.7× prefill cost)
Cross-continent (~10 000 km)0.1 GB/s~50–80 ms41.6 s640 ms✗✗
  • Practical placement at Iter 2: same DC (≤ ~1 km) — the tighter of the two binds. Beyond same-DC, the cross-pool transfer starts dominating the (already-broken) cold-path cost; same-metro pushes xfer past prefill time.
  • Bind A is loose because cold-path is already over SLO at Iter 2. The placement question becomes tightly SLO-bound only once the cold-path prefill cost is reduced (later iteration via prefix caching) or the transfer is pipelined into prefill compute (later iteration via chunked prefill).
  • The §1.2 NFR “single metro” row is load-bearing — cross-region placement is structurally out at the cold path even before any SLO argument (xfer = 5.7× prefill cost; 4 s extra TTFT).
  • p99 conv (256 K cold): 82 GB cold transfer is over the entire TTFT budget at every distance class (same-rack 25 GB/s → 3.28 s). The p99 cold tail is an independent problem addressed at a later iteration.
  • Aggregate fabric BW sanity check. Mean per-request cross-pool data at workload mix = 0.20 × 4.16 GB + 0.80 × 64 MB ≈ 883 MB/req. At λpeak = 100 K req/s, aggregate fabric load = ~88 TB/s spanning both pools — absorbable by within-DC fabric switching (thousands of parallel 25 GB/s NDR400/RoCE-400 GbE flows). Per-pool ingress/egress bandwidth is the real capacity-planning number for the interconnect.
  • Under layer-wise streaming (DC2 alternative), the binding would change to per_layer_xfer ≤ per_layer_compute ⇒ BW ≥ 52 MB / 9.1 ms = 5.7 GB/s — same-DC still clears comfortably, same-metro just misses the overlap threshold (5 GB/s < 5.7 GB/s) and pays ~115 ms instead of ~2 ms. The placement ceiling under either DC2 option lands at same-DC for clean operation.
Why the “flat $/mo” is the right answer. Conventional wisdom expects every iteration to drop the cost line; Iter 2 deliberately doesn’t. The split is the architectural precondition for downstream wins, not a cost-saving itself: it isolates a decode-only pool so decode-only optimisations (layout fixes, batching) can land later without dragging prefill into the same kernel launch, and a prefill-only pool so prefill-only optimisations (chunked + packed kernel) can land later without competing with decode for SMs. Heterogeneous-hardware follow-on (cheaper HBM-rich chips on decode) becomes possible after this split too. Bundling the split with any single later throughput-multiplying iteration would hide the topology decision under that headline. Single-purpose contracts per iteration.

8.6  What breaks

  1. Cold-path TTFT regression (the 166 ms tax). Cold transfer adds 166 ms to TTFT on turn-1 of a new conv (vs Iter 1b 730 ms → Iter 2 896 ms). At p99 context, transfer is 3.28 s — well over TTFT SLO on its own (and decoupled from the 65 s p99 prefill cost B6 still owes). Detection: per-request TTFT histogram split by tier_residency_for(r) at the decode task. Mitigation: addressed at later iterations — prefix-cache reduction of unmatched-token count shrinks the transfer; pipelining transfer with first-decode-tick setup hides part of the cost; CP handles the 1 M-context outlier separately.
  2. P/D fleet-sizing imbalance. Prefill demand spikes faster than decode (a burst of long prompts saturates prefill before decode notices). With per-pool autoscale on independent wait signals, the prefill pool may scale up before decode — or vice-versa — producing transient mismatch. Detection: per-pool wait_p95 ratio + cross-pool handoff success rate. Mitigation: per-pool autoscale on its own approx_wait_* signal (so each tracks its own rooflined load); independent ramp budgets per pool (±10%/min from Iter 1b DC4); pre-warmed standbys (Iter 1b DC2 (b)) on the pool that historically leads the burst.
  3. Cross-pool transport failure mid-stream. gRPC stream from prefill task to decode task drops (TCP reset, decode task dies, IB link flap) after partial K,V transfer. Decode task can’t make progress with a partial slab. Detection: KVHandoffAck timeout at the prefill task (per-stream watchdog ~SLO-aware). Mitigation: prefill task is the source of truth (it still holds the K,V it just computed); on timeout, prefill task re-picks via CH(conv_id, decode_ring) — the same key after the broker-gossiped ring update may resolve to a different decode task (if the original died and the ring re-balanced). Bounded retry budget (3); excess → 503 to client via Router. The full FT story including in-flight conv recovery lands at a later iteration.
  4. Stale binding on the Router. PrefillHandoff is async; until it arrives, Router’s next-turn decision for the same conv_id uses the stale binding (or has none). Detection: Router’s binding-cache hit-vs-prefill-CH-lookup ratio; PrefillHandoff end-to-end latency p99. Mitigation: binding cache is best-effort; on miss, prefill task re-computes the decode pick from its in-memory decode_ring — same answer, just one O(log V) CPU lookup. No correctness impact; only a ~µs-scale latency hit on miss.
  5. Decode-pool admission-control gap. If decode pool fills (all slots bound to live convs) while prefill is still computing K,V, the KVHandoff has nowhere to land. Detection: decode-side free_slots hitting 0 plus queue > 0. Mitigation: decode-side admission gate — KVHandoff carries an admit token negotiated at PrefillHandoff time; if no token, prefill task buffers up to T_handoff_grace (~100 ms; ~20 decode ticks at βper_decode_tok = 5.22 ms — long enough for one or two EOS-driven slot releases on the bound decode task to land) then fails the request via Router back to client.
