The full per-user admission-control story (token bucket + concurrent-conv cap) lands at a later iteration; this is the lower-level data-plane gate.
  6. B2 + B3-fragmentation still unhandled. Decode at B=1 still HBM-BW-wasted (MBU ~1%); contiguous-slab KV layout still over-allocates HBM per decode slot. Recovery: later iterations introduce paging and continuous batching on the decode pool the split just isolated.
  7. B6 still unhandled. A 256 K cold prefill is still ~65 s on its prefill task — the split alone doesn’t shrink the worst-case prefill cost. Recovery: a later iteration introduces chunked + packed prefill on the prefill pool the split just isolated.

8.7  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 1b)Post-iter (Iter 2)ΔNote
TTFT p99~730 ms (cold mix-bounded)~896 ms (cold + 166 ms bulk xfer)↑Mild regression by design: the 166 ms cold cross-pool transfer (per-request bulk RDMA per DC2 CHOSEN) is paid before the first decode token. p99 is bounded by the cold-path tail, which dominates the tail-mix. Recovery path: later iterations cut the unmatched-token count (prefix caching), pipeline the transfer with prefill compute (chunked prefill drives per-chunk streaming), and attack the βcold(N) tail itself. Layer-wise streaming alternative (DC2 alternative) would give ~732 ms (only last-layer 2 ms exposed), but is rarely shipped due to per-layer sync complexity.

Cross-pool transfer overhead at the mean mix is sub-ms and doesn’t move $/mo. The architectural investment is paid in this iteration; the headline payout lands at a later iteration.

Iter 2 splits the rooflines (B9) and pays the cross-pool KV transport cost (B10(i)) — ~166 ms cold-path TTFT regression under per-request bulk RDMA (CHOSEN) or ~2 ms under the rarely-shipped layer-wise streaming alternative, and ~0.06% extra fleet cost — in exchange for the topology that makes the next several iterations possible. The decode pool is now isolated and ready for layout + batching changes; the prefill pool is isolated and ready for kernel-level wins (including chunked prefill, which naturally promotes the KV transfer to per-chunk pipelining).

9. Iter 3 — PagedAttention (the layout fix that unlocks decode batching)

Bottleneck addressed: B3-fragmentation (contiguous slab over-allocates ~5.9× at workload-mean, up to ~24× at p99-context tail). Architectural / accounting iteration — per-task throughput is unchanged at B=1; the win is the HBM headroom that a later iteration converts into Bdecode. Deferred to later iterations: B2, B5, B6, B10(ii).

Notation introduced in this section — paging machinery + the HBM-capacity accounting. (Workload T… symbols and KVper_tok are owned by §1.3 / §4.2.)

block_size
PagedAttention allocation unit — 16 tokens of K,V (fixed config constant; vLLM default).
block_KV_bytes
bytes one block holds = block_size × KVper_tok = 5.12 MB.
total_blocks
paged blocks per task = GKV / block_KV_bytes ≈ 87 890.
block_id
index of one physical 5.12 MB block in the page pool (the alloc/free unit).
BlockTable[seq_id]
This IS a map: sequence → ordered list of its block_ids (the scattered-layout index). One block appended per 16 decoded tokens.
tier_tag[block_id]
This IS a map: block → its current tier ∈ {HBM, DRAM, SSD}. Updated on swap-in / evict.
slot_mapping
int32[active_B] — the next physical block_id each active slot writes its new token’s K,V into. Rebuilt by the CPU scheduler every decode tick.
GKV
HBM-for-KV budget per task = HBM_total − weights − activations = 450 GB.
max_reservation_tokens
pre-PA worst-case tokens a slot must reserve at admit = Tin_at_admit + Tout_max = 78 K.
Tin_at_admit
prompt length at admit (= Tin_mean at the workload-mean admit; the reservation-formula alias).
reserved_per_slot
pre-PA static-max HBM per slot = max_reservation_tokens × KVper_tok = 24.96 GB.
avg_used_per_slot
post-PA HBM a slot actually occupies, averaged over its decode life = (Tin_mean + Tout_mean/2) × KVper_tok = 4.22 GB. Also written avg_steady_state_kv_per_active_slot / avg_kv_per_active_slot elsewhere — same quantity.
Bmax,static
max concurrent decode slots under static reservation = ⌊GKV/reserved_per_slot⌋ ≈ 18 (≈ 4 if one slot is a p99-context admit).
Bmax,paged
max concurrent decode slots after paging = ⌊GKV/avg_used_per_slot⌋ ≈ 106. The capacity ceiling Iter 6’s Bdecode lifts toward.
hbm_resident_slots
active-decode ceiling = GKV / avg_steady_state_kv_per_active_slot ≈ 106 (numerically = Bmax,paged).
avg_dram_resident_kv_per_conv
per-conversation DRAM footprint = (Tconv_hist_median + Tout_mean) × KVper_tok = 2.69 GB.
dram_resident_convs / ssd_resident_convs
conversation KVs the DRAM / SSD tier can hold ≈ 297 / 2 416.
slot_resident_total
resident conversation KVs per task across all three cache tiers ≈ 2 819 (vs ~106 actively decoding).

9.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① BlockTable indirection (CPU → GPU) — each seq_id row stores the scattered physical block_ids that hold its K,V. The attention kernel reads K,V via this indirection; no need for any seq’s K,V to be contiguous in HBM. This pointer-chase IS the layout fix.
  • ② per-tick slot_mapping upload — CPU scheduler builds slot_mapping: int32[active_B] each tick (the next physical block_id each live slot will write its newly decoded tok’s K,V into) and DMAs it to GPU. Dirty-row optimization: only rows whose block_ids changed since last tick are uploaded.
  • ┄┄ ③ HBM → DRAM eviction (red dashed, async) — when the free-list count crosses the HBM low-watermark, the LRU non-pinned block’s 5.12 MB content is D2H’d to a DRAM page; the tier_tag[block_id] flips from HBM to DRAM; the block_id returns to the free-list. Coalesced into Tswap-window batches.
  • ④ DRAM → HBM swap-in (solid) — on admit / next-turn access for a block currently tagged DRAM, the scheduler pops a free block_id, H2D’s the 5.12 MB page, and flips the tag back to HBM. Cost = βDRAM(N).
  • ┄┄ ⑤ DRAM → SSD spill (red dashed, async) — DRAM at its watermark spills coldest blocks via filesystem write; GPUDirect Storage handles the SSD-side write without bouncing through the CPU page cache.
  • ⑥ SSD → DRAM swap-in (solid) — on access, GDS reads the 5.12 MB page; block goes via DRAM en route back to HBM (two-hop), or direct SSD→HBM via GDS on a single-tier-miss path.

9.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Enabling prior. Iter 2’s disagg gave us a decode-only pool — the entire HBM-for-KV budget per task targets a homogeneous decode workload (prefill activations no longer share). The next per-task move on that pool is raising the decode batch (the throughput win the disagg topology was an investment for). But it doesn’t fit yet, because of the next bullet.
  • The constraint that flipped — HBM accounting blocks the decode-batch win. The contiguous-slab layout inherited from Iter 1a reserves a fixed budget per slot at admit time. Pre-PA, an engine that wants to admit a slot needs to guarantee the worst-case length it might reach — otherwise mid-decode growth would have to grow a contiguous slab in place, which means either pre-reserving or expensively re-locating the slot. Production engines (e.g. vLLM v0) reserved Tin_at_admit+Tout_max per slot. At this workload that is (13 K+65 K) × 320 KB ≈ 25 GB / slot against GKV = 450 GB usable on a decode task → Bmax,static ≈ 18; the p99-context tail ((256 K+65 K) × 320 KB ≈ 103 GB / slot) caps a single such admit at Bmax,static ≈ 4. Either ceiling kills the decode-batching plan before it starts.
  • PagedAttention’s job is HBM accounting, NOT throughput. Fixed 16-token blocks (block_size = 16) [G — K2 #paging], one per 5.12 MB chunk of K,V; BlockTable[seq_id] = [block_id, block_id, …] stores the scattered mapping; the attention kernel reads K,V via this indirection. Each slot now consumes HBM proportional to its actual current length, not its worst-case reservation. Effective concurrent-request capacity at fixed HBM multiplies by the workload-mean over-allocation ratio (~5.9×). This is what unlocks raising decode B on the decode pool later — the throughput multiplier that pays back the whole Iter 2 + Iter 3 architectural investment.
  • Why this is its own iteration (not bundled with the decode-batching move that consumes it). PagedAttention by itself moves no SLO — per-task throughput at B=1 is unchanged (0.479 req/s/task on the decode pool from §8.5 carries through). Bundling it with the batching move would hide the layout decision under the throughput headline; isolating it makes the layout-fix-before-throughput dependency explicit. One bottleneck per iteration.
  • “Why not batch first and fix layout later?” — can’t. With the contiguous slab, admitting B > Bmax,static slots OOMs before the first decode tick completes. The order must be layout fix → batching; the reverse doesn’t survive a single admit.
  • “Why not larger HBM (bigger GPU) instead?” — H100-80GB is the largest single-GPU HBM available in this hardware era. Doubling per-GPU HBM only buys 2× concurrent capacity; PagedAttention recovers ~5.9× at workload-mean and up to ~24× on the p99 tail. The structural fix dominates the hardware fix by an order of magnitude, at zero extra dollars.
  • “Why not just pad every slot to Tout_max and call it good?” — that is the static-max reservation; it is what the contiguous slab already pays. Padding is the disease, not the cure.

9.3  Design choices

[param] block_size (PagedAttention): contiguous static-max slab → 16-token paged blocks — Iter 3. Why: static-max reserves worst-case per slot (~5.9× mean / ~24× p99 waste); paging into fixed 16-tok blocks ends over-reservation and lifts the active-batch ceiling from Bmax,static ≈ 18 to Bmax,paged ≈ 106 — the headroom Iter 6’s Bdecode consumes. Cliff: larger blocks → more internal fragmentation; smaller → more block-table overhead; 16 is the vLLM sweet spot.

DC1 — KV layout (the per-slot HBM accounting unit)

Problem: at admit time, the engine has to allocate HBM for a slot whose final K,V size is unknown (depends on how many output tokens decode produces). Pick a layout that lets many such slots co-exist in GKV = 450 GB without each one blocking worst-case headroom.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Padded contiguous tensor (every slot padded to Tout_max) — rejected: kernel reads/writes go through the padded region too, wasting HBM BW on positions that don’t exist; same accounting waste as static-max and a per-tick BW tax. Strictly worse.
  • Static-max contiguous slab (reserve Tin_at_admit+Tout_max per slot at admit) — this is what Iter 0–2 inherit. Layout is simple, kernels see one contiguous tensor per slot. The B3-fragmentation cost: at workload-mean, reserved/slot ≈ 25 GB vs used/slot ≈ 4.22 GB over the slot’s decode life → ~5.9× waste; at p99-context, reserved/slot ≈ 103 GB → ~24× waste (the vLLM-paper figure). Blocks the decode-batching plan that the disagg topology was an investment for. This is the bottleneck this iteration retires.
  • Per-slot grow-in-place contiguous slab (allocate exactly the prompt at admit, append decode toks contiguously into a re-locatable region) — rejected: re-locating a multi-GB slab mid-decode would stall the kernel for ~100 ms per move; and the free-list under churn (admit / evict cycling) fragments into useless gaps that a single big slot can’t fill. Compaction would be a global pause — unshippable on the hot path.
  • PagedAttention — fixed-size 16-token blocks indexed by BlockTable — CHOSEN: each block holds 16 toks × 320 KB = 5.12 MB of K,V; the page pool is a flat array of ~87 890 such blocks per task; BlockTable[seq_id] = [block_id, block_id, …] stores the scattered mapping; attention kernels gather K,V via this indirection. Allocation = pop from free-list (O(1)); release = push to free-list (O(1)); slot HBM consumption grows by exactly one block per 16 decoded toks. The static-max reservation simply disappears. [G — K2 #paging]

Why PagedAttention wins:

  • Cheap indirection, huge unlock. ~3–5% kernel cost (pointer-chase + scattered gather, per K2) buys ~5.9× concurrent capacity at fixed HBM.
  • Lopsided crossover. The indirection cost is dwarfed by the capacity the next iteration turns into throughput.
  • No alternative closes the gap. Nothing else bridges “25 GB reserved per slot” vs “4.22 GB actually used” without re-locating slabs mid-decode (unshippable) or paying the worst-case length tax (status quo).

Why block_size = 16 tok specifically. Trade-off between (i) internal fragmentation (smaller blocks → less waste at the trailing partial block — bounded by (16−1) × 320 KB = 4.8 MB / slot at any block size 16; the wasted fraction shrinks with block size relative to a slot’s typical length), (ii) BlockTable row length (smaller blocks → longer rows — at 13 K toks, 16-tok blocks give 813 entries / row, comfortably below the per-tick PCIe upload budget), and (iii) kernel tile alignment (16 matches H100 TensorCore tile sizes for K,V layouts). The vLLM default landed at 16 after sweeping; cite K2.

9.4  Multi-tier paged-block residency (extends Iter 1a tiering to block granularity)

The paged-block pool is not HBM-only. When the HBM free-list count crosses the low_watermark, evicted blocks don’t drop — they spill down the Iter 1a tier hierarchy at block granularity. The scheduler maintains tier_tag[block_id] ∈ {HBM, DRAM, SSD} on the CPU side; on admit / next-turn access, non-HBM blocks are swapped in (PCIe DMA for DRAM→HBM; GPUDirect Storage for SSD→HBM) before kernel launch. Population numbers per tier substituted from the §1.5 hardware spec:

The per-tier victim policy carries over from Iter 1a unchanged — only the unit shrinks. Each tier keeps its Iter 1a DC5 / DC6 rule (HBM = LRU + ref_count pin; DRAM and SSD = plain LRU), and eviction stays non-cascading tier-skip: the coldness gate (last_access ∧ ref_count==0 ∧ session_idle) now ranks individual 16-token blocks rather than whole contiguous slabs, so a cold block skips straight to the cheapest adequate tier instead of dragging through every one. (No remote tier yet — that lands at Iter 3b.) The watermark trigger, the DRAM→SSD pre-offload, and how the HBM / DRAM watermarks are sized are built out in cache_eviction_policy.html.

TierUsable capacity per taskRead latency to GPU (per Iter 1a β-functions)Population it can hold (derived)
HBM (active decode set) ~450 GB [D] βHBM ≈ 5 ms (already resident; decode-tick setup only) hbm_resident_slots = GKV / avg_steady_state_kv_per_active_slot = 450 GB / 4.22 GB ≈ 106 active decode slots [D] — this is the structural ceiling a later iteration’s decode batch lifts toward.
CPU DRAM (warm L2 cache of recent convs) ~800 GB (1 TB host − 200 GB OS / scheduler) [G-FA] βDRAM(N) = N × 320 KB / 25 GB/s · at N=13 K → 166 ms dram_resident_convs = usable_DRAM / avg_dram_resident_kv_per_conv = 800 GB / 2.69 GB ≈ 297 conversation KVs [D] — per-conv DRAM footprint = (Tconv_hist_median+Tout_mean) × KVper_tok = 8.4 K × 320 KB = 2.69 GB
Local NVMe SSD (cold L3 cache of dormant convs) ~6.5 TB (7 TB raw − FS overhead) [G-FA] βSSD(N) = N × 320 KB / 7 GB/s · at N=13 K → 594 ms ssd_resident_convs = usable_SSD / 2.69 GB ≈ 2 416 conversation KVs [D]
Re-prefill (triple-tier miss; from prompt_tokens) ∞ (always recoverable, RPO=0) βcold(N) per §4.1 · at N=13 K → 730 ms; at N=256 K → 65.5 s fallback only on confirmed triple-tier miss
Total resident across all three cache tiers ~7.75 TB tier-dependent on miss slot_resident_total ≈ 106 + 297 + 2 416 ≈ 2 819 conversation KVs per task [D]

This is what makes the fleet-wide intra_session_hit_rate = 0.80 achievable. HBM-only math would give decode_task_num × hbm_resident_slots resident convs fleet-wide. At Iter 2’s sizing (decode_task_num ≈ 271 399), HBM-only residency would be 271 399 × 106 ≈ 28.8 M active-decode slots — well above the 3 M peak-concurrent-sessions population from §1.3, but only because Iter 2 is over-provisioned at B=1. Once a later iteration raises decode B, decode_task_num shrinks proportionally; the multi-tier residency keeps the fleet able to hold all active conversations even when the active-decode set collapses to a fraction of the fleet. Per-task: ~2 819 resident conv KVs vs ~11 concurrently-assigned live convs (3 M active sessions / ~271 K decode tasks; only 1 actively decoding at this iteration’s B=1, against the Bmax,paged ≈ 106 capacity ceiling) ⇒ turn-N requests landing back on the same task (via Iter 1b CH-on-conv_id) pay βDRAM or βSSD instead of βcold in the overwhelming majority of cases.

Active-batch vs residency-cache are distinct concepts. hbm_resident_slots is the active-decode ceiling at steady-state continuous-batching averaging. avg_used_per_slot uses the (Tin_mean+Tout_mean/2) upper bound for any individual slot; the batch-mean at steady state is lower because short conversations turn over faster than long ones and early-decode slots haven’t grown to Tout_mean/2 yet. The resident-cache slot_resident_total in DRAM + SSD lets the next turn of any of those conversations land back on the same task and pay βDRAM or βSSD instead of βcold.

9.5  Number chain — the Bmax multiplier (capacity unlock, throughput unchanged)

Per-task throughput at Iter 3 is unchanged from Iter 2: decode_per_task_iter3 = decode_per_task_iter2 ≈ 0.479 req/s/task, prefill_per_task_iter3 = prefill_per_task_iter2 ≈ 6.53 req/s/task, total_serving_iter3 ≈ 291 308 tasks, $/mo_iter3 ≈ $20.62 B/mo. What changes is the structural capacity: the per-slot HBM accounting unit shrinks ~5.9×, lifting Bmax,paged from Bmax,static ≈ 18 to ~106. A later iteration consumes this headroom to multiply per-task decode throughput. Iter 3’s number chain spells out the capacity ratio so the later throughput math is auditable.

Step 1 — HBM-for-KV budget per task

HBM_total_per_task = 8 GPU × 80 GB / GPU = 640 GB [G — §1.5] weights_per_task = P × bytes_per_element [G — §4.1] = 70e9 × 2 B = 140 GB (BF16; sharded TP=8 → 17.5 GB/GPU) activations_per_task ≈ 50 GB [G-FA — impl plan §1.9] GKV (HBM-for-KV budget) = 640 − 140 − 50 = 450 GB / task [D]

Step 2 — block size and total block count

block_size = 16 tok [G — K2 #paging; vLLM default] block_KV_bytes = block_size × KV_per_tok = 16 × 320 KB = 5.12 MB [D — §4.2] total_blocks = GKV / block_KV_bytes = 450 GB / 5.12 MB ≈ 87 890 blocks / task [D]

Step 3 — pre-PA (static-max) per-slot reservation and Bmax,static

max_reservation_tokens = Tin_at_admit + Tout_max [G — vLLM v0 reservation policy] = Tin_mean + Tout_max (workload-mean admit) = 13 K + 65 K = 78 K tok reserved_per_slot = max_reservation_tokens × KVper_tok = 78 000 × 320 KB = 24.96 GB / slot [D] Bmax,static = ⌊ GKV / reserved_per_slot ⌋ = ⌊ 450 / 24.96 ⌋ = 18 slots / task [D] p99-context worst case (one slot can be a p99-history admit): p99_reserved_per_slot = (Tconv_hist_p99 + Tout_max) × KVper_tok = (256 K + 65 K) × 320 KB = 321 K × 320 KB = 102.72 GB / slot [D] Bmax,static,p99 = ⌊ 450 / 102.72 ⌋ = 4 slots / task [D] → a single p99 admit in an active batch caps B at 4 — structurally fragile.

Step 4 — post-PA per-slot footprint and Bmax,paged

avg_used_per_slot = (Tin_mean + Tout_mean / 2) × KVper_tok = (13 000 + 200) × 320 KB = 13 200 × 320 KB ≈ 4.224 GB / slot (averaged over the slot’s decode life) [D] Bmax,paged = ⌊ GKV / avg_used_per_slot ⌋ = ⌊ 450 / 4.224 ⌋ = 106 slots / task [D] Why the "+Tout_mean/2" term: in continuous-batching steady state, an arbitrary slot is somewhere in its decode arc — uniformly distributed in [0, Tout_mean] in expectation, so the average position is Tout_mean / 2. Using Tout_mean in full would over-state the per-slot footprint by exactly 2× on the decode portion.

Step 5 — the multiplier (the headroom a later iteration consumes)

workload-mean ratio = reserved_per_slot / avg_used_per_slot = 24.96 GB / 4.224 GB ≈ 5.9× [D] p99-context worst-case ratio = p99_reserved_per_slot / avg_used_per_slot = 102.72 GB / 4.224 GB ≈ 24.3× [D] — matches the “~22×” vLLM-paper figure within ~10% rounding (K2 #paging).

Step 6 — SLO / throughput / cost delta at Iter 3 (the iteration whose number chain is intentionally flat)

decode_per_task_iter3 = decode_per_task_iter2 ≈ 0.479 req/s/task [D] prefill_per_task_iter3 = prefill_per_task_iter2 ≈ 6.53 req/s/task [D] prefill_task_num_iter3 ≈ 19 909 tasks [D — §8.5 Step 5] decode_task_num_iter3 ≈ 271 399 tasks [D — §8.5 Step 5] total_serving_iter3 ≈ 291 308 tasks [D] $/mo_iter3 = 291 308 × $70 790 ≈ $20.62 B / month [D] → flat by design vs Iter 2. PagedAttention is the layout fix that enables the batching throughput win on the decode pool; the throughput payout is booked at the later iteration that raises B on the now-paged decode pool. Iter 3’s job is to surface and retire B3-fragmentation so the next iteration is structurally possible. One bottleneck per iteration.

9.6  What breaks

  1. Block-table-GPU-upload cost on busy ticks. Each tick the CPU scheduler computes slot_mapping: int32[active_B] + any dirty BlockTable rows and DMAs them to GPU before kernel launch. At active_B = 1 (this iteration’s ceiling) the upload is microseconds; at the higher Bdecode a later iteration raises to (~100), a naive per-tick full-table upload would compete with the decode kernel for PCIe BW. Mitigation: dirty-row uploads only — track which rows changed since last tick (admit, evict, block-append events); upload exactly those rows. Bounded by admit/evict rate, not B. Detection: per-tick CPU→GPU bytes counter; alert if > ~10% of PCIe budget.
  2. Free-list fragmentation under churn (per tier). Admit / evict cycling at the HBM tier scatters free block_ids across the pool; the free-list itself stays compact (it’s a stack of int IDs) but the physical blocks they point to are fragmented. This is harmless at the HBM tier (the kernel gathers via indirection regardless) but at the DRAM and SSD tiers a fragmented page layout interacts poorly with prefetch and FS-level page-cache heuristics. Mitigation: coalesce DRAM page writes within a Tswap window (~5 ms); group SSD pages into FS-level extents large enough to amortize FS overhead. Detection: per-tier eviction-batch size histogram; SSD IOPS / MB ratio.
  3. Per-tick slot_mapping construction CPU cost. The scheduler must, every tick, compute the next block_id each live slot will write into. At active_B = 1 this is one O(1) free-list pop; at the higher Bdecode later, the loop becomes O(B) per tick. Mitigation: keep this on the engine-loop hot path (no Python; C / Rust); pre-allocate the slot_mapping buffer. Detection: scheduler-tick latency p99 vs decode-tick latency p99.
  4. DRAM swap thrash under bursty admit. A burst of new admits (e.g. autoscale-fresh decode task receiving its first BLCH-spilled conv_ids) can drive HBM evictions faster than DRAM can absorb them, then drive DRAM evictions faster than SSD can absorb them → chain spill. Mitigations: (i) high DRAM watermark sized so DRAM has ≥ burst_window × eviction_rate headroom; (ii) user-space mlock the HBM-resident active set so the OS page cache can’t evict the working set under memory pressure; (iii) coalesced D2H batches every Tswap ≈ 5 ms rather than per-tick (admit a small burst of dirty blocks per coalesced transfer; amortize PCIe setup). Detection: per-tier eviction rate; mlock failure counter; PCIe utilization p99.
  5. NVMe DWPD wear under hot eviction. Sustained SSD writes accumulate against the drive’s Drive Writes Per Day budget. At eviction_rate × block_KV = 5.12 MB × rate, a 7 TB NVMe with ~3 DWPD endurance burns out in (3 × 7 TB × 365 days × 5 years) / (5.12 MB × rate) seconds. Mitigation: budget the design’s steady-state SSD eviction rate against the chosen NVMe’s DWPD; rotate cold blocks at the FS level so writes are spread; replace drives on a planned cadence. Detection: cumulative TBW telemetry per drive vs the rated TBW.

9.7  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 2)Post-iter (Iter 3)ΔNote
TTFT p99~896 ms (cold mix-bounded)~896 ms→Unchanged. PagedAttention changes where K,V live, not how fast they’re built. The cold-prefill tail (TTFTcold = βcold(13 K) + xfer = 730 ms + 166 ms = 896 ms) is still the bound; addressed by later iterations (chunked prefill on the prefill pool; prefix caching to shrink unmatched-token count).
TPOT p99~5.2 ms~5.2 ms→Unchanged. Decode per-tick BW is the HBM-aggregate budget; paging adds an indirection (a single scatter-gather in the attention kernel, ~3–5% kernel cost per K2 measurement) but doesn’t change the BW-bound character of the tick at B=1.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→Unchanged at one task. PagedAttention raises the per-task Bmax ceiling (concurrent capacity) but not the per-request length ceiling — a single 1 M-tok request still doesn’t fit in 450 GB at B=1 (320 GB just for its KV; addressed by context parallelism later).
Max-output~65 K (with static reservation)~65 K (with paged growth)→Unchanged at the cap, but the structural meaning changes: pre-PA every admitted slot had to pre-reserve up to Tout_max = 65 K tok of HBM regardless of expected length (the static-max tax); post-PA the slot grows its block-list lazily and only pays for what it actually generates. The cap stays at 65 K (API contract), but its HBM cost is now realized not reserved.
Tenant fairness→→→Unchanged — addressed at a later per-user admission iteration.
λpeak supported100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)→Flat by design. Iter 3 is the layout fix; the throughput multiplier (raising the active decode batch on the now-paged HBM) lands at a later iteration. The capacity headroom this iteration unlocks — Bmax,paged ≈ 106 vs Bmax,static ≈ 18 — is what makes that later move possible. Without Iter 3, the later iteration OOMs on its first admit.
$/mo at λpeak~$20.62 B/mo~$20.62 B/mo→Flat by design. The investment booked this iteration pays out at the next throughput-moving iteration on the decode pool. The dollar headline is the capacity headroom, not the cost line.

Iter 3 retires B3-fragmentation: the contiguous-slab layout’s ~5.9× workload-mean over-allocation and ~24× p99-context over-allocation collapse into the paged-block layout’s actual-length footprint. No SLO moves; per-task throughput stays at 0.479 req/s/task on decode and 6.53 req/s/task on prefill; fleet sizing and $/mo carry through from Iter 2 unchanged. The structural payout — Bmax from 18 to ~106 — lands as a throughput multiplier at the later iteration that raises the active decode batch on the now-paged HBM. The multi-tier residency table (§9.4) extends Iter 1a’s HBM/DRAM/SSD hierarchy to block granularity, letting per-task resident conversation KVs grow to ~2 819 (vs ~106 active decode slots) — the structural support for the intra_session_hit_rate = 0.80 assumption at all subsequent iterations.

9b. Iter 3b — Pooled cross-task KV store (a write-back victim cache for cross-turn conv K,V)

Bottleneck addressed: B12 (cross-turn conv K,V has no shared home after the P/D split). Two faces of one gap: (i) the prior assistant output lives only on DⒶ (decode) — a warm prefill on a cold/rebalanced PⒶ must otherwise recompute that ~400-tok delta (§8.5); (ii) any conv whose KV was evicted/migrated off its owning task’s local tier (Iter 1a) has nowhere shared to land. Mechanism: an RDMA-fronted, NVMe-backed pooled kv-store-job (Mooncake / LMCache-style), conv_id-keyed, run as a write-back victim cache — the decode task writes back on eviction (gated by a predictor-free length gate), the prefill pool pulls lazily, and recompute (RPO=0) is the floor. Why here: the binding dependencies — conv_id (Iter 1b) + block-granular paging (Iter 3) — are now both in hand; this is the earliest clean point. Deferred to later iterations: B2/B5/B6; the per-user user_sys working-set overflow onto this store (when reuse-recency LRU + ref_count can’t keep it HBM-resident) is a later consumer-scale event, not new infrastructure.

Notation introduced in this section — the pooled store, its write-back/length-gate machinery, the lookup path, and the admission-flow sizing variables.

kv-store-job / kv_store_job_task_num
the pooled cross-task KV store (RDMA-DRAM front + NVMe back, Mooncake-style) / its replica count ≈ 36 (CH-on-conv_id on its own ring).
PoolConvIndex
This IS a map (authoritative, sharded): conv_id → {owning_pool_task, block_ids[], tier} — resolves a present conv to exact blocks in one RDMA.
Hop / write-back / pull / recompute
the four KV-arrival mechanisms — Option 0 Hop (Iter 2 intra-request handoff, kept), Option 2 write-back (async DⒶ→store) + pull (lazy store→PⒶ), Option 3 recompute (the RPO=0 floor).
write-back victim cache
the store’s discipline — admit only on eviction (not write-through), gated by length; recompute is the correctness floor.
length gate / recompute-vs-fetch crossover
admit iff cumulative ctx > ~1.9 K tok (the conservative 7 GB/s crossover from §6.5) — below it, recompute beats a pool round-trip.
RPO=0
Recovery Point Objective zero — KV is always recoverable from token IDs by recompute, so the store may lose data freely.
pool_filter / PoolFilterUpdate
optional (§S1) union existence summary (~144 KB) probed before a pull / its gossip delta — main line is a direct CH(conv_id, pool_ring) probe (no filter).
Bloom / Counting-Bloom / Cuckoo / FPR
the candidate existence filters and their False-Positive-Rate — deletion is required (a victim cache evicts constantly), so plain Bloom is out.
padmit
per-turn pool-admit probability = P(out of all local) × P(length gate) — the two-factor demotion flow (replaces the retired fpool).
P(gap>15min) / P(reroute)
the two ways a conv leaves all local tiers — idle longer than the local-eviction horizon (Pareto return-gap) / rerouted to a task that never held it. Population sizing only, never a per-request predictor.
Tevict / Wpool
local-eviction time horizon (HBM+DRAM only, post-DC7) ≈ 17 min / the pool working-set size it implies.
measured_β / N*(αobs, βobs)
rolling-median per-tier β the scheduler already tracks / a live (adaptive) crossover — the §S2 upgrade off the main line.
P(continue) / LPC
Learned Prefix Caching — an optional expected-value admit refinement (§S2 sidecar); the main line stays predictor-free.

9b.1  Architecture (diagram first)

The store sits below the per-task local tiers as the shared cross-task cold tier (ranked L4 here; DC7 below retires local SSD now this pool exists, so the post-3b local cascade is HBM → local DRAM and the store is the cold tier directly below DRAM). Two cross-turn mechanisms ride it — an asynchronous post-turn write-back off the decode task, and a lazy pull on the prefill pool — and they coexist with Iter 2’s synchronous intra-request Hop (which is unchanged).

“L4” is a cost/latency rank, NOT a cascade every conv flows through. Two movements are easy to conflate — they are different:
  • Demotion / spill (HBM ↓ DRAM) — the local, per-task ladder (Iter 1a’s third step, local SSD, is retired by DC7 below). A demoted conv is still on its owning task, parked in DRAM to free HBM for active decodes; the next turn promotes it back up. Reversible. Most active convs stay in HBM and never reach DRAM — demotion fires only under watermark pressure.
  • Eviction = falling off the bottom of that ladder (out of local DRAM — the bottom local tier after DC7 retires SSD). Now the conv has left the task entirely — this is the boundary the pool addresses. Pre-Iter-3b, eviction meant “gone → full recompute on return.”
The pool is not tier-4 that everything cascades into; it is a selective victim catch at the eviction boundary: only an evicting conv is a candidate (a still-resident conv is served locally for free and is never pooled), and only if it clears the DC2 value gate. Most convs never reach the pool at all — they end after their turn, their KV is freed, and they are never written (writing all of them is precisely the 3.84 TB/s write-through DC2 rejects). The pool is also cross-task, which the local tiers are not — local DRAM dies with the task; a conv evicted from task A’s local DRAM and a next turn routed to cold task B have no shared local tier, which is the whole reason a pooled cross-task tier exists.
  • ── ① Hop (Option 0) — PⒶ → DⒶ — the existing Iter 2 synchronous, intra-request KV handoff (GPUDirect RDMA HBM→HBM, ~25 GB/s; DRAM/TCP fallback). One consumer, latency-bound. Unchanged — it coexists with, and is orthogonal to, the cross-turn store.
  • ┄┄ ② Write-back (Option 2 write) — DⒶ → store — asynchronous, post-turn, off the decode hot path; carries the turn’s K,V including the assistant output PⒶ never saw. Fires only when the admission gate passes (eviction + a predictor-free length gate; the P(continue) EV variant is the §S2 sidecar).
  • ── ③ Pull (Option 2 read) — store → PⒶ — the warm prefill on a cold/rebalanced task lazily pulls the missing prior conv K,V (the asst delta, or the whole evicted conv) only when the turn actually arrives — no speculative-push waste.
  • ┄┄ ④ Recompute floor (RPO=0) — PⒶ self-loop — on a store miss, re-prefill from token IDs (always correct, always possible). The store only has to beat this, which it does decisively at long context (§9b.4).
  • ┄┄ ⑤ Build + publish (OPTIONAL — §S1 sidecar; flows ⑤–⑦ run only if the summary is enabled) — kv-store-job task → broker — the conv’s store owner (a kv-store-job task, = CH(conv_id) on the store’s own ring — not the decode task) builds its shard’s summary (insert on admit, delete on evict / rebalance) and publishes the delta every Tgossip = 100 ms.
  • ┄┄ ⑥ Fan-out — broker → Router — the broker-job (3-task HA Redis Streams) republishes the membership sketch to every Router on the same read-replica bus that already carries ring-version + wait-time.
  • ┄┄ ⑦ Relay — Router → prefill task — each Router relays PoolFilterUpdate on the channel it already uses to route; the prefill task merges it into its union pool_filter (~144 KB) and probes it locally before any pull. Full topology + lookup path: kv_store.html §4.

Per-component treatment (why exists / what stores / when updated)

  • kv-store-job RDMA/DRAM hot front — why: to beat recompute at the median (single-NVMe ties recompute at the 8 K median — only the ~25 GB/s front wins, §9b.4); stores: the warm-conv working set (recently-evicted long convs likely to return); updated: on write-back admit (promote into DRAM front) and on pull (LRU touch).
  • kv-store-job NVMe back — why: durable capacity below the DRAM front, and it outlives the task (local DRAM dies with the task); stores: cold long-conv KV that aged out of the front; updated: on front eviction (spill down) and rare cold re-pull (promote up).
  • PoolConvIndex (+ optional §S1 existence summary) — the pool’s exact location map; the membership summary in front of it is an optional §S1 sidecar, not the main path.
    • Why it exists — one RDMA to a hit; one cheap RTT to a miss. The index resolves a present conv to its exact blocks in a single RDMA (no broadcast); an absent conv costs one cheap CH probe before recompute — affordable on the main line (DC4/DC5), so no front summary is required. An optional §S1 summary turns that miss-probe into a local memory check during correlated bursts.
    • What it stores — an authoritative map; an optional summary in front of it. The index is the authoritative map conv_id → {owning_pool_task, block_ids[], tier} (this is a map), sharded CH-on-conv_id. The optional §S1 summary is a “present?” digest of that map (prefer an exact set; the Cuckoo variant is ~1.6 B/key), replicated to every prefill task only if enabled.
    • When it’s updated — index synchronously on write; filter lazily by gossip. The owning store task — the kv-store-job task that CH-owns this conv_id on the store’s own ring, a separate no-GPU storage task, not the decode task — writes the index synchronously on admit (insert) / eviction (delete) / pool-task rebalance (re-key); the filter trails it eventually-consistently as those same deltas gossip out to the prefill pool. (Decode task sends the write-back; the store task writes the index — three CH owners, three rings.)
    • Who gossips to whom — store task → broker → Router → prefill task. Each store task publishes its shard’s filter deltas to the broker, which fans them out to the Routers, which relay PoolFilterUpdate to every prefill task.
    • Full detail — the design enumeration, then the impl walkthrough. Broadcast vs CH-locate vs filter, Bloom vs Cuckoo, and why approximate is safe → DC4. Step-by-step build/use, the gossip-topology diagram, and the lookup path → kv_store.html (the impl page for this store).
  • Admission gate — why: write-through’s 3.84 TB/s of mostly-never-read ingest is unaffordable (§9b.4); stores: nothing persistent — it is the decode task’s per-eviction decision; updated: evaluated on every eviction / EOS, on a predictor-free rule — cumulative context vs the ~1.9 K recompute-vs-fetch crossover. (The learned P(continue) EV refinement, shared with the user_sys top-N pin signal, is the §S2 sidecar — off the main line.)

9b.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Enabling priors are exactly in hand — and no earlier. The store is conv_id-keyed, so it needs the conv_id route key (Iter 1b) and clean block-granular store/fetch (Iter 3 paging — pre-paging, pulling a conv back into HBM needs a contiguous static-max slot, the very fragmentation paging exists to remove). Both now exist; Iter 3b is the earliest clean point.
  • The constraint that flipped — the P/D split (Iter 2) stranded the assistant KV. Pre-split, one unified task held the whole conversation. Post-split, DⒶ decodes the assistant reply locally and PⒶ never sees it (§8.5). Until now the only recourse on a cold/rebalanced PⒶ was recompute (the 600-tok warm floor, ~+17% prefill FLOPs). This iteration gives that stranded KV a shared home.
  • Why not bundle into Iter 2 or defer to a late “long-tail” iteration. Bundling at Iter 2 is ugly (no paging → fetch-back needs a contiguous slot). Deferring to the late consumer-scale iteration mis-frames it as a user_sys-only problem — the store’s first and most expensive job is cross-turn conv K,V. The late iteration is where the user_sys working set overflows onto this already-existing store (when reuse-recency LRU + ref_count can’t keep it HBM-resident); that is a scale event, not new infrastructure.
  • Why a pooled (remote) store, not just the local CPU-DRAM tier (Iter 1a). Local DRAM is already used — and for a conv that stays sticky and unevicted on a healthy task it is sufficient (the pool isn’t touched). The pool is the shared tier below it, needed only where local residency structurally fails: (a) reachability — next turn may route to a different task (rebalance / autoscale / failure) whose DRAM lacks this conv; (b) aggregate capacity — ~800 GB usable DRAM/task holds only ~9–10 of the 82 GB (256 K) long convs and can’t be shared across tasks; (c) durability — local DRAM dies with the task, the pool outlives it.

9b.3  Design choices

DC1 — how does the prior conv K,V reach a warm-turn prefill? Problem: after the P/D split the assistant delta lives on DⒶ, not PⒶ; on a cold/rebalanced PⒶ the whole prior conv may be absent. By what mechanism does it arrive?

All solutions enumerated (two orthogonal axes — where the warm prefill compute runs, and how the prior K,V reaches it):

  • Option 0 — Hop (intra-request handoff): KEEP. The Iter 2 cross-pool transfer. Synchronous, intra-request, one consumer, latency-bound. Solves a different problem (this-turn handoff) and coexists with whatever cross-turn option we pick — not an alternative to the others.
  • Option 1 — decode→prefill write-back push: REJECT. DⒶ eagerly pushes the conv K,V to a prefill task after the turn. Speculative waste (the user may never return → bytes nobody reads) and wrong-target-unknown (we don’t know which PⒶ the next turn routes to; the ring may rebalance). Pushing guesses.
  • Option 2 — decode→shared store; prefill pulls (lazy): RECOMMENDED for cross-turn. DⒶ writes the turn’s K,V to the pooled store asynchronously; the next turn’s prefill pulls only what it needs, only when it arrives. Lazy pull → no speculative-push waste (fixes Option 1). conv_id-keyed (identity suffices — the pool is private per-conv KV).
  • Option 3 — recompute: the FLOOR, not the answer. PⒶ re-prefills the missing prior KV from token IDs. Zero new infra and always correct (RPO=0), but burns the scarce, fleet-sizing-bottleneck prefill-GPU FLOPs, and is quadratic on long context (catastrophic at the 256 K p99). Kept as the store-miss correctness floor.
  • Option A — decode-side incremental prefill: REJECT for this architecture. Run the warm turn’s small prefill on DⒶ (which already holds the conv) so no KV moves. Tempting (data locality), but: it still launches a prefill-shaped kernel (different kernel from decode), re-introduces the exact P/D interference Iter 2 paid to remove (a prefill burst stalls the decode tick → TBT jitter for every co-batched conv), and runs under decode’s sharding (wrong parallelism for TTFT). It only saves a data transfer while still needing a distinct prefill kernel. It belongs to the unified / chunked-prefill architecture we deliberately rejected at Iter 2.

Why 0 (Hop) + 2 (Store), strict P/D separation, 3 as the floor:

  • Different problems, keep both. Hop is synchronous / intra-request / one-consumer / latency-bound (HBM→HBM speed); Store is asynchronous / cross-turn / many-consumers / capacity-&-cost-bound (reach).
  • SLO isolation preserved. The decode task never runs prefill (A rejected), keeping the Iter 2 split intact.
  • Cheap price, big win. A ~5 ms RDMA pull (or ~18 ms recompute) of the asst delta on a cold warm-turn is cheap; the store’s real win is at long context + FLOPs offload (§9b.4).

[param] Pool write-back length gate: none → admit iff cumulative ctx > recompute-vs-fetch crossover ≈ 1.9 K tok — Iter 3b. Why: write-through every turn would cost ~3.84 TB/s of mostly-never-read ingest; the predictor-free length gate writes back only KV whose later re-fetch beats recompute (the ~1.9 K SSD-crossover, §6.5). Below the gate, recompute is cheaper than a pool round-trip, so don’t store it.

DC2 — admission: does DⒶ write back on every turn? Problem (step by step): (1) Option 2 says the decode task writes the turn’s K,V to the store post-turn. (2) The obvious default is write-through (every turn). (3) But every request decodes Tout_mean = 400 tok = 128 MB of KV whether or not the conv ever returns. (4) At fleet QPS that ingest is enormous and mostly never read.

All solutions enumerated:

  • Write-through (every decoded turn) — ingest @ λavg = 30 K × 128 MB = 3.84 TB/s (= ~154 RDMA paths for writes alone); @ λpeak = 12.8 TB/s. The vast majority is never read (most convs end after the turn) — Option 1’s speculative waste, relocated to the store and multiplied by QPS. Rejected.
  • Write-back victim cache + predictor-free length-gated admission — CHOSEN. Admit a conv only where cheap local recompute is unavailable — i.e. where local residency fails — and it is long enough that fetching beats recomputing (the ~1.9 K crossover). Triggers:
    1. Long accumulated context. The total context the next turn must reconstruct (median ~8 K, p99 ~256 K, capped at the 1 M SLO). A cold PⒶ reconstructing it pays quadratic recompute (~65 s at 256 K vs ~3.3 s pull). Gate on cumulative context above the recompute-vs-pull crossover — ~1.9 K tok, essentially any multi-turn conv.
      Which crossover? The conservative one. ~1.9 K is the crossover at ~7 GB/s — the pool’s slowest serving tier (the single-NVMe back; post-DC7 this is the remote pool’s NVMe back, not the retired local NVMe). The pool’s 25 GB/s RDMA DRAM front has NO crossover (§6.5: pull beats recompute at every length). We deliberately gate against the slow path so a conv is admitted only if a pull beats recompute even on the cold NVMe back / contended fabric — benchmarking against the fast front would admit everything and lose on short convs whenever the pull is actually slow. The contention-aware exact form is Npull* (plan §8b, vs ρfabric × 25 GB/s): no finite crossover at ρfabric ≥ ~0.29, finite (≥ 1.9 K) below — so ~1.9 K is the robust floor, not a universal constant. The proper cost form is α + β·n: the fixed per-pull α (RTT + index lookup) is negligible at median (so the gate keeps ~1.9 K) but its p99 PFC tail would push the crossover to ~3.6 K — absorbed by the abandon-to-recompute fallback, not a wider gate (full model + the crossover’s α-sensitivity in §6.5).
      Concrete example. A coding-assistant conv at turn 12: the user pasted a 30 K-token file plus 11 prior Q&A turns → cumulative context ≈ 45 K tok. Recompute cold on a rebalanced PⒶ = βcold(45 K) with its quadratic attention term ≈ ~3.4 s of scarce prefill FLOPs; the pull is 45 K × 320 KB = 14.4 GB / 25 GB/s ≈ ~580 ms of cheap I/O. 45 K ⋙ the ~1.9 K crossover → clears the gate easily. Contrast a 2-turn “what’s the weather” chat at ~300 tok cumulative: recompute is ~13 ms — below the crossover, so even if evicted it is not worth a cross-machine write.
    2. Eviction / long session gap. The conv fell out of the local tier (LRU pressure, idle minutes) but the user returns. Write-back on eviction so a returning turn finds it.
      Concrete inter-turn trace (what “long session gap” means). Turn 1 answered → KV in the decode task’s HBM. User reads ~2 min → other active convs need HBM → this idle conv demotes to DRAM. User pauses ~5 min → DRAM pressure → demotes to SSD. User steps away ~40 min → during that idle gap LRU evicts it from SSD entirely. Turn 2 arrives → routes to a (possibly cold/rebalanced) prefill task → not in any local tier. Without the pool: full recompute of the whole history. With this store: the decode task wrote it back at the SSD-eviction moment (it cleared the value gate), so the returning prefill pulls it (linear I/O) instead of recomputing (quadratic). “Long session gap” is just the common cause of that eviction — the user-pause is long enough that LRU ages the conv out while other convs claim the space.

    Predictor-free by construction. Triggers 1–2 are the entire main-line gate: admit iff (evicted from all local tiers) AND (cumulative context > ~1.9 K). No learned signal is on the main path. Two scoping points the gate is easy to over-read: (a) it is a victim-cache test — it fires only on eviction, never write-through, so a conv resident locally is never pooled; and the ungated local HBM→DRAM spill is a different move (LRU, no gate, stays on-task). (b) transfer_cost is the future pull-back read (vs recompute), not the write cost — we admit when a later pull would beat recompute, accepting that some admitted convs never return (the write is async/cheap; pool capacity-LRU + the §S2 sidecar handle that waste). An optional refinement — a learned P(continue) expected-value call (store iff P(continue) × recompute_saved > write_cost) that can also catch a single long answer in an otherwise-short conv — is the §S2 sidecar (LPC); it is not inherited by later iterations, which all assume the pure length gate.

Why write-back over write-through:

  • Recompute is never cumulative. On a sticky, unevicted conv prefill is missing only asst_{N−1} (~400 tok) + new user_msg_N (~200) ≈ 600 tok ≈ ~18–27 ms (§8.5).
  • Bad trade to write-through. Writing 128 MB you mostly throw away to save ~18 ms doesn’t pay.
  • Tiny write-back ingest. A small fraction of 3.84 TB/s — you only write convs that both outlive their local cache and clear the length gate (cumulative ctx > ~1.9 K), with recompute (RPO=0) as the floor.
  • Predictor-free main line. The gate ships as pure length + eviction — deterministic, nothing to train or mis-estimate. The learned P(continue) refinement (§S2 sidecar) is an optional later add-on; every subsequent iteration is built as if it is absent.

DC3 — key & placement: conv_id vs content-addressing; pooled vs local. Problem: what key does the pool use, and does it duplicate Iter 5’s content-addressed block_hash?

  • conv_id (+ block_id for partial fetch) — CHOSEN. The pool stores private per-conv / per-user KV (conv_hist + assistant deltas + long-tail user_sys), so identity keying suffices — available from Iter 1b.
  • Content-addressed block_hash (the later prefix-cache key) — only buys cross-user dedup of identical content. But the one big cross-user-shared blob, hidden_sys, is pinned per prefill task, never pooled — so there is essentially nothing in the pool for content-addressing to dedup. A user’s own convs sharing user_sys dedup on the single-segment user_sys_hash, not the full chained block-hash. Adopting block_hash later is a uniformity upgrade, not a requirement, and not a reason for the placement.

Why a dedicated iteration right after paging (3b), not 2b or a late iteration:

  • Gradient, not cliff. Value ramps in (median spill early; long-context most; user_sys overflow much later), so no iteration forces it — placement is a problem→fix-locality call.
  • First clean point. Right after paging (block-granular fetch); 2b would work but the pre-paging fetch-back is ugly (contiguous-slot fragmentation).
  • Orthogonal to Context-Parallelism. CP shards the active long request’s KV and parallelizes its O(N²) prefill now; the store persists that KV across turns, replacing the re-run with a linear I/O fetch. Different lifecycle phases, not alternatives.

DC4 — the lookup path: how does a warm prefill find (or rule out) a conv in the pool before paying for it? Problem (step by step): (1) a warm turn lands on PⒶ; it must learn does the pool hold this conv_id, on which task, in which blocks/tier before it can pull. (2) Because the pool is a write-back victim cache (DC2), only the admitted tail is present (a small fraction of convs — pending re-derivation, §9b.4). (3) So the common warm-turn lookup is a miss: the conv was never admitted. (4) Any mechanism that makes PⒶ touch the network to discover a miss has put a wasted RDMA round-trip (RTT) + owner-CPU probe on the latency-critical path before the recompute fallback even starts.

Main line (Change 2 / §S1): NO filter — the lookup is a direct CH(conv_id, pool_ring) probe on local miss. The owning store task is computed from the key, so a present conv resolves in a single RDMA and an absent one costs one cheap RTT before the recompute floor. The direct probe is affordable on its own: the per-task probe load is ≤ λ_peak / kv_store_job_task_num = 100 K / 36 = ~2 800 /s ≈ 0.03% of a CPU core, and is QPS-bounded by construction even as the pool grows — derived + shown robust to the pending fpool in §S1 (the wasted-RTT latency is itself noise against a hundreds-of-ms recompute). The gossiped existence filter analysed below is therefore an OPTIONAL §S1 sidecar — burst-insurance for correlated mass-miss events (rolling deploy / large autoscale / fabric blip), not a requirement — and if a membership summary is added, the exact membership set is preferred over a Cuckoo filter (no false-positive rate, trivial delete, no load-factor cliff; ~7× the memory, which is noise against HBM). This reverses the earlier “filter required” conclusion; the CHOSEN marks below are retained as the §S1 sidecar deep-dive (the Cuckoo variant), not the main line.

All solutions enumerated (two stacked questions — how to locate a present conv, and how to cheaply reject an absent one):

  • Hot-path broadcast / scatter-probe — PⒶ asks all kv_store_job_task_num pool tasks “do you have conv_id?”. O(tasks) messages per warm turn × fpull-filtered warm QPS → an all-to-all storm that grows with the pool. Rejected: the cost scales with fleet size and pays it on every miss.
  • Locate by consistent hashing (no location lookup at all) — CHOSEN for placement. The pool is CH-on-conv_id, so the owning pool task is computed from the key (owning_pool_task = pool_ring.lookup(conv_id), on the kv-store-job’s own ring — not the decode/prefill ring) — zero messages to find where. The owning task holds the authoritative PoolConvIndex shard (conv_id → {block_ids[], tier}, this is a map) for its slice, so a present conv resolves to exact blocks in a single RDMA to one known task — not a broadcast. This is the main-line miss path too: a miss costs that one cheap RTT to learn “not here” before recompute — affordable per the banner above (§S1), so no extra structure is required.
  • A gossiped existence filter in front of the index — OPTIONAL (§S1 sidecar), NOT the main line. Burst-insurance only (correlated mass-miss events); if added at all, an exact membership set is preferred over the Cuckoo variant detailed here. Each PⒶ would keep a compact, approximate, local replica of “what conv_ids the pool currently holds,” gossiped from the pool tasks. Warm-turn flow: (a) check the local filter; (b) filter says absent → skip the pool entirely, go straight to recompute (the common case — no network touched); (c) filter says present → CH to the owner, one RDMA, pull exact blocks. The filter converts the common-case miss from a wasted RTT into a local memory probe. Who gossips to whom: each store task owns its CH-shard filter and publishes deltas to the broker, which fans out to the Routers, which relay PoolFilterUpdate to every prefill task (the same read-replica bus that already carries ring + wait-time) — full topology in kv_store.html §4.

Sub-choice — which filter? Bloom vs Cuckoo. The structure must support deletion, because a victim cache evicts constantly (every admit has a matching future evict); a filter that can’t delete would accumulate stale “present” bits and rot toward a false-positive rate (FPR) = 1 (every lookup wrongly says “present”).

  • Plain Bloom filter — cannot delete (clearing bits would corrupt other keys). Dead on the eviction requirement.
  • Counting Bloom filter — deletes, but replaces each bit with a ~4-bit counter → ~4× the space for the same FPR. Rejected on footprint (the filter is replicated to every prefill task).
  • Cuckoo filter — best of the probabilistic options (the exact set is preferred overall — §S1; this sub-choice only decides which probabilistic filter to use if a probabilistic one is chosen) (Fan et al., CoNEXT 2014). Stores a short fingerprint per key in a cuckoo-hash table: supports delete natively, is more space-efficient than Bloom below ~3% FPR (the regime we want), and a lookup touches only 2 candidate buckets (cache-friendly vs Bloom’s k scattered probes). FPR (the chance the filter says “present” for an absent key) is tuned by fingerprint width f: FPR ≈ 2b / 2f (b = bucket size); an f ≈ 12-bit fingerprint gives FPR ≈ 0.1–1% at ~1.6 B/key. Real systems: the Cuckoo/fingerprint-index pattern is exactly how Mooncake / LMCache front their global prefix–KV stores; CDN/edge existence checks use the same.

Why an approximate filter is safe here (the asymmetry that makes it work):

  • Recompute is the floor. A stale filter has two failure modes and both are non-fatal because recompute (RPO=0) is always available.
  • False positive → slower. Just-evicted conv still shows “present” → one wasted RTT to the owner, then recompute — still correct.
  • False negative → one missed offload. Just-admitted conv not yet gossiped → PⒶ recomputes instead of pulling — still correct.
  • Never wrong KV. Stale by a full gossip interval (~1 s) is fine — it’s a pure cost optimizer on the miss path, which is why approximate is acceptable where the index itself must be exact.

Why a filter, not just replicate the full index to every prefill:

  • Full index is too big. PoolConvIndex carries block_ids[] + tier per conv (KBs/conv) and mutates on every admit/evict — fleet-wide replication is ~GBs per prefill task and perpetually stale.
  • Filter is 1 bit/key. The Cuckoo “present?” summary at ~1.6 B/key: 90 K convs × 1.6 B ≈ ~144 KB per prefill task; even at consumer scale (~2.7 M keys) it is ≈ ~4.3 MB — trivially resident.
  • Update cadence. Each pool task owns its CH shard’s filter and gossips inserts/deletes (delta or interval-batched); the authoritative PoolConvIndex shard writes synchronously on admit/evict/rebalance, the filter eventually-consistent behind it (chain in kv_store.html §4).

DC5 — is a gossiped Cuckoo existence filter over-engineering? Problem (step by step): (1) the filter adds machinery — a per-shard structure on every store task, a delta stream on the broker bus, a union replica merged on every prefill task, and eventual-consistency reasoning (DC4). (2) That is a lot of moving parts for a pure miss-path optimizer. (3) So the fair challenge: could a simpler design carry the same load, making the filter unjustified complexity?

Verdict reversed by §S1: the simpler no-filter design does carry the load — the direct-CH-probe is the main line, the filter is optional. At steady state the probe load is affordable (≤ ~2 800/s/store-task), so the filter is not required — it is burst-insurance for correlated mass-miss events, and if added the exact set is preferred over Cuckoo. Read the “CHOSEN” below as “the sidecar variant, when a summary is warranted (consumer scale / pull-storms)”; the enumeration is retained as that rationale.

All solutions enumerated (cheapest-first — does a simpler one suffice?):

  • No lookup structure at all — direct CH-probe the owner — MAIN LINE (§S1). On every local miss, PⒶ does the single CH-computed RDMA to the owner (DC4 placement option) and lets the owner answer present/absent. Why it carries the steady state: the pool-consult rate is just the local-miss warm rate (the same order as pool_pulls, §9b.4b) → a few hundred to ~2 800 probes/s/store-task — a hashmap lookup the owner CPU shrugs off (~0.03%). The catch (why an OPTIONAL summary can help at scale): (a) the absent case dominates, so most of those probes are a wasted RDMA RTT (~tens of µs) + owner-CPU index touch on the latency-critical path before recompute — tolerable individually, but (b) the probe load lands on the store tasks (the scarcest tier) and amplifies on correlated mass-miss bursts (rolling deploy / autoscale / pull-storm, §9b.5). The summary absorbs those bursts locally — insurance, not a base-scale necessity.
  • Local negative-cache (TTL set of recently-absent conv_ids) — catches repeat-misses without the pool, but a returning conv is a cold conv_id on a rebalanced PⒶ (no prior local miss to seed the set), so it misses exactly the case that matters, and it still needs a gossip path to be invalidated on admit. Same bus, less coverage.
  • Gossiped existence summary (DC4) — OPTIONAL §S1 sidecar (Cuckoo variant). If a summary is warranted (consumer scale / pull-storms), the deciding asymmetry is cost ≈ 0 vs burst-amplification it prevents: it rides the already-built ring + wait-time read-replica bus (Iter 1b DC5) — no new job, no new RTT — and removes the wasted critical-path RTT on the absent common case during a burst, keeping the absent-probe load off the store tier. (Prefer the exact set here over Cuckoo — no FPR, no load-factor cliff; §S1.)

Why it clears the over-engineering bar (the honest reading):

  • Marginal at base scale, decisive at consumer scale. At 4 K/s fleet-wide the no-filter probe is survivable — so the filter is cheap insurance, not a base-scale necessity. The justification is that it costs ~nothing to carry (144 KB on an existing bus) while the thing it prevents — store-tier probe amplification — scales with the pool (×30 at consumer scale, §9b.4) and spikes on pull-storms.
  • It reuses, it doesn’t add. No filter ⇒ a new hot-path RTT class; the filter ⇒ one more delta topic on a bus that already fans ring + wait-time to the same tasks. The simpler-looking “no structure” option is the one that adds latency-critical work.
  • Correctness never rides on it. Recompute (RPO=0) is the floor (DC4), so the filter is a pure cost optimizer — it can be approximate and stale, which is exactly why it stays small and cheap. Over-engineering would be making it exact (that is the full-index replica DC4 already rejected).

DC6 — can the Cuckoo filter carry this scale (keys, mutations, fan-out)? Problem (step by step): (1) the filter is replicated to every one of prefill_task_num ≈ 19.9 K prefill tasks (§8.5). (2) It must stay accurate as the keyspace grows from base to consumer scale (§9b.4). (3) It mutates on every pool admit and evict, and each mutation must fan out to all 19.9 K replicas. Three stress axes — accuracy under growth, mutation throughput, fan-out bandwidth — must each hold.

§S1-sidecar scope. This applies only if the optional Cuckoo summary is deployed (the main line is the filter-free direct probe, DC4/DC5). It is kept because it answers “could the Cuckoo variant scale if we added it?” — yes. But §S1 prefers the exact membership set (no FPR, no load-factor cliff) until its ~7× memory actually bites at consumer scale — that footprint crossover is the only reason to reach for the Cuckoo variant this DC analyses. The key-count figures here are illustrative (the working-set sizing is pending re-derivation, §9b.4).

Axis 1 — accuracy is invariant to keyspace size (the load-bearing property).

  • Cuckoo FPR ≈ 2b / 2f (DC4) depends only on fingerprint width f and bucket size b — not on the number of keys N. So at fixed f ≈ 12 bit the 0.1–1% FPR holds identically at 90 K and at 2.7 M keys. Growing traffic does not degrade the filter’s answer — it only grows its size, linearly.
  • Size scales linearly at ~1.6 B/key: 90 K × 1.6 B ≈ 144 KB → 2.7 M × 1.6 B ≈ ~4.3 MB per prefill task (§9b.4). 4.3 MB against a CPU task’s tens of GB of RAM is a rounding error — the linear term never bites.

Axis 2 — mutation throughput.

  • In steady state admits = evicts. Each pooled conv is admitted once and is either pulled once or expires, so the admit rate is the same order as the productive read rate: admits ≈ pool_pulls ≈ 4 K/s → ~8 K filter-ops/s (admit + evict) fleet-wide at base scale; ~240 K ops/s at consumer scale (×30 working set).
  • Each op is a ~12-bit fingerprint + insert/delete bit ≈ ~4 B, batched per Tgossip = 100 ms (the §9b.1 control-plane cadence). Per prefill-task ingress: 8 K ops/s × 4 B ≈ ~32 KB/s (base) → ~1 MB/s (consumer) — both trivial to apply (a cuckoo insert/delete is O(1), 2 candidate buckets).

Axis 3 — fan-out bandwidth (the real cost, and it still fits).

  • The delta is a broadcast down the existing broker → router → prefill bus, so the heavy leg is the router→prefill relay to 19.9 K tasks split across the 3-task broker-job/router relay: ~6.6 K tasks/relay × 1 MB/s ≈ ~6.6 GB/s per relay at consumer scale — within a single 50 GB/s NIC, and on a bus already sized for the ring + wait-time fan-out (Iter 1b).

Where it would actually break (and the guard):

  • Load-factor cliff, not a traffic cliff. Cuckoo insertion degrades only as the table passes ~95% occupancy (kick-out relocation cascades, then insert failure). Guard: size each shard’s table for the shard’s admitted-key count with headroom and grow on the rare resize, exactly as the linear 1.6 B/key budget already assumes — occupancy, not QPS, is the knob.
  • FPR floor is set by f, not by N. If consumer-scale traffic ever wanted a tighter FPR, widen the fingerprint (f: 12 → 16 bit) at a linear ~1.3× footprint — still KBs–MBs. There is no key-count wall.
  • Verdict. The structure scales because its one growth term (size) is linear and tiny and its accuracy term is flat in N; mutation + fan-out ride an existing bus with >7× NIC margin. It supports the scale.

[param] Local tier ladder: HBM + DRAM + local NVMe → HBM + DRAM + remote pool (local NVMe retired) — Iter 3b / DC7. Why: the RDMA-fronted pool (~25 GB/s DRAM front) supersedes the local-NVMe L3 (7 GB/s) and survives a task reroute (local tiers die with the task). So the local stack collapses to HBM→DRAM; the cross-task cold tier moves to the pool. Reverses the Iter 1a three-tier ladder’s SSD rung.

DC7 — which local victim tiers still earn their place now the pool exists? (all-remote, vs retiring local SSD). Problem (step by step): (1) Iter 1a built a 3-deep local cascade — HBM → local DRAM (~800 GB) → local NVMe (~6.5 TB). (2) Iter 3b adds the shared pool, and it changes the calculus two ways: its striped NVMe back (4×, ~25 GB/s) is faster than local single-NVMe (~7 GB/s), and — decisively — it is the only tier reachable after a reroute. (3) A conv loses all its local tiers whenever its next turn lands on a different task, which happens for exactly two reasons:

  • CH ring-membership change — a serving task is added / drained / autoscaled / fails → the ring arc re-maps (ring updates ride the autoscale-event path, §7) → the next turn routes to a task that never held this conv_id.
  • BLCH spill — the owning vnode is over its load cap → the request is deflected to the next task on the ring, off its owning machine.

For both, every local tier on the old machine is a guaranteed miss — that is the irreducible reason a remote tier must exist. But the remote tier rides the shared fabric (queueing → p99 ms; RoCE PFC/retransmit → +ms; a sync spill lands on TPOT — DC8), so HBM-local-only is also wrong. The question is which local tiers survive. (HBM can’t be dropped — attention reads KV from HBM.)

All solutions enumerated:

  • Option A — all-remote: HBM only locally, every spill → the shared pool. It deletes the assumption §9b.4(a) is built on (“capacity > load ⇒ the median conv survives in some local tier between turns”), so the pool’s fpool ≈ 3% tail becomes ~100%. The killer is dropping local DRAM — the off-fabric buffer that serves the frequent short-idle (within-session) returns. Four costs, all from page numbers:
    1. Pool capacity ~3.6×. Wpool ≈ 2.1 PB → ~36 tasks today (the live count, §9b.4); all-remote holds toward Nactive = 3 M × 2.56 GB ≈ 7.7 PB ⇒ ~128 store tasks (a floor).
    2. Read traffic ~20×. Local DRAM serves the bulk of warm restores off-fabric today (only fpull = 5% → pool_pulls = 4 K/s); all-remote makes every non-HBM warm turn a pool pull → ~80 K/s, ≥ ~10 TB/s across the fabric.
    3. Spill onto the fabric. The free local-PCIe HBM→DRAM hop becomes RDMA at ≥ the same 3.84 TB/s write-through (DC2) already rejected; vs today’s ~36 × 100 GB/s ≈ 3.6 TB/s pool fabric, the load forces the ~3.6× resize just to find bandwidth.
    4. Blast radius 3% → ~100%. The pool moves onto the critical path of every inter-turn restore; a pool brownout stalls warm prefills fleet-wide.
  • Option B — keep all three local tiers (HBM + DRAM + local SSD), pool for the rebalanced tail (the Iter 1a shape). Correct before Iter 3b — but the pool now makes local SSD redundant. Local SSD is the coldest local tier, so by construction it holds the longest-idle convs — and the calc below shows that population is almost entirely dead-or-rerouted, i.e. 6.5 TB/task of storage with a near-zero hit rate, while the pool serves the same returns faster and from anywhere.
  • Option C — keep HBM + local DRAM (off-fabric buffer for the short-idle bulk); RETIRE local SSD; the pool (DRAM front + striped-NVMe back) is the cold tier — CHOSEN. Local DRAM keeps the frequent same-machine returns off the fabric; everything colder — which is also everything most likely to be rerouted — lives in the one tier that can serve it after a reroute. (Iter 1a’s local SSD was right when no remote tier existed; Iter 3b supersedes it.)

Why retire local SSD (Option C over B) — three legs, all quantified:

  • 1 · No median-latency edge — the pool is actually faster. Local single-NVMe: βSSD(8 K) = 2.56 GB / 7 GB/s = 366 ms. Pool striped NVMe back: 2.56 GB / 25 GB/s = 102 ms + pipelined RDMA ≈ ~150 ms — ~2.4× faster. Local SSD’s only remaining edge is the off-fabric tail — and that edge applies only to convs that return to the same machine.
  • 2 · Its residents are mostly dead-or-rerouted (the idle-decay calc). A conv reaches local SSD only after aging through HBM + DRAM. DRAM holds ~300 conv KVs/task (§9.4); at the per-task arrival λpeak / serving_tasks = 100 K / 291 500 ≈ 0.34 turns/s, Little’s law gives a DRAM residency of 300 / 0.34 ≈ ~15 min — so an SSD resident has already been idle ≥ ~15 min. Against a session lifetime ≈ 150 s (§1.3), 15 min ⋙ 150 s: such a conv is well past its session — most have ended and never return; the few that resume do so cross-session (hours later). And over a multi-hour gap a ring change is near-certain (every autoscale / deploy / drain / failure re-maps the arc, §7) → P(same machine | resume) → low. So hit_value ≈ P(resume while resident) × P(same machine) ≈ low × low ≈ ~0 per stored conv — 6.5 TB/task buffering convs it will almost never serve.
  • 3 · The pool catches what actually matters — and is the only tier that can. A worthwhile resume lands wherever routing sends it; the pool (reachable from any task) serves it at βpool. The rerouted resume — the dominant case for the long-idle tail — is one local SSD could never serve (wrong machine → miss → recompute). So moving the cold tail into the pool is a correctness upgrade for the rerouted tail, not just a tidy-up; the dead/low-value remainder simply ages out → recompute on the rare return (RPO=0 floor, DC1 Option 3).
  • Honest cost (it is not free). Retiring SSD moves the pool’s admit point from aged-out-of-SSD (~2 hr idle) up to aged-out-of-DRAM (~15 min idle), so the pool now also buffers the worthwhile 15 min–2 hr resumers — a moderate working-set increase (size at apply-time [D]), but bounded far below all-remote’s 8× because local DRAM still absorbs the <15 min short-idle bulk off-fabric. Net trade: save 6.5 TB/task of near-dead local storage; pay a moderate pool-capacity + fabric-read increment, and convert the rerouted-medium-idle case from a recompute into a pool hit.
  • The mirror temptation (“just stripe local SSD 4× instead”) also fails. 4× NVMe ≈ 28–56 GB/s would match the pool on throughput and stay off-fabric — but striping fixes throughput, not latency (each SSD ~10–100 µs vs RDMA ~1–5 µs), is per-machine isolated (zero help for the rerouted tail, which is the whole problem), and pays the 4× SSD cost on every serving task vs concentrating it on ~16–128 store tasks. Wrong direction.

Resulting tier model (Iter 3b): HBM → local DRAM (off-fabric, short-idle buffer) → remote pool DRAM front → remote pool striped-NVMe back → recompute floor. Local NVMe is retired here. (Downstream residency tables that still show a local SSD tier — §9.4, the Iter 4/5 diagrams — update to this 3-tier-local→pool model when the surgical pass lands; the decision and its calc live here.)

DC8 — is the write-back spill synchronous (on the decode critical path) or watermark-driven (async)? Problem (step by step): (1) reclaiming an HBM slot evicts a cold block down the cascade HBM→DRAM→remote/SSD. (2) The HBM eviction needs a free DRAM slot; if DRAM is full it must first drain a block downward — and at Iter 3b that downward hop can be the network (DRAM→remote pool). (3) If that drain runs synchronously, the HBM eviction — and the decode step that triggered it — waits on an RDMA write. (4) So: how long is that wait, and can a decode tick afford it?

All solutions enumerated:

  • Synchronous spill (evict-on-demand — block the reclaim until the remote write ACKs). Simplest to reason about, but it couples a decode tick to a network round-trip. The cost breakdown below is why it is rejected.
  • Watermark-driven background drain — CHOSEN. DRAM is not drained only at 100% full: a background worker keeps free headroom by trickling cold blocks DRAM→remote/SSD ahead of demand, so an HBM eviction always finds an already-free DRAM slot and never waits on the network. This is the same low_watermark discipline Iter 1a already uses for the local HBM→DRAM hop (§6.1); Iter 3b extends it to the slow network hop, where blocking would hurt most.

Why a synchronous spill can’t sit on the decode tick — the wait = fixed + payload, and payload dominates:

  • Fixed cost ≈ 1–3 µs, size-independent. One in-DC RDMA write round trip = doorbell/post + PCIe→NIC + a few switch hops + remote PCIe + ACK. Constant regardless of block size — and, as the next line shows, negligible for KV.
  • Payload cost = page_bytes / effective_path_BW — this is the real wait. A paged block = 16 tok × KV_per_tok, with KV_per_tok = 2 · n_kv_heads · d_head · n_layers · bytes_per_element (§4.2). For this page’s Llama-3-70B (n_layers=80, n_kv_heads=8, d_head=128, fp16): 320 KB/tok → 16 × 320 KB = 5.12 MB/page. Over one NDR400 path (§1.5, ~25 GB/s effective): 5.12 MB / 25 GB/s ≈ ~205 µs; on a slower 100 Gb/s path (~12.5 GB/s): ≈ ~410 µs. (A smaller ~8B model — 32 layers → 128 KB/tok → 2 MB/page — is ~80–160 µs; spill time scales with n_layers.) So one synchronous page spill is tens to low-hundreds of µs, bandwidth-bound; the 1–3 µs fixed term is noise beside it.
  • The tail — not the median — is why sync is avoided. ~205 µs is ~4% of the ~5.2 ms TP=8 decode tick at this iteration’s B=1 (§6.6; falls to ~1% once Iter 6 batches decode to a ~22 ms tick), so a single spill is survivable in isolation. But: (a) the NIC + fabric are shared across tasks/machines → queueing delay → p99 jumps to ms under contention; (b) RoCE congestion / packet loss → PFC pause or retransmit → +ms spikes; (c) a synchronous spill blocks the decode step that triggered it, so that ms-scale tail lands directly on inter-token latency (TPOT) — and on a co-batched tick it stutters every sequence in the batch, not just the evicting one.
  • Net. Watermark drain moves the whole spill distribution (median and tail) off the decode path: the network write happens in the background against pre-reserved headroom, and the eviction itself becomes a pure local pointer update — O(1), lock-free, never NIC-bound. The eviction decision stays local for the same reason: it reads a local block-table field, never a synchronous remote probe.

9b.3.1  Per-tier eviction policy (the post-DC7 cascade)

After DC7 the cascade is HBM → local DRAM → remote pool DRAM front → remote pool striped-NVMe back → recompute — no local SSD. Each hop has its own trigger, victim rule, and critical-path discipline:

HopTrigger (when)Victim (which / how many)Conditional? (bytes saved)Critical path?
1. HBM → local DRAM
(local PCIe, 25 GB/s)		 HBM free-KV crosses low_watermark — pressure, NOT end-of-forward-pass. A background drainer keeps headroom ahead of demand (DC8). The coldest blocks (LRU among ref_count==0) — not the just-finished pass’s KV (that is the hottest; it stays). Enough to climb back to high_watermark, a batch — not “all.” n/a (everything cold spills down one tier). No — fully hidden. Async on a copy engine; a 5.12 MB page ≈ 205 µs &Lll; the 5–21 ms tick, so dozens drain/tick behind compute; the next pass never waits (slot pre-freed).
2. local DRAM → pool DRAM front
(network — RDMA, 25 GB/s)		 DRAM crosses its watermark; the drainer trickles the coldest DRAM blocks to the pool ahead of demand (so hop 1 never blocks on the network — DC8). Coldest DRAM blocks by LRU. Aged out of DRAM ≈ idle ≥ ~15 min (Little’s law, DC7). Yes — two filters before bytes hit the wire. (a) Dedup: if a clean pool copy already exists, send nothing (clean-drop). (b) Value gate: admit only convs worth cross-machine reach (length / recompute-vs-fetch crossover, §9b.4); the rest are discarded → recompute floor. Pool space is the pool’s own LRU problem — the sender posts and moves on, never blocking on a remote space-check. No — async / watermark-driven. A synchronous spill here would be the one that lands on TPOT (DC8); it is kept off the decode path by construction.
3. pool DRAM front → pool NVMe back
(intra-store, no network)		 Pool DRAM front crosses its watermark (pool-side, off the request path). Coldest pool entries by LRU; demoted within the same store task — no fabric hop. n/a (internal demotion). No — pool-side background. Restore from here (NVMe back → HBM) is striped, ~150 ms at the median (DC7) — under TTFT for the common case; long convs still beat recompute.
4. local SSD — retired (DC7) Removed at Iter 3b: it held the coldest, longest-idle convs (idle ≥ ~15 min ⋙ the 150 s session), which are mostly ended-or-rerouted → near-zero hit rate; and the pool’s striped NVMe is faster than local single-NVMe anyway. The cold tail lives in the pool, where a rerouted return can still reach it.

9b.3.2  Write & read paths — exactly when to offload, exactly when to pull

Two corrections to the “len > 1.9 K ⇒ offload / pull” reading:
  • Offload is NOT “len > 1.9 K” alone — it is eviction AND length. A long conv that is still resident locally is never written to the pool (it’s served from HBM/DRAM for free). The write-back fires only when the conv is evicted from all local tiers (idle-aged-out or rerouted); the length gate (> ~1.9 K) is then the filter on whether that evicted conv is worth a cross-machine write. Eviction is the trigger; length is the filter.
  • Pull does NOT re-test length — it tests presence. The length gate was applied at write time, so anything in the pool already cleared it. On a warm-turn local miss we just ask “is conv_id in the pool?”: present ⇒ pull (it’s there because a pull beats recompute, by the write gate’s construction); absent ⇒ recompute. So your “matched len > crossover ⇒ pull” is right in effect, but the threshold lives at the write, not the read.

Write path (offload) — decode-side, on eviction / EOS

on EOS or local-tier eviction of conv X (decode task DⒶ): if X.is_clean(): # Iter 5: a clean pool copy already exists → send nothing (clean-drop) free_local(X); return if cumulative_ctx(X) > ~1.9 K: # the length gate — the ONLY admit test (predictor-free) enqueue_writeback(X → pool[CH(conv_id, pool_ring)], tier_hint=L3_FRONT) # async, OFF the decode tick else: discard(X) # short conv → recompute floor (RPO=0); not worth a cross-machine write

Read path (pull) — prefill-side, on a warm turn

on warm turn for conv X (prefill task PⒶ): if X in local tier (HBM/DRAM): # sticky owner, still resident use local # gap was short → DRAM hit, NO pool, NO network else: # local miss: idle-evicted, OR rerouted to a task that never held X owner = CH(conv_id, pool_ring) # computed, 0 messages [§S1 summary may short-circuit an absent] resp = rdma_probe_and_pull(owner, X) if resp.present and pull_within_TTFT_budget: pull blocks → H2D # present ⇒ cleared the write gate ⇒ pull beats recompute else: recompute(X) # absent (never admitted / evicted), OR pull blew its TTFT budget (p99 fabric — abandon)

Impl (the maybe_writeback / pull_or_recompute algorithms) lives in kv_store.html.

Should the threshold be estimated in real time? (your EWMA question)

The write gate stays a fixed length threshold; the read side already has lightweight real-time awareness, and a full live crossover is a documented §S2 upgrade.
  • Write gate = fixed ~1.9 K (median-α), deliberately. It is a coarse “is this conv worth caching at all” call; making it chase live bandwidth invites a feedback loop (the gate tightens exactly when the fabric is congested, starving the pool of the long convs that most need it). Predictor-free is the main-line virtue (Move D).
  • The read side is already adaptive — two existing hooks. (1) The scheduler keeps measured_β = a rolling-median per-tier β (the wait-time signal), so the live effective bandwidth is observed. (2) The abandon-to-recompute fallback is a real-time check: a pull that exceeds its TTFT budget under contention is dropped for local recompute. That already absorbs the p99-α tail without any predictor.
  • Your full proposal — fold observed latency / effective BW / queue depth / p99 per tier into an EWMA and compute a live crossover N*(αobs, βobs) — is a real improvement (pull only when the fabric is actually fast enough, rather than gate-fixed + abandon-after-the-fact). It belongs in the §S2 sidecar (the adaptive/learned-refinement lane): the building blocks (measured_β rolling median) already exist; promoting them to drive a live crossover is the upgrade path, kept off the main line for simplicity.
