Is the local-miss → pool-probe → recompute round-trip acceptable?

Yes. The absent-case probe is one CH-computed RDMA round-trip — α ≈ µs median, ~ms p99 (PFC). Against the recompute floor it gates (βcold(8 K) ≈ 410 ms … seconds), that wasted RTT is noise (< 0.1% median; a few % p99). And the optional §S1 summary removes even that RTT during correlated mass-miss bursts. So inserting the pool probe ahead of recompute costs ~one RTT and is comfortably affordable (the per-task probe-rate derivation: ≤ λpeak/kv_store_job_task_num ≈ 0.03–0.06% CPU).

Where does the remote store live? (placement)

Same data centre, same NDR400 IB / RoCE fabric — CH-on-conv_id over the store’s own ring (~36 kv-store-job tasks, §9b.4). One-way latency by distance [G — NEW_BOE]: same-rack ~1 µs, same-row ~2 µs, same-DC-building ~5–10 µs — all of which is the α term, negligible against the BW-bound β·n for the > 1.9 K convs the gate admits. So same-row / same-DC placement is fine; cross-region is NOT (RTT jumps to ~ms and per-flow BW collapses to ~1 GB/s — the pull would lose to recompute and blow TTFT). The constraint is “same fabric, µs-RTT”; exact rack/row assignment is an S6 topology detail.

Worst case: pulling KV the size of a whole GPU’s HBM

A returning 256 K-context conv = 256 K × 320 KB ≈ 82 GB — about one H100’s 80 GB HBM. Pull vs recompute:
  • Pull @ 25 GB/s RDMA front = 82 / 25 ≈ 3.28 s; recompute βcold(256 K) ≈ 65.5 s. So even the worst-case whale pull is ~20× faster than recompute — the store still wins decisively, and the length gate would have admitted it.
  • But 3.28 s ⋙ the 400 ms TTFT SLO — neither pull nor recompute makes a returning 256 K conv interactive. This is an accepted rare p99.9 tail, not a contradiction: the store’s job is cross-turn persistence (3.28 s ≪ 65.5 s), not making a 256 K cold-start fast. The real latency answer for million-token single requests is the later context-parallelism iteration (Iter 9 CP).
  • Bandwidth hot-spot: an 82 GB pull occupies one store task’s ~25 GB/s NIC for 3.28 s — the “hot-key pull storm” failure mode (§9b.5 / kv_store.html §8). Bounded by: CH-on-conv_id spreads whales across the ~36 store tasks (no one task owns them all), the 4-SSD-striped NVMe back + front-replication of the hottest convs, multi-path RDMA, and the rarity (p99 context). The abandon-fallback does not help here (recompute is 20× worse) — you ride the 3.28 s out or shed via Iter 9.

9b.4  Number chain

Post-DC7 pool sizing — from the admission FLOW (2026-05-31; flagged model, revisable). fpool the posited fraction stays RETIRED (D5) — nothing here assumes a “% of convs”; the only derived probability is the per-turn pool-admit probability padmit (the flow’s two-factor product), and Wpool falls out of it. DC7 retires local NVMe (local cascade = HBM → DRAM only), moving the admit point ~2 hr → ~15 min idle (Tevict = (106+297)/(106+297+2416) × 2 hr ≈ 17 min, from the §9.4 populations — derived, no model):
padmit = P(out of all local) × P(length gate) # the D5 two-factor demotion flow P(out of local) = P(gap > 15 min) + P(reroute) # gap = inter-turn idle gap (wall-clock to a conv's NEXT turn). P(gap > t) = a power-law / Pareto # SURVIVAL tail ∝ t^(−α) (human return-gaps are heavy-tailed; exponential under-counts the long tail). # P(gap > 15 min) = "this conv goes cold locally (ages out of HBM+DRAM) before it returns". # NB — this is a POPULATION SIZING statistic, NOT a per-request predictor. Runtime never forecasts the gap: # a turn just checks local → (miss) → CH-probe pool → (miss) → recompute. The local MISS *is* the realized # long-gap, observed after the fact. The write-back is reactive on LRU eviction (idle-so-far via last_turn_ts), # not a future-gap guess. P(gap>15min) only sizes W_pool / pull traffic — it is not in any request's code path. P(gap > 15 min) = P(gap > 2 hr) × (2 hr / 15 min)α ≈ 1.3% × 80.7 ≈ 5.6% [flagged: α=0.7 heavy-tail; anchor P(gap>2hr)≈1.3% back-solved from pre-DC7 pool fraction] # P(reroute) = next turn routes to a DIFFERENT task (ring rebalance / autoscale / failure, or BLCH spill) # ⇒ all local tiers on the old task miss regardless of the gap; the OTHER path into the pool. # P(length gate) = fraction of cold convs LONG enough to be worth pooling (cumulative ctx > ~1.9 K, # the recompute-vs-fetch crossover); short single-exchange convs are discarded (recompute is cheaper). P(reroute) ≈ 2% (≤ the 5%/epoch BLCH cap, Iter 1b DC4), P(length gate) ≈ 0.9 ⇒ padmit ≈ (5.6% + 2%) × 0.9 ≈ ~6.8% [DERIVED outcome of the flow — replaces the dead fpool≈3% INPUT; sensitivity α∈[0.5,1.0] ⇒ ~5–11%] [D] Wpool = returning-tail working set the flow fills (Little: demotion_rate × residency × size; steady-state stock = padmit × Nactive) = padmit × Npeak_active_convs × mean_admitted_conv_size = 0.068 × 3 M × 10.4 GB ≈ ~2.1 PB [D] kv_store_job_task_num = ⌈2.1 PB / 60 TB⌉ ≈ ~36 store tasks (range ~27–60 over α) [D]
The dominant uncertainty is the heavy-tail exponent α (the inter-turn idle/return distribution isn’t a stated workload primitive) — the chain scales ~linearly in it, so this is a flagged model, not a settled constant. α is a capacity-sizing input ONLY. It never enters a runtime decision (offload = eviction + length gate; pull = local-miss → direct CH probe), the length-gate crossover (~1.9 K, set by FLOPs + bandwidth, α-independent), or correctness (RPO=0 floor). So a wrong α only mis-provisions the store fleet — too few tasks ⇒ the pool’s capacity-LRU evicts more (lower hit rate → bounded extra recompute, self-correcting); too many ⇒ wasted storage $. No request is mis-routed or mis-served. Knock-on: the §S1 per-task probe rate falls to ≤ λpeak/36 ≈ 2 800/s ≈ 0.03% CPU (more tasks ⇒ lower per-task load ⇒ the filter-optional conclusion strengthens). The fpool≈3% / 90 K / 16-task chain in the SSD-tier steps below is the dead pre-DC7 input, kept only as audit trail; the write-through-avoided cost (Step 1) + recompute-vs-pull crossover (Step 2) are DC7-independent and stand.

Three derivations: the write-through cost the admission policy avoids, the recompute-vs-pull crossover (why pull beats recompute), and the store sizing (pre-DC7 audit trail per the banner above).

Step 1 — the write-through ingest the admission gate avoids

decoded delta / turn = Tout_mean × KVper_tok = 400 × 320 KB = 128 MB [D — §1.3, §4.2] ingest @ λavg = 30 000 req/s × 128 MB = 3.84 TB/s [D] ingest @ λpeak = 100 000 req/s × 128 MB = 12.8 TB/s [D] RDMA front per path = ~25 GB/s ⇒ write-through needs 3.84 TB/s ÷ 25 GB/s = ~154 paths for writes alone → and the vast majority of those bytes are never read — so write-back, not write-through.

Step 2 — recompute vs pull (why the store beats the floor)

A pull from the store is just another tier read, so the crossover is the same algebra as the §6.5 tier-vs-re-prefill crossover, with BWpull in place of the tier BW.

What to make availableRecompute (prefill, §4.1)Pull — RDMA front (25 GB/s)Pull — single NVMe (7 GB/s)
asst delta — 400 tok / 128 MB400 × 44.2 µs ≈ ~18 ms128 MB / 25 GB/s ≈ ~5 ms128 MB / 7 GB/s ≈ ~18 ms
conv_hist median — 8 K / 2.56 GBβcold(8 K) ≈ ~407 ms (lin+attn)2.56 GB / 25 GB/s ≈ ~102 ms2.56 GB / 7 GB/s ≈ ~366 ms
conv_hist p99 — 256 K / 82 GBβcold(256 K) ≈ ~65.5 s (quadratic)82 GB / 25 GB/s ≈ ~3.28 s82 GB / 7 GB/s ≈ ~11.7 s
  • Bandwidth break-even (§6.5 algebra): BW* = KVper_tok × Fsustained / 2P = 3.2e5 × 3.17e15 / 1.4e11 ≈ 7.2 GB/s. Any pull path faster than ~7.2 GB/s beats recompute at every length. The RDMA front (25 GB/s) and 4-SSD stripe (25 GB/s) clear it; single-NVMe (7 GB/s) sits right on it — which is why single-NVMe ties recompute at the 8 K median (366 vs 407 ms) and only the RDMA front wins the median (~102 ms, ~4×).
  • Length crossover (single-NVMe): N* ≈ 1 870 tok (identical to the §6.5 SSD-vs-cold crossover) — pull wins for any conv_hist > ~1.9 K, i.e. essentially every multi-turn conv.
  • The dominant argument is FLOPs offload, not median latency. Recompute burns the bottleneck prefill-GPU compute that prefill_task_num (and the $ line) is sized against; a pull burns cheap I/O BW. Every conv served from the store instead of recomputed shrinks the prefill fleet — that is why Option 2 beats Option 3 even where latency ties. With the RDMA front there is effectively no crossover: pull always wins for cross-turn reuse.

Step 3 — store sizing (storage- vs QPS-bound)

kv_store_job_task_num = max(storage_sized, qps_sized). The illustrative point estimate below is storage-bound. Every input marked G-FA is a flagged operating point (it moves the count, not the shape); the load-bearing claim is the shape — a small DRAM front over a large NVMe capacity tier, fed by eviction, not by every turn.

(a) Storage-sized — the pooled-conv working set (NOT all 3 M sessions). First, units: every count here is a concurrent stock, not a per-second rate. The 3 M active convs are a Little’s-law stock (§1.3: 20 K new sessions/s × 150 s lifetime), so CH-on-conv_id assigns each task only 3 M / task_num ≈ a few hundred to ~1 K convs resident at any instant — well under the ~2 800–3 000-conv local capacity one TP=8 task holds across HBM + DRAM + local NVMe SSD (§9.4, same hardware). Capacity > load ⇒ the median conv survives in some local tier between turns.

(Pre-DC7 derivation — SSD-inclusive residency. DC7 retires local SSD, moving the pool’s admit point from ~2 hr to ~15 min idle, so fpool and the pool working set rise above the 3% / 90 K below — re-derived once the local cascade is reduced to HBM/DRAM (the figures here are the pre-retirement, SSD-inclusive values). The eviction ∧ value-gate logic itself is unchanged.) “Evicted” here means out of all local tiers — not merely off HBM, and not the same event as the 3%. Demotion to local disk is the common case and is still a local hit (βSSD, no network): the local SSD tier alone holds ~2 416 conv KVs per task (§9.4), so a great many convs spill to local disk between turns. The cross-task pool is a fourth, networked tier below local SSD; fpool ≈ 3% is the fraction that reaches that — far smaller than the fraction that merely demotes to local disk. A conv reaches the pool only when the admission bar — a conjunction (§9b DC2), not a length filter — passes: (i) it fell out of all local tiers incl. local SSD (idle long enough that LRU aged it out while the task churned newer convs, or it was BLCH-spilled / rebalanced to a cold task that never held it) AND (ii) it clears the value gate (cumulative ctx > ~1.9 K tok, or high P(continue)×recompute_saved). So fpool ≈ P(out of all local tiers before next turn) × P(clears value gate) — both factors filter (a short conv that returns quickly is evicted yet skipped by the value gate; only a long-idle or long-context conv clears both), so neither eviction nor length alone defines the 3%:

pooled_convs = fpool × Nactive = 0.03 × 3 M = 90 K convs [DEAD pre-DC7 INPUT — fpool retired (D5); superseded by padmit≈7% above] size_pooled_conv = (Tpooled_ctx + Tout_mean) × KVper_tok = (32 K + 0.4 K) × 320 KB ≈ 10.4 GB [G-FA — Tpooled_ctx tail-weighted; pooled convs skew long, median stays local] Wpool = pooled_convs × size_pooled_conv = 90 K × 10.4 GB ≈ ~0.94 PB [D] per_store_task_NVMe = ~60 TB usable (NVMe-dense storage task, 8 × 7.68 TB) [G-FA — HW choice] storage_sized = Wpool / per_store_task_NVMe = 0.94 PB / 60 TB ≈ ~16 tasks [D]

(b) QPS-sized — the RDMA-front pull throughput. Most warm turns hit locally (sticky CH-on-conv_id); only the rebalance / BLCH-spill / autoscale-fresh fraction (§7.6) misses and pulls from the pool:

warm_turns = intra_session_hit_rate × λpeak = 0.80 × 100 K = 80 K/s [D — §1.3] pool_pulls = fpull × warm_turns = 0.05 × 80 K = 4 K/s [G-FA — fpull, the local-miss fraction] pull_BW = pool_pulls × mean_pull_bytes = 4 K/s × 128 MB ≈ 512 GB/s [D — asst-delta-dominated] per_store_task_front_BW = ~4 × 25 GB/s NDR400 paths ≈ 100 GB/s [G-FA — multi-NIC; K20] qps_sized = pull_BW / per_store_task_front_BW = 512 / 100 ≈ ~6 tasks
⇒ kv_store_job_task_num = max(16, 6) = ~16 tasks (storage-bound, pre-DC7 dead input; live count ≈ 36 per §9b.4 top) [G-FA] $ check (live ~36-task count): ~36 NVMe-dense storage tasks (NO GPU) × ~$4 K/mo ≈ ~$144 K/mo ≈ ~7 ppm of the $20.62 B/mo serving fleet — confirms the “I/O-sized, much cheaper” SLO claim: the store trades a rounding-error of $ for a prefill-FLOPs offload.

DRAM front is sized for QPS + latency, not task count: it holds the warm subset (recently-evicted, likely-to-return) so the median pull stays ~102 ms (front-resident) not ~366 ms (single-NVMe). At consumer scale (the later, demoted “introduce the store” slot) the user_sys working-set overflow lands on this same store — the returning-user population whose user_sys isn’t HBM-resident under reuse-recency (the §11.5 f_evict tail + structural misses, ~2.6 M-class): ~2.6 M users × Tuser_sys_mean × KVper_tok = 2.6 M × 1 K × 320 KB ≈ 0.85 PB → +~14 tasks (§11.5) — lifting the pool from ~36 to ~50 tasks, still I/O-sized. The exact count settles at S6 against the measured working set; the iteration’s claim is the shape and the <~10-ppm-of-fleet cost, both robust to the flagged inputs.

9b.5  What breaks

  • Write-back loses the writeback-vs-next-turn race. If the inter-turn gap is shorter than the async write-back, the next turn pulls a stale/absent entry and falls to recompute (the 600-tok warm floor). Blast radius: one turn, correctness intact (RPO=0). Why rare: interactive inter-turn gap ≈ seconds ⋙ async write-back ≈ ms. Detection: pull-miss rate on warm turns. Mitigation: prioritize the write-back queue by context length (longest-recompute first); the Hop already delivered this turn’s decode regardless.
  • Pool hot-key / pull storm. A returning whale conv (256 K, 82 GB) pulled by a freshly-rebalanced PⒶ saturates a pull path (~3.3 s @ RDMA). Mitigation: 4-SSD stripe / front replication of the hottest convs; CH-on-conv_id spreads convs across kv_store_job_task_num tasks so no single pool task owns all whales.
  • Length-gate coarseness. A conv just below the ~1.9 K crossover that does return pays recompute; one just above it that never returns wastes a write. Mitigation: self-bounding by construction — near the crossover recompute ≈ fetch, so either error is cheap; the cost only matters far from the crossover, where the gate is decisive. (The learned P(continue) refinement that sharpens the boundary is the §S2 sidecar, not the main line.)
  • Pool-task failure. A kv-store-job task dies → its convs are absent on pull. Recovery: recompute floor (RPO=0); the durable NVMe back + replication of the front bound the loss. Detection: heartbeat gap (and the §S1 summary delta, if enabled).

9b.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 3)Post-iter (Iter 3b)ΔNote
TTFT p99~896 ms (cold mix-bounded)~896 ms cold; warm asst delta now ~5 ms pull (was ~18 ms recompute)↓Cold turn-1 is unchanged (nothing prior to pull). The win is on warm turns landing on cold/rebalanced tasks and on returning long convs — the quadratic re-prefill (up to ~65 s at 256 K) becomes a linear pull (~3.3 s). The big TTFT headline is still Iter 5 (prefix caching shrinks the prefill itself).
TPOT p99~5.2 ms~5.2 ms→Unchanged — write-back is async, OFF the decode hot path; the decode tick never blocks on the store.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→Unchanged ceiling — the store persists long-context KV across turns; it does not raise the single-request length ceiling (that is the later Context-Parallelism iteration).
Max-output~65 K~65 K→Unchanged — decode-pool concern.
Tenant fairness→→→Unchanged — per-user admission lands at a later iteration.
λpeak supported100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)100 000 req/s (~291 308 serving + ~36 store tasks, §9b.4)→Serving fleet flat. The store adds ~36 NVMe-dense (no-GPU) kv-store-job tasks (post-DC7 sizing, §9b.4; the pre-DC7 audit trail shows ~16); its payoff is realized as a prefill-FLOPs offload — it removes the 600-tok warm recompute floor so the warm prefill returns to ~200 tok, which is the condition a later prefix-caching iteration’s ~1.85 K unmatched-token mean assumes.
$/mo at λpeak~$20.62 B/mo~$20.62 B/mo serving + ~$144 K/mo store (~7 ppm)→Serving line flat at this iteration; the store cost is a rounding error (no-GPU storage tasks). It trades cheap I/O bytes for scarce prefill FLOPs (the offload is booked where the prefill fleet is sized — it underwrites a later prefix-caching iteration’s warm 200-tok floor rather than the 600-tok recompute floor).

Iter 3b retires B12: a conv_id-keyed, RDMA-fronted/NVMe-backed pooled kv-store-job, run as a write-back victim cache (eviction + predictor-free length-gated admission, recompute floor at RPO=0), gives the cross-turn conv K,V stranded by the P/D split a shared home. Hop (Option 0) and Store (Option 2) coexist; the decode pool never runs prefill (Option A rejected), preserving Iter 2’s SLO isolation. The store wins decisively at long context (linear pull vs quadratic recompute) and offloads bottleneck prefill FLOPs everywhere — the mechanism that lets a warm prefill return to the ~200-tok floor the later prefix-caching mean depends on. Next: Iter 4 unblocks long-prefill head-of-line on the prefill pool.

10. Iter 4 — Prefill batching evolution: Chunked → Packed → Varlen (+ Flash Attention v2)

Bottleneck addressed: B6 (long-prefill head-of-line blocking on the prefill pool — a 256 K prefill is βcold ≈ 65 s on its task and serialises every request queued behind it). Staged iteration — one bottleneck, three sequential layout moves (A chunked → B packed → C varlen), each surfacing the next one’s limitation; final Iter 4 state = Stage C varlen on Flash Attention v2. Deferred to later iterations: B2/B5 (decode batching → Iter 6; prefix caching → Iter 5), the long request’s own intrinsic 65 s TTFT at 256 K (→ Iter 9 CP).

Notation introduced in this section — the prefill attention kernel + the chunk/pack/varlen knobs and the chunk-granular wait formula.

FA v2
Flash Attention v2 — SRAM-tiled attention, never materializes the N×N score matrix → HBM I/O O(N²)→O(N). The kernel base reused by Iter 6 (Flash Decoding) and Iter 9 (Ring Attention).
chunk_size C
prefill slice = 4 K tok (band [4 K, 8 K]) — below it MFU starves, above it activations/HoL re-grow.
ridge
roofline ridge point = Fpeak / HBM_BW = 295 FLOP/byte — below this arithmetic intensity a kernel is BW-bound; the floor under C.
B_pack
chunks packed per prefill kernel launch — Stage B knob; varlen (Stage C) makes its padding waste collapse.
cu_seqlens
FA v2 varlen offset array — concatenates all live chunks into one flat buffer so per-slot padding disappears (waste → one tile width).
Stage A / B / C
the prefill batching evolution — Chunked (breaks HoL, starves SMs) → Packed (feeds SMs, re-pays padding) → Varlen (CHOSEN final).
βchunk
per-chunk prefill kernel time = prefill_time(C) ≈ 190 ms at C=4 K — the unit the scheduler interleaves; bounds worst-case HoL slip.
prefill_tok_throughput
sustained prefill rate = F_sustained / (2·P) ≈ 22 600 tok/s (linear regime, MFU 40%).
chunks_ahead(tp) / remaining_unmatched_tokens(r′)
chunks queued ahead of a request = Σ ceil(remaining/C) — the new wait driver; replaces the max-βcold HoL term.

10.1  Architecture (diagram first)

Read the diagram: the top strip is the prefill pool’s problem — whole-request FIFO lets one 256 K prefill (65 s) head-of-line-block every short request behind it. The chunk-granular scheduler slices the long request into 256 K / C = 64 chunks and picks the next chunk from any request, so a short request slips in after at most one chunk (~190 ms), not 65 s. The three panels are the same kernel launch drawn at each stage: Stage A wastes the GPU (one chunk under-fills the SMs), Stage B fills it by packing B_pack chunks but re-pays per-slot padding every tick, Stage C concatenates into one flat cu_seqlens buffer so the only waste is one tile-width at the very end. The bottom panel shows chunking also upgrades Iter 2’s KV transfer: each chunk’s KV streams the instant it finishes, hiding all but the last chunk.

Does chunk k re-loading chunks 0…k−1’s KV accumulate and blow up HBM? No — two different memory pools, and chunking only touches the small one.
  • Chunking is HBM-neutral on the KV cache. Each token’s K,V is computed and written exactly once — when its own chunk is processed — and then stays resident. When chunk k attends to chunks 0…k−1 it reads that already-resident KV (FA v2 streams it from HBM through SRAM tiles, DC1); it does not re-allocate or duplicate it. End-of-prefill footprint is exactly N × KV_per_tok — identical to a single-shot, non-chunked prefill. Chunking changes the order the work is done, not the byte count.
  • What does grow is the KV cache as a function of context length N — independent of chunking. A 256 K prompt is 256 K × 320 KB = 82 GB; 1 M is 320 GB (§4.2), chunked or not. That is the B3-capacity / long-context problem, handled by other iterations: PagedAttention (Iter 3 — grow block-by-block, no fragmentation), HBM→DRAM→SSD tiering (Iter 1a — spill the cold KV), and context parallelism (Iter 9 — partition the sequence across CP ranks). Chunking neither causes nor worsens it.
  • What chunking bounds is the transient in-kernel working set, not the KV. The thing that would actually explode on a long prompt is the attention scratch — the N×N score matrix + Q/K/V/O softmax buffers. FA v2 keeps the score matrix in SRAM tiles (never materialises N×N in HBM, DC1), and a fixed chunk size C caps that scratch at a per-chunk budget f(C) regardless of N (DC2, ceiling part (1)). So chunking reduces peak transient HBM — the opposite of blowing it up.
  • The real cost of chunking is bandwidth, not capacity. Re-reading the resident prior KV at each chunk boundary is intrinsic to causal attention (O(N²) reads — the same pattern a single-shot prefill pays); “Iter 4 conserves total prefill FLOPs” (§10.6). The DC2 lower floor (C ≥ 4 K) exists precisely so the weight re-reads (17.5 GB/GPU once per chunk) don’t multiply N/C times — a bandwidth concern, never a capacity one.
Packed multi-request case (req1.c2 + req2.c2 in one launch): where does the prior-KV read latency hide? Premise first: by the time c2 runs, both requests’ c0, c1 KV is already in HBM — written when those chunks were processed moments earlier, and kept resident because an actively-prefilling sequence holds ref_count > 0 on its blocks (never an eviction victim, §6.4 DC5). There is no DRAM→HBM “load” step for them.
  • The only “load” is HBM→SRAM, and FA v2 already hides it. The attention kernel streams each prior K,V tile from HBM into on-chip SRAM and double-buffers: the async copy of tile j+1 is issued while the tensor cores compute Q·Kᵀ + online-softmax on tile j. The prior-KV read is overlapped with the matmul by construction — exactly what “tiles through SRAM” (DC1) buys; no scheduler arrangement needed.
  • Batching the c2s fills the SMs, not HBM. Packing req1.c2 + req2.c2 (Stage B/C) is a compute-saturation move — feed enough tokens per launch to clear the MFU floor (DC2). The KV cache (all of c0,c1,c2 for both requests) occupies HBM regardless of whether their c2s are co-batched; batching changes SM utilisation, not HBM occupancy. cu_seqlens keeps the two segments isolated — req1.c2 attends only to req1’s prior KV, never req2’s.
  • The one case where prior KV is loaded into HBM from a slower tier is a prefix-cache hit / warm-conv pull (Iter 5 / Iter 3b): c0,c1 match a cached prefix sitting in DRAM/SSD/pool rather than being freshly computed. There the latency hides via scheduler prefetch — the block table is known, so those blocks are pulled (DRAM→HBM / RDMA→HBM) ahead of the chunk that needs them, overlapping earlier chunks’ compute — and, on the decode handoff, via per-chunk KV streaming (§10.4).

10.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Enabling priors. Iter 2’s disagg gave a prefill-only pool whose whole FLOPs + HBM budget targets a homogeneous prefill workload; Iter 3’s PagedAttention freed the HBM headroom that lets the prefill pool hold several in-flight requests’ KV at once — the precondition for interleaving chunks across requests on one task. Without paging, holding multiple partial prefills concurrently would re-hit the contiguous-slab over-allocation Iter 3 just retired.
  • The constraint that flipped — B6 is now the dominant prefill tail. Post-Iter-2 the prefill pool runs at B=1 at full-request granularity (it must stay there — prefill is compute-bound, so batching whole requests grows TTFT linearly, per Iter 2’s Why-NOW). At the p99 input length 256 K, βcold = 65.5 s (§4.1); at p99.9 = 1 M, 871 s. Whole-request FIFO makes every request queued behind a long one wait its full βcold — fleet TTFT p99 is bounded by the worst-case prefill in flight, not by the request’s own length. What the 65 s actually is post-Iter-3b: a genuinely-new long prompt (e.g. a document paste, or turn-1 of a long context) — nothing prior exists to pull. A returning long conv is served by the Iter 3b store as a ~3.3 s linear pull, not a 65 s quadratic recompute, so it is no longer a recompute-HoL source; B6’s remaining long-prefill population is new long prompts, which chunk-granular interleaving (below) is what unblocks.
  • This is staged, not a single move (DC3). Each stage fixes what the previous one exposes — chunked breaks the HoL block but under-feeds the GPU; packed refills the GPU but re-pays per-slot padding every tick; varlen removes the padding. Presenting A and B is not “alternatives left on the table” — it is the derivation that makes Stage C non-arbitrary.
  • Why Flash Attention v2 lands HERE (DC1) — one kernel decision, three downstream users. Stages B + C are implemented on FA v2’s batched / varlen kernel: without FA’s tile-through-SRAM behaviour, packing would re-materialise the N×N score matrix in HBM and lose the win. FA is also the base for Iter 6’s Flash Decoding (KV-split decode variant) and Iter 9’s Ring Attention (FA across CP ranks). Landing it at its first user is right; deferring forces a backfill at Iter 6.
  • “Why not SRTF (shortest-remaining-time-first)?” — SRTF helps only when the queue is mixed short/long. At Tin_mean = 13 K, p99 = 256 K, most requests are long enough that SRTF degenerates toward FIFO; it reorders the queue but never preempts the in-flight long request, so a 256 K prefill already on the SMs still blocks for its full duration. Chunking attacks B6 structurally; SRTF only at the margin.
  • “Why not priority-preempt the long prefill?” — preemption means save/restore the in-progress attention state to HBM/DRAM at a full-layer boundary (~tens of ms per move, plus the HBM churn). Chunked prefill’s preemption boundary is between chunks — the chunk has already committed its KV to paged blocks, so there is nothing to save. Free preemption vs paid preemption.
  • “Why not vanilla attention for prefill?” — vanilla quadratic attention’s N×N score matrix is O(N²) HBM I/O; at 13 K mean and 256 K p99 the score matrix alone is GB-scale per request. FA-family kernels are non-optional at these context lengths, not a polish item.
  • Why not also resize the fleet here? — the big prefill-token reduction comes from Iter 5 prefix caching (it shrinks the unmatched tokens each request must prefill from Tin_mean = 13 K to ~1.85 K). Iter 4 conserves total prefill FLOPs (chunking slices work, it does not delete it); the $ payout is booked at Iter 5. One bottleneck per iteration.

10.3  Design choices

DC1 — Attention kernel (the foundational primitive used at Iter 4, 6, and 9)

Problem: prefill computes attention over the whole prompt. Vanilla attention writes the N×N score matrix to HBM, which is O(N²) traffic — at 256 K that matrix alone dwarfs the model’s weight reads. Pick the kernel that makes long-context prefill compute-bound rather than HBM-bound and supports the ragged-batch packing Stages B + C need.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • Naive / standard quadratic attention — rejected: materialises the full N×N score matrix in HBM; reads/writes are O(N²); SM utilisation collapses at long context. Structurally unusable at this workload.
  • xformers memory-efficient attention (Meta) — the pre-FA precursor with the same SRAM-tiling idea but a different implementation; FA v2’s varlen kernel is the production-default that superseded it. Name it only as the migration source if the team has prior xformers integration.
  • Flash Attention v2 (Dao et al., v2 2023) — CHOSEN: tiles Q × Kᵀ × V through SRAM / shared memory; never materialises the attention matrix in HBM; HBM I/O drops from O(N²) to O(N). Picked because (i) its varlen mode is the prerequisite for Stage C’s flat cu_seqlens packing; (ii) it is the FA-family member with the widest production deployment and the most mature decode-side variants (Flash Decoding at Iter 6, Ring Attention at Iter 9). [G — K17]
  • Flash Attention v3 (Hopper-specialised: TMA + async warp specialisation; ~1.5× v2 on H100) — rejected only on timing: v3’s stability + varlen support matured later than this design’s reference era. A straight drop-in upgrade once both land in the same release — no architectural change.
  • FlexAttention (PyTorch-native, 2024; torch.compile-fused score-modifier API) — rejected for the hot path: great for non-standard patterns (sliding-window, local+global) but does not beat hand-tuned FA v2 on vanilla full-attention prefill. Worth naming as the “extend without writing CUDA” forward path.

Why FA v2 wins:

  • Three boxes at once. (a) compute-bound at long context (O(N) HBM I/O), (b) varlen-capable (the packing primitive Stage C builds on), (c) production-hardened with the decode + distributed variants Iter 6 and Iter 9 need.
  • Others fail one. v3 is strictly better on H100 but off the reference-era stability bar; everything else fails (a), (b), or both.

[param] chunk_size C: ∞ (whole-request prefill) → C ∈ [4 K, 8 K] (worked at 4 K) — Iter 4. Why: a whole-request 256 K prefill HoL-blocks the pool for ~65 s; slicing into 256 K / C chunks bounds the block to βchunk ≈ 190 ms. Floor: below ~4 K, MFU saturates < 40%; ceiling: above ~8 K, the per-chunk activation budget + HoL grow. Enumeration below.

DC2 — Chunk size C (the prefill-throughput knob, between two cliffs)

Problem: chunked prefill slices each request into fixed C-token chunks. C is a real design number, not a free parameter — it is bounded below by a compute-bound floor (too small → the kernel is weight-bandwidth-bound and re-reads weights for nothing) and above by a three-part ceiling (too large → HBM activation re-explodes, short prompts mismatch, HoL re-grows). Pick C strictly between them.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • C too small (256 / 512 / 1 K) — fails the compute-bound floor. A prefill GEMM with C tokens has arithmetic intensity ≈ C FLOP/byte (it reads the weight shard once and does 2·C·d·d′ FLOPs against it). The H100 roofline ridge is ridge = peak_BF16 / HBM_BW = 989 TFLOP/s / 3.35 TB/s = 295 FLOP/byte [[D]]. Below C ≈ 295 the kernel is literally weight-BW-bound — and splitting a length-N prefill into N/C chunks re-pays the 17.5 GB/GPU weight read N/C times for no extra useful compute. Even above the ridge, achieved MFU keeps ramping (GEMM tile + wave quantisation on small token counts) and only plateaus near the 40% sustained value around C ≈ 4 K for a 70 B model on H100 [G — K17]. So the practical floor is 4 K, above the 295 ridge.
  • C too large (16 K+) — fails the three-part ceiling: (1) HBM activation re-explodes — chunking’s whole point is bounding the in-kernel Q/K/V/O + softmax working set to a fixed per-chunk budget; large C re-violates it on the p99 256 K / p99.9 1 M tail, undoing Iter 3. (2) Short-prompt mismatch — 80% of the live mix is 200-tok turn-N messages; a 16 K chunk either pads them (Stage-B waste → 15 800 / 16 000 ≈ 98.75% of the slot) or admits them as un-chunked exceptions (losing the fixed-shape uniformity Iter 6’s CUDA-graphs need). (3) HoL re-grows — worst-case wait for an arriving short request is one βchunk ∝ C; doubling C doubles HoL exposure, partly undoing B6.
  • C ∈ [4 K, 8 K] — CHOSEN (worked at C = 4 K): clears the MFU-saturation floor (every kernel runs at sustained 40% MFU), fits the per-chunk activation budget, matches the short-prompt distribution (a 4 K chunk packs many 200-tok messages), and keeps HoL bounded at βchunk = prefill_time(4 K) ≈ 190 ms. Concrete value settled against the short-tail length distribution from §1.3.

Why [4 K, 8 K] wins:

  • The only safe band. Above the 4 K MFU-saturation floor and below the 16 K activation/HoL ceiling.
  • Asymmetric cliffs. Below the floor you lose throughput (BW-bound re-reads); above the ceiling you lose the HoL fix and the HBM headroom Iter 3 bought.
  • Smallest compute-bound C. 4 K is the smallest fully compute-bound chunk — exactly what minimises HoL while staying efficient.
  • Not Bdecode. C bounds per-chunk tokens; Bdecode (Iter 6) bounds concurrent decode slots — different axes, different cliffs.

[param] B_pack (chunks packed per prefill kernel): 1 (Stage A) → B_pack (Stage B) → varlen (Stage C) — Iter 4. Why: one chunk per kernel under-fills the SMs; packing B_pack chunks saturates them but re-pays per-slot padding every tick; varlen concatenates all live chunks into one cu_seqlens buffer so waste collapses from O(B_pack × C)/tick to one tile-width/buffer. Final state = varlen.

DC3 — Prefill batching evolution (three sequential stages; final state = Stage C)

Problem: under “prefill must stay B=1 at full-request granularity” (TTFT linearity) and “long prefills must not HoL-block short ones” (B6), find the kernel-batch layout that interleaves work across requests at chunk granularity and keeps the GPU saturated and wastes no compute on padding.

Three stages, each a strict superset of the previous in batching capability, each surfacing the next one’s bottleneck:

  • Stage A — Chunked (one chunk per kernel launch). Solves: (i) a long prefill no longer has to fit-or-fail one kernel — the in-kernel footprint is bounded to ~f(C) regardless of N; (ii) fixed C gives the scheduler a small captured set of kernel shapes (Iter 6 CUDA-graph enabler); (iii) B6 HoL — the scheduler picks the next chunk from any request, so shorts interleave between a long request’s chunks. Exposes: at one chunk per launch, a long request advances only one chunk per tick and one chunk under-fills the SMs (below the DC2 floor for typical C) — throughput sags.
  • Stage B — Packed (B_pack chunks per kernel via FA’s batched/varlen mode; rectangular padded layout, attention mask zeroes the pad). Solves: Stage A’s under-utilisation — B_pack (request, chunk) pairs land in one launch, the SMs are fed, sustained MFU is restored, and long requests advance multiple chunks per tick while shorts still preempt at chunk granularity. Exposes: every slot reserves C tokens but actual chunk length ∈ [1, C] — the last chunk of every request is Tin mod C < C by construction — so worst-case padding waste = B_pack × (C−1) tokens per launch, re-paid every tick.
  • Stage C — Varlen (FA v2 cu_seqlens mode: all live chunks concatenated into one flat [Ntotal, d] buffer; cu_seqlens[i] marks per-segment offsets so attention masks only within each segment) — CHOSEN. Solves: Stage B’s padding — no slot reservation, each chunk consumes exactly its token count. Residual waste: tile-rounding at the end of the flat buffer only — bounded by one FA SRAM-tile width (~128 tok), independent of B_pack; orders of magnitude below Stage B. Per-request chunking is unchanged (still fixed C), so the HBM-budget and CUDA-graph benefits survive; varlen only changes how the batched layout is packed for the kernel. [G — K17]

Why Stage C wins:

  • Utilisation without padding. Only layout that keeps Stage B’s SM saturation while removing its padding tax.
  • Waste collapses. From O(B_pack × C) per tick (Stage B) to O(tile_width) once per buffer — at B_pack = 16, C = 4 K, ~65 K padded slots/tick → ~128 tok/buffer.
  • A and B earn their place. Each is the honest precondition for the next: no varlen flat buffer without first hitting packed’s padding; no packing without first hitting chunked’s starvation.

10.4  Cross-pool KV streaming — chunking upgrades Iter 2’s transport

Iter 2 chose per-request bulk transfer: wait for the full prefill, then bulk-RDMA the whole KV slab to the decode task (the 166 ms cold-path tax). Once prefill is chunked, that trigger moves for free — each chunk’s KV is complete the instant the chunk finishes, so it ships immediately while later chunks are still computing. This is the chunked-prefill-driven pipelining pattern: it captures the per-layer-overlap goal without the per-layer synchronisation complexity (which is why per-layer streaming is rarely shipped), by piggy-backing on a chunk boundary the scheduler already maintains.

Iter 2 (per-request bulk): exposed xfer = full slab / RDMA_BW = 4.16 GB / 25 GB/s = 166 ms [D — §4.2, §4.3] Iter 4 (per-chunk pipeline): chunks 1…(n−1) stream while 2…n compute → only the LAST chunk’s KV is exposed at the end: exposed xfer = C × KV_per_tok / RDMA_BW = 4 096 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s = 1.31 GB / 25 GB/s = ~52 ms [D] → cold-path TTFT transfer term: 166 ms → ~52 ms (−114 ms)

The KV-handoff protocol itself is unchanged from Iter 2 (gRPC server-stream over RDMA, KVHandoff / KVHandoffAck); only the “when to send” trigger moves from end-of-prefill to end-of-chunk. Decode still cannot emit its first token until the last chunk’s hidden state exists, so a request’s own TTFT is not shortened by pipelining — but the exposed cross-pool term that sat on the critical path shrinks ~3.2×.

10.5  Wait-time formula delta — the HoL term disappears

The Router’s consumer is unchanged (it still reads one scalar per prefill task); the shape of what that scalar measures changes. Pre-Iter-4 the prefill wait was a sum of whole-request βcold values — dominated by the single largest prefill ahead (the HoL term, up to 65 s). Post-Iter-4 the scheduler interleaves at chunk granularity, so a request waits only for the chunks ahead of it, processed at the prefill task’s sustained chunk rate:

Pre-Iter-4 (whole-request FIFO): approx_wait_prefill(t_p) = Σr′ ahead βcold(Nr′) → dominated by max βcold (one 256 K req = 65 s HoL) [D] Post-Iter-4 (chunk-granular): βchunk = prefill_time(C) = prefill_time(4 K) ≈ 190 ms [D — §4.1 table N=4K] prefill_tok_throughput = F_sustained / (2·P) = 3.17·10¹⁵ / 1.4·10¹¹ ≈ 22 600 tok/s (linear regime, sustained MFU=40%) [D] chunks_ahead(t_p) = Σr′ live ceil(remaining_unmatched_tokens(r′) / C) approx_wait_prefill(t_p)= chunks_ahead(t_p) × (C / prefill_tok_throughput) = chunks_ahead(t_p) × βchunk [D] → key change: a short request’s chunks_ahead counts other requests’ CURRENT chunk shares, NOT the full length of any long request in flight. The max-βcold HoL term is gone; worst-case interleave slip behind a whale = one βchunk ≈ 190 ms.

Total prefill FLOPs are conserved — chunking does not change throughput, it changes who is ahead of you. Because the scheduler can reorder at chunk granularity, the long request no longer monopolises the queue head; its remaining chunks are scheduled fairly against everyone else’s. (Packing/varlen is what keeps prefill_tok_throughput at the sustained 22 600 tok/s value — without it the short-heavy mix would run sub-floor; see §10.6.) Why this smooths routing: pre-Iter-4 the wait signal spiked whenever a long prefill landed (false-hot triggers, needless BLCH spill); post-Iter-4 it tracks the fair share of total prefill work → smoother, fewer spurious spills.

10.6  Number chain — throughput conserved, TTFT p99 unblocked, fleet deferred

Iter 4 conserves total prefill FLOPs; its job is to unblock TTFT p99 (HoL removal) and to realise the sustained-MFU prefill throughput the §4.1 model already assumes — not to resize the fleet (that lands at Iter 5 when prefix caching shrinks the unmatched-token count). The number chain spells out the stage-by-stage utilisation so the “flat per-task throughput” claim is auditable.

Step 1 — why packing is needed to hold the modelled throughput

MFU-saturation floor (DC2) = C ≈ 4 K tok [G — K17] warm-fraction request length = Tuser_msg_mean = 200 tok [G — §1.3] → an UNPACKED 200-tok prefill kernel (80% of the mix) is far below the 4 K floor → runs at a fraction of the 40% sustained MFU. → Stage A alone (one chunk/kernel) cannot hold prefill_per_task = 6.53 req/s on the short-heavy mix; Stage B/C packing co-schedules shorts into one ≥ 4 K-token launch that DOES clear the floor. [D]

So does packing “increase the batch size”? Only in the saturation sense, never the throughput-multiplier sense. Packing co-batches B_pack chunks from different requests into one launch — a genuine token-level batch — but its job is to hold the modelled MFU on tiny chunks, not to raise throughput. This is the opposite of decode’s Bdecode (Iter 6), which is a throughput multiplier, and the difference is the roofline regime: decode is HBM-bandwidth-bound (1 token’s compute per full weight read → batching Bdecode sequences amortises that one read across Bdecode tokens → near-linear gain), whereas prefill is already compute-bound (a chunk packs C tokens per weight read, above the 295-FLOP/byte ridge of DC2 → no idle compute for batching to reclaim). Packing only stops you dropping below sustained MFU on a 200-tok chunk; it cannot push you past it. Hence Iter 4 conserves total prefill FLOPs — chunked prefill does not increase the batch size as a throughput lever; it re-organises work to keep the SMs fed and to kill HoL.

Step 2 — Stage-B padding waste vs Stage-C residual (the A→B→C payoff)

Stage B worst-case pad = B_pack × (C − 1) tok / launch = 16 × (4 096 − 1) ≈ 65 520 padded slots / tick [D] (re-paid every tick; ~50% of a 16×4K launch in the degenerate all-short case) Stage C residual = one FA tile width, end of buffer, once ≈ 128 tok / buffer (independent of B_pack) [G — K17] ratio (B / C waste) ≈ 65 520 / 128 ≈ ~512× less waste at Stage C [D] Stage C throughput = Stage B / (1 − pad_fraction) → recovers the padded compute

Step 3 — per-task throughput and fleet (held flat; resize deferred to Iter 5)

prefill_per_task_iter4 = prefill_per_task_iter3 ≈ 6.53 req/s/task [D] (now actually achievable on the short-heavy mix — Stage C packing clears the MFU floor that an unpacked B=1 kernel could not; the §8.5 model assumed this MFU, Iter 4 delivers it) decode_per_task_iter4 = decode_per_task_iter3 ≈ 0.479 req/s/task [D] prefill_task_num_iter4 ≈ 19 909 tasks (unchanged — B6 is a latency fix) [D] decode_task_num_iter4 ≈ 271 399 tasks (unchanged this iteration) [D] total_serving_iter4 ≈ 291 308 tasks [D] $/mo_iter4 = 291 308 × $70 790 ≈ $20.62 B / month [D] → flat $/mo by design (mirrors Iter 2/3): Iter 4 is the LATENCY + structural iteration; the prefill-token reduction that shrinks prefill_task_num lands at Iter 5 (unmatched tokens 13 K → 1.85 K). One bottleneck per iteration.

Step 4 — TTFT p99: the SLO that actually moves

Pre-Iter-4 effective TTFT p99 (HoL-bounded): a short request landing behind an in-flight 256 K prefill waits its full βcold(256 K) = 65.5 s → whenever a p99 request is in flight, the prefill-pool TTFT p99 is in the TENS OF SECONDS. [D — §4.1] Post-Iter-4 TTFT p99 (HoL removed): bulk-traffic TTFT p99 = cold-mix prefill + exposed xfer + interleave slip ≈ 730 ms (cold 13 K) + 52 ms (1-chunk xfer, §10.4) + ≤ 190 ms (one-βchunk slip) ≈ ~972 ms [D] (the long request’s OWN 65 s at 256 K is intrinsic and rare (p99 length), no longer contaminates the bulk-traffic p99; the 1 M case → Iter 9 CP.)

10.7  What breaks

  1. Chunk boundary at a non-natural-token position. A chunk boundary can fall mid-sentence / mid-token-group. Impact: none on correctness or quality — attention is positional, not boundary-aware; the chunk only bounds how many tokens enter one kernel, not which tokens attend to which (causal masking via cu_seqlens is unchanged). Detection: output-quality canary unaffected by C sweeps confirms it. No mitigation needed.
  2. Per-task chunk counter drift under failover. If a prefill task dies mid-request, its in-flight chunk progress is lost. Blast radius: one request (no cross-task chunk coupling — each request’s chunks run on one prefill task). Detection: KVHandoffAck gap + missing terminal chunk. Recovery: re-pick via CH(conv_id, prefill_ring) — the new PⒶ is exactly the cold/rebalanced case the Iter 3b pooled store exists for, so it pulls the conv’s prior turns from the store (linear I/O) and re-prefills only the genuinely-new tail; full recompute from prompt_tokens is the RPO=0 floor when the store misses. Bounded retry budget as in Iter 2.
  3. FA varlen kernel tuning at very-ragged batch shapes. A flat buffer mixing one 4 K chunk with many 200-tok segments can leave SRAM tiles partly idle if segment offsets align poorly with tile width. Detection: per-launch achieved-MFU telemetry vs the 40% sustained target. Mitigation: pad-to-nearest-tile at the end of the buffer only (bounded by one tile width regardless of B_pack — the Stage-C residual, already accounted); optionally per-workload tile-width tuning.
  4. cu_seqlens construction CPU cost on busy ticks. Each tick the scheduler builds the per-segment offset array for the packed batch. Cost: O(segments_per_tick) — negligible vs the kernel runtime at any plausible B_pack. Mitigation: keep it on the engine-loop hot path (C/Rust, pre-allocated buffer), same discipline as Iter 3’s slot_mapping uploader. Detection: scheduler-tick p99 vs prefill-kernel p99.
  5. Stage-C adversarial under-performance. If SRAM tile boundaries align pathologically with segment offsets, a crafted workload could see throughput below Stage B. Blast radius: throughput only (never correctness). Detection: achieved-MFU regression alarm. Mitigation: a scheduler flag to fall back to Stage-B rectangular packing for the offending batch shape; tile-width tuning before production cutover. The fallback is local and per-tick — no fleet-wide impact.

10.8  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 3b)Post-iter (Iter 4)ΔNote
TTFT p99HoL-bounded — tens of s when a long prefill is in flight~972 ms (bulk traffic; HoL removed)↓The headline win. Whole-request FIFO let a 256 K prefill (65.5 s) serialise everyone behind it; chunk-granular interleaving caps a short request’s slip at one βchunk ≈ 190 ms. The per-chunk KV stream also trims the cold-path transfer term 166 → 52 ms. Still > the 400 ms SLO on the cold path — closed at Iter 5 (prefix caching shrinks the prefill itself). The 256 K request’s own 65 s is intrinsic and rare; the 1 M case → Iter 9 CP.
TPOT p99~5.2 ms~5.2 ms→Unchanged — Iter 4 is a prefill-pool iteration; decode tick time is set by HBM BW on the decode task. Decode batching lands at Iter 6.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→Unchanged ceiling, but chunking removes the “long prefill can’t fit one kernel” failure: a 256 K prefill now runs as 64 bounded-footprint chunks instead of one impossible launch. The single-request 1 M length ceiling still needs CP (Iter 9).
Max-output~65 K~65 K→Unchanged — output length is a decode-pool concern; prefill chunking does not touch it.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — per-user admission (token bucket + concurrent-conv cap) lands at a later iteration. Chunk interleaving improves cross-request fairness on one prefill task but is not the per-user control.
λpeak supported100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)→Flat by design — total prefill FLOPs are conserved (B6 is a latency fix, not a throughput multiplier). Packing makes the modelled 6.53 req/s/task actually achievable on the short-heavy mix. Fleet shrink lands at Iter 5.
$/mo at λpeak~$20.62 B/mo~$20.62 B/mo→Flat — mirrors the task count. Iter 4 buys TTFT p99 + the CUDA-graph fixed shapes Iter 6 consumes; the $ payout is the prefill-token reduction booked at Iter 5.

Iter 4 retires B6: chunk-granular scheduling on the FA v2 varlen kernel decouples a short request’s TTFT from the length of any long request in flight, collapsing the HoL term from max βcold (up to 65 s) to one βchunk ≈ 190 ms. The three-stage progression — chunked breaks HoL but starves the SMs, packed feeds them but re-pays per-slot padding every tick, varlen keeps the utilisation and drops the padding to a single end-of-buffer tile — is why Stage C is the non-arbitrary endpoint. Total prefill FLOPs are conserved, so per-task throughput and $/mo stay flat (the “flat by design” pattern of Iter 2/3); the structural deposits — CUDA-graph fixed shapes for Iter 6, per-chunk KV streaming, and chunk-granular prefix matching — are spent at Iter 5 (prefix caching, the prefill-token reduction) and Iter 6 (decode batching).

11. Iter 5 — Prefix caching (the prefill-token reduction — bulk of the prefill $ saving)

Bottleneck addressed: B5 (the shared prefix — hidden_sys + user_sys + conversation history — is recomputed from scratch on every request, even though most of it is byte-for-byte identical to a prefix already prefilled somewhere on the fleet). Mechanism: a content-addressable, position-aware block-hash chain that lets any request reuse a previously computed KV prefix at chunk granularity, so each request prefills only its unmatched tail. Leans on Iter 3b: prefix caching surfaces the long-tail-user user_sys that reuse-recency LRU + ref_count can’t keep HBM-resident — those users fall back to the already-existing Iter 3b pooled KV store (B12 was retired there). At consumer scale the user_sys working-set overflow onto that store becomes load-bearing — a later scale event, not new infrastructure. Deferred: decode-side $ (continuous batching → later decode-batching iteration); per-user admission (token bucket → later admission iteration).

Notation introduced in this section — the content-addressed cache key, the reuse-recency retention, the prompt segments as residency units, and the prefill-work reduction. Reuses ref_count / watermarks from §6.4.

block_hash = H_block_i
the cache key = hash(parent_hash ‖ tokens_i) — a chained, content-only, position-aware hash; a block matches only if its entire prefix matches (K2 D5 correctness).
parent_hash / chain_hash
the running prefix hash folded block-by-block / the full-request chain key (used for single-flight dedup).
hidden_sys / user_sys / conv_hist
the prompt segments as reuse units — platform prefix (~100% reuse, fleet-wide) / per-user prefix (~95%) / history (warm-turn decode-side residency, not a prefill hit).
reuse-recency LRU + ref_count
the retention policy (DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE Change 5; no hard pin) — a prefix stays resident because it is re-hit; ref_count gives correctness for free.
τ_resident
local residency window over HBM+DRAM ≈ 60 s — comfortably > the ~30 s think-gap, so most returns are local hits.
f_evict
the tail fraction whose gap outlasts τ_resident (or structural miss) — falls back to the Iter 3b pool; sizes pool QPS.
single_flight[chain_hash]
This IS a map (Router, 200 ms TTL): chain_hash → InFlightPrefill — dedups a thundering herd of identical cold prefills.
unmatched_tokens(r)
mean prefill work per request after caching — Tin_mean 13.3 K → ~1.85 K; drives the ~86% prefill cut + ~7× prefill-fleet shrink (19 909 → ~2 767).
seed(class)
the per-user-seed hardening lever (DC2 (c), not shipped) — tenant_id on user-private blocks, NULL on public hidden_sys; closes the front-of-chain timing probe.
clean-drop / tier_locs
eviction-cost move (DC5) — if a lower tier already holds the block_hash (block is_clean via the tier_locs bitmap), evict by dropping, not copying down.
W-TinyLFU
the documented scan-resistance upgrade on the eviction tail (a frequency-aware admission filter) — off the main line.

11.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Request prompt → chain builder — the engine tokenises the prompt and folds each block into the running parent_hash (solid = synchronous, on the admit path).
  • ② Chain hashes → flat-hash index — each block_hash is looked up in the prefill task’s local index; a hit serves cached KV from the resident tier and skips that block’s prefill.
  • ┄┄ ③ Tail miss → pool fallback (dashed gray) — a user_sys whose between-turns gap outlasted the reuse-recency residency window τ_resident (the f_evict tail), or a structural miss (BLCH spill / autoscale-fresh / brand-new user), fetches user_sys KV from the already-existing Iter 3b pooled KV store (async fallback). The prior PinUserSys Router→pin-list control arrow is removed (Change 5) — retention is now reuse-recency LRU + ref_count on the task itself.

Read the diagram. The top strip is why this iteration is the prefill-$ headline: of the 13.3 K-token mean prompt, hidden_sys (4 K, identical for every user) and most of user_sys are already prefix-cached on the prefill side, and conv_hist is reused per-request (80% of requests are warm follow-ups whose prior conv K,V is already materialized — decode-side resident on DⒶ plus a pull of the most-recent assistant delta from the Iter 3b pooled store; only the 20% cold starts re-prefill the full ~8 K). Net, mean unmatched tokens fall to ~1.85 K (given the Option-2 store pull; recompute floor ~2.13 K, §11.6), so the fleet’s prefill demand drops ~86%. The “80% per-token conv_hist hit on the prefill side” would be the wrong reading — conv_hist reuse is decode-side residency, and the assistant-output K,V reaches a cold prefill task only via the Iter 3b store. The left column is the key that makes a block reusable: a chained, content-only hash — H_block_i = hash(parent_hash ‖ tokens_i) — so a block matches only if its entire prefix matches (position-aware correctness, K2 D5), and hidden_sys hashes identically on every task so it caches once fleet-wide. The middle column is one prefill task’s cache: a flat-hash index (no radix tree — linear chat has no forks), retention by reuse-recency LRU + ref_count (DC4 (a′); no hard pin — hidden_sys stays resident because it is re-hit on ~100% of requests, not because it is excluded from the candidate set), and a watermark background-thread evictor on the released (ref_count == 0) LRU (Least-Recently-Used) tail. The right column is the Router state this needs — just a single-flight dedup that stops a thundering herd of identical cold prefills; the prior pin control-plane (PinUserSys / the per-user active-task map) is removed (Change 5). The long-tail escape hatch: the residency window τ_resident ≈ 60 s is over the local HBM+DRAM hierarchy (not HBM alone) and comfortably exceeds the ~30 s think-gap, so the overwhelming majority of returning turns get a local hit (HBM, or DRAM at βDRAM) — the §9b.4 result. Only the small tail beyond τ_resident (which overlaps the cold-start ~20%) falls to the Iter 3b pool, which pulls the ~400-tok assistant delta (~128 MB), not a 320 MB user_sys. No pin needed.

11.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Enabling priors. Iter 1a’s tiered storage (HBM / DRAM; local SSD retired at Iter 3b / DC7 → cold prefixes live in the Iter 3b pool) is the place cached prefixes live; Iter 3’s PagedAttention made block-granular residency cheap (no contiguous-slab over-allocation); and Iter 4’s chunked prefill is what lets prefixes match at chunk granularity. Without chunking, prefix matching is all-or-nothing per request — a single differing token anywhere voids the whole match; with chunking, a request reuses every chunk-aligned prefix block it shares and only prefills the unmatched tail. All three are necessary; bundling this into Iter 1a would have made that iteration un-defensible (the cache-key correctness debate, the privacy debate, and the eviction-policy debate each need their own airtime).
  • The constraint that flipped — B5 is the single largest remaining prefill $. Every request re-prefills the 4 K-token hidden_sys from scratch: βcold(4 K) ≈ 190 ms (§4.1) of GPU time on byte-for-byte identical work, paid λpeak = 100 K times per second → 190 ms × 100 K = 19 000 GPU-seconds/s burned fleet-wide on one shared string — and that’s before counting the shared user_sys and conversation-history prefixes. Iter 1a’s cache was keyed per conv_id (it fixed B1); it does not match across conv_ids on a shared prefix. B5 is exactly that cross-conv_id reuse.
  • Why “bulk of the prefill saving”. Mean unmatched tokens fall from Tin_mean = 13.3 K to ~1.85 K (§11.6 per-component derivation) → fleet prefill tok/s falls ~86% → prefill_task_num shrinks ~7× (19 909 → ~2 767). That is the largest single reduction in the prefill line on the whole page. Honest scope: it shrinks the prefill fleet, not the decode fleet — decode still dominates total $/mo until the later decode-side iterations attack it, so the fleet total moves from ~$20.62 B/mo to ~$19.41 B/mo (the prefill line itself drops ~$1.21 B/mo).
  • “Why not just hash by request content (no chain)?” (DC2 (a)) — identical tokens at different positions in two prefixes would collide and a block from prefix X could be wrongly served for prefix Y. Block-position semantics matter; the parent_hash chain encodes them. Fails K2 D5.
  • “Why content-only chaining — and why NOT per-user-seed it?” This is B2C: tenant_id is an end user (§1.3), not an enterprise. Chaining already buys two of three requirements outright — correctness (K2 D5) and isolation of all deep content (a block hits only if every preceding block matches, so probing a message buried in another user’s history needs that user’s entire prior chain reproduced byte-for-byte). The one residual K2 D52 timing side-channel is at the front of the chain (user_sys / the first message after the public hidden_sys), where the prefix is guessable, so a user could replay a guess and detect a cache hit via TTFT. Per-user seeding (DC2 (c)) would close that narrow probe at ~0 hit-rate cost — but we deliberately do not seed: the exposure is front-of-chain only, seeding forgoes the (small) cross-user reuse of identical front-of-chain content, and for a consumer chat product that residual is judged acceptable. The seed stays documented as the hardening lever if the threat model tightens.
  • “Why not a radix tree (SGLang-style)?” (DC1 (b)) — a radix tree’s win is fork handling (branching conversations sharing a common prefix prefix-tree node). §1.1 declares linear chat only; the fork-handling machinery is dead weight. A vLLM-style flat hash on chained block hashes wins on simplicity under this workload.
  • Why not also resize decode here? — prefix caching is a prefill-side mechanism (it deletes redundant prefill work). Decode generates genuinely new tokens, so it cannot be cached; its $ is attacked by continuous batching in a later iteration. One bottleneck per iteration.

11.3  Design choices

DC1 — Index structure (how a request finds a reusable prefix)

Problem: given a request’s chained block hashes, the prefill task must look up — in the allocation hot path — which of those blocks already have computed KV resident. The structure must be fast (per-tick, on every admit) and must match the conversation shape this workload actually has.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • SGLang-style radix tree (prefix tree over token blocks; nodes shared across branches) — rejected: its distinguishing capability is fork handling — two conversations that share a prefix then diverge reuse the shared prefix-tree node. §1.1 is linear chat (no branching), so every “tree” is a single path; the tree’s split/merge bookkeeping is pure overhead with no reuse it unlocks.
  • vLLM-style flat hash on chained block hashes — CHOSEN: a flat block_hash → block_id hashmap. O(1) lookup per block, no tree maintenance, trivially shareable. Under linear chat it captures all the available reuse (the only prefixes that recur are linear ones) at a fraction of the radix tree’s complexity. [G — K2 D5]

Why flat hash wins:

  • Tree’s edge is unused. The radix tree’s only advantage is fork reuse — an operation this workload never performs.
  • Same reuse, cheaper. Stripped of forks, both expose the same reuse; flat hash does it with O(1) lookups and no tree rebalancing.
  • Workload-determined. Not a general “hash beats tree” claim — a branching-agent workload would flip it.

DC2 — Block-hash chain construction (the cache key — correctness, reuse, privacy)

Problem: the cache key (block_hash) must (1) uniquely identify the prefix that produced a block’s KV — correctness; (2) let hidden_sys (identical for every user) cache once fleet-wide — reuse; (3) not leak one user’s content to another — privacy. The third is a deliberate trade in a B2C setting where tenant_id is an end user, not an enterprise.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • (a) Per-block content hash — hash(tokens_i): collides on identical tokens at different positions (block from prefix X served for prefix Y). Fails requirement (1). Rejected.
  • (b) Chained content-only hash — H_block_i = hash(parent_hash ‖ tokens_i) — CHOSEN: position-aware (the parent_hash binds each block to its full prefix → satisfies (1), closing K2 D5) and shares identical content fleet-wide including hidden_sys (satisfies (2)). Leaves a narrow, deliberately accepted front-of-chain K2 D52 timing side-channel (see Why).
  • (c) Two-class per-user-seeded chain — considered, NOT shipped; kept as the hardening lever: flip seed = tenant_id on at the user_sys boundary while hidden_sys stays unseeded/public. Closes the front-of-chain probe (two users with an identical user_sys hash differently) at ~0 hit-rate cost. Not shipped because chaining alone already isolates deep content and the residual front-of-chain exposure is acceptable for consumer chat; revisit if users routinely place secrets in user_sys. One line to enable.
    # tenant_id = the end user (§1.3: B2C). Two classes only: platform-public vs user-private. seed(class) = NULL if class is platform-public (hidden_sys) tenant_id if class is user-private (user_sys, conv_history, current turn) H_block_i = hash( seed(class_of_block_i) ‖ parent_hash ‖ tokens_i )
  • (d) Naive per-user-seed across all blocks (including hidden_sys) — rejected hardest: kills hidden_sys sharing → DAU × Thidden_sys × KV_per_tok of redundant fleet-wide work for content with zero confidentiality (the same prompt every user sees). Breaks requirement (2).

Why (b) — two requirements are hard, the third is a deliberate trade:

  • Correctness. Chaining via parent_hash makes block_hash uniquely identify the prefix; unchained (a) breaks it.
  • Reuse. Content-only hashing lets hidden_sys cache once fleet-wide; all-block seeding (d) breaks it.
  • Privacy (the trade). Chaining already isolates all deep content (a probe needs the victim’s entire prior chain reproduced); the only gap is front-of-chain (user_sys / first message), which seeding (c) would close but which we accept — the exposure is narrow and seeding forgoes the small cross-user reuse of identical front-of-chain content.
  • Not enterprise multi-tenancy. The live question is per-user front-of-chain privacy; the answer is “chaining is sufficient; accept the front-of-chain probe; keep (c) as the documented hardening lever.” [G — K2 D5 (correctness) + K2 D52 (timing side-channel evaluated and accepted)]

Implication for hidden_sys storage: the per-task hidden_sys pin (1.3 GB × prefill_task_num, §11.5) works precisely because content-only hashing makes every task compute the same block_hash chain for the identical public hidden_sys and pin the same blocks — automatic now that nothing is seeded. The rejected all-block seeding (d) would have degenerated the per-task pin into per-(user, task) and multiplied its cost by DAU.

DC3 — Eviction when (the allocation-path cost of making room)

Problem: HBM is bounded; admitting a new block’s KV may require freeing an old one. When that freeing runs determines whether the allocation tick blocks on it.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • (a) On-demand — free synchronously inside the allocation tick: ~100 µs–1 ms of blocking work injected into the hot path on every eviction, exactly when the system is under memory pressure (worst time to add latency).
  • (b) Watermark (background thread; tunable (low, high)) — CHOSEN: a background evictor wakes when free HBM crosses low, reclaims the non-pinned LRU tail down to high, and sleeps. Allocation stays O(1) (it never frees inline). Established precedent — Linux kswapd, Memcached LRU crawler, Redis maxmemory background eviction.

Why watermark wins:

  • Off the hot path. Eviction never runs inside the latency-critical allocation tick.
  • Absorbs bursts. The (low, high) band reclaims ahead of demand, not at the moment of starvation.
  • On-demand loses. Its only merit is simplicity, paid for with hot-path jitter precisely under load.

[param] HBM retention policy: → reuse-recency LRU + ref_count (no hard pin) — Iter 5 (DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5; reversed from a 3-source hard pin-list). Why: a hard pin needs an oracle for the hot set and can starve the cache; ref_count gives correctness for free and reuse-recency keeps a prefix resident because it is re-hit, self-tuning to a rotating hot set (eviction = a recoverable pool fetch, not OOM). W-TinyLFU / soft-priority = the documented upgrade.

DC4 — Eviction which (the replacement policy on the non-pinned tail)

Problem: once the background evictor runs, which non-pinned blocks does it drop? The policy must survive the workload’s signature hazard — scanned-once cold-prompt floods (a brand-new conversation’s blocks, seen once, never reused) that would evict genuinely hot warm prefixes under a naive recency rule.

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • (b) LFU (Least-Frequently-Used; frequency only) — new hot prefixes can’t displace stale aged-in ones (frequency count cold-start problem).
  • (c) LRU-K (sliding-window blend of recency + frequency) — works, but tuning K is per-workload tuning we’d rather not own.
  • (d) W-TinyLFU (Window TinyLFU; Caffeine-style admission filter + SLRU main) — near-optimal on mixed workloads with low metadata overhead; the production default in general-purpose caches. Its admission filter is exactly the “reject scan-once floods” job. The documented scan-resistance upgrade (Change 5, Q5.3): promote off the main line only if the measured f_evict / pool-QPS proves too high; a TensorRT-LLM-style soft retention priority is the equivalent.
  • (e) ARC (Adaptive Replacement Cache; IBM; balances recency/frequency via T1/T2 ghost lists) — strong general policy, more metadata + patent history; the pool-backstop covers scan-once more cheaply.
  • (f) 2Q (two-queue: A1in for scan-once, Am for reused) — simpler than ARC, same idea; the pool-backstop already catches the scan-once tail.
  • (g) Pin-list + LRU on the non-pinned tail — REJECTED (was the prior choice; reversed by DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5): a hard pin removes a block from the eviction candidate set entirely — never a victim, no matter how stale. Production-grade serving (vLLM Automatic Prefix Caching (APC) — content-hashed, ref_count-kept blocks that, once freed, sit on an LRU list and are reused if re-hit before eviction; SGLang RadixAttention) does not do this; the closest analog is TensorRT-LLM’s soft retention priority. Why not: (1) redundant where it’s safe — for hidden_sys the §11.5 arithmetic shows ref_count + reuse-recency already keep it resident, so the pin buys zero residency benefit (its one cold-start gain is delivered by warmup-prefill, not the pin); (2) a priority-inversion where it bites — a hard user_sys pin removes an idle user’s block from the candidate set, forcing the evictor to drop a live conv’s about-to-be-reused conv_hist tail instead; (3) it needs an oracle for a hot set that rotates through the day (reuse-recency measures it instead), plus a Router pin control-plane, a fleet pin-budget, and an HBM-crisis guard against pinned overshoot. (Full redundancy/inversion calc: §11.5 + DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md §5.3/§5.3a.)
  • (a′) APC + reuse-recency LRU + ref_count; scan-resistance via the Iter 3b pool-backstop — CHOSEN: ref_count gives correctness for free (in-use blocks never evicted); reuse-recency orders the released (ref_count == 0) tail by measured re-hit, so the hot set is self-tuning to a rotating population and an eviction is a recoverable ~13 ms pool fetch, never an OOM. Plain LRU’s one weakness — a scan-once cold-prompt flood can evict an idle user_sys — is caught by the already-existing Iter 3b pool (the f_evict cost, §11.5). This is what vLLM ships.

Why (a′) wins:

  • The hot set is measured, not asserted. A pin freezes an operator’s belief about what’s hot; reuse-recency keeps a block resident exactly while it is actually re-hit and drops it the moment it stops — self-correcting as the hot user set rotates through the day.
  • Eviction is recoverable, not fatal. A hard pin removes blocks from the candidate set, so a mis-pinned block can starve the cache (the OOM the §11.7 guard exists for). Reuse-recency’s worst case is a useful block re-fetched from the pool — latency, never an allocation failure.
  • ref_count does the only load-bearing pin. “Don’t evict a block a live sequence points at” is correctness, enforced deterministically by ref_count; reuse-recency then orders the released tail by demand. Two mechanisms cover both jobs with one counter and one ordered list — no Router protocol, no budget, no guard cap. vLLM/SGLang ship exactly these two and skip the hard pin.
  • Documented upgrade path. If the measured f_evict / pool-QPS ever proves too high, (d) W-TinyLFU (or a soft retention priority) restores frequency-aware scan-resistance without a hard pin.
“Why not just make the global policy LFU?” LFU is an estimator that infers the hot set from frequency counts — and a single global LFU applies the wrong signal to the bulk of the cache:
  • Wrong signal for ~90% of HBM. The evictor runs on the HBM tier. Of the ~450 GB usable HBM/task, the shared-prefix class is small (hidden_sys 1.31 GB + the reuse-warm user_sys slice); the bulk is HBM-hot session-tail conv_hist. Session-tail wants recency — a dead conv that ran many turns (high count) must not outrank a fresh active conv (low count) — and global LFU inverts exactly that. Reuse-recency LRU is correct for ~90% of the bytes; LFU is wrong for them.
  • Reactive, not proactive (warm-up cost). LFU keeps a block only after enough accesses build a count. A boot-time warmup-prefill of hidden_sys makes it resident at boot — a freshly-autoscaled task serves request #1 with a local hit instead of paying βcold(4 K) ≈ 190 ms while LFU warms up — and ref_count + reuse-recency then hold it (re-hit on ~100% of requests). For user_sys, CH-on-conv_id stickiness lands a returning user back on the task that already cached them, so reuse-recency keeps it warm without waiting for an LFU count to build.
  • Aging / cold-start (the DC4(b) reason) — and LRU-tiebreak does not fix it. The hot user set rotates through the day: yesterday’s heavy user keeps a stale high count that plain LFU won’t drop, blocking today’s fresh heavy user (low count). LFU-with-LRU-tiebreak only resolves equal-count ties; aging is a different-count problem (stale-high vs fresh-low) the tiebreak never touches. Reuse-recency LRU has no such failure — a block’s position is its last re-hit, so a no-longer-hot user ages out for free. A decaying LFU (W-TinyLFU, (d)) also fixes aging — the documented scan-resistance upgrade.
So it is not “LFU is bad.” A recency signal is correct for the volume-dominant session-tail class, and reuse-recency keeps the shared-prefix class warm because it is re-hit (no estimator needed). Why now (Iter 5): the shared-prefix class — one block_hash shared across many convs (ref_count ≫ 1) — does not exist before Iter 5; only content-addressed prefix caching (DC2) creates it. Pre-Iter-5 every cached block is private session-tail KV, served correctly by the plain LRU of §6.4 DC5. (A hard pin on the shared-prefix class was the prior design; DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5 reversed it to reuse-recency — see DC4 (g) above.)

DC5 — Eviction cost: copy-down vs clean-drop (the move content-addressing unlocks)

Problem: DC3 set when the evictor runs and DC4 set which non-pinned block it picks — neither said what the eviction costs. Pre-Iter-5, freeing an HBM block always meant copying it down to the tier below (PCIe to DRAM, RDMA to the pool) even when an identical copy was already sitting there. Can we ever free the HBM slot with no copy at all?

Possible solutions enumerated:

  • (a) Always copy-down — the only safe option before a content hash exists: a tier holds a single canonical copy, so you cannot prove the lower tier’s bytes match this block → you must re-ship them. Wastes PCIe / RDMA bandwidth re-writing bytes already present below.
  • (b) Clean-drop when a clean copy provably exists below — CHOSEN (Move B2) — Iter 5’s content-addressed block_hash makes “is the copy below byte-identical?” a free lookup: same hash ⇒ same bytes. Track residency in a per-block tier_locs atomic bitmap (HBM | DRAM | REMOTE); dirty / clean are derived (clean = some lower tier already holds this block_hash). On eviction: a clean block is freed with no copy-down (an O(1) free-list push, zero bytes moved); only a dirty block (no lower copy yet) pays a write — and that write is itself the Iter 3b length gate (write down iff recompute_cost(N) > transfer_cost(N), else discard to the recompute floor).

Why clean-drop can only land now:

  • It needs a provable identity. Clean-drop is safe only if the lower copy is guaranteed identical. The content-only block_hash chain (DC2) is exactly that proof — impossible at Iter 1a/3, where each tier swapped a single canonical copy with no way to assert equality. So Move B2 is a capability of the content-addressed model; stating it any earlier would be a forward-reference error.
  • It turns the common eviction into a metadata op. A hot shared-prefix block (e.g. a hidden_sys block) is pinned and already resident below; when a surplus copy is evicted, clean-drop frees it for the cost of a bitmap check — no 5.12 MB re-ship. The full coldest-first decision tree (pinned / ref_count → coldness gate → is_clean() clean-drop → dirty: length-gate write-down vs discard) is walked end-to-end in cache_eviction_policy.html §7.
Block taxonomy (Move E) — naming what the policy already separates. Two block classes, one mechanism. Shared-prefix blocks (hidden_sys, hot user_sys — many convs point at them, ref_count ≫ 1) are kept resident because they are re-hit constantly — reuse-recency rides them to the LRU front and ref_count holds the in-use ones (§11.4), no hard pin, no new policy. Session-tail blocks (one conv’s private history) are kept by recency — same reuse-recency LRU tail (DC4 (a′)). Reuse-recency + ref_count serve both classes; Iter 5 just names the split. (Ordering session-tail eviction by a learned P(continue) — or a soft retention-priority for the shared-prefix class — instead of raw recency is the §S2 sidecar, not the main line.)

11.4  Hot-key handling — retention by reuse-recency, not a hard pin

“Hot key” here is a hot prefix (a block_hash many requests want), not a hot conv_id (that was Iter 1b’s BLCH spill). Reversed (DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5): the prior design modeled retention as a three-source hard pin-list; the reversal keeps hot prefixes resident with content-addressed APC + reuse-recency LRU + ref_count (the vLLM / SGLang pattern), with the Iter 3b pool as the backstop on eviction. An explicit pin survives only as an optional soft retention-priority hint.

“Resident” = re-hit before the LRU tail reaches it — not hard-excluded from the evictor. The eviction-relevant recency is cache-management recency (the last_access the evictor orders by), and the commitment that makes this work (Change 5 §5.3) is: the evictor keys last_access on a prefix-cache hit / reuse at prefill admission (and on ref_count → 0 free-recency), not on per-token kernel reads (vLLM free-queue-reappend semantics). Under that key:
  • hidden_sys is re-hit on ~100% of requests, so every reuse refreshes it to the LRU front — it is the single most-reused string on the platform, so for it to be evicted every other resident block would have to be more recently reused than it: impossible. It is warm because it is re-hit, not because it is pinned. (The earlier “read-once-at-prefill ⇒ LRU would pick it” framing conflated kernel-read recency with cache-management recency — the evictor keys on the latter.)
  • hot user_sys goes idle for the ~30 s think-gap (ref_count == 0); it survives iff re-hit within the reuse-recency residency window τ_resident (§11.5). For heavy users CH-on-conv_id stickiness lands them back fast (gap ≪ τ_resident) → they stay warm for free. The tail that ages past τ_resident falls to the Iter 3b pool (the f_evict cost).
ref_count (Iter 1a/2) remains the only load-bearing pin — the correctness pin that protects a block while a live sequence points at it. The popularity “pin” is gone: reuse-recency keeps a block resident across the idle gap only if it is actually re-hit, which is the measured truth rather than an asserted one.

Detection — which prefix is hot? (a signal, not a pin)

  • (a) Per-task wait-time spike inference — tells you a task is hot but not which prefix caused it; too coarse.
  • (b) Per-block-hash access counter (local LFU / LRU-K sliding window) — CHOSEN as a signal — identifies the specific hot block_hash directly; cheap (one counter per resident block). It now feeds the optional soft-priority / W-TinyLFU frequency signal, not a never-evict pin — a genuinely hot block stays warm by reuse-recency for the same reason hidden_sys does.
  • (c) Static pre-declared pin-list (operator config) — brittle; right only for the known block (hidden_sys) and blind to emergent ones.

Retention — how each class stays resident (the reversed main line)

  • ref_count correctness pin (KEEP) — a block with a live sequence pointing at it is never a victim. Production-standard; the only load-bearing pin.
  • hidden_sys (was the fleet-static hard pin) — held resident by reuse-recency (re-hit ~100% → LRU front) + ref_count (an in-flight prefill almost always references it) + a boot-time warmup-prefill that closes the single cold-start miss on a freshly-autoscaled task. No never-evict semantics needed (1.3 GB, §11.5).
  • user_sys (was the Router PinUserSys top-N pin) — mid-turn held by ref_count; between turns kept warm by CH-on-conv_id stickiness + reuse-recency for the heavy users who return inside τ_resident; the evicted tail falls back to the already-existing Iter 3b pooled KV store (the f_evict × ~13 ms cost, §11.5). Miss-path savings split by case:
    RDMA_fetch(N) = N × KV_per_tok / RDMA_BW (per §4.3); β_cold(N) = prefill_time(N) (per §4.1) New-user (no conv_hist yet; prompt = hidden_sys[resident] + user_sys + user_msg): without pool: β_cold(T_user_sys_mean + T_user_msg_mean) = β_cold(1 224) ≈ 54 ms with pool: RDMA_fetch(T_user_sys_mean) + β_cold(T_user_msg_mean) = (1 024 × 320 KB / 25 GB/s) + β_cold(200) ≈ 13 ms + 9 ms = 22 ms → dominant savings Spill-target / autoscale-fresh (existing user; target task misses the conv): without pool: β_cold(T_user_sys_mean + T_conv_hist + T_user_msg_mean) with pool: RDMA_fetch(T_user_sys_mean) + β_cold(T_conv_hist + T_user_msg_mean) → pool saves only the user_sys segment; conv_hist still cold on the spill target
    The Iter 3b pool fallback is load-bearing, not optional, at consumer scale — it is what keeps the ~2.6 M-class peak-active tail (§11.5) off the full cold path.
  • Local emergent hot blocks (was the local hard pin) — the per-block counter survives as the soft-priority signal above; a hot block stays warm by reuse-recency.
  • Hard pin (any of the three former sources) as the primary mechanism — rejected (DC4 (g)): redundant where safe, priority-inverting where it bites, and it imports a control-plane + budget + OOM guard.
