Fleet-wide runtime pin on every task stays rejected a fortiori (DAU × Tuser_sys_mean × KV_per_tok × prefill_task_num, infeasible).

11.5  Placement & residency topology — where hidden_sys lives, and what reuse-recency keeps resident

Re-derivation, not a re-label (DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5 / Q5.4). Under reuse-recency LRU there is no fleet_pinnable_HBM denominator — the old 2 767 × budget pinnable-HBM term was the pin and has no meaning without one. The user_sys working-set numerator survives (the 0.96 PB below); the tail-to-pool figure is re-derived as an effective-HBM-residency question: the reuse-recency window τ_resident, the eviction fraction f_evict, and the pool QPS that fraction adds. The two old pin-placement tables (budget → gap → Npinned → tail) are deleted, not relabeled.

hidden_sys (1.3 GB total KV per TP=8 group; 4 K tokens) — per-prefill-task residency

After Iter 2’s P/D split, hidden_sys is consumed at admit time on serving-prefill-job tasks, so it is held resident per prefill task — not by a hard pin, but by reuse-recency + ref_count + boot warmup (below). 4 096 tok × 320 KB/tok = 1.31 GB [D — §4.2].

Why hidden_sys is never the LRU victim — the redundancy calc (Change 5 §5.3) [D]. It is re-hit on ~100% of requests, so reuse-recency refreshes its LRU position at the per-task prefill rate (λpeak / prefill_task_num ≈ 100 K / 2 767 ≈ 36 req/s); for it to be evicted, every other resident block on the task would have to be more recently reused than the single most-reused string on the platform — impossible. ref_count nearly pins it on its own (an in-flight prefill almost always references it). The only gap a static pin closed — request #1 on a freshly-autoscaled task paying βcold(4 K) ≈ 190 ms before it is cached — is closed by a boot-time warmup-prefill (one prefill at boot per (B, model_rev)), which needs no never-evict semantics. So: two mechanisms (ref_count + reuse-recency) hold it in steady state, warmup closes the cold edge, and the hard pin buys nothing.
Within a task, residency spans all 8 GPUs, not one. The 1.31 GB is the TP-group total. TP=8 shards the KV cache by KV head, and with n_kv_heads = 8 that is exactly one KV head per GPU — so each GPU holds 320 KB / 8 = 40 KB/tok, and hidden_sys occupies 1.31 GB / 8 ≈ 164 MB (= 4 096 tok × 40 KB) on each of the 8 GPUs. No single GPU holds a complete block — a 7/8-present block is unusable (attention needs every KV head) — so the block manager allocates and evicts a block as one atomic unit across all 8 shards; “resident on one of 8” is not a valid state. Orthogonal to this within-task sharding is the fleet axis: hidden_sys is held on every prefill task (each computes the same content-only block_hash and re-hits it); a user’s user_sys is reuse-warm only on the prefill tasks CH-on-conv_id actually routes that user to.
GranularityStorage costFetch latencyvs re-prefill (190 ms)
Per-prefill-task ✔prefill_task_num × 1.3 GB ≈ 2 767 × 1.3 GB ≈ 3.6 TB fleet (≪ 1% of fleet HBM — 2 767 × 640 GB ≈ 1.77 PB)0 (local hit)wins by 190 ms
Per-rackn_racks × 1.3 GB (trivial)52 ms RDMA (1.3 GB / 25 GB/s)wins 138 ms over re-prefill; loses 52 ms vs per-task
Per-DC / per-metrotrivial~52 ms (transfer-dominated)similar to per-rack

Per-task wins decisively: at 3.6 TB the fleet-wide storage is rounding error against ~1.77 PB of fleet HBM, and it buys a 0-latency local hit on the single most-reused string on the platform. Content-only hashing (DC2 (b)) is what makes the per-task copies identical — every task derives the same block_hash for the same public bytes, and re-hits it on essentially every request (which is exactly why reuse-recency keeps it without a pin).

user_sys — the working set, and what reuse-recency can keep resident

At consumer scale the full active-user user_sys working set is far larger than HBM. The numerator (how much user_sys KV exists) is solid — it is the same number whether or not there is a pin:

users_with_active_conv ≈ active_concurrent_sessions_peak = 3 M [§1.3] avg_M_T per active user ≈ 1 (most users ≤ 1 concurrent conv → 1 task) [§1.3] user_sys KV per user = T_user_sys_mean × KV_per_tok = 1 024 × 320 KB ≈ 320 MB [D] user_sys_working_set = 3 M × 1 × 320 MB ≈ 0.96 PB (SOLID — numerator survives the reversal) [D]
What can stay HBM-resident — the reuse-recency window, step by step
  1. Each prefill task owns ~1 084 active convs, whose working set tiers across the local hierarchy — it does not cram into HBM. CH-on-conv_id spreads the 3 M active convs across the (post-prefix-cache) prefill_task_num: 3 M / 2 767 ≈ 1 084 convs / task [D]. Per conv, conv_hist KV = 8 K × 320 KB ≈ 2.56 GB; across 1 084 convs that is ~2.78 TB / task [D] — ~6× the 450 GB usable HBM, but that 6× is exactly what the multi-tier hierarchy absorbs (§8 warm-hit residency / §9b.4): the hottest blocks in HBM, the warm remainder in local DRAM (~800 GB), the Iter 3b pool below. So yes something is always being evicted from HBM — but it lands one tier down in local DRAM and is a βDRAM hit on return, not a pool fetch.
  2. The reuse-recency residency window τ_resident — over the local HBM+DRAM cache, not HBM alone. A released (ref_count == 0) block falls all the way out of local residency only when the eviction frontier has swept the whole local cache. With it always full, the frontier moves at the new-block admission rate:
    admission_rate = (λpeak / prefill_task_num) × unmatched_tokens(r) × KV_per_tok = 36 req/s × 1.85 K tok × 320 KB ≈ ~21 GB/s of new KV per task [D] C_local ≈ usable_HBM + usable_DRAM ≈ 450 + 800 ≈ ~1.25 TB (the LOCAL residency cache, HBM→DRAM; local NVMe retired at Iter 3b/DC7; §9b.4) [D] τ_resident = C_local / admission_rate ≈ 1.25 TB / 21 GB/s ≈ ~60 s [D]
    A block re-hit within ~60 s is a local hit (HBM, or DRAM at βDRAM); only one not touched for ~60 s falls to the pool. (Using HBM alone — 449 GB → ~21 s — was the prior, too-tight reading; it ignored the local DRAM tier the design already commits to. DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 6 §6.5.)
  3. τ_resident (~60 s) comfortably exceeds the ~30 s think-gap — so the overwhelming majority of returning turns get a local hit (HBM, or DRAM at βDRAM), exactly the §9b.4 result (“land back on the same task and pay βDRAM instead of βcold in the overwhelming majority of cases”). The user_sys vs conv_hist reuse-over-time asymmetry still holds — user_sys barely changes, so it is valuable across every future session, while conv_hist decays after the conv’s ~150 s lifetime — and the reversal lets reuse-recency measure that instead of a pin asserting it. (Promoting that asymmetry into an explicit bias — a W-TinyLFU tail or a soft retention-priority — is the documented upgrade, DC4 (d)/(b), if the measured f_evict ever proves too high.)
The eviction fraction and the pool QPS it adds — the honest trade
f_evict(user) = P( inter_turn_gap(user) > τ_resident ) [D — given a gap distribution; G-FA input] τ_resident ≈ 60 s ⋙ ~30 s gap → MOST returns are LOCAL hits (HBM / DRAM) → f_evict SMALL the tail (gap > τ_resident) OVERLAPS the cold-start ~20% the workload already counts added_pool_QPS = f_evictrequest-weighted × warm_turn_QPS warm_turn_QPS = λpeak × warm_fraction = 100 K × 0.80 = 80 K req/s pool FETCH on the tail = the ~400-tok ASSISTANT DELTA (T_out_mean × KV_per_tok ≈ 128 MB; the B12 job §9b.4 sizes), NOT the 320 MB user_sys — user_sys front-of-chain is re-prefilled (1 K tok) or still local
Notation — the residency derivation, symbol by symbol. Read the chain as one sentence: user_sys exists in bulk; a block stays warm for τ_resident; if a user’s think-gap beats that window they pay a pool fetch; the fraction who do is f_evict, and f_evict × warm_turn_QPS is the bill.
  • user_sys_working_set (≈ 0.96 PB) — total user_sys KV in flight (users_with_active_conv × Tuser_sys_mean × KV_per_tok). Signifies: how much wants to be warm — the “numerator,” the same with or without a pin.
  • KV_per_tok (320 KB) — KV-cache bytes per token (all layers, this model at TP=8). Signifies: why one user_sys = 1 024 × 320 KB = 320 MB and why a fetch is heavy.
  • C_local (≈ 1.25 TB) — usable_HBM + usable_DRAM (~450 + ~800 GB), the local residency cache (HBM→DRAM; local NVMe retired at Iter 3b). Signifies: the size of the cache the eviction frontier must sweep before a block falls to the remote pool — an HBM eviction is a DRAM hit on return, still local.
  • admission_rate (≈ 21 GB/s) — new KV written per task per second ((λpeak/prefill_task_num) × unmatched_tokens(r) × KV_per_tok). Signifies: the speed the LRU eviction frontier moves — each prefilled token allocates a block and pushes the tail out.
  • τ_resident (≈ 60 s) — C_local / admission_rate, the reuse-recency residency window over HBM+DRAM. Signifies: how long an idle (ref_count==0) block stays locally resident before falling to the pool. Re-hit within τ_resident ⇒ local hit (HBM/DRAM); only past it ⇒ pool. ~60 s ⋙ the ~30 s gap, so most returns are local.
  • inter_turn_gap(user) (~30 s mean, G-FA) — a user’s think-time between turns. Signifies: how long their user_sys sits idle, aging toward the frontier.
  • f_evict(user) = P(inter_turn_gap > τ_resident) — per-user probability the return is “too late.” Signifies: the knob that turns the residency window into a cost — the chance a warm turn finds user_sys gone and must pool-fetch it.
  • f_evictrequest-weighted — f_evict averaged over requests, not users. Signifies: the value that sizes pool QPS (heavy users count more — they send more requests).
  • f_evicttop-N — f_evict for the formerly-pinned heaviest cohort. Signifies: the net new cost of dropping the pin — those users used to get a 0 ms pinned hit, now a f_evicttop-N × ~13 ms pool fetch.
  • warm_fraction (0.80) & warm_turn_QPS (80 K/s = λpeak × warm_fraction) — the share / rate of warm follow-up turns. Signifies: only warm turns can suffer a user_sys eviction (cold turns re-prefill everything anyway).
  • added_pool_QPS = f_evictrequest-weighted × warm_turn_QPS — the extra load on the store. Signifies: the number that decides whether the pool is big enough. The fetch on the tail is the ~400-tok assistant delta (RDMA_fetch(Tout_mean) ≈ 128 MB / 25 GB/s, the B12 job §9b.4 already sizes), not the 320 MB user_sys (which is re-prefilled or still local).
DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 6 (§6.5, RESOLVED): the residency is the local HBM+DRAM hierarchy (not HBM alone) and the warm pull is the assistant delta (not user_sys) — both already established at Iter 1a/2/3b. So τ_resident ≈ 60 s ⋙ gap ⇒ f_evict is small and overlaps the cold-start ~20%; the exact value stays G-FA on the gap distribution, re-resolved at S6.

The trade is real, but small — and the pool fetch is the assistant delta, not a user_sys storm (Change 5 §5.3 / Change 6 §6.5). Because the local cache is HBM + DRAM (~1.25 TB, not 449 GB of HBM alone), τ_resident ≈ 60 s exceeds the ~30 s think-gap, so the overwhelming majority of returning turns get a local hit — HBM, or DRAM at βDRAM — exactly the §9b.4 residency result. f_evict (eviction all the way out to the pool) is therefore small and largely overlaps the cold-start ~20% the workload already counts. On that tail the pool serves the ~400-tok assistant delta (the B12 stranded-delta job, ~128 MB, already the basis of the §9b.4 pool sizing), with the user_sys front-of-chain re-prefilled (1 K tok, βcold ≈ 54 ms) or still locally resident — not a 320 MB/fetch storm. Bounded, measurable, never an OOM. The exact f_evict stays G-FA on the inter-turn-gap distribution, re-resolved at S6.

What f_evict controls (a sizing knob, not a correctness knob). The design is correct at any f_evict — an eviction is a recoverable pool fetch of the ~128 MB assistant delta (with user_sys re-prefilled or still local), never an OOM or a wrong answer. f_evict sets only two costs and one decision:
  • Added TTFT on the evicted tail ≈ f_evict × (assistant-delta pull ~5 ms + any user_sys re-prefill ~54 ms), averaged.
  • Added pool QPS ≈ f_evictrequest-weighted × 80 K req/s — what sizes the kv-store-job QPS term (§9b.4 / Iter 5b number chain), against the ~128 MB assistant-delta fetch.
  • The go/no-go on the documented upgrade: if measured f_evict / pool-QPS comes out too high, promote W-TinyLFU on the tail or a soft retention-priority (DC4 (d)/(b)) — without a hard pin.
Change 6 (§6.5) already bounds f_evict small (local residency is HBM+DRAM ⇒ τ_resident ≈ 60 s ⋙ gap, and the pull is the assistant delta the pool already sizes). What stays G-FA to S6 is only the exact value: (1) the inter-turn-gap distribution is an assumption with no measured data; (2) the precise local-DRAM capacity the APC gets (hence the exact τ_resident) is workload-dependent. A later per-user abuse-prevention iteration that strips scripted-client noise from λpeak only lowers admission_rate → lengthens τ_resident → f_evict falls further (the drift is favorable, not adverse). “Tighten at S6” = swap those for measured values; the structure is settled, only the precise number isn’t.
The structural insight — prefix caching squeezes τ_resident, the DRAM tier absorbs it. Pre-prefix-cache there were 19 909 prefill tasks → 3 M / 19 909 ≈ 151 convs / task → the per-task working set was ~7× smaller and τ_resident ~7× longer. Prefix caching shrank the fleet ~7× (19 909 → 2 767), and 151 × 7.2 ≈ 1 084 — the same ~7–8× that re-appears as a residency squeeze. So the two optimizations pull in opposite directions: cheaper-per-request prefill lets you use fewer tasks, but fewer tasks concentrate more convs onto each one and shorten τ_resident. The local HBM+DRAM hierarchy (~1.25 TB) is what absorbs it — even after the ~7× squeeze τ_resident lands ~60 s, still comfortably above the ~30 s gap. The conclusion is invariant: reuse-recency keeps the warm set locally resident, and the Iter 3b pool backstops the small tail — load-bearing, not optional, at consumer scale.

Long-tail handling. A conv that is not locally resident (fell out of HBM+DRAM, gap > τ_resident) or a structural miss (BLCH spill, autoscale-fresh, brand-new user) pays one of: (a) pooled-KV-store fetch of the ~400-tok assistant delta RDMA_fetch(Tout_mean) ≈ 5 ms (the already-existing Iter 3b store, where B12 was retired — load-bearing, not optional); plus, if the front-of-chain user_sys also fell out, (b) a βcold re-prefill of its 1 K tok (~54 ms) — cheaper than a 320 MB pool fetch. Caveat (flagged G-FA): the 60/10 user-skew and the inter-turn-gap distribution move f_evict and the pool QPS, but not the structural conclusion — reuse-recency keeps the warm set locally resident and the pool backstops the rest.

11.6  Number chain

[param] unmatched_tokens(r) (mean prefill work per request): Tin_mean 13.3 K → ~1.85 K — Iter 5. Why: content-addressed prefix caching reuses hidden_sys (~100%) + user_sys (~95%) + warm conv_hist (80% of turns), so the common request prefills only its unmatched tail. Drives the ~86% fleet prefill cut and the ~7× prefill-fleet shrink (19 909 → ~2 767 tasks).

Two derivations: the per-component unmatched-token count (the input to everything), then the fleet reduction it produces.

Step 1 — mean unmatched_tokens(r)

The 0.80 intra-session reuse applies only to conv_hist, not the whole prompt — a naive Tin_mean × (1 − 0.8) ≈ 2.66 K over-shoots by ~50%. But the per-component table below is an accounting device for the fleet mean — read literally it tells a misleading per-token story (“every request matches 80% of its conv_hist tokens”). The honest reading is the per-request warm/cold split underneath it.

ComponentLength (§1.3)Fleet-mean reuseUnmatched tokens (fleet mean)
Thidden_sys4 K [G]~100% (platform-public; fleet-resident via reuse-recency + boot warmup — a genuine prefill-side prefix hit)~0
Tuser_sys_mean1 K [G-FA]~95% (per-user; cross-session prefill-side hit, warmed by recent turns + pool)1 024 × 0.05 ≈ 50
Tconv_hist_median8 K [G-FA]0.80 — NOT a per-token prefill hit; it is a per-request property (warm follow-up vs cold start), see note0.80×0 + 0.20×8 K = 1.6 K (all of it from the 20% cold starts)
Tuser_msg_mean200 [G-FA]0% (new content every turn)200
TotalTin_mean ≈ 13.3 K [D]—unmatched_tokens(r) ≈ 1.85 K [D]
The honest reading: a per-request warm/cold split, and conv_hist reuse is decode-side residency — not a prefill-side prefix hit.
  • Where the prefill-side prefix cache actually delivers. Only hidden_sys (fleet-wide, cross-user) + user_sys (cross-session, per-user) + the cold-path conv_hist reconstruction are genuine prefill-side prefix hits. The “80% conv_hist hit on the prefill side” is the misleading framing.
  • Warm vs cold is a property of the turn, not a cache line. A warm turn is a follow-up in a live multi-turn conversation: the prior turns’ K,V already exists (prefill task’s own one-turn-late re-prefill-and-cache, plus the decode task DⒶ which holds the complete conv incl. its own decoded output), so the turn re-prefills only the genuinely-new user_msg_N ≈ 200 tok. A cold turn is a brand-new conversation (or one whose K,V was evicted/migrated): the full accumulated context (~8.25 K incl. user_sys+msg, median) is reconstructed from scratch. The 0.80 / 0.20 is the fraction of requests that are continuations vs cold starts.
  • Per-request mean (the same 1.85 K, decomposed correctly):
    unmatched_mean = 0.80 (warm) × 200 + 0.20 (cold) × ~8.25 K ≈ 160 + 1 650 = ~1.81 K ≈ the per-component 1.85 K (rounding) [D]
  • The warm × 200 is conditional — it assumes the assistant delta arrives as a store pull (I/O), not a GPU prefill. The prefill task PⒶ never saw the prior assistant output (~400 tok); that K,V lives on DⒶ (decode). For the warm × 200 to hold, that ~400-tok asst_{N−1} delta must reach the cold/rebalanced prefill task as a pull from the Iter 3b pooled cross-task KV store (so it leaves the prefill-FLOP count). In the store-miss / recompute-floor limit the warm term is × 600 (200 new + 400 asst delta), adding 0.80 × 400 = 320 prefill tok/req ≈ +17% prefill FLOPs on warm traffic, and the mean rises to ~2.13 K. In practice the store wins the writeback-vs-next-turn race (interactive inter-turn gap ≈ seconds ⋙ async writeback ≈ ms), so 1.85 K is the expected case — but it is conditional on Option-2 store adoption + a high store-hit rate on the asst delta, not unconditional.

Step 2 — fleet prefill reduction and the prefill resize

Cold (pre-Iter-5): λ_peak × T_in_mean = 100 K × 13.3 K ≈ 1.33 G tok/s [D — §1.3] Post-Iter-5: λ_peak × unmatched(r) = 100 K × 1.85 K ≈ 185 M tok/s [D] Reduction: 1 − 185 M / 1.33 G ≈ 86% [D] prefill_task_num_iter5 = prefill_task_num_iter4 × (185 M / 1.33 G) = 19 909 × 0.139 ≈ 2 767 tasks (~7× shrink) [D] decode_task_num_iter5 = 271 399 tasks (UNCHANGED — prefix caching is prefill-side) [D] total_serving_iter5 = 2 767 + 271 399 ≈ 274 166 tasks [D] $/mo_iter5 = 274 166 × $70 790 ≈ $19.41 B / month [D] → prefill LINE saving = (19 909 − 2 767) × $70 790 ≈ $1.21 B/mo (the headline) → fleet total moves $20.62 B → $19.41 B (only ~6%): DECODE still dominates the total until the later decode-side iterations attack it. Honest scope: Iter 5 is the PREFILL-$ headline, not the fleet-$ headline.

The TTFT effect: the common warm turn-N request now prefills only ~1.85 K unmatched tokens, so β_cold(1.85 K) ≈ 82 ms (linear regime, §4.1) + the per-chunk exposed transfer (~24 ms) ≈ ~106 ms — comfortably under the 400 ms TTFT SLO that Iter 4 alone left at ~972 ms on the cold path. This is the iteration that closes the TTFT SLO for the common case.

11.7  What breaks

  1. Reuse-recency LRU evicts a still-warm block under a scan-once burst. A cold-prompt flood can age an idle conv out of the local HBM+DRAM hierarchy before its owner’s next turn. Impact: that returning turn pays an Iter 3b pool pull of the ~400-tok assistant delta (~5 ms) and/or a ~54 ms re-prefill of the 1 K-tok user_sys front-of-chain, instead of a 0 ms local hit (the f_evict cost, §11.5) — correctness intact, latency only, never an OOM. Mitigation: tune the (low, high) watermarks so reclaim runs ahead of starvation; if the measured f_evict / pool-QPS is too high, promote the per-block frequency counter into the W-TinyLFU tail or a soft retention-priority (DC4 (d)/(b)) — the documented scan-resistance upgrade, not a hard pin.
  2. Block-hash collision. Two different prefixes hash to the same block_hash → wrong KV served. Impact: silent correctness bug if it ever happened. Mitigation: 64-bit+ hash (xxh3-64 / SHA-truncated); collision probability is negligible at fleet block counts (birthday bound ≪ hardware error rates). Optional full-token verify on hit for the paranoid tier.
  3. hidden_sys version churn (the failure a hard pin would have caused). hidden_sys is platform-owned and mutable (a new prompt version, or embedded volatile content like the current date → daily churn). Impact under a content-hash hard pin: the dead old version would be stranded in HBM and the new one not auto-protected until a fleet-wide unpin-old/pin-new deploy ran. Why the reversal removes it: reuse-recency ages the old hash out the moment it stops being hit and warms the new one for free — no deploy step, no stranded HBM (DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md §5.3a #3). The old “HBM crisis on pinned overshoot” failure mode (and its max_pinned_KV_per_task ≤ 50% guard) is moot without a hard pin budget: reuse-recency cannot over-commit HBM — its worst case is an early eviction + pool re-fetch (latency), not an allocation failure.
  4. Thundering-herd cold prefill on a conv’s first turn. Many requests of one new conv_id can arrive before its first prefill completes, each seeing a miss and each launching the same cold prefill. Impact: N× redundant prefill of identical work. Mitigation: the Router’s single_flight[chain_hash] → InFlightPrefill map (200 ms TTL) — the first request registers the in-flight prefill, the rest await its result instead of recomputing.
  5. Front-of-chain timing probe (the accepted K2 D52 residual). A user can replay a guessed user_sys / first message and infer a cache hit from TTFT. Impact: a narrow, front-of-chain-only confidentiality leak (deep content stays isolated by the chain). Mitigation: accepted for B2C chat; the per-user seed (DC2 (c)) is the one-line hardening lever if the threat model tightens.

11.8  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 4)Post-iter (Iter 5)ΔNote
TTFT p99~972 ms (cold path; HoL removed but full 13.3 K still prefilled)~106 ms (common warm turn-N)↓The headline SLO win. Prefix caching shrinks the prefill itself — the common request prefills ~1.85 K unmatched tokens, not 13.3 K. Now under the 400 ms SLO. The rare 256 K-conv tail still relies on chunking (Iter 4) + the intrinsic long-prefill cost (later CP iteration).
TPOT p99~5.2 ms~5.2 ms→Unchanged — prefix caching is prefill-side; decode tick time is HBM-BW-bound. Decode batching lands in a later iteration.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→Unchanged ceiling; caching reduces how much of a long context is re-prefilled (the matched prefix is skipped), not the single-request length ceiling.
Max-output~65 K~65 K→Unchanged — output length is a decode-pool concern.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — per-user admission lands in a later iteration. Iter 5 does introduce the per-user privacy question (front-of-chain probe), evaluated and accepted in DC2.
λpeak supported100 000 req/s (~291 308 tasks)100 000 req/s (~274 166 tasks)↓Same λ on fewer tasks — prefill fleet 19 909 → ~2 767 (~7×). Decode fleet unchanged.
$/mo at λpeak~$20.62 B/mo~$19.41 B/mo↓Prefill line drops ~$1.21 B/mo (the largest single prefill-$ reduction on the page). Fleet total moves only ~6% because decode dominates until its own iterations.

Iter 5 retires B5: a content-only chained block hash (correctness via parent_hash, fleet-wide hidden_sys sharing via no-seed, deep-content isolation for free, front-of-chain probe accepted) plus a flat-hash index, reuse-recency LRU + ref_count retention (no hard pin; DESIGN_CHOICE_CHANGE.md Change 5), and a watermark evictor collapse mean unmatched tokens from 13.3 K to ~1.85 K — an 86% fleet prefill cut that shrinks the prefill fleet ~7× (~$1.21 B/mo) and finally drops common-case TTFT under the 400 ms SLO. It leans on the already-existing Iter 3b store: the user_sys that reuse-recency can’t keep HBM-resident (the f_evict tail + structural misses, ~2.6 M-class) falls back to that pooled KV store (B12 — the cross-turn-conv-K,V stranding — was retired there). At consumer scale that user_sys working-set overflow onto it becomes load-bearing — a later scale event, not new infrastructure. The decode line is untouched here and waits for the later decode-batching and speculative-decode iterations.

12. Iter 6 — Continuous batching on the decode pool + Flash Decoding + CUDA-graphs

Bottleneck addressed: B2 (decode at B=1 is HBM-bandwidth-bound — the per-tick read of the whole weight tensor produces a single token, so Model-Bandwidth-Utilization (MBU) is ~1%, K1 §3.5). Mechanism: decode-only continuous batching at Bdecode — the one weight-tensor read per tick is amortized across Bdecode concurrent sequences — with Flash Decoding (KV-sequence-split attention so a single-token query still saturates the SMs at long context) and CUDA-graphs at a captured-set (kills per-tick launch overhead at the 200 Hz tick). Why here: needs Iter 2 (an isolated decode pool), Iter 3 (PagedAttention freed the HBM headroom Bdecode consumes), and Iter 4 (Flash Attention v2 is the kernel base Flash Decoding extends) — all three are prerequisites. Deferred: decode-pool admission fairness (→ Iter 8 token bucket); the per-request accept-rate $ multiplier (→ Iter 7 spec decode), so the fleet decode_task_num / $ resize is finalized at S6.

Notation introduced in this section — the decode-batching knobs (Flash Decoding split, the batch ceiling, the CUDA-graph set) and the per-tick throughput chain. Consumes the Bmax,paged headroom from §9.4.

Flash Decoding / split_kv
decode attention variant — splits the KV-sequence across SM groups + a log-sum-exp reduce, since at decode Q=1 there is no query dim to tile. split_kv = the chunk count, tuned to context length.
Bdecode
the decode batch = 1 → ~106 — amortizes the once-per-tick weight read across the batch. Ceiling = G_KV / avg_kv_per_active_slot (the §9.4 paged bound) and TPOT-SLO-bounded.
avg_kv_per_active_slot
steady-state continuous-batching per-slot KV (lower than §9’s avg_used_per_slot — short convs turn over fast); the divisor that sets Bdecode.
active_B
the live (instantaneous) batch size each tick — padded up to the nearest captured graph size.
CUDA-graph captured set
pre-recorded kernel-launch graphs at a set of B values (dense head {1,2,4,8} + constant step to Bdecode) — replays a fixed shape, killing the ~5–10%/tick eager-launch tax at 200 Hz; warmup paid once per (B, model_revision).
kv_per_seq_per_GPU
per-sequence KV read per GPU per tick = (Tin_mean+Tout_mean/2)·KVper_tok/8 ≈ 528 MB.
tick(Bdecode)
batched decode tick = βper_decode_tok + Bdecode·kv_per_seq_per_GPU/HBM_BW ≈ 22 ms — this is TPOT (KV reads now dominate).
thr(B) / multiplier / req_throughput
per-task token rate = B/tick(B) (192 → ~4 818 tok/s); multiplier ≈ ~25× (not 106× — the tick grew ~4×); req_throughput ≈ 12 req/s/task.

12.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Continuous batching → Flash Decoding — the Bdecode-wide batch DC2 builds is fed through the decode-specialized attention kernel; without DC1 the single-token query would under-feed the SMs and attention, not weight reads, would cap the batch.
  • ② Flash Decoding → CUDA-graphs — the per-tick kernel sequence (attention + the rest of the decode step) is captured once and replayed, so the 200 Hz tick pays no eager-launch tax.
  • ③ The composed decode step → result — one weight read serving Bdecode tokens, with SMs saturated and launch overhead gone, is the ~25× per-task throughput jump (TPOT still under the 30 ms SLO).

Read the diagram. The top strip is the disease: at B=1 the decode tick is dominated by reading the 17.5 GB-per-GPU weight shard (βper_decode_tok ≈ 5.22 ms), and that read produces exactly one token — arithmetic intensity ~1, MBU ~1%. The cure is to make the same read serve many tokens. Box 1 (continuous batching) is the throughput mechanism — the engine loop admits and retires sequences every tick (not at iteration boundaries), holding Bdecode ≈ 106 active slots, which fits in HBM only because Iter 3 paged the KV out of static-max reservation. Box 2 (Flash Decoding) is what keeps that batch from being gated by attention: a decode query is a single token, so Iter 4’s Q-dim tiling has nothing to tile — Flash Decoding instead splits the KV sequence across SM groups, computes partial softmax numerator/denominator per chunk, and log-sum-exp-reduces them into the exact output. Box 3 (CUDA-graphs) removes the last per-tick tax: at 200 Hz, eager launches cost ~5–10% of the tick, so the step is captured once per (B, model_revision) at a geometric set of batch sizes and replayed, padding to the nearest captured B. The bottom strip is the payoff and the new bound: throughput jumps ~25×, but because the KV read now dominates the tick (16.7 ms vs the 5.22 ms weight read), TPOT rises to ~22 ms — still under the 30 ms SLO, and the new ceiling decode optimizations push against.

12.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Three hard prerequisites are all in hand — not before. Iter 2 gave decode its own pool, isolated from the prefill roofline (you cannot batch decode while it shares a task with compute-bound prefill). Iter 3 PagedAttention is what makes Bdecode fit: static-max reservation would force Bdecode = G_KV / reserved_per_slot ≪ the paged value (the page’s Bmax,paged ≈ 106 vs Bmax,static ≈ 18, §9.4). Iter 4 Flash Attention v2 is the SRAM-tiling kernel Flash Decoding extends. Remove any one and this iteration cannot fire.
  • B2 is the dominant remaining throughput tax. Prefill-$ fell ~7× at Iter 5, so the fleet is now decode-dominated (Iter 5 SLO note). Decode at B=1, MBU ~1%, is the single biggest inefficiency left — and continuous batching is the single largest per-task throughput multiplier of any iteration.
  • Why Flash Decoding lands here, not at Iter 4 with FA v2. Iter 4’s user was prefill, where Q has many tokens and FA v2’s Q-dim tiling parallelizes naturally. At decode Q = 1; Q-parallelism collapses and SMs starve at long context. Flash Decoding adds KV-sequence-dim splitting with a log-sum-exp reduction — same tiling principle, split-axis flipped — and composes with continuous batching because both are decode-pool-only.
  • “Why not batch prefill too?” Prefill is compute-bound: TTFT grows linearly with full-request B for no throughput gain (Iter 2 Why-NOW). Iter 4 already batches prefill at the chunk level, which doesn’t break TTFT — see the decode-only table below.
  • “Why not plain FA v2 for decode and skip Flash Decoding?” It runs, but with Q=1 there is no Q-tile to spread across SMs; long-context decode under-utilizes the GPU and attention — not the weight read — caps the batch. Flash Decoding is load-bearing once decode reaches long context, not a polish item.

12.3  Design choices

[param] split_kv (Flash Decoding KV-split): 1 (FA v2, Q-tiled) → split_kv chunks (KV-seq-split) — Iter 6. Why: at decode Q=1 there is no query dimension to tile, so SMs starve at long context; splitting the KV sequence across SM groups + a log-sum-exp reduce restores occupancy. Cliff: too few splits under-feed the SMs; too many add reduction overhead — tuned to the context-length distribution.

DC1 — Decode attention variant (Q is a single token — the query dimension can’t tile)

Problem: continuous batching gives the decode step many sequences, but each contributes a single-token query. Iter 4’s FA v2 parallelizes attention along the query dimension — with Q=1 there is nothing to tile, so at long context a handful of SMs do all the work while the rest idle, and attention (not the weight read) becomes the bottleneck of the batched step.

  • (a) Flash Attention v2 directly (the Iter 4 kernel) — correct output, but Q-dim parallelism is gone at Q=1; SMs under-utilized at long context, so the Bdecode throughput win is gated by attention rather than weight-tensor reads.
  • (b) Flash Decoding — CHOSEN: split along the KV-sequence dimension into split_kv chunks; compute a partial softmax numerator + denominator on each chunk in parallel across SM groups; log-sum-exp reduce at the end. Same HBM reads and the same SRAM-tiling foundation as FA v2 (so it inherits the O(N) HBM-I/O property), with parallelism restored along the only long axis decode has. K-page: K17.

Why (b): the batched decode step is only as fast as its slowest kernel; with Q=1 that kernel is attention, and only KV-split restores SM occupancy at the long-context tail. FA v2 stays the foundation (Flash Decoding is its decode-specialized variant), so this is an extension, not a replacement.

[param] B (decode batch): 1 → Bdecode ≈ 106 — Iter 6. Why: at B=1 the once-per-tick weight read makes one token (MBU ~1%); a Bdecode-wide continuous batch amortizes that read across the batch. Ceiling = G_KV / avg_kv_per_active_slot ≈ 106 (the §9.4 HBM-capacity bound, fits only because Iter 3 paged the KV); the realized batch is also TPOT-SLO-bounded (KV reads dominate the tick past ~106). Enumeration below.

DC2 — Batching policy (the throughput mechanism)

Problem: the decode tick’s cost is dominated by reading the weight tensor once, regardless of how many tokens it produces. At B=1 that read is ~pure waste (MBU ~1%). How do we amortize it — without re-introducing the per-slot padding waste that made static batching weak?

  • (a) Static padding — pad every slot to a fixed max length; the weight read is amortized, but per-slot padding waste is large and the whole batch waits for the longest sequence to finish before the next admits.
  • (c) Iteration-level batching with length-bucket sort (vLLM-v0 style) — better than static, but PagedAttention already removed the per-slot padding waste that motivated bucketing, so the extra machinery buys little here.
  • (b) Continuous batching at Bdecode — CHOSEN: admit a waiting sequence and retire an EOS sequence every tick, keeping the batch full. Bdecode = G_KV / avg_kv_per_active_slot, where G_KV is the per-task HBM-for-KV budget and the per-slot KV is the continuous-batching steady-state mean (lower than the §8 avg_used_per_slot, since short conversations turn over fast and early-decode slots haven’t grown to Tout_mean/2). Fits only because of Iter 3 paging. K-page: K4.

Why (b): it gives the full weight-read amortization with none of static batching’s padding waste or head-of-line stall, and paging makes the high Bdecode physically fit. The exact Bdecode is settled at S6 against the measured steady-state batch-mean; ~106 (the §9.4 HBM-capacity ceiling) is the design anchor.

[param] CUDA-graph capture set: eager (no graph) → captured set (dense head {1,2,4,8} + constant step to Bdecode) — Iter 6. Why: at 200 Hz, eager launch costs ~5–10%/tick; a captured graph replays a fixed shape, so per tick you pick the smallest captured B ≥ active_B and pad with no-ops. The spacing trades graph count (boot warmup + HBM) vs pad waste — see DC3 below.

DC3 — Kernel launch mode (the captured-set knob)

Problem: at a 200 Hz tick, eager kernel launches cost ~5–10% of the tick budget. CUDA-graphs remove that — but a graph captures a fixed shape, and continuous batching changes B every tick (slots enter and leave). Re-capturing per tick would pay the ~100 ms warmup constantly.

  • (a) Eager launch — no warmup, but the per-tick launch tax is permanent at 200 Hz.
  • (b) CUDA-graph at a single B — kills launch overhead at that one shape, but every B drift forces a ~100 ms re-capture — constant under continuous batching.
  • (c) Captured-set — CHOSEN: capture a graph at each of a set of batch sizes; per tick pick the smallest captured B ≥ active_B and pad unused slots with no-op tokens; warmup is paid once per (B, model_revision) at fleet boot, never on the hot path. K-page: K17. The set is not a constant — it is the sub-knob below.

Sub-knob — the spacing rule (this is the design question; the specific numbers fall out of it). It trades two costs:

  • Graph count — each captured B costs ~100 ms boot warmup plus static HBM for that graph’s captured buffers. Fewer graphs = cheaper.
  • Pad waste — when active_B lands between captured sizes ci and ci+1, the tick replays graph ci+1 and runs ci+1 − active_B no-op slots; worst-case waste at a gap ≈ (ci+1 − ci) / ci+1. Denser spacing = less waste.

Why a set (and which spacing): one graph (option b) re-captures forever; eager (a) taxes every tick. A set captures the launch-overhead win across the whole live active_B range for a bounded, boot-time cost. Pure geometric spacing (e.g. {1, 4, 16, 64, 128}) minimizes graph count but is only safe if active_B clusters near the top — at a ratio-4 gap the worst-case pad waste is (64−16)/64 ≈ 75% of the tick. So production (vLLM) instead captures a small dense head {1, 2, 4, 8} + a constant step (8) to the max (cudagraph_capture_sizes), which bounds the absolute pad waste to ≤ one step regardless of B; TensorRT-LLM is similar. The rule, therefore: fit the captured set to the empirical active_B distribution with a pad-waste bound — dense where the distribution has mass, coarse only in a thin tail — settled at compose, not a fixed constant.

Why a captured graph can only replay on an identical shape + addresses — the constraint that forces the set. A CUDA-graph is a frozen DAG of kernel launches with two things baked in at capture: the launch dimensions (grid/block sizes, derived from the batch shape) and the device pointers each kernel reads/writes. Replay re-issues those launches verbatim — so anything they depended on must be bit-identical. Three things must match, each with its fix:
  1. Launch shape — the batch dimension B. The decode hidden state is [B, d_model]; the grid was sized for the B at capture, so a graph captured at B=64 cannot run active_B=17. Fix: discretize B to the captured set and pad active_B up to the nearest captured size with no-op slots — this is what the set is for.
  2. Buffer addresses. The graph hard-codes the device pointers, not the values. Fix: static persistent I/O buffers (input_ids, positions, slot_mapping, block_table, logits) at fixed addresses; each tick memcpy the live step’s data into them, then replay — never allocate inside the captured region.
  3. Kernel sequence / control flow. Replay cannot take a host-side branch. The subtle case: varying KV length is not a new graph shape. PagedAttention keeps a fixed-max-shape block_table + a per-sequence length array; the attention kernel loops over the length at runtime (data), so the launch shape is stable across any context length (sampling variants are kept branch-free or outside the graph). This is a core reason Iter 3 paging is the prerequisite for graphing decode.
So the only shape that varies tick-to-tick is B — which the captured set + padding pins — and that is why warmup is per (B, model_revision): B fixes the launch shape, model_revision fixes the pointer layout and the kernel sequence.

12.4  Why batching is decode-only

The two pools sit on opposite sides of the roofline, so batching helps exactly one of them:

PoolRooflineEffect of batching the full request
PrefillCompute-bound (~140 GFLOPs/tok × N)TTFT grows linearly with B for no throughput gain → don’t batch full requests. Iter 4 batches at the chunk level instead (end-of-buffer-only padding waste at varlen).
DecodeBW-bound (weights_per_GPU read per tick, independent of B)At Bdecode the same HBM read serves Bdecode tokens → MBU climbs from ~1% toward the KV-bandwidth ceiling; the ~25× per-task throughput jump derived below.

12.5  Wait-time formula delta (decode pool)

The formula shape is unchanged from Iter 2; the B scalar simply graduates from the placeholder B=1 (the pre-continuous-batching baseline) to the real Bdecode, and the wait-time value drops Bdecode×. The Router consumer is untouched — it still reads a scalar.

approx_wait_decode(t_d) = max(0, queue − free_slots) × (avg_seq_lifetime / Bdecode) avg_seq_lifetime = Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok (decode-pool slot occupancy in the P/D-split topology) ≈ 400 × 5.22 ms ≈ 2.09 s [D] Bdecode per DC2 (≈ 106) → the per-admit wait drops ~106× vs the B=1 placeholder

12.6  Number chain

Two derivations: the per-tick cost (which sets TPOT and the new bound), then the per-task throughput multiplier. The fleet $ resize is deferred (see note).

# Per-tick decode cost — weight read is fixed; KV read grows with B βper_decode_tok = weights_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU = 17.5 GB / 3.35 TB/s ≈ 5.22 ms [D — §6.6 / §4] kv_per_seq_per_GPU = (Tin_mean + Tout_mean/2) × KV_per_tok / 8 = 13.2 K × 40 KB ≈ 528 MB [D] tick(Bdecode) = βper_decode_tok + Bdecode × kv_per_seq_per_GPU / HBM_BW_per_GPU = 5.22 ms + (106 × 528 MB / 3.35 TB/s) = 5.22 + 16.7 ≈ 22 ms [D] → this IS TPOT, < 30 ms SLO # Per-task throughput multiplier (tokens/s) thr(B=1) = 1 / βper_decode_tok = 1 / 5.22 ms ≈ 192 tok/s/task [D] thr(Bdecode) = Bdecode / tick(Bdecode) = 106 / 22 ms ≈ 4 818 tok/s/task [D] multiplier = thr(Bdecode) / thr(B=1) = 4 818 / 192 ≈ ~25× [D] # NOT the full 106×: the tick grows ~4× because KV reads now dominate — the honest, BW-bounded number req_throughput = thr(Bdecode) / Tout_mean = 4 818 / 400 ≈ ~12 req/s/task (vs 0.479 at B=1) [D]
Fleet $ / decode_task_num deferred to S6 (per the plan). This iteration moves decode_throughput_per_task ~25×, but Iter 7 (speculative decoding) multiplies it again by the accept-rate factor — so resolving decode_task_num = ⌈λpeak / req_throughput × headroom⌉ and the $/mo line now would just be re-done at Iter 7. Direction is unambiguous: decode is the fleet-dominant cost after Iter 5, and a ~25× per-task decode throughput is the page’s largest fleet-$ lever — the intermediate ~271 K → ~11 K decode-task order-of-magnitude is shown for shape; the exact figure lands at S6 after the accept-rate factor.

12.7  What breaks

  1. Decode-pool admit unfairness. One tenant’s flood fills the waiting queue and starves others’ admits. Impact: fairness, not correctness. Mitigation: per-user token bucket + max-concurrent-conv cap — a later admission-control iteration (the decode batch itself stays work-conserving).
  2. CUDA-graph captured-set mis-shape. When active_B falls between captured values, the pad slots waste a slice of the tick; a brand-new (B, model_revision) not yet captured pays a ~100 ms warmup once. Mitigation: geometric spacing tuned to the empirical active_B distribution; warmup amortized at boot.
  3. TPOT rises with Bdecode (the new bound). KV reads now dominate the tick (16.7 ms of the 22 ms), so pushing Bdecode higher trades throughput for TPOT. Impact: at the ~106 anchor TPOT ~22 ms is under the 30 ms SLO, but it is the binding constraint, not HBM capacity, at long context. Mitigation: cap Bdecode at the TPOT-SLO crossover; Flash Decoding keeps the KV read SM-efficient.
  4. Flash Decoding split_kv tuning. Too few splits under-feed the SMs at long context; too many add reduction overhead at short context. Mitigation: split_kv tuned to the context-length distribution; benefits accrue at the long tail.

12.8  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 5)Post-iter (Iter 6)ΔNote
TPOT p99~5.2 ms (B=1, weight-bound)~22 ms (Bdecode≈106, KV-bound)↑Rises but stays under the 30 ms SLO — the deliberate trade for ~25× throughput. KV reads now dominate the tick; this is the new ceiling later decode work pushes on.
TTFT p99~106 ms~106 ms→Unchanged — continuous batching is decode-only; prefill stays B=1 (batching it would grow TTFT linearly).
Decode throughput / task192 tok/s (0.479 req/s)~4.8 K tok/s (~12 req/s)↑The headline win — ~25×. One weight read serves Bdecode tokens; MBU climbs from ~1% toward the KV-BW ceiling.
Max-output~65 K~65 K→Unchanged ceiling; batching changes throughput, not the single-request output length cap.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — the shared decode queue can be monopolized; per-user admission lands at Iter 8.
$/mo at λpeak~$19.41 B/mo (decode-dominated)↓ large (deferred)↓The largest fleet-$ lever on the page — decode dominates after Iter 5 and this cuts per-task decode cost ~25×. Exact figure settled at S6 after Iter 7’s accept-rate factor.

Iter 6 attacks B2: continuous batching at Bdecode ≈ 106 amortizes the once-per-tick weight read across the whole batch (MBU ~1% → toward the KV-BW ceiling), Flash Decoding keeps that batch from being attention-gated at long context, and CUDA-graphs at a captured-set strip the 200 Hz launch tax — together a ~25× per-task decode throughput jump at TPOT ~22 ms, still under the 30 ms SLO. It is the page’s single largest per-task throughput win and the dominant fleet-$ lever, but it leaves the per-request decode cost at vanilla autoregressive — one token per weight read per sequence — which the next iteration attacks with speculative decoding.

13.  Iter 7 — Speculative decoding (cost-only)

Bottleneck addressed: none — this is the page’s first cost-only iteration (§1.5 “cost-only” row). Every latency SLO is already met (Iter 5 closed TTFT, Iter 6 left TPOT at ~22 ms < 30 ms). Mechanism: a small draft model proposes γ tokens autoregressively; the 70B target model verifies all γ+1 positions in one forward pass and accepts the longest correct prefix — so a single 5.22 ms target weight-read yields E[accept] ≈ 2.8 tokens instead of exactly one. Why here: the draft→verify orchestration (extra kernel launches, rejection sampling, KV rollback) rides Iter 6’s batched tick + CUDA-graph captured set — it amortizes over Bdecode; at B=1 the per-launch overhead would have exceeded the autoregressive cost it replaces. Deferred: the final summed serving_task_num / fleet $ total — settled at S6 once prefill + decode pools are added with their respective per-task throughputs.

Notation introduced in this section — the speculative-decode accept math and the per-task decode-throughput multiplier it buys. Consumes decode_throughput_per_task from §12.6 and the $70 790/task constant from §11.6.

γ (draft length, K13’s K)
tokens the draft model proposes per target step. = 4 [G-FA — K13]. Cliff: too small wastes the parallel verify; too large wastes draft work on tokens that get rejected (the accept run rarely reaches the tail).
accept_rate α
per-token probability the target accepts a draft proposal. = 0.70 [G-FA — K13] for chat/code with a well-matched 1B draft; workload-dependent — monitored per-tenant, with a vanilla fallback when it degrades. What / used-for / updated-when / by-whom: a measured ratio; sets the throughput multiplier; recomputed continuously from the verify accept/reject stream; owned by the decode scheduler.
E[accept]
expected tokens accepted per target forward = (1 − αγ+1) / (1 − α) — the truncated geometric mean of the accept run. ≈ 2.77 at α=0.7, γ=4 [D].
accept_rate_speedup_factor
E[accept] discounted for the draft forwards + the larger verify KV read + reject-rollback waste ≈ 2.0× [D] — the honest net per-task decode throughput multiplier (not the raw 2.77).
decode_throughput_per_task_iter7
= decode_throughput_per_task_iter6 × accept_rate_speedup_factor ≈ 9 636 tok/s/task (~24 req/s/task) [D].

13.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Draft → Target — the γ proposed tokens are handed to the target as a single γ+1-position batch to verify; solid = synchronous on the decode critical path.
  • ② Target → Accept — the target’s per-position logits are compared against the draft’s proposals; the longest correct prefix is accepted.
  • ③ Reject → rollback (dashed red = the speculative tail is discarded) — KV for the rejected suffix is freed and the next draft round restarts from the last accepted token; this rollback waste is the ~28% discount on E[accept].

Read the diagram. The framing strip is the point of the whole iteration: there is no latency SLO to fix — Iter 5 put common-case TTFT at ~106 ms and Iter 6 left TPOT at ~22 ms, both inside spec. What remains is that each decode tick still reads the entire 17.5 GB-per-GPU weight shard (5.22 ms) to emit exactly one token. Box 1 spends a tiny amount of extra work — a ~1B draft model proposes γ=4 tokens in four cheap (~0.075 ms) forwards. Box 2 is the trick: the 70B target verifies all γ+1=5 candidate positions in one forward pass — the same weight read it would have done anyway, because decode is bandwidth-bound and the extra compute over five positions is free. Box 3 accepts the longest correct prefix; because the verify uses the exact target distribution, the output is provably identical to vanilla sampling — no quality loss. The expected accept run is (1−αγ+1)/(1−α) ≈ 2.77 tokens; after discounting the draft forwards, the slightly larger verify KV read, and the rejected-tail rollback, the honest net multiplier is ~2×. The bottom strip is the payoff: per-task decode throughput doubles, the decode fleet roughly halves, and $/mo drops ~$382 M — with every latency SLO flat.

13.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • The enabling prior is Iter 6’s batched tick. Speculative decoding adds orchestration — γ draft launches, a verify launch, rejection sampling, and KV rollback — per decode step. That overhead is fixed-per-launch, so it only pays off when amortized over many sequences: at Bdecode the verify checks Bdecode × (γ+1) candidate tokens per kernel launch, riding the same CUDA-graph captured set Iter 6 built. Pre-Iter-6 at B=1 the per-launch overhead would have exceeded the single autoregressive forward it replaces — the math is upside-down without the batch.
  • The constraint that flipped: the SLOs are met, so the next visible target is $/mo. After Iter 5 (prefill-$ down ~7×) and Iter 6 (decode throughput up ~25×), the fleet is decode-dominated and every latency target is inside spec. The remaining inefficiency is purely economic: decode still pays one full weight read per token. This is the first iteration whose justification is cost, not a latency cliff.
  • Why speculative decoding and not just “a bigger batch”? Bdecode is already at the TPOT-SLO crossover (Iter 6 — KV reads dominate the tick past ~106). Pushing B higher trades throughput for TPOT. Speculative decoding instead raises the tokens per weight read without growing the tick’s weight-read cost — an orthogonal lever.
  • “Why not Medusa / EAGLE tree variants?” Same family, more kernel complexity (tree attention over a candidate tree). Defer until a workload shift makes the extra accept rate worth the engineering; the external-draft pairing (DC2 (c)) is the production-default.
  • “Why not Prompt-Lookup / Lookahead (model-free)?” Both are real and strong fits for the Claude-Code prompt-echo workload (file contents echoed verbatim) — PLD can beat draft-model accept rate on echo-heavy traffic. They are weaker on free-form chat. The page treats them as a workload-mix review item at S6, and they compose (PLD-first, draft-model fallback).

13.3  Design choices

[param] (γ, accept_rate α) → accept_rate_speedup_factor: 1× (vanilla) → ≈ 2.0× — Iter 7. Why: a small draft proposes γ=4 tokens; at α=0.7 the target accepts a run of E[accept]≈2.77 per forward, net ~2× after draft + rollback overhead. Cliff: γ too large wastes draft work past the accept run; α drifts with workload — monitored per-tenant with a vanilla fallback.

DC1 — Decode throughput strategy (more tokens per weight read, without raising the tick)

Problem: at Bdecode the decode tick is already at the TPOT-SLO crossover — the weight read makes one token per sequence and growing B further trades TPOT for throughput. How do we extract more tokens per weight read on an axis orthogonal to batch size?

  • (a) Vanilla autoregressive — one token per target forward; the Iter 6 baseline (decode_throughput_per_task_iter6). Nothing wrong with it; just leaves the weight-read amortization at one-token-per-read.
  • (b) Speculative decoding — CHOSEN: a draft model proposes γ tokens, the target verifies γ+1 positions in one forward, the longest correct prefix is accepted. Output is provably identical to vanilla sampling (the verify uses the exact target distribution — no quality trade). Composes cleanly with Iter 6 batched decode (verify amortizes over the batch) and accept-rate is workload-stable for chat/code. K-page: K13.
  • (c) Medusa / EAGLE tree variants — same family, higher accept rate via a candidate tree, but tree-attention kernel complexity; defer until workload shift justifies it.
  • (d) Prompt-Lookup Decoding (PLD, model-free) — matches running-output n-grams against the prompt itself; strong on the Claude-Code prompt-echo workload, weak on free-form chat. Composes with (b) as a PLD-first fallback path; S6 workload-mix review item.
  • (e) Lookahead Decoding (Jacobi-iteration, model-free) — no draft model needed; lower implementation cost, but accept-rate lags (b) on long-form generation.

Why (b): it is the production-default that raises tokens-per-weight-read on an axis orthogonal to B, with zero output-quality change and clean composition with the batched tick. The model-free variants ((d)/(e)) are named because the prompt-echo workload genuinely favors them — but as a fallback layer, not the base mechanism, until the workload mix is measured at S6.

DC2 — Draft model choice (what proposes the γ tokens)

Problem: the draft must be cheap enough that γ draft forwards cost far less than one target forward, yet aligned enough with the target that the accept rate stays high. The two pull in opposite directions.

  • (a) Self-distilled small model (~1B) — highest alignment (distilled from the target), but a separate training pipeline + revision-sync burden every time the target updates.
  • (b) Skip-layer / early-exit variant of the target — no extra weights, but couples draft and target kernels and complicates the verify path.
  • (c) External small model paired with the target — CHOSEN: a stock ~1B model run as a sidecar on the same task (~10 GB HBM). Production-typical; decouples draft and target lifecycles; accept rate ~0.7 on chat/code is adequate for the ~2× net win. K-page: K13.

Why (c): it is the lowest-operational-burden option that clears the accept-rate bar — the marginal accept-rate gain from a self-distilled draft (a) does not pay for its training/sync pipeline at this workload, and the skip-layer variant (b) entangles the kernel that Iter 6 worked to keep graph-capturable.

13.4  Wait-time formula delta (decode pool — gains an accept-rate factor)

The decode-pool wait-time shape is unchanged from Iter 6; the effective service rate per slot simply gains the accept-rate multiplier, so the per-admit wait drops a further accept_rate_speedup_factor×. The Router consumer still reads one scalar.

approx_wait_decode(t_d) = max(0, queue − free_slots) × (avg_seq_lifetime / (Bdecode × accept_rate_speedup_factor)) avg_seq_lifetime = Tout_mean × βper_decode_tok ≈ 400 × 5.22 ms ≈ 2.09 s [D] accept_rate_speedup_factor ≈ 2.0 (DC1) → per-admit wait drops a further ~2× vs Iter 6

13.5  Number chain

The accept math first, then the per-task throughput multiplier, then the fleet/$ resize (now derivable because the accept-rate factor is in hand — the figure Iter 6 deferred to here).

# Accept run per target forward (truncated geometric) E[accept] = (1 − αγ+1) / (1 − α) = (1 − 0.7⁵) / (1 − 0.7) = (1 − 0.16807) / 0.3 ≈ 2.77 tok / target weight-read [D — α=0.7, γ=4 G-FA K13] # Net multiplier after overhead (draft reads + larger verify KV + reject rollback) draft_overhead = γ × (draft_weights/GPU / HBM_BW/GPU) = 4 × (0.25 GB / 3.35 TB/s) ≈ 0.3 ms / tick [D] discount ≈ 0.72 (0.3 ms draft + larger verify KV over γ+1 positions + ~rollback waste) [G-FA] accept_rate_speedup_factor = E[accept] × discount = 2.77 × 0.72 ≈ 2.0× [D] # Per-task decode throughput decode_throughput_per_task_iter7 = decode_throughput_per_task_iter6 × 2.0 = 4 818 tok/s × 2.0 ≈ 9 636 tok/s/task (~24.1 req/s/task) [D — §12.6] # Fleet / $ resize (the figure Iter 6 deferred to here) decode_task_num_iter7 = ⌈ λpeak × headroom / req_throughput_iter7 ⌉ = ⌈ 100 K × 1.3 / 24.1 ⌉ ≈ 5 396 tasks [D] (vs decode_task_num_iter6 = ⌈ 100 K × 1.3 / 12.05 ⌉ ≈ 10 793 tasks — the Iter 6 “for-shape” number, now halved) Δ$/mo = (10 793 − 5 396) × $70 790 ≈ 5 397 × $70 790 ≈ $382 M/mo saved [D — $70 790/task, §11.6]
Final fleet total still lands at S6. This iteration resolves the decode pool’s task count and its $ delta (the accept-rate factor Iter 6 was waiting on). The summed serving_task_num = prefill_task_num + decode_task_num and the single fleet $/mo headline are assembled at S6, where the prefill pool (~2 767 tasks, Iter 5) and the decode pool (~5 396 tasks, here) are added. Direction is unambiguous: decode-$ roughly halves, the dominant remaining fleet cost after Iter 5.

13.6  What breaks

  1. Draft model takes its own HBM (~10 GB/task). Impact: trims G_KV from ~450 to ~440 GB, nudging Bdecode 106 → ~104 — a ~2% capacity dip absorbed in headroom, not a re-derivation. Mitigation: the draft is small by construction; if HBM pressure ever bites, the skip-layer variant (DC2 (b)) needs zero extra weights.
  2. Accept-rate degrades on out-of-distribution workloads. A workload the draft was not aligned to (e.g. a new language, or adversarial input) can push α well below 0.7, at which point speculative decoding can be slower than vanilla (rollback waste exceeds the accept run). Impact: throughput regression on that traffic slice, never a correctness or quality bug (output is exact regardless of α). Mitigation: per-tenant accept-rate monitoring with an automatic fallback to vanilla autoregressive below a break-even α.
  3. Verify-in-parallel branching complicates the per-task scheduler. The decode loop now has a variable-length accept run per sequence per tick, so slot bookkeeping and KV rollback must stay branch-free enough to keep the CUDA-graph capture valid. Impact: engineering complexity, and a mis-handled rollback would corrupt a sequence’s KV. Mitigation: rollback is a pointer/length reset on the paged block table (no data move); the accept count is a runtime data value, not a new graph shape (same property that let Iter 6 graph decode).
  4. Draft ↔ target revision skew. A target weight update with a stale draft silently lowers accept rate. Mitigation: version the (draft, target) pair; the per-tenant accept-rate monitor catches a skew as a fleet-wide α drop.

13.7  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 6)Post-iter (Iter 7)ΔNote
TTFT p99~106 ms~106 ms→No change — speculative decoding is decode-side; prefill (TTFT) is untouched.
TPOT p99~22 ms~22 ms→No change by design — the verify tick is ~one decode tick; throughput rises by emitting a run per tick, not by shortening the tick. Cost-only iteration.
Max-context~256 K~256 K→No change — the single-request context ceiling is a capacity question (later CP iteration), not a decode-speed one.
Max-output~65 K~65 K→No change — output length cap is unaffected; speculative decoding changes how fast those tokens are produced, not how many are allowed.
Tenant fairness→→→No change — still no per-user admission control; lands at the next iteration. (Speculative decoding does add a per-tenant accept-rate signal, reused there.)
Decode throughput / task~4.8 K tok/s (~12 req/s)~9.6 K tok/s (~24 req/s)↑~2× — the same weight read yields E[accept]≈2.8 tokens (net ~2× after overhead).
$/mo at λpeakdecode ~$764 M/mo (10 793 × $70 790)decode ~$382 M/mo (5 396 × $70 790)↓The only SLO that moves. Decode fleet roughly halves → ~$382 M/mo saved. Fleet total assembled at S6.

Iter 7 is the page’s first cost-only move: a ~1B draft proposes γ=4 tokens, the 70B target verifies all γ+1 in one bandwidth-bound forward, and the longest correct prefix is accepted — output provably identical to vanilla sampling, but each target weight read now yields E[accept]≈2.8 tokens (net ~2× after draft + rollback overhead). It rides Iter 6’s batched tick (the orchestration amortizes over Bdecode; at B=1 it would have lost), moves zero latency SLOs, and roughly halves the decode fleet — ~$382 M/mo. The next iteration leaves the throughput story and turns to fairness: at consumer scale, abuse outliers, not heavy tenants, are the threat to a normal user’s admit ordering.

14.  Iter 8 — Per-user abuse-prevention: token-bucket rate-limit + max-concurrent-conv cap

Bottleneck addressed: B8b, reframed for B2C. At 50 M-DAU consumer scale no single user can dominate a 3 M-concurrent-session fleet — the B2B “one heavy tenant floods 5% of decode” failure mode does not exist here. What does exist is abuse: scripted clients, runaway agents, and accidental tight loops that blow a user’s fair share and preempt normal users’ admit ordering. Mechanism: a per-user token bucket (rate-limit with burst) + a per-user max-concurrent-conversation cap, both enforced at the Router, O(1) per admit. Why here: Iter 1b’s CH-on-conv_id + BLCH already gives per-conv fairness; the missing primitive is per-user fairness, and aggregate capacity is now proven, so abuse becomes the next visible constraint. Deferred: nothing — this is a pure admission-control add with ~$0 GPU cost.

Notation introduced in this section — the per-user admission state on the Router. Sized against DAU from §1.3 and router_task_num from §7.3.

token bucket (capacity, refill_rate, tokens, last_refill_ts)
per-user rate-limit state. A request admits iff tokens ≥ 1; on admit tokens −= 1; tokens refill at refill_rate up to capacity. What / used-for / updated-when / by-whom: 4 fields per user; gates admission; updated on every admit + lazily on read (refill computed from now − last_refill_ts); owned by the Router shard for that user. This IS a per-user map user_id → bucket, sharded by hash(user_id) % router_task_num.
(capacity, refill_rate)
the two knobs. Worked values (10 req, 1 req/s) [G-FA] — allow a 10-request burst, then 1 req/s steady. Tiered by tier_class (premium users get larger buckets; the Tenant entity, §2.1).
max_concurrent_conv
per-user cap on simultaneously-open conversations. = 5 [G-FA]; 1 int per user. Bounds a scripted client’s admit-slot footprint independent of its request rate.
bucket_bytes_per_user
packed bucket state ≈ 32 B [D] (4 + 4 + 4 + 8 + slack, C/Go layout).

14.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Normal user → Router — a bursty-then-idle request stream; solid = the request proceeds to the admission check.
  • ② Abuser → Router (dashed red = high-rate scripted traffic) — floods the Router, where the per-user bucket is the gate.
  • ③ Router → ADMIT — bucket has tokens and open_conv < 5: decrement a token, route to the CH-on-conv_id ring. Normal users never hit the cap.
  • ④ Router → 429 (dashed red = rejected before consuming a serving admit slot) — bucket empty or open_conv = 5: return 429 + Retry-After; the abuser is clamped to ≤ 1 req/s and ≤ 5 open conversations.

Read the diagram. The two left lanes are the whole point of the B2C reframe: a normal user fires a flurry of edits and then idles (the bucket’s burst capacity is sized for exactly this), while an abuser — a scripted client or runaway agent, ~0.1% of users ≈ 50 K — emits a high-rate stream. Both hit the Router’s per-user admission check. The token bucket admits while tokens ≥ 1 (decrementing one per admit, refilling at refill_rate up to capacity); the max_concurrent_conv cap independently bounds how many admit slots one user can hold regardless of rate. The normal user’s burst fits under capacity = 10 and admits; the abuser drains its bucket and gets 429 Retry-After before consuming a scarce serving admit slot — so it can no longer preempt the admit ordering of the 99.9% of normal users. The right box is the cost argument: although the full DAU bucket table is 1.6 GB, only the ~3 M users actually chatting at peak need resident buckets (~96 MB), sharded across the 3 Router tasks at ~32 MB each — well under 100 MB, O(1) per admit, ~$0 of GPU.

14.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • The B2C reframe is the key insight. At 50 M DAU a user holds at most a few conv_ids out of ~3 M concurrent sessions — no single user can dominate the fleet, so the classic B2B “one heavy tenant floods 5% of decode” bottleneck (the original B8b) simply does not occur. The real failure is abuse: scripted floods, runaway agents, accidental tight loops — outliers that blow per-user fair share without ever threatening aggregate capacity.
  • The enabling prior: Iter 1b already gives per-conv fairness. CH-on-conv_id + BLCH spreads conv_ids across the fleet and spills hot vnodes, so no single conversation monopolizes a task. The missing primitive is per-user fairness: a script firing 10 conv_ids at once still occupies 10 admit slots ahead of other users’ first turns — invisible to a per-conv mechanism.
  • The constraint that flipped: first-turn latency for a normal user behind an abuser. When ~50 K users run scripted clients, their unbounded conv-creation can preempt admit ordering for the ~99.9% of normal users — an SLO violation that only becomes visible once aggregate capacity is in hand (Iter 6/7) and the remaining tail is contention, not throughput.
  • “Why now, not at Iter 1b alongside BLCH?” Iter 1b had to use a generic signal (approx_wait_time_ms) — baking in per-user semantics there would have committed the design to user-awareness before the BOE proved aggregate capacity could meet baseline. With capacity now proven, abuse-prevention is the next visible constraint, and the per-tenant accept-rate signal Iter 7 already added is reused here.
  • “Why token bucket and not WFQ?” Weighted Fair Queueing shares capacity across heavy contributors; at B2C scale there are no heavy contributors, just abuse outliers, so WFQ degenerates to FIFO when all weights are equal. Token bucket cleanly separates “normal” from “abusive” at O(1) and far less state.

14.3  Design choices

[param] admit policy → per-user token bucket (capacity 10, refill 1/s) + max_concurrent_conv = 5 — Iter 8. Why: allows a normal user’s burst (≤ capacity) then clamps steady-state to refill_rate; the conv cap bounds admit-slot footprint independent of rate. Cliff: capacity too low clips legitimate bursts (UX), too high lets abusers spike; tiered by tier_class.

DC1 — Admit policy (how the Router decides whose request enters the serving fleet)

Problem: a small population of abusers (~0.1%) can preempt admit ordering for everyone else. We need to clamp them without harming the 99.9% of normal users — who genuinely do burst (edit-then-idle) — at O(1) per admit and trivial state, on the same Router that already does CH routing.

  • (a) Pure FIFO — no per-user notion; an abuser’s flood fills admit slots in arrival order and starves normal users. The status quo this iteration fixes.
  • (b) Per-user token bucket + max-concurrent-conv cap — CHOSEN: O(1) per admit; allows bursts within capacity (matches the normal edit-then-idle pattern); a single canonical algorithm the operator already understands; the conv cap independently bounds parallel admit slots. K-page: K15; see rate-limiting for the bucket family.
  • (c) Leaky bucket — smoother output rate, but disallows bursts; rejected on UX grounds (normal users do burst).
  • (d) GCRA (Generic Cell Rate Algorithm) — mathematically equivalent to (b) but stores a single timestamp per user instead of (tokens, last_refill_ts). Adopt as the implementation form of (b) if Router heap pressure bites; same admit policy, less state.
  • (e) WFQ-over-tokens — for fairness across heavy contributors; none exist at B2C, so it degenerates to FIFO. Rejected.
  • (f) DRF (Dominant Resource Fairness) — multi-resource fair share over GPU compute + HBM; useful only if heavy-prompt and fast-turn users competed on different dominant resources. At this workload the dominant resource is uniformly HBM-BW, so DRF degenerates to single-resource fair share = (e). Named as the next-step generalization if the workload diverges.
  • (g) Strict per-user quota, no sharing — wastes capacity when most users idle (the common case at 50 M DAU / 3 M active). Rejected.

Why (b): it is the only option that clamps the ~0.1% abusers while letting the 99.9% burst freely, at O(1) and ~32 B/user. WFQ/DRF (e/f) collapse to FIFO at this distribution; leaky bucket (c) breaks the burst UX; strict quota (g) wastes the idle majority’s capacity. GCRA (d) is the same policy in a leaner state representation — an implementation lever, not a different design.

14.4  Number chain

# Per-user state, packed (C/Go layout) bucket_bytes_per_user = capacity(4) + refill_rate(4) + tokens(4) + last_refill_ts(8) + slack ≈ 32 B [D] # Full DAU footprint vs resident working set full_bucket_state = DAU × 32 B = 50 M × 32 B ≈ 1.6 GB [D — §1.3 DAU] resident_working_set = chatting_at_peak × 32 B = 3 M × 32 B ≈ 96 MB [D — idle buckets lazily evicted; §1.3 ~3 M active] per_router_state = 96 MB / router_task_num = 96 MB / 3 ≈ 32 MB / Router (< 100 MB) [D — router_task_num = 3, §7.3] # packed array keyed by hash(user_id) % router_task_num — NOT a Python dict (4–6× overhead) # Effective bound on an abuser with (capacity = 10 req, refill = 1 req/s, max_concurrent_conv = 5): steady-state ≤ 1 req/s, burst ≤ 10, open conversations ≤ 5 [G-FA] → well below the threshold to preempt normal-user admit ordering at any plausible abuser count (~50 K) cost: O(1) per admit; max_concurrent_conv = 1 int/user; ~$0 GPU

14.5  What breaks

  1. Bucket-state hot writes. A viral user_id (or a hash hot-spot) concentrates bucket writes on one Router shard. Impact: Router-side contention, not a serving issue. Mitigation: shard by hash(user_id) % router_task_num so writes spread; a single user’s bucket is a few atomics, not a bottleneck.
  2. Refill cadence drift. Per-Router clock skew makes refill_rate slightly inconsistent across shards. Impact: a user re-sharded after a Router change could see a small refill discontinuity. Mitigation: compute refill from a monotonic timestamp captured at each admit (now − last_refill_ts), not a periodic timer.
  3. Heavy legitimate user clipping. A power user (or a sanctioned automation) trips the same cap as an abuser. Impact: false-positive 429s on good traffic. Mitigation: tiered buckets — premium tier_class gets larger (capacity, refill_rate), declared in the Tenant entity (§2.1); the cap is policy, not a constant.
  4. Bucket-shard loss on Router death. A Router task dies and its in-memory buckets vanish. Impact: the affected users briefly get a fresh full bucket (fail-open) — a momentary lenience, never a wrongful block. Mitigation: acceptable by design (rate-limit state is soft); buckets rebuild from the next admits on the rebalanced shard.

14.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 7)Post-iter (Iter 8)ΔNote
Tenant fairness→↑↑The headline. Per-user token bucket + conv cap clamps the ~0.1% abusers so they can no longer preempt the admit ordering of the 99.9% of normal users. First per-user fairness primitive on the page.
TTFT p99 (normal user behind an abuser)at risk under abuse~106 ms (protected)↓The abuser no longer holds admit slots, so a normal user’s first-turn TTFT is no longer inflated by scripted contention.
TTFT p99 (normal user, no contention)~106 ms~106 ms→Unchanged — normal users never hit the cap; the O(1) bucket check is sub-microsecond on the Router.
TPOT p99~22 ms~22 ms→No change — admission control is pre-serving; decode tick is untouched.
Max-context / Max-output~256 K / ~65 K~256 K / ~65 K→No change — orthogonal to per-request size ceilings.
$/mo at λpeak(Iter 7 decode ~$382 M/mo)+ ~32 MB/Router state→Effectively unchanged — O(1)/admit, ~$0 GPU. (Marginally reduces wasted GPU by rejecting abuse before it consumes serving slots.)

Iter 8 retires B8b in its B2C form: at 50 M DAU the threat is not a heavy tenant but abuse outliers, and a per-user token bucket (capacity 10, refill 1/s) + a max_concurrent_conv = 5 cap, enforced O(1) at the Router over a ~96 MB resident working set (~32 MB/Router), clamps the ~0.1% abusers to ≤ 1 req/s and ≤ 5 open conversations — protecting the 99.9% of normal users’ admit ordering at ~$0 GPU cost. With fairness now in hand, the only remaining gap is the long-context tail: the ~0.1% of requests whose 1 M-token KV does not fit on a single task at useful concurrency.

15.  Iter 9 — Context parallelism for 1 M-context single requests

Bottleneck addressed: B10 (the 1 M part). A single request’s KV at the p99.9 tail is 1 M × 320 KB = 320 GB — it fits on one task’s ~450 GB HBM-for-KV budget only by dropping the task to dedicated B=1, which destroys fleet capacity; at any useful B>1 it does not fit. Mechanism: context parallelism (CP) partitions the sequence dimension across N_CP = 8 ranks, each owning a ~40 GB KV slice, joined by Ring Attention — Flash Attention’s tiling extended across tasks via RDMA. Why here: Ring Attention is Iter 4’s Flash Attention with the tile boundaries moved from SRAM↔HBM to rank↔rank; it could not be reached before that foundation landed. Deferred: nothing — this closes the last latency-SLO gap (the long-context tail).

Notation introduced in this section — the CP partitioning + Ring-Attention collective. Builds on KV_per_tok (§4.2), the ~450 GB G_KV budget (§9.4), and Flash Decoding (§12.3 DC1).

N_CP (CP-rank count)
tasks a single long request is split across. = 8 [G-FA] — static; covers 1 M context (320 GB / 8 = 40 GB/rank) with headroom. Cliff: dynamic cardinality adds ring-resize protocol complexity; static 8 is the design pick.
CP_FORK_THRESHOLD_BYTES
the Router’s est_kv cutoff for sending a request to the CP ring vs the normal ring. = 256 GB ≈ 800 K tokens [D]. What / used-for / updated-when / by-whom: a fixed byte threshold; routes long requests; evaluated at admit from len(prompt_tokens) × KV_per_tok; owned by the Router.
est_kv
= len(req.prompt_tokens) × KV_per_tok — the Router’s admit-time estimate of a request’s KV footprint; the fork predicate is est_kv > CP_FORK_THRESHOLD_BYTES.
Ring Attention
the CP attention collective — each rank holds its KV slice locally; query tiles + partial-softmax (log-sum-exp) state pass around a ring; identical numerics to Flash Attention v2, one memory level wider.
cp_ring / normal_ring
the two Router targets: an 8-task CP group (Ring Attention) for est_kv > threshold; the Iter 1b path otherwise. Sticky conv_id → cp_group_id for multi-turn long-doc Q&A.

15.1  Architecture (diagram first)

  • ① Router fork → cp_ring — when est_kv > 256 GB, the Router emits CPAssign(rank, world_size, peers, conv_id) to all 8 CP-group members and binds conv_id → cp_group_id stickily; otherwise the request stays on normal_ring.
  • ② Ring pass — the query tile + partial log-sum-exp state circulates rank↔rank over RDMA; each rank computes its 40 GB KV slice’s contribution with the FA v2 SRAM-tiled kernel, and the partials combine via a numerically-stable log-sum-exp reduction (no full-softmax materialization).

Read the diagram. The top strip is the Router’s admit-time decision: it estimates est_kv = len(prompt_tokens) × KV_per_tok and forks. The 99.9% of requests below 256 GB (~800 K tokens) stay on the normal_ring — a single task fits them at useful B>1. The ~0.1% above the cutoff cannot be served at any useful per-task concurrency: a 1 M-context request’s 320 GB of KV fits one task’s ~450 GB budget only by collapsing it to dedicated B=1, throwing away the whole task’s throughput. The center is the fix: context parallelism splits the sequence dimension across N_CP = 8 ranks, each holding a 40 GB slice, and Ring Attention joins them — a query tile plus partial log-sum-exp state circulates the ring over RDMA, each rank attending to its local slice. The right box is the load-bearing intuition: Ring Attention is the same algorithm as Iter 4’s Flash Attention v2 and Iter 6’s Flash Decoding — SRAM-tiling with a log-sum-exp reduction — with the tile boundary moved one memory rung wider each time (SRAM↔HBM within a GPU → KV-split across SM groups within a task → KV-split across tasks). The bottom strip derives the cost: for a single decode token the lone query must visit all 8 KV slices sequentially around the ring, so the per-token time is 5.22 ms (weight) + 8 × 1.49 ms (the 8 serialized slice reads) + ring RDMA ≈ 17 ms — under the 30 ms TPOT SLO for these rare tail requests.

15.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • The enabling prior is Iter 4’s Flash Attention v2. Ring Attention is FA v2’s tiled-attention-with-log-sum-exp algorithm extended across CP-ranks via RDMA — the SRAM-tiling principle applied at the inter-task memory hierarchy. Without that foundation (and Iter 6’s Flash Decoding, which is the within-task KV-split of the same idea), there is no kernel to extend.
  • The constraint that flipped: single-request HBM blow-up at p99.9. A 1 M-context request is 320 GB of KV. The per-task budget is ~450 GB only at dedicated B=1 (8×80 − 140 GB weights − ~50 GB activations); under Iter 6’s Bdecode continuous batching the per-slot share collapses to a few GB. So any request above the ~256 GB threshold (~800 K tokens) cannot be served at useful per-task concurrency by construction — the tail is a capacity wall, not a speed one.
  • CP is the only axis that works. TP=16+ crosses the NVLink-4 domain to NIC AllReduce (~50× collective-latency penalty, K20) and scales the wrong (hidden) dimension; PP bubbles and does not distribute the per-layer KV that blows HBM; RDMA fetch on demand at every attention step is absurd (~320 KB × 80 layers × 1 M tokens). Only CP partitions the sequence dim so each rank owns a slice and ranks pass query tiles + log-sum-exp state around the ring (K11).
  • “Why now and not earlier?” CP only earns its keep above the ~256 GB threshold (~0.1% of QPS). Earlier in the sequence, the 99.9% of requests that fit one task gained nothing, and the CP protocol overhead would have amortized badly over the tiny affected population. The tail becomes the dominant remaining gap only after Iter 4–8 settle everything else.
  • “Why a static 8-rank group?” Dynamic CP cardinality means ring-resize on the fly — real protocol complexity. Static 8 covers 1 M context (320 GB / 40 GB per rank) with headroom; dynamic is a future iteration if the workload mix shifts.
  • “Why is FT (the next iteration) after this and not before?” CP introduces a new failure mode (a CP-rank dying mid-1 M-request); fault-tolerance design needs CP’s topology already on the table, so it is sequenced after — the rebuttal lives in the next iteration’s what-breaks.

15.3  Design choices

DC1 — Long-context strategy (when single-request KV > one task’s HBM-for-KV budget)

Problem: a 1 M-context request is 320 GB of KV — it exceeds what one task can hold at any useful concurrency. We must distribute one request’s KV across tasks without paying a prohibitive per-step transfer or collective penalty.

  • (a) Full prefill on one task — impossible at B>1; only fits at dedicated B=1, collapsing the task to ~1 token-stream and destroying fleet capacity.
  • (b) RDMA fetch on demand — pull the remote KV slice per attention step; ~320 KB × 80 layers × 1 M tokens of transfer per token is not viable.
  • (c) Context parallelism (CP) — CHOSEN: partition the sequence dimension across N_CP ranks; each rank owns its KV slice locally; ranks exchange query tiles + log-sum-exp partial state around a ring. The only axis that distributes the KV that blows HBM. K-pages: K11 (CP) + K7/K8 (alternatives) + K17 (Flash Decoding composition).
  • (d) Pipeline parallelism (PP) — bubble cost; distributes layers, not the per-layer KV; wrong axis.
  • (e) Larger TP (TP=16) — crosses the NVLink-4 domain to NIC AllReduce (~50× latency, K20); scales hidden dim, not sequence.
