Why (c): of the parallelism axes, only CP partitions the dimension that is actually overflowing (the sequence’s KV). TP and PP scale the wrong dimensions and pay cross-domain penalties; on-demand fetch is bandwidth-prohibitive; single-task only fits by destroying concurrency.

[param] CP_FORK_THRESHOLD_BYTES: — → 256 GB ≈ 800 K tokens — Iter 9. Why: matches the “1 M-context, ~0.1% of QPS” framing — above it the per-task budget cannot hold the request at useful B>1. Cliff: 50–80 GB fires at p99 (~1% of QPS, too aggressive); 320 GB misses sub-1 M long-docs that also blow HBM at B>1.

DC2 — CP fork threshold (when does the Router send to the CP ring?)

Problem: the Router must decide at admit, from prompt length alone, whether a request goes to the (expensive, scarce) CP ring or the normal ring. Too low a cutoff wastes CP capacity on requests that fit one task; too high leaves HBM-blowing requests on the normal ring.

ThresholdToken equiv.Fires atVerdict
50 GB~150 Kp99 ≈ 1% of QPStoo aggressive — narrative is 0.1%
80 GB~250 Kp99matches p99 = 256 K tokens, but still over-forks
256 GB ✅~800 Kp99.9 ≈ 0.1% of QPSmatches the “1 M-context single requests, ~0.1% of QPS” framing
320 GB~1 Monly true 1 Mmisses sub-1 M long-docs that also blow HBM at B>1

Why 256 GB: below it the per-task HBM-for-KV budget (~450 GB at dedicated B=1, ~120 GB at B=4 sharing) fits the request comfortably; above it CP is required. It is the cutoff that forks exactly the population the iteration exists for (~0.1% of QPS) and no more. The Router emits CPAssign(rank, world_size, peers, conv_id) to the 8 group members and keeps a sticky conv_id → cp_group_id binding for multi-turn long-doc Q&A.

DC3 — CP collective for attention (how the ranks combine their slices)

Problem: attention needs every query to see the whole KV, but the KV is now scattered across 8 ranks. Materializing the joined KV at every rank would defeat the entire point (and Flash Attention’s HBM-I/O savings).

  • (a) All-gather full KV per attention step — high BW; materializes the joined KV at every rank, defeating Flash Attention’s tiling.
  • (b) Ring Attention — CHOSEN: Flash Attention’s tiling extended to distributed memory across CP-ranks. Each rank holds its KV slice locally; query tiles + partial-softmax state pass around a ring; each rank computes its slice’s contribution with the same SRAM-tiled algorithm as FA v2; partials combine via log-sum-exp (the same numerically-stable reduction Flash Decoding uses for KV-split). K-pages: K11 + K17.
  • (c) Striped Attention / DistFlashAttn (2024) — refinements that fix Ring Attention’s causal-mask load imbalance (late-position ranks do more work). Named as the forward-looking upgrade if that imbalance becomes the dominant CP bottleneck; cite at compose.

Why (b): it is the only option that keeps each rank’s KV local (no full materialization) while preserving exact attention numerics — because it is Flash Attention, one memory level wider. All-gather (a) throws away the HBM-I/O win that made FA worth adopting; Striped/DistFlash (c) are strict refinements that only pay off once load imbalance dominates.

Why Ring Attention is “the distributed Flash Attention” — the load-bearing intuition. Flash Attention v2 (Iter 4 DC1) tiles Q × Kᵀ × V through SRAM to avoid materializing the N × N score matrix in HBM. Ring Attention applies the same tiling, but the tile boundaries are now across tasks: each CP-rank’s KV slice is one tile; the ring-pass replaces SRAM↔HBM streaming with rank↔rank RDMA streaming. The numerical recipe — log-sum-exp partial reduction, no full-softmax materialization — is identical; only the memory hierarchy widens by one level. Composition with Flash Decoding: within each CP-rank, Flash Decoding runs over the local ~1 M / N_CP token slice, and the cross-rank log-sum-exp reduce stitches them — so the chain is “FA foundation → Flash Decoding (KV-split within a task) → Ring Attention (KV-split across tasks),” all sharing one SRAM-tiling principle and one log-sum-exp reduction.

15.4  Number chain

# The capacity wall a 1 M request hits on one task per_req_KV(1 M) = 1 M × KV_per_tok = 1 M × 320 KB = 320 GB [D — §4.2] G_KV (B=1) = 8×80 GB − 140 GB weights − ~50 GB activations ≈ 450 GB (only at dedicated B=1) [D — §9.4] → 320 GB fits one task ONLY at B=1 (collapses the task to ~1 stream); at useful B>1 it does NOT fit # CP split N_CP = 8 [G-FA] per_rank_KV = per_req_KV / N_CP = 320 GB / 8 = 40 GB/rank (fits in HBM at B=1 for the long request) [D] CP_FORK_THRESHOLD = 256 GB ≈ 256e9 / 320e3 = 800 K tokens [D — DC2] # Per-token decode at 1 M context (Ring Attention; the lone query visits all 8 slices serially) tick(1 M, CP=8) = βper_decode_tok + N_CP × (per_rank_KV / HBM_BW_per_rank) + ring_RDMA = 5.22 ms + 8 × (40 GB / 26.8 TB/s) + ~0.8 ms = 5.22 + 8 × 1.49 + 0.8 = 5.22 + 11.9 + 0.8 ≈ 17 ms [D] < 30 ms TPOT SLO # the 8 slice-reads are SERIALIZED around the ring for a single query — the load imbalance (c) addresses; # the other 7 ranks idle this query, which is why CP only pays for the ~0.1% tail, never the common case affected_QPS = ~0.1% of λpeak (p99.9 = 1 M context) [G-FA — §1.3]

15.5  What breaks

  1. RDMA NIC contention under long-context bursts. Several concurrent 1 M-requests sharing the ring saturate the per-task NIC budget — which is already reserved for Iter 2 KV hand-off + Iter 3b pool traffic. Impact: ring-pass latency inflates, pushing the ~17 ms tick toward the TPOT ceiling. Mitigation: the ~0.1% population rarely bursts concurrently; admission can serialize CP-group assignment so at most a bounded number of long requests share a ring.
  2. CP-rank cardinality is static (8). A request between the threshold and 1 M wastes part of the 8-rank group; a hypothetical > 1 M request would not fit even at 8. Impact: under-utilization on the small side, a hard ceiling on the large side. Mitigation: static 8 covers the stated 1 M ceiling with headroom; dynamic ring-resize is a future iteration if the mix shifts.
  3. A CP-rank dies mid-1 M-request. One of the 8 ranks fails during a long decode. Impact: the whole request stalls. Mitigation: the same recompute-as-fallback the next iteration introduces, with a longer βcold (the request re-prefills its slice on a replacement rank); this is exactly why FT is sequenced after CP.
  4. Ring Attention causal-mask load imbalance. Late-position ranks attend to more prior context, so they do disproportionately more work. Impact: the slowest rank gates each ring step. Mitigation: Striped Attention / DistFlashAttn (DC3 (c)) permute per-rank token assignment to balance load — adopt if the imbalance becomes the dominant CP cost.

15.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 8)Post-iter (Iter 9)ΔNote
Max-context supported~256 K (1 M rejected / B=1-only)1 M (served on the CP ring)↑The headline. CP splits the 320 GB single-request KV across 8 ranks (40 GB each), so 1 M context is served at useful concurrency for the first time. Closes the last open NFR (§1.2 max-context = 1 M).
TPOT p99 (1 M-context tail)N/A (couldn’t serve)~17 ms↓Per-token ~17 ms (weight + 8 serialized ring slice-reads + RDMA), under the 30 ms SLO for these p99.9 requests.
TTFT p99 (common case)~106 ms~106 ms→No change — CP fires only for the ~0.1% above the 256 GB threshold; the 99.9% stay on the normal ring untouched.
TPOT p99 (common case)~22 ms~22 ms→No change — CP is a separate ring; the normal decode pool is unaffected.
Tenant fairness↑↑→No change from Iter 8 — CP requests are still subject to per-user admission.

Iter 9 closes B10’s 1 M half: a 320 GB single-request KV that fit one task only by collapsing it to B=1 is instead split across N_CP = 8 ranks (40 GB each), joined by Ring Attention — Flash Attention’s SRAM-tiling-plus-log-sum-exp extended one memory rung wider, to rank↔rank RDMA. The Router forks on est_kv > 256 GB (~0.1% of QPS), per-token decode lands at ~17 ms (under the 30 ms TPOT SLO), and the 99.9% common case is untouched. With max-context = 1 M finally served, every functional and latency NFR is met — what remains is availability: the steady-state design still drops in-flight requests when a serving task dies.

16.  Iter 10 — Serving-task fault tolerance (recompute-as-fallback)

Bottleneck addressed: B11 — a serving-task death mid-flight loses its local KV, and the design as built drops those in-flight requests. Mechanism: recompute-as-fallback — KV is reproducible from prompt_tokens (RPO = 0 by construction), so on task death the CH ring rebalances its conv_ids to neighbours and the target rebuilds; for any conv that was admitted to the Iter 3b pool, the rebuilt target pulls the KV (linear I/O) instead of full recompute. Why here: needs Iter 1b’s CH ring (the rebalance primitive) + Iter 1a’s cache-reproducibility (the recompute primitive), and is sequenced after Iter 9 because CP adds a failure mode (a rank dying mid-1 M-request) that FT must cover. 0% extra steady-state HBM — the cheapest answer when KV is cheap to recompute.

Naming note. This is per-task FT in the serving fleet (CH-ring rebalance + recompute), distinct from the pooled kv-store-job’s own replication/FT (set at Iter 3b). Interaction: when a serving task dies and its conv_ids migrate, a rebalanced target whose conv is pool-resident pulls the prior KV instead of recomputing — so the store turns this iteration’s per-request RTO from βcold(N) into a pull(N) for any admitted conv.

Notation introduced in this section — the failure-response primitives. Reuses the CH ring (§7), the Iter 3b pool pull(N) (§9b), βcold(N) (§4.1), and K21 cold-start.

RPO (Recovery Point Objective)
data lost on failure. = 0 [G — §1.2] by construction — KV is derived state, always reproducible from prompt_tokens (which live on the client). Nothing durable is lost.
RTO (Recovery Time Objective)
time to recover. Three components: per-request RTO = pull(N) if pool-resident else βcold(N) recompute; fleet-capacity RTO ≈ 10 min (autoscale a replacement task); client SSE RTO = SDK reopen with Last-Event-ID.
pull(N)
linear I/O fetch of an N-token conv’s KV from the Iter 3b pool = N × KV_per_tok / BW_pull; e.g. pull(13 K) ≈ 166 ms (clean 25 GB/s) vs βcold(13 K) = 730 ms recompute.
ring_version / epoch
the CH-ring generation counter; a task death bumps it and the bump is broadcast so Routers drop the dead task from prefill_ring / decode_ring / cp_ring atomically.

16.1  Architecture (diagram first — the failure-response sequence)

  • ①–② Dₓ dies → broker — a mid-flight serving-task death loses its local KV; the broker catches it on a heartbeat miss; solid = synchronous control path.
  • ③ broker → Router — the Router drops Dₓ from decode_ring / cp_ring, bumps ring_version, and broadcasts the new epoch.
  • ④ Router → Dy — Dx’s conv_ids migrate to neighbour Dy by the CH K/n key-migration guarantee.
  • ⑤ Dy recovers — green solid = fast path: pull the KV from the Iter 3b pool (linear I/O, ~166 ms for a 13 K conv) if pool-resident; dashed = fallback: βcold(N) recompute (730 ms) otherwise. RPO = 0 either way.

Read the diagram. The sequence runs left to right. A serving-decode task Dx dies mid-flight, losing its local KV; the broker catches it on a heartbeat miss; the Router drops Dx from decode_ring / cp_ring, bumps ring_version, and broadcasts the new epoch so every Router agrees on the topology atomically. Dx’s conv_ids migrate to a neighbour Dy by consistent hashing’s K/n key-migration guarantee (only the dead task’s share moves, not the whole keyspace). Dy then rebuilds via one of two paths: the fast path — if the conv was admitted to the Iter 3b pool, pull its KV by linear I/O (pull(13 K) ≈ 166 ms, the common case for a long/active conv); or the fallback — recompute from prompt_tokens (βcold(13 K) = 730 ms). Either way RPO = 0: the KV is derived state, the tokens live on the client, nothing durable is lost. The right panel is the design argument: recompute-as-fallback costs 0% extra steady-state HBM, vs +100% for full replication or +50% for erasure coding — a clear win because KV is cheap to recompute, so paying steady-state to avoid recompute is bad ROI.

16.2  Why introduce this NOW

  • Both enabling primitives are in hand. Iter 1b’s CH ring is the rebalance primitive — a dead task’s keys migrate to neighbours automatically (the CH K/n guarantee, K15). Iter 1a’s cache-reproducibility is the recompute primitive — KV is not durable state, it is derived from prompt_tokens (RPO = 0, §1.2), so it is always reproducible. Without both, recompute-as-fallback is not an option.
  • The constraint that flipped: availability is the last missing property. Iter 0–9 settle the steady-state design and meet every latency, capacity, and fairness SLO. A working design that drops requests when a task dies is not production-shippable — availability is now the next visible gap.
  • Recompute-as-fallback is the no-storage-overhead answer. RPO = 0 by construction; RTO = βcold(N) recompute (or pull(N) from the pool); 0% extra HBM vs +100% (replication) or +50% (erasure). For LLM serving the trade is clear: KV is cheap to recompute (tokens are on the client), so paying steady-state HBM to avoid recompute is bad ROI.
  • “Why not full 2× replication?” +100% HBM for an event that fires < 1×/task/week. Replication’s only win is RTO = 0 (immediate failover); recompute pays βcold but at 0% steady-state cost — and βcold is already bounded by Iter 4 (packed prefill) + Iter 5 (prefix caching).
  • “Why not erasure coding (6+3)?” Still +50% HBM, plus erasure-decode complexity on the hot decode path, and it saves at most one βcold per failure. Not worth it when βcold itself is bounded.
  • “Why after Iter 9 (CP)?” CP adds a failure mode — a CP-rank dying mid-1 M-request — that FT must cover, so FT needs CP’s topology already on the table. Reversing the order would mean revisiting this iteration once CP landed.

16.3  Design choices

DC1 — Fault response on serving-task death

Problem: a serving task dies with live KV in its HBM. We must restore service for its in-flight conv_ids without paying a steady-state storage tax that does not pencil out for state that is reproducible from data the client already holds.

  • (a) Full replication (2×) — +100% HBM; RTO ≈ 0 (immediate failover). Steady-state cost prohibitive for KV that is cheap to recompute.
  • (b) Erasure coding (6+3) — +50% HBM; ~10 ms recovery; complex erasure-decode on the hot decode path; saves at most one βcold(N) per failure.
  • (c) Recompute-as-fallback + Iter 3b store-pull fast path — CHOSEN: RPO = 0 by construction; per-request RTO ≈ pull(N) (linear I/O from the pool for an admitted conv) else βcold(N) recompute on the rebalanced target; 0% extra steady-state HBM — the pool already exists for cross-turn reuse, so FT reuses it for free. The cheapest option when KV is reproducible from prompt_tokens. K-page: K22.
  • (d) Async replicate KV to a peer task’s DRAM tier — per-block delta-replicate over PCIe + IB; +0% HBM, +~5–15% per-task RDMA-NIC + DRAM-BW; RTO ≈ K × KV_per_tok / RDMA_BW (~a factor of 10 faster than βcold). The middle ground; rejected here because (i) the cross-task NIC budget at peak is already reserved for Iter 2 hand-off + Iter 9 CP all-gather + Iter 3b pool traffic — adding replicate traffic forces a NIC re-derivation; (ii) the recompute path is already bounded by Iter 4 + Iter 5, so the marginal RTO win does not justify the BW reservation. Named as the upgrade path if S6 failure-rate observations push recompute’s tail outside SLO.
  • (e) Checkpoint to NVMe + lazy peer-fetch — a peer reads the dead task’s shared-storage snapshot; only works on shared-NVMe topologies, not this shared-nothing design.

Why (c): when KV is reproducible from client-held tokens, every storage-replication option pays steady-state HBM (or NIC) to avoid a recompute that is already bounded by Iter 4/5 — bad ROI for a < 1×/task/week event. Recompute-as-fallback pays nothing in steady state and, because the Iter 3b pool already holds the cross-turn KV, gets a pull(N) fast path for free on exactly the long/active convs whose recompute would hurt most. Option (d) is the documented upgrade if the measured failure tail ever demands a faster RTO.

16.4  Number chain

# Storage overhead extra_steady_state_HBM = 0% [D] (vs +100% replication, +50% erasure 6+3) # Per-request RTO — two paths pool-resident: pull(N) = N × KV_per_tok / BW_pull pull(13 K) = 13 K × 320 KB / 25 GB/s ≈ 166 ms (clean; ~555 ms under ρfabric=0.3) [D — §4.3] not-resident: βcold(N) = βcold(13 K) = 730 ms recompute on the rebalanced target [D — §4.1] → the pool turns RTO from 730 ms recompute into ~166 ms pull for any admitted conv # Fleet-capacity RTO (autoscale a replacement task) fleet_RTO ≈ 10 min = Borg-schedule (find a free 8×H100 host, 3–7 min) + weight-load (60–180 s, K21) + warmup (~100 ms) [D] # reconciles with Iter 1b cold-start: cold-start is weight-load ONLY (60–180 s); fleet_RTO bundles scheduling + warmup failure_rate ≈ < 1× / task / week [G-FA] → recompute’s per-event cost is paid rarely; 0% steady-state cost is the win client_SSE_RTO = SDK reopen with Last-Event-ID (gateway, not Router)

16.5  What breaks

  1. A CP-rank dies mid-1 M-request (the Iter 9 failure mode). One of the 8 CP-ranks fails during a long decode. Impact: the whole request stalls; recovery pays a longer βcold (re-prefill the lost slice) or a pull if the slice was pooled. Mitigation: same recompute-as-fallback, scoped to the dead rank’s slice rather than the whole request; the sticky conv_id → cp_group_id binding lets the Router re-assign one replacement rank.
  2. In-flight SSE streams die. A task death cuts every open stream it served. Impact: clients see a broken stream mid-token. Mitigation: the client SDK must implement resumable retry — reopen with Last-Event-ID at the gateway; the rebuilt target resumes from the last delivered token (idempotent on conv_id).
  3. Broker partition mid-detection. A broker network partition delays or duplicates the heartbeat-miss signal. Impact: a late drop (requests briefly routed to a dead task → timeout) or a spurious drop (a live task evicted). Mitigation: idempotent ring_version bumps (a duplicate epoch broadcast is a no-op) + a quorum on the 3 HA brokers before a drop commits.
  4. Cascading failure / recovery storm. A correlated failure (rack power, a bad deploy) drops many tasks at once, and a synchronized recompute storm can overload the survivors. Impact: a thundering-herd re-prefill that pushes the fleet past capacity. Mitigation: rate-limited recovery (bound concurrent re-prefills per survivor) + autoscale spinning up replacements; the Iter 3b pool absorbs the pull-path load for admitted convs.

16.6  SLO impact

SLO (from §1.2)Pre-iter (Iter 9)Post-iter (Iter 10)ΔNote
Availability (in-flight on task death)requests droppedrecovered (RPO = 0)↑The headline. A task death no longer loses work — conv_ids migrate via the CH ring and rebuild by pull (pool-resident) or recompute. The last missing production property.
Durability / RPO0 (by construction)0 (now realized on failure)→Always 0 — KV is reproducible from client tokens; this iteration makes the recovery path that uses that property explicit.
TTFT p99 (on a recovery)N/A (dropped)+ pull(N) ~166 ms or βcold 730 ms, one-time↑A recovered request pays a one-time rebuild on the rebalanced target; < 1×/task/week, so it does not move steady-state p99.
TTFT / TPOT p99 (steady state)~106 ms / ~22 ms~106 ms / ~22 ms→No change — recompute-as-fallback adds 0% steady-state cost; the recovery path only fires on failure.
Max-context / Max-output / Fairness1 M / ~65 K / ↑1 M / ~65 K / ↑→No change — FT is orthogonal to the steady-state capacity and fairness properties already met.
$/mo at λpeak(Iter 7–9 baseline)+ 0% (no extra HBM)→0% steady-state overhead — vs +100% (replication) or +50% (erasure). FT is free in steady state because the recompute/pull paths already exist.

Iter 10 retires B11 and closes the design: recompute-as-fallback gives RPO = 0 by construction (KV is reproducible from client-held tokens) and 0% extra steady-state HBM — a clear win over +100% replication or +50% erasure for state this cheap to rebuild. On a task death the CH ring migrates the dead task’s conv_ids to a neighbour, which rebuilds them by a ~166 ms pull(N) from the Iter 3b pool (the common case for a long/active conv) or a 730 ms βcold recompute otherwise; fleet capacity is restored in ~10 min by autoscale. With availability in hand, every functional, latency, capacity, fairness, and durability NFR from §1.2 is met — the steady-state design is complete, and S6 assembles the final summed fleet and $/mo.

17.  S6 — Final Design

What this is: the whole design assembled in one place — the final-fleet architecture (every iteration folded into the job it shaped), the deferred $/mo sum, the degradation contract, and the on-call / rollout playbook. Nothing new is derived — every number is carried from the iteration that produced it (linked in-cell). Sizing convention throughout: tasks = ⌈ λpeak × headroom / per_task_throughput ⌉, headroom = 1.3, $_per_TP8_task_per_mo = $70 790 (§7.4).

17.1  Final fleet architecture (every iteration in one diagram)

The five-job fleet in its settled form. Each serving job carries the mechanisms its iterations added (labelled Iter N inside the box); the control plane (Router + broker), the shared cross-task kv-store-job, and the on-demand cp_ring close out the picture. This is D13 — the “all iterations stacked” view.

  • ① Clients → L4 LB — HTTP opens an SSE stream; the managed L4 LB terminates TLS and holds the stable VIP.
  • ② L4 LB → router-job — round-robin across the 3 HA Router tasks (stateless CPU).
  • ③ Router → prefill (CH on prefill_ring) — the hot-path Route, issued only after the Iter 8 admit gate clears; BLCH spill + autoscale-on-wait apply per pool.
  • ── ④ Prefill → Decode (KV gRPC server-stream) — prefill ships the new turn’s K,V to the conv’s sticky decode task (Iter 2; chunk-streamed since Iter 4).
  • ⑤ Decode → Clients (SSE) — output tokens stream back through the L4 LB; the internal prefill→decode handoff is invisible to the client.
  • ── ⑥⑦ Serving pool ↔ kv-store — write-back on local eviction (async, length-gated > ~1.9 K tok) down; pull on local miss (sync) up; recompute is the RPO = 0 floor (Iter 3b).
  • ┄┄ ⑧ Both pools → broker (async gossip) — each prefill & decode task publishes approx_wait + ring epoch every T_gossip = 100 ms (Iter 1b).
  • ┄┄ ⑨ Broker → Router (async fan-out) — ring epochs + per-task wait sketches fanned out to every Router (read-replica pattern).
  • ⋯⋯ ⑩ Router → cp_ring (on-demand fork) — a request whose ctx > 256 GB (~800 K tok) forks onto the 8-rank CP ring running Ring Attention (Iter 9; ~0.1% of QPS).

17.2  Fleet $/mo across the iteration arc

Each row is the whole-fleet snapshot after that iteration. The two deep cuts are Iter 5 (prefill −86%) and Iter 6–7 (decode ≈50× combined) — decode dominates the total until its own iterations fire.

AfterLeverServing tasks (prefill + decode)Fleet $/mo
Iter 0naive TP=2, no cache (the anchor)1 128 700~$19.97 B
Iter 1bcache + TP=8 + scale-out + Router291 500~$20.6 B
Iter 2–4P/D split · paging · chunked prefill (architectural)291 308 (19 909 P + 271 399 D)~$20.62 B
Iter 5prefix caching → prefill −86% (19 909 → 2 767 P)274 166 (2 767 P + 271 399 D)~$19.41 B
Iter 6continuous batching → decode ~25× (271 399 → 10 793 D)13 560 (2 767 P + 10 793 D)~$0.96 B
Iter 7speculative decoding → decode ~2× (10 793 → 5 396 D)8 163 (2 767 P + 5 396 D)~$578 M
Iter 8–10abuse cap (Router, ~$0) · CP fork (~0.1% QPS) · FT (0% steady HBM)8 163 (+ CP ranks on demand)~$578 M
  • Iter 8/9/10 add ~$0 steady-state GPU. Iter 8 is Router-side admission (~32 MB/Router, no GPU); Iter 9 CP only forks the ~0.1% of QPS above the ~800 K-token threshold onto an 8-rank group (a rounding-error of fleet GPU); Iter 10 FT is recompute-as-fallback (0% extra steady-state HBM, §16).
  • Why the total rose Iter 0 → Iter 1b. Iter 0’s $19.97 B carries no head-room; Iter 1b adds the 30% over-provision (apples-to-apples without it is ~$15.9 B, §7.4). The real-money arc is the $20.6 B → $578 M descent that the decode-side iterations drive.

17.3  Final per-job fleet & cost

JobPer-task throughput (final)Task countPer-task $/mo$/mo
serving-prefill-jobunmatched ~1.85 K tok/req (Iter 5)2 767$70 790~$195.9 M
serving-decode-job~24 req/s (Iter 7: Bdecode × accept-rate)5 396$70 790~$382.0 M
router-job100 K dec/s/task (Iter 1b)3 (HA)CPU-only~negligible
broker-job~2.9 M msg/s (Iter 1b)3 (HA)CPU / mem~negligible
kv-store-jobI/O-sized (Iter 3b)~36~$4 K (no GPU)~$144 K

17.4  Grand total

serving_task_num = prefill_task_num + decode_task_num = 2 767 (Iter 5) + 5 396 (Iter 7) = 8 163 TP=8 H100 tasks (⇒ 8 163 × 8 = 65 304 H100) [D] $/mo_serving = 8 163 × $70 790 = ~$577.9 M / mo [D] $/mo_kv_store = ~36 × ~$4 K = ~$0.14 M / mo [D] router + broker = 6 CPU tasks ≈ negligible vs the GPU fleet ------------------------------------------------------------------ GRAND TOTAL ≈ ~$578 M / month (decode ~$382 M = 66% · prefill ~$196 M = 34%) [D] vs Iter 0 anchor ($19.97 B/mo, 1.13 M TP=2 tasks = 2.26 M GPUs): ~35× cheaper (8 163 TP=8 tasks = 65 K GPUs; cost ∝ GPUs, so 2.26 M → 65 K H100 = 34.6× fewer) vs the §5.4 no-cache floor ($2.07 T/mo): ~3 600× cheaper
SLO close-out — every §1.2 NFR is met at this fleet.
  • TTFT p99 ~106 ms < 400 ms — Iter 5 prefix caching shrinks the common prefill to ~1.85 K tok.
  • TPOT p99 ~22 ms < 30 ms — Iter 6 continuous batching (KV read dominates the tick).
  • Max context 1 M — Iter 9 CP fork above ~800 K tok (~17 ms/tok at 1 M).
  • Per-user fairness — Iter 8 token bucket + max-concurrent-conv cap.
  • Durability RPO = 0 — KV reproducible from client tokens; Iter 10 recompute-as-fallback.
What stays flagged to a measured-workload pass. The headroom (1.3), accept-rate (α=0.7 → ~2×), steady-state Bdecode mean (~106), intra_session_hit_rate (0.80), and the pool working-set (⇒ ~36 store tasks) are all [G-FA] operating points — they scale the absolute $ by a constant factor, not the shape of the arc. The load-bearing results — the ~35× reduction from the anchor and the decode-dominated final mix (66%) — are robust to the flagged inputs.

17.5  Degradation contract — what each failure falls back to

Every dependency has a defined fallback that keeps the system correct — RPO = 0 holds throughout because the KV is always reproducible from the client’s own tokens, so the worst case is always “recompute,” never “lose data.” Rows ordered by widening blast radius.

FailureDetectionFallbackSLO / blast radiusFrom
Broker partition (gossip stops) broker push-lag > few×Tgossip (100 ms) / stale ring epoch Router drops the wait sketches, routes on the CH ring + raw per-task queue depth (no sketches) routing slightly less optimal (queue-depth proxy vs wait-time); no correctness loss, no SLO breach at headroom §7.3
All in-ring replicas hot fleet wait_p95 > Θ×median BLCH spill to the broader ring; if still hot, accept a cache miss (cold-prefill elsewhere) and let autoscale add tasks hit-rate dips, TTFT rises within budget until autoscale catches up (~Iter 1b RTO) §7.3
Tenant exceeds quota per-user token bucket empty / > max_concurrent_conv 429 + Retry-After that user throttled to ≤ 1 req/s & ≤ 5 open convs; the rest of the fleet is protected §14.3
kv-store pool unavailable RDMA pull timeout / CH probe miss cold-prefill on the local serving task (recompute the tail from tokens) that conv’s TTFT rises toward βcold; RPO = 0 preserved — the pool is a victim cache, never the source of truth §9b.5
Serving-task death heartbeat miss CH ring rebalances (vnode remap); the new owner cold-prefills from client tokens (or pulls warm KV from the pool if length-gate-admitted) in-flight requests on the dead task recompute; per-request RTO ≈ one cold prefill; fleet capacity restored by autoscale §16.3

17.6  Operations — on-call metrics, rollout, headroom

The five on-call metrics

MetricWhat it catchesPage when
TTFT p99 (per pool)prefill saturation / cold-miss storm / routing regression> 400 ms, 5 min
TPOT p99decode-tick overrun (batch too large, spec-decode accept-rate drift)> 30 ms, 5 min
Fleet prefix-cache hit-ratecache cliff (eviction policy or CH-stickiness regression)< ~0.7 (vs 0.80 target)
Wait-time fleet p99 (approx_wait)leading indicator — rises before TTFT/TPOT breach; also the autoscale triggersustained > Θ×median
Broker push-laggossip staleness → degraded routing (degradation row 1)> few× Tgossip (100 ms)

approx_wait p99 is the canonical leading signal: it is the one number the routing + autoscale loop already consumes (§4.0, evolving Iter 1a→7), so on-call watches the exact metric the system self-regulates on — a breach there fires before the user-facing SLOs move.

Rollout — shadow → canary → ramp

  • Shadow — mirror a copy of live traffic to the new build; no client effect; diff outputs + the five metrics against production.
  • Canary — bind one CH vnode (a small % of conv_ids) to the new build; watch hit-rate + TTFT/TPOT + an output-quality canary.
  • Ramp — progressively raise the new build’s ring weight; rollback at any gate is a single CH ring-epoch bump (instant, no drain — the same epoch mechanism the broker already gossips).

Headroom — +30% on every pool

Every pool is sized at headroom = 1.3 (+30% over λpeak) so a single rack/AZ loss or an autoscale lag doesn’t breach SLO. The +30% is already inside every task count in 17.3–17.4 (e.g. the 8 163 serving tasks); stripping it gives the ~$15.9 B apples-to-apples Iter 0 comparison noted in 17.2. Routing-tier cost is < 0.1% of GPU cost (§7.4), so the Router/broker tier is over-provisioned without a second thought.

18.  [param] change ledger — every tuned knob, its full progression, and where each step is derived

What this is: the consolidated view of every [param] the design tunes across the iterations — one row per progression step, with the Derived at link jumping to the section that actually does the math. A parameter that evolves over several iterations (the active-batch B, the storage-tier ladder) keeps one merged Parameter cell spanning its steps, so the whole arc reads top-to-bottom. Every number here is carried from its deriving section — nothing is introduced in this table.

Parameter Iter Progression at this step — from → to, and the constraint that picks it Derived at
TP — tensor-parallel degree Iter 1a 2 → 8 — stays inside one NVLink-4 domain (TP=16 crosses to NIC AllReduce, ~50× slower) and leaves ~56 GB/GPU + 26.8 TB/s aggregate HBM BW for the later decode batch. Cliffs on both sides. §6.4 · [param]
KV storage tiers — where evicted/dormant KV parks Iter 1a HBM-only → HBM + DRAM + NVMe-SSD — HBM-only sends every eviction to βcold (730 ms, ~2× over the TTFT SLO); DRAM (βDRAM 166 ms) + single-SSD (βSSD 594 ms) keep evicted KV ~4× faster than re-prefill, on hardware already in the box. §6.4 · [param]
Iter 3 + per-paged-block tier placement — residency now tracked at 16-tok block granularity, so a task holds ≈ 2 819 resident conv KVs across HBM/DRAM/SSD instead of only what fits whole in HBM. §9.4
Iter 3b retire local NVMe → HBM + DRAM local + cold tier in the remote pool — the RDMA-fronted pool (~25 GB/s DRAM front) supersedes local NVMe (7 GB/s) and survives a task reroute (local tiers die with the task). Reverses the Iter 1a SSD rung. §9b.3 · [param]
B — active batch (concurrent in-flight requests per task) Iter 1a chosen B=1 (TTFT-bound). Static-max HBM ceiling, per regime: mean 13 K + 65 K = 78 K × 320 KB ≈ 25 GB/slot → B=18; p99 256 K + 65 K → ~103 GB/slot → B≈4; max 1 M + 65 K → ~341 GB/slot → B=1 (contiguous slab must reserve worst-case per slot). §6.4 · DC4
Iter 2 unified B splits into prefill B=1 + decode B (independently sized) — prefill is compute-bound, decode BW-bound; one pool forced a shared roofline. Decode B stays 1 here, but is now free to grow on its own. §8.3 · [param]
Iter 3 PagedAttention shrinks the per-slot accounting unit ~5.9× → ceiling Bmax,static ≈ 18 → Bmax,paged ≈ 106. Pure capacity unlock — realized B is still 1; this is the headroom Iter 6 spends. §9.5
Iter 6 continuous batching consumes the headroom — decode B: 1 → Bdecode ≈ 106. One weight read per tick amortized across the batch (MBU ~1% → KV-BW ceiling); realized batch is TPOT-SLO-bounded. §12.3 · [param]
block_size — PagedAttention block granularity Iter 3 contiguous static-max slab → 16-token paged blocks — static-max wastes ~5.9× (mean) / ~24× (p99) per slot; paging ends over-reservation and is what lifts the B ceiling above (see B · Iter 3). Cliff: larger blocks → more internal fragmentation; smaller → more block-table overhead; 16 is the vLLM sweet spot. §9.3 · [param]
Θ — BLCH spill threshold Iter 1b — → 1.5–2.0 (spill budget ≤ 5%/epoch) — a sticky CH-on-conv_id route spills to the second-choice vnode when wait > Θ × global-median. Elbow between Θ=1 (locality collapses) and Θ→∞ (unbounded whale tasks). §7.3 · [param]
length gate — pool write-back admission Iter 3b none → admit iff cumulative ctx > ≈ 1.9 K tok — store back only KV whose later re-fetch beats recompute (the SSD recompute-vs-fetch crossover). Write-through every turn would cost ~3.84 TB/s of mostly-never-read ingest. §9b.3 · [param]
chunk_size C — prefill slice width Iter 4 ∞ (whole-request prefill) → C ∈ [4 K, 8 K] (worked at 4 K) — a whole 256 K prefill HoL-blocks the pool ~65 s; slicing bounds the block to βchunk ≈ 190 ms. Floor: below ~4 K, MFU < 40%; ceiling: above ~8 K, activation + HoL grow. §10.3 · [param]
B_pack — chunks packed per prefill kernel Iter 4 1 (Stage A) → B_pack (Stage B) → varlen (Stage C) — one chunk under-fills the SMs; packing saturates them but re-pays per-slot padding; varlen concatenates live chunks into one cu_seqlens buffer, collapsing waste from O(B_pack × C)/tick to one tile-width. §10.3 · [param]
HBM retention policy — what stays resident Iter 5 3-source hard pin-list → reuse-recency LRU + ref_count (no hard pin) — a hard pin needs a hot-set oracle and can starve the cache; ref_count gives correctness for free and reuse-recency self-tunes to the rotating hot set (eviction = a recoverable pool fetch, not OOM). §11.3 · [param]
unmatched_tokens(r) — mean prefill work / request Iter 5 Tin_mean 13.3 K → ~1.85 K — content-addressed prefix caching reuses hidden_sys (~100%) + user_sys (~95%) + warm conv_hist (80% of turns), so the common request prefills only its unmatched tail. Drives the ~86% fleet prefill cut. §11.6 · [param]
split_kv — Flash Decoding KV-split Iter 6 1 (FA v2, Q-tiled) → split_kv chunks (KV-seq-split) — at decode Q=1 there is no query dimension to tile, so SMs starve at long context; splitting the KV sequence across SM groups + a log-sum-exp reduce restores occupancy. §12.3 · [param]
CUDA-graph capture set — replayed decode shapes Iter 6 eager (no graph) → captured set (dense head {1,2,4,8} + constant step to Bdecode) — eager launch costs ~5–10%/tick at 200 Hz; replay a fixed shape, picking the smallest captured B ≥ active_B and padding with no-ops. Spacing trades graph count vs pad waste. §12.3 · [param]
accept_rate_speedup_factor — speculative-decode multiplier Iter 7 1× (vanilla) → ≈ 2.0× — a ~1B draft proposes γ=4 tokens; at α=0.7 the target accepts E[accept]≈2.77/forward, net ~2× after draft + rollback overhead. Cliff: γ too large wastes draft work past the accept run; α drifts with workload — per-tenant monitor + vanilla fallback. Output is identical to vanilla sampling (cost-only). §13.3 · [param]
admit policy — per-user rate-limit Iter 8 FIFO → token bucket (capacity 10, refill 1/s) + max_concurrent_conv = 5 — allows a normal user’s burst then clamps the ~0.1% abusers to ≤ 1 req/s & ≤ 5 open convs. Cliff: capacity too low clips legit bursts (UX), too high lets abusers spike; tiered by tier_class. O(1)/admit, ~32 MB/Router. §14.3 · [param]
CP_FORK_THRESHOLD_BYTES — CP-ring fork cutoff Iter 9 — → 256 GB ≈ 800 K tok — above it a single request’s KV can’t fit one task at useful B>1, so the Router forks to the 8-rank cp_ring (Ring Attention). Cliff: 50–80 GB over-forks at p99 (~1% of QPS); 320 GB misses sub-1 M long-docs that also blow HBM. Fires for ~0.1% of QPS. §15.3 · [param]
